The Da Vinci Code

FACT:

The Priory of Sion—a European secret society started in 1099—is a real group. In 1975, the National Library of Paris found documents called Les Dossiers Secrets, listing many members of the Priory of Sion, like Sir Isaac Newton, Botticelli, Victor Hugo, and Leonardo da Vinci.

The Vatican group called Opus Dei is a very religious Catholic group that has been in the news lately because of stories about mind control, pressure, and a harmful practice called "corporal mortification." Opus Dei just finished building a $47 million world headquarters at 243 Lexington Avenue in New York City.

All descriptions of artwork, buildings, documents, and secret customs in this book are correct.

Prologue

Louvre Museum, Paris 10:46 P.M.

Famous curator Jacques Saunière stumbled through the arched entrance of the museum's Grand Gallery. He reached for the closest painting, a Caravaggio. Holding the gold frame, the seventy-six-year-old man pulled the artwork toward him until it came off the wall, and Saunière fell backward under the canvas.

As he expected, a loud iron gate slammed shut nearby, blocking the entrance to the area. The wooden floor vibrated. In the distance, an alarm went off.

The curator paused, catching his breath, assessing the situation. I am still alive. He crawled out from under the canvas and looked around the large space for a place to hide. A voice spoke, very close. "Do not move."

On his hands and knees, the curator froze, turning his head slowly.

Only fifteen feet away, outside the sealed gate, the huge form of his attacker stared through the iron bars. He was wide and tall, with pale skin and thinning white hair. His eyes were pink with dark red pupils. The albino drew a pistol from his coat and aimed it through the bars, directly at the curator. "You shouldn't have run." His accent was hard to place. "Now tell me where it is."

"I already told you," the curator stammered, kneeling defenseless on the floor of the gallery. "I have no idea what you're talking about!"

"You're lying." The man stared at him, perfectly still except for the gleam in his pale eyes.

"You and your group have something that doesn't belong to you."

The curator felt a rush of adrenaline. How could he possibly know this?

"Tonight the true guardians will get it back. Tell me where it's hidden, and you'll live." The man pointed his gun at the curator's head. "Is it a secret you'll die for?" Saunière couldn't breathe.

The man tilted his head, looking down the barrel of his gun.

Saunière held up his hands in defense. "Wait," he said slowly. "I'll tell you what you need to know." The curator spoke his next words carefully. The lie he told was one he had practiced many times... each time hoping he would never have to use it.

When the curator had finished speaking, his attacker smiled with satisfaction. "Yes. That's exactly what the others told me."

Saunière recoiled. The others?

"I found them, too," the huge man sneered. "All three of them. They confirmed what you have just said."

It cannot be! The curator's real identity, along with the identities of his three sénéchaux, was nearly as protected as the ancient secret they guarded. Saunière now understood his sénéchaux, following strict rules, had told the same lie before they died. It was part of the plan.

The attacker aimed his gun again. "When you are gone, I will be the only one who knows the truth."

The truth. In an instant, the curator understood the real horror of what was happening. If I die, the truth will be lost forever. Without thinking, he tried to crawl away to safety.

The gun fired, and the curator felt a burning heat as the bullet hit his stomach. He fell forward... fighting the pain. Slowly, Saunière rolled over and looked back through the bars at his attacker.

The man was now aiming right at Saunière's head.

Saunière closed his eyes, his thoughts a jumble of fear and sadness. The click of an empty gun echoed in the corridor.

The curator's eyes snapped open.

The man looked down at his weapon, seeming almost entertained. He reached for another magazine, but then seemed to change his mind, smirking calmly at Saunière's stomach. "My work here is done." The curator looked down and saw the bullet hole in his white linen shirt. It was surrounded by a small circle of blood a few inches below his breastbone. My stomach. Cruelly, the bullet had missed his heart. As a veteran of la Guerre d'Algérie, the curator had seen this terrible, slow death before. For fifteen minutes, he would live as his stomach acids leaked into his chest, slowly poisoning him.

"Pain is good, sir," the man said.

Then he was gone.

Now alone, Jacques Saunière looked again at the iron gate. He was stuck, and the doors would not open for at least twenty minutes. By the time anyone reached him, he would be dead. Even so, the fear he felt now was worse than the fear of dying. I must pass on the secret.

He stood up, thinking of his three murdered friends. He thought of the generations before them... of the mission they all had. A continuous chain of knowledge.

Suddenly, despite all the safety measures... despite all the backups... Jacques Saunière was the only one left, the only protector of one of the most important secrets ever kept. Shivering, he stood up.

I must find a way....

He was trapped inside the Grand Gallery, and there was only one person he could trust with the secret. Saunière looked up at the walls of his fancy prison. The world's most famous paintings seemed to smile at him like old friends. Wincing in pain, he gathered his thoughts and strength. He knew that the desperate task ahead would take every last second of his life.

CHAPTER 1

Robert Langdon woke up slowly.

A telephone was ringing in the dark—a thin, strange ring. He reached for the lamp and turned it on. Looking around, he saw a fancy bedroom with old-style furniture, painted walls, and a huge bed. Where am I?

The robe hanging on the bed had the letters: HOTEL RITZ PARIS. Slowly, he started to remember.

Langdon answered the phone. "Hello?"

"Monsieur Langdon?" a man said. "I hope I didn't wake you?" Confused, Langdon looked at the clock. It was 12:32 A.M. He had only been asleep for an hour, but he felt exhausted.

"This is the front desk, sir. Sorry to bother you, but someone is here to see you. He says it's important."

Langdon still felt unclear. A visitor? He saw a crumpled flyer on the table.

THE AMERICAN UNIVERSITY OF PARIS is proud to present AN EVENING WITH ROBERT LANGDON PROFESSOR OF RELIGIOUS SYMBOLOGY, HARVARD UNIVERSITY

Langdon groaned. Tonight's lecture—a slide show about pagan symbols hidden in the stones of Chartres Cathedral—had probably bothered some conservative people in the audience. Most likely, some religious expert had followed him home to start an argument.

"I'm sorry," Langdon said, "but I'm very tired and—"

"Mais, monsieur," the concierge insisted, lowering his voice to an urgent whisper. "Your guest is an important man."

Langdon didn't doubt it. His books on religious paintings and cult symbols had made him a reluctant celebrity in the art world, and last year Langdon's fame had increased greatly after he was involved in a well-known event at the Vatican. Since then, the endless stream of self-important historians and art lovers arriving at his door seemed never-ending.

"If you would be so kind," Langdon said, trying to remain polite, "could you take the man's name and number, and tell him I'll try to call him before I leave Paris on Tuesday? Thank you." He hung up before the concierge could complain.

Sitting up now, Langdon frowned at his bedside Guest Relations Handbook, whose cover read: SLEEP LIKE A BABY IN THE CITY OF LIGHTS. SLUMBER AT THE PARIS RITZ. He turned and gazed tiredly into the full-length mirror across the room. The man staring back at him was a stranger—disheveled and weary.

You need a vacation, Robert.

The past year had been hard on him, but he didn't like seeing the proof in the mirror. His usually bright blue eyes looked tired and dull tonight. Dark stubble covered his strong jaw and dimpled chin. The gray around his temples was spreading, going further into his thick black hair. His female colleagues said the gray made him look more attractive in an intellectual way, but Langdon knew better.

If Boston Magazine could see me now.

Last month, to Langdon's dismay, Boston Magazine had named him one of the ten most interesting people in Boston—a strange honor that led to endless jokes from his Harvard colleagues. Tonight, three thousand miles from home, the award had come back to bother him at the lecture he was giving.

"Ladies and gentlemen..." the hostess announced to a packed room at the American University of Paris's Pavilion Dauphine, "Our guest tonight needs no introduction. He is the author of many books: The Symbology of Secret Sects, The Art of the Illuminati, The Lost Language of Ideograms, and when I say he wrote the book on Religious Iconology, I mean it. Many of you use his textbooks in class."

The students in the crowd nodded eagerly.

"I was going to introduce him by sharing his impressive resume. However..." She looked playfully at Langdon, who was sitting onstage. "Someone in the audience has just given me a much more... intriguing introduction."

She held up a copy of Boston Magazine.

Langdon cringed. Where did she get that?

The hostess started reading parts of the silly article, and Langdon felt himself sinking in his chair. Thirty seconds later, the crowd was smiling, and the woman didn't seem to want to stop. "And Mr. Langdon's refusal to talk about his unusual role in last year's Vatican meeting certainly makes him more intriguing." The hostess teased the crowd. "Would you like to hear more?"

The crowd applauded.

Somebody stop her, Langdon begged as she dove back into the article.

"Although Professor Langdon might not be considered classically handsome like some of our younger winners, this forty-something academic has plenty of scholarly charm. His captivating presence is highlighted by an unusually low, baritone voice, which his female students describe as 'chocolate for the ears.'"

The hall burst into laughter.

Langdon forced a strained smile. He knew what was coming—some silly line about

"Harrison Ford in Harris tweed"—and because he had thought it was finally safe to wear his Harris tweed and Burberry turtleneck this evening, he decided to act.

"Thank you, Monique," Langdon said, standing up too soon and guiding her away from the podium. "Boston Magazine clearly has a talent for fiction." He turned to the audience with an embarrassed sigh. "And if I find out who gave them that article, I'll have you deported."

The crowd laughed.

"Well, folks, as you all know, I'm here tonight to talk about the power of symbols..."

The ringing of Langdon's hotel phone broke the silence again.

He answered the phone with a groan. "Yes?"

It was the concierge, just as he thought. "Mr. Langdon, I'm very sorry to bother you again. I'm calling to let you know that your guest is on his way to your room. I wanted to warn you." Langdon was now fully awake. "You sent someone to my room?"

"I'm sorry, sir, but a man like this... I couldn't stop him."

"Who is he?"

But the concierge had hung up.

Almost at once, someone banged hard on Langdon's door.

Feeling unsure, Langdon got out of bed, his toes sinking into the soft carpet. He put on the hotel bathrobe and went to the door. "Who is it?"

"Mr. Langdon? I need to talk to you." The man's English had an accent—a harsh, commanding tone. "My name is Lieutenant Jerome Collet. Direction Centrale Police Judiciaire." Langdon stopped. The Judicial Police? The DCPJ was like the U.S. FBI. Leaving the chain on, Langdon opened the door a crack. The face looking back at him was pale and gaunt. The man was very thin, wearing a blue uniform that looked official.

"May I come in?" the agent asked.

Langdon paused, feeling uneasy as the stranger's pale eyes examined him. "What's this about?"

"My captain needs your help with something personal."

"Now?" Langdon asked, surprised. "It's after midnight."

"Are you the one who was supposed to meet with the Louvre's curator tonight?" Langdon felt a sudden worry. He and the respected curator Jacques Saunière had planned to have drinks after Langdon's talk, but Saunière never arrived. "Yes. How did you know?"

"We saw your name in his schedule."

"I hope everything is okay?"

The agent sighed heavily and slipped a Polaroid picture through the small opening in the door. When Langdon saw it, his body froze.

"This picture was taken less than an hour ago. Inside the Louvre." As Langdon looked at the strange image, his initial disgust and shock turned into anger. "Who would do this?"

"We were hoping you could help us figure that out, since you know about symbols and were meeting with him."

Langdon stared at the picture, his horror now mixed with fear. The image was awful and very strange, giving him an uneasy feeling of déjà vu. Just over a year ago, Langdon had received a picture of a dead body and a similar request for help. A day later, he almost died in Vatican City. This photo was completely different, but something about the situation felt disturbingly familiar.

The agent looked at his watch. "My captain is waiting, sir." Langdon barely heard him. He was still staring at the picture. "This symbol here, and the way his body is so strangely..."

"In place?" the agent asked.

Langdon nodded, feeling a chill as he looked up. "I can't imagine who would do this to someone." The agent looked grim. "You don't understand, Mr. Langdon. What you see in this photograph..." He paused. "Monsieur Saunière did that to himself."

CHAPTER 2

One mile away, the huge albino named Silas limped through the front gate of the fancy brownstone house on Rue La Bruyère. The spiked belt he wore around his leg cut into his skin, but his soul felt happy, knowing he was serving the Lord. Pain is good.

His red eyes looked around the lobby as he entered the house. Empty. He climbed the stairs quietly, not wanting to wake any of the others who lived there. His bedroom door was open; locks were not allowed here. He went in, closing the door behind him.

The room was simple—hardwood floors, a plain dresser, a canvas mat in the corner that was his bed. He was a visitor here this week, but for many years he had been lucky enough to have a similar safe place in New York City.

The Lord has given me shelter and a reason to live.

Tonight, Silas felt he had finally started to pay back what he owed. He hurried to the dresser, found the cell phone hidden in the bottom drawer, and made a call.

"Yes?" a man's voice answered.

"Teacher, I have returned."

"Speak," the voice ordered, sounding glad to hear from him.

"All four are gone. The three leaders... and the Head Master himself." There was a short pause, as if for prayer. "Then I assume you have the information?"

"All four agreed. Separately."

"And you believed them?"

"They agreed too much for it to be a coincidence."

An excited breath. "Excellent. I was afraid the brotherhood's reputation for secrecy would win."

"The fear of death is a strong motivator."

"So, my student, tell me what I need to know."

Silas knew the information he had gotten from his victims would be shocking. "Teacher, all four confirmed the existence of the clef de voûte... the legendary keystone." He heard a quick breath over the phone and could feel the Teacher's excitement. "The keystone. Just as we thought."

According to legend, the brotherhood had created a map of stone—a clef de voûte... or keystone —an engraved tablet that showed the final resting place of the brotherhood's greatest secret... information so powerful that protecting it was the reason the brotherhood existed.

"When we have the keystone," the Teacher said, "we will be almost there."

"We are closer than you think. The keystone is here in Paris."

"Paris? Incredible. It is almost too easy."

Silas told the story of what happened earlier that night... how each of his four victims, right before dying, had desperately tried to save their sinful lives by telling their secret. They all told Silas the same thing—that the keystone was cleverly hidden in a specific spot inside one of Paris's old churches—the Eglise de Saint-Sulpice.

"Inside a house of the Lord," the Teacher cried out. "How they make fun of us!"

"As they have for centuries."

The Teacher paused, as if letting the victory of this moment sink in. Finally, he spoke.

"You have done a great service to God. We have waited centuries for this. You must get the stone for me. Right away. Tonight. You know what's at risk."

Silas knew the stakes were huge, but what the Teacher was asking now seemed impossible. "But the church is like a fortress. Especially at night. How will I get in?" With the assured tone of a man with great power, the Teacher explained what to do.

When Silas hung up the phone, his skin felt alive with excitement.

One hour, he told himself, thankful that the Teacher had given him time to do what was needed to cleanse himself before entering a house of God. I must rid my soul of today's sins. The sins he committed today had a sacred purpose. Acts of war against God's enemies had been going on for centuries. Forgiveness was certain.

Even so, Silas knew, forgiveness needed sacrifice.

Pulling his shades, he stripped naked and knelt in the center of his room. Looking down, he examined the spiked cilice belt clamped around his thigh. All true followers of The Way wore this device—a leather strap, studded with sharp metal barbs that cut into the flesh as a constant reminder of Christ's suffering. The pain caused by the device also helped fight against the desires of the flesh.

Although Silas already had worn his cilice today longer than the required two hours, he knew today was no ordinary day. Grasping the buckle, he cinched it one notch tighter, wincing as the barbs dug deeper into his flesh. Exhaling slowly, he savored the cleansing ritual of his pain. Pain is good, Silas whispered, repeating the sacred mantra of Father Josemaría Escrivá—the Teacher of all Teachers. Although Escrivá had died in 1975, his wisdom lived on, his words still whispered by thousands of faithful servants around the globe as they knelt on the floor and performed the sacred practice known as "corporal mortification." Silas turned his attention now to a heavy knotted rope coiled neatly on the floor beside him. The Discipline. The knots were caked with dried blood. Eager for the purifying effects of his own agony, Silas said a quick prayer. Then, gripping one end of the rope, he closed his eyes and swung it hard over his shoulder, feeling the knots slap against his back. He whipped it over his shoulder again, slashing at his flesh. Again and again, he lashed.

Castigo corpus meum.

Finally, he felt the blood begin to flow.

CHAPTER 3

The sharp April air rushed through the open window of the Citroën ZX as it sped south past the Opera House and crossed Place Vendôme. In the passenger seat, Robert Langdon felt the city blur by as he tried to clear his thoughts. His quick shower and shave had made him look reasonably neat but had done little to calm his worry. The horrifying image of the curator's body stayed stuck in his mind.

Jacques Saunière is dead.

Langdon couldn't help but feel a deep sense of loss at the curator's death. Despite Saunière's reputation for being private, his recognition for dedication to the arts made him easy to respect. His books on the secret codes hidden in the paintings of Poussin and Teniers were some of Langdon's favorite texts to use in class. He had been very much looking forward to tonight's meeting, and he was disappointed when the curator hadn't shown up. Again the image of the curator's body flashed in his mind. Jacques Saunière did that to himself?

Langdon turned and looked out the window, forcing the picture out of his mind. Outside, the city was just now settling down for the night—street vendors pushing carts of candied almonds, waiters carrying bags of garbage to the curb, a pair of late night lovers cuddling to stay warm in a breeze that smelled like jasmine blossoms. The Citroën moved through the chaos easily, its loud, two-tone siren cutting through the traffic like a knife.

"Le capitaine was pleased to find out you were still in Paris tonight," the agent said, speaking for the first time since they'd left the hotel. "A lucky coincidence." Langdon felt anything but lucky, and he didn't completely trust the idea of coincidence. As someone who had spent his life studying the hidden connections between different symbols and ideas, Langdon saw the world as a web of deeply connected histories and events. The connections may be invisible, he often told his symbology classes at Harvard, but they are always there, hidden just beneath the surface.

"I assume," Langdon said, "that the American University of Paris told you where I was staying?" The driver shook his head. "Interpol."

Interpol, Langdon thought. Of course. He had forgotten that the seemingly harmless request of all European hotels to see a passport at check-in was more than just a formality—it was the law. On any given night, all across Europe, Interpol officials could know exactly who was sleeping where. Finding Langdon at the Ritz had probably taken only five seconds. As the Citroën sped south across the city, the bright outline of the Eiffel Tower appeared, reaching upward in the distance to the right. Seeing it, Langdon thought of Vittoria, remembering their playful promise a year ago that every six months they would meet again at a different romantic spot on the globe. The Eiffel Tower, Langdon thought, would have been on their list. Sadly, he had last kissed Vittoria in a noisy airport in Rome more than a year ago.

"Did you mount her?" the agent asked, looking over.

Langdon glanced up, sure he had misheard. "I'm sorry?"

"She is lovely, yes?" The agent pointed through the windshield at the Eiffel Tower. "Have you been up it?"

Langdon rolled his eyes. "No, I haven't climbed the tower."

"It is the symbol of France. I think it is perfect."

Langdon nodded absently. Experts often joked that France—a country known for its macho image, womanizing, and short, insecure leaders like Napoleon and Pepin the Short—couldn't have picked a more fitting national symbol than a giant phallus. When they reached the intersection at Rue de Rivoli, the light was red, but the Citroën didn't slow down. The agent sped the car through the intersection and onto a wooded part of Rue Castiglione, which led to the north entrance of the Tuileries Gardens—Paris's version of Central Park. Most tourists thought Jardins des Tuileries meant the thousands of tulips that bloomed there, but Tuileries actually referred to something less romantic. The park had once been a large, dirty pit where Parisian builders dug clay to make the city's red roofing tiles—or tuiles. As they entered the empty park, the agent reached under the dashboard and turned off the loud siren. Langdon sighed, enjoying the sudden quiet. Outside the car, the headlights shone on the gravel path, and the tires made a steady, hypnotic sound. Langdon had always thought of the Tuileries as a special place. It was in these gardens that Claude Monet had experimented with form and color, inspiring the Impressionist movement. But tonight, it felt strangely ominous. The Citroën turned left, heading west down the park's main path. It went around a circular pond, then across a deserted road into a large open area. Langdon could now see the end of the Tuileries Gardens, marked by a large stone arch. Arc du Carrousel.

Despite the wild rituals once held at the Arc du Carrousel, art lovers admired this place for another reason. From the open area at the end of the Tuileries, you could see four of the world's best art museums... one in each direction. Out the right window, south across the Seine, Langdon saw the brightly lit front of the old train station—now the Musée d'Orsay. Looking left, he could see the top of the modern Pompidou Center, which housed the Museum of Modern Art. Behind him to the west, Langdon knew the ancient obelisk of Ramses stood above the trees, marking the Musée du Jeu de Paume.

But it was straight ahead, to the east, through the archway, that Langdon now saw the massive Renaissance palace that had become the most famous art museum in the world. Musée du Louvre.

Langdon felt a familiar sense of awe as he tried to take in the entire building. Across a huge plaza, the Louvre's impressive front rose like a fortress against the Paris sky. Shaped like a giant horseshoe, the Louvre was the longest building in Europe, longer than three Eiffel Towers placed end to end. Even the large open plaza between the museum wings couldn't compete with the grandeur of the building's width. Langdon had once walked the entire perimeter of the Louvre, an amazing three-mile walk.

Although it would take about five days to truly appreciate the 65,300 works of art in the museum, most tourists chose a quick visit Langdon called "Louvre Lite"—a fast run through the museum to see the three most famous pieces: the Mona Lisa, Venus de Milo, and Winged Victory. Art Buchwald had once joked that he'd seen all three masterpieces in five minutes and fifty-six seconds.

The driver took out a walkie-talkie and spoke quickly in French. "Monsieur Langdon est arrivé. Deux minutes."

An unclear reply crackled back.

The agent put away the device and turned to Langdon. "You will meet the capitaine at the main entrance."

The driver ignored the signs that said cars weren't allowed on the plaza, revved the engine, and drove the Citroën over the curb. The Louvre's main entrance was now visible, standing out in the distance, surrounded by seven triangular pools with illuminated fountains. La Pyramide.

The new entrance to the Paris Louvre had become almost as famous as the museum itself. The controversial, modern glass pyramid designed by the Chinese-American architect I. M. Pei still made traditionalists angry, as they felt it ruined the elegance of the Renaissance courtyard. Goethe had described architecture as frozen music, and Pei's critics said this pyramid was like nails on a chalkboard. But fans of progress praised Pei's seventy-one-foot-tall transparent pyramid as a stunning mix of old structure and new methods—a symbolic bridge between the past and present—helping the Louvre move into the next thousand years.

"Do you like our pyramid?" the agent asked.

Langdon frowned. It seemed the French loved to ask Americans this. It was a tricky question, of course. If you said you liked the pyramid, you seemed like an American with bad taste, and if you said you didn't like it, you were insulting the French.

"Mitterrand was a bold man," Langdon replied, choosing a middle ground. The late French president who had ordered the pyramid was said to have a "Pharaoh complex." He was responsible for filling Paris with Egyptian obelisks, art, and artifacts. François Mitterrand was so interested in Egyptian culture that the French still called him the Sphinx.

"What is the captain's name?" Langdon asked, changing the subject.

"Bezu Fache," the driver said, as they approached the pyramid's main entrance. "We call him le Taureau." Langdon glanced at him, wondering if every Frenchman had a mysterious animal nickname.

"You call your captain the Bull?"

The man raised his eyebrows. "Your French is better than you let on, Monsieur Langdon." My French is terrible, Langdon thought, but I know a lot about zodiac symbols. Taurus was always the bull. Astrology was a symbol that stayed the same all over the world.

The agent stopped the car and pointed between two fountains to a large door in the side of the pyramid. "There's the entrance. Good luck, monsieur."

"You're not coming?"

"My orders are to leave you here. I have other things to do." Langdon sighed and got out. It's your circus.

The agent started the engine and drove off quickly.

As Langdon stood alone and watched the taillights disappear, he knew he could easily change his mind, leave the courtyard, get a taxi, and go home to bed. But something told him it was a bad idea.

As he walked toward the mist from the fountains, Langdon felt like he was crossing an invisible line into another world. The dreamlike feeling of the evening was surrounding him again. Twenty minutes ago he had been asleep in his hotel room. Now he was standing in front of a clear pyramid built by the Sphinx, waiting for a policeman called the Bull. I'm trapped in a Salvador Dali painting, he thought.

Langdon walked to the main entrance—a huge revolving door. The lobby inside was dimly lit and empty.

Do I knock?

Langdon wondered if any of Harvard's respected Egyptologists had ever knocked on the front door of a pyramid and expected an answer. He raised his hand to hit the glass, but from the darkness below, a figure appeared, walking up the curved staircase. The man was short and dark, almost like a Neanderthal, wearing a dark suit that was tight across his wide shoulders. He came forward with clear authority on short, strong legs. He was talking on his cell phone but ended the call when he arrived. He gestured for Langdon to come in.

"I am Bezu Fache," he said as Langdon pushed through the revolving door. "Captain of the Central Directorate Judicial Police." His voice matched his appearance—a deep rumble... like a storm brewing.

Langdon held out his hand to shake. "Robert Langdon."

Fache's large hand gripped Langdon's tightly.

"I saw the photo," Langdon said. "Your agent said Jacques Saunière himself—"

"Mr. Langdon," Fache's dark eyes stared intently. "What you see in the photo is just the start of what Saunière did."

CHAPTER 4

Captain Bezu Fache moved like an angry ox, his wide shoulders back and his chin tucked in. His dark hair was slicked back, showing a sharp widow's peak that looked like the front of a ship. As he walked, his dark eyes seemed to burn the ground, showing a fierce focus that matched his reputation for strictness.

Langdon followed the captain down the marble staircase into the atrium under the glass pyramid. They passed two armed police guards with machine guns. The message was clear: No one enters or leaves without Captain Fache's permission.

Going underground, Langdon felt uneasy. Fache's presence was not friendly, and the Louvre felt like a tomb at this hour. The staircase was lit by soft lights in each step, like in a dark movie theater. Langdon heard his footsteps echo off the glass above. He looked up and saw the faint mist from the fountains fading against the transparent roof.

"Do you like it?" Fache asked, nodding up with his chin. Langdon sighed, too tired to play games. "Yes, your pyramid is magnificent." Fache grunted. "A scar on the face of Paris."

Strike one. Langdon knew his host was hard to please. He wondered if Fache knew that this pyramid, built at President Mitterrand's request, had exactly 666 panes of glass—a strange detail that conspiracy theorists often discussed, claiming 666 was the number of Satan.

Langdon decided not to mention it.

As they went deeper into the underground foyer, the large space slowly came into view. Built fifty-seven feet below ground, the Louvre's new 70,000-square-foot lobby stretched out like a vast cave. Made of warm ocher marble to match the Louvre's honey-colored stone above, the hall was usually full of sunlight and tourists. But tonight, it was empty and dark, giving the space a cold, tomb-like feel.

"What about the museum's regular security staff?" Langdon asked.

"In quarantine," Fache replied, sounding as if Langdon were questioning his team's honesty. "Clearly, someone got in tonight who wasn't supposed to. All Louvre night guards are in the Sully Wing being questioned. My own agents have taken over museum security for the night."

Langdon nodded, hurrying to keep up with Fache.

"How well did you know Jacques Saunière?" the captain asked.

"Actually, not at all. We never met."

Fache looked surprised. "Tonight was supposed to be your first meeting?"

"Yes. We planned to meet at the American University reception after my lecture, but he never arrived."

Fache scribbled some notes in a small book. As they walked, Langdon caught a glimpse of the Louvre's lesser-known pyramid— La Pyramide Inversée —a huge upside-down skylight that hung from the ceiling like a stalactite in a nearby part of the mezzanine. Fache led Langdon up a short flight of stairs to the entrance of an arched tunnel, above which a sign read: DENON. The Denon Wing was the most famous of the Louvre's three main sections.

"Who asked for tonight's meeting?" Fache asked suddenly. "You or him?" The question seemed strange. "Mr. Saunière did," Langdon replied as they entered the tunnel. "His secretary contacted me a few weeks ago by email. She said the curator had heard I was lecturing in Paris this month and wanted to talk about something with me while I was here."

"Talk about what?"

"I don't know. Art, I guess. We have similar interests."

Fache looked doubtful. "You have no idea what your meeting was about?" Langdon did not. He'd been curious then but hadn't felt right asking for details. The respected Jacques Saunière was known for his privacy and gave very few meetings; Langdon was thankful just to meet him.

"Mr. Langdon, can you at least guess what our murder victim wanted to talk about with you on the night he was killed? It might help."

The directness of the question made Langdon uneasy. "I really can't guess. I didn't ask. I was honored to be contacted. I admire Mr. Saunière's work. I use his books often in my classes."

Fache wrote that down in his book.

The two men were now halfway up the Denon Wing's entry tunnel, and Langdon could see the two escalators at the far end, both still.

"So you had things in common?" Fache asked.

"Yes. I've spent most of the last year writing a book that covers Mr. Saunière's main area of knowledge. I wanted to get his thoughts on it." Fache looked up. "Sorry?"

The saying didn't seem to translate. "I wanted to hear what he thought about the topic."

"I see. And what is the topic?"

Langdon paused, unsure how to explain. "It's about the symbols of goddess worship—the idea of female holiness and the art and symbols related to it."

Fache ran a thick hand through his hair. "And Saunière knew about this?"

"Nobody knew more."

"I see."

Langdon felt Fache did not see at all. Jacques Saunière was known as the world's leading expert on goddess symbols. Saunière was very interested in objects related to fertility, goddess worship, Wicca, and the sacred feminine. Also, during his twenty years as curator, Saunière had helped the Louvre gather the largest collection of goddess art in the world—double axes from the priestesses' ancient Greek temple in Delphi, gold serpent wands, hundreds of Tjet symbols that looked like small standing angels, sistrum rattles used in ancient Egypt to ward off evil spirits, and many statues showing Horus being nursed by the goddess Isis.

"Maybe Jacques Saunière knew about your book?" Fache suggested. "And he called the meeting to offer help with it."

Langdon shook his head. "Actually, nobody knows about my book yet. It's still a draft, and I haven't shown it to anyone except my editor."

Fache was quiet.

Langdon did not add the reason he hadn't shown the book to anyone else. The three-hundred-page draft—called Symbols of the Lost Sacred Feminine —suggested some very strange interpretations of well-known religious symbols that would likely cause controversy.

Now, as Langdon neared the still escalators, he stopped, noticing Fache was no longer next to him. Turning, Langdon saw Fache standing a few steps back at a service elevator.

"We'll take the elevator," Fache said as the doors opened. "As you know, the gallery is quite a walk."

Although Langdon knew the elevator would make the long, two-story climb to the Denon Wing faster, he didn't move.

"Is something wrong?" Fache was holding the door, looking impatient. Langdon sighed, glancing longingly back at the open escalator. Nothing's wrong at all, he told himself, walking back toward the elevator. As a boy, Langdon had fallen down an old well and almost died struggling in the water in the small space for hours before being rescued. Since then, he'd had a strong fear of enclosed spaces—elevators, subways, squash courts. The elevator is perfectly safe, Langdon kept telling himself, never believing it. It's a small metal box hanging in a closed shaft! Holding his breath, he stepped into the elevator, feeling the familiar rush of adrenaline as the doors closed. Two floors. Ten seconds.

"You and Mr. Saunière," Fache said as the elevator started moving, "you never spoke at all? Never wrote to each other? Never mailed anything to each other?"

Another strange question. Langdon shook his head. "No. Never." Fache tilted his head, as if making a mental note of that. Saying nothing, he stared straight ahead at the shiny doors. As they went up, Langdon tried to focus on anything but the four walls around him. In the reflection of the shiny elevator door, he saw the captain's tie clip—a silver cross with thirteen black stones. Langdon was a little surprised. The symbol was known as a crux gemmata —a cross with thirteen gems—a Christian symbol for Christ and His twelve apostles. Langdon hadn't expected the captain of the French police to show his religion so openly. But then, this was France; Christianity wasn't just a religion here, but something people were born into.

"It's a crux gemmata," Fache said suddenly.

Startled, Langdon looked up and saw Fache watching him in the reflection. The elevator stopped with a jerk, and the doors opened.

Langdon quickly stepped out into the hallway, wanting the open space of the Louvre galleries with their high ceilings. But the world he stepped into was not what he expected.

Surprised, Langdon stopped.

Fache glanced over. "I assume, Mr. Langdon, you've never seen the Louvre after hours?" I guess not, Langdon thought, trying to get his bearings.

Usually brightly lit, the Louvre galleries were surprisingly dark tonight. Instead of the usual bright white light from above, a soft red glow seemed to come from the baseboards—patches of red light spreading onto the tile floors. As Langdon looked down the dark corridor, he realized he should have expected this. Almost all major galleries used red service lighting at night—low-level, non-intrusive lights that helped staff move around while keeping the paintings in relative darkness to reduce fading from too much light. Tonight, the museum felt almost oppressive. Long shadows were everywhere, and the high vaulted ceilings seemed like a low, black void.

"This way," Fache said, turning sharply right and heading through a series of connected galleries.

Langdon followed, his eyes slowly getting used to the dark. All around, huge oil paintings began to appear like photos developing in a giant darkroom... their eyes watching him as he moved through the rooms. He could smell the familiar scent of museum air—a dry, purified smell with a slight hint of carbon—created by industrial dehumidifiers that ran constantly to fight the damaging carbon dioxide breathed out by visitors. High on the walls, the visible security cameras sent a clear message: We see you. Do not touch anything.

"Any of them real?" Langdon asked, pointing to the cameras.

Fache shook his head. "Of course not."

Langdon wasn't surprised. Video surveillance in museums this big was too expensive and didn't work well. With so many galleries to watch, the Louvre would need hundreds of technicians just to watch the feeds. Most large museums now used "containment security." Instead of keeping thieves out, keep them in. Containment started after hours. If someone stole a piece of art, exits would seal around that gallery, and the thief would be trapped before the police arrived.

The sound of voices echoed down the marble hallway ahead. The noise seemed to be coming from a large alcove up ahead on the right. A bright light shone into the hallway.

"Office of the curator," the captain said.

As he and Fache got closer to the alcove, Langdon looked down a short hallway into Saunière's fancy study—warm wood, Old Master paintings, and a huge antique desk with a two-foot-tall model of a knight in armor on it. Several police agents were in the room, talking on phones and taking notes. One was sitting at Saunière's desk, typing on a laptop. The curator's private office had become the DCPJ's temporary command center for the evening.

"Messieurs," Fache called out, and the men turned. "Do not disturb us under any circumstances. Understood?"

Everyone inside the office nodded.

Langdon had seen enough DO NOT DISTURB signs on hotel doors to understand the captain's orders. Fache and Langdon were not to be bothered under any circumstances. Leaving the small group of agents behind, Fache led Langdon farther down the dark hallway. Thirty yards ahead was the entrance to the Louvre's most popular section—la Grande Galerie—a seemingly endless corridor that held the Louvre's most valuable Italian masterpieces. Langdon already knew that this was where Saunière's body was; the Grand Gallery's famous parquet floor had been easy to recognize in the Polaroid.

As they got closer, Langdon saw the entrance was blocked by a huge steel grate that looked like something medieval castles used to keep out attacking armies.

"Containment security," Fache said, as they approached the grate. Even in the darkness, the barrier looked like it could stop a tank. Reaching it, Langdon peered through the bars into the dimly lit spaces of the Grand Gallery.

"After you, Mr. Langdon," Fache said.

Langdon turned. After me, where?

Fache pointed toward the floor at the base of the grate.

Langdon looked down. In the darkness, he hadn't noticed. The barrier was raised about two feet, leaving a tight space underneath.

"This area is still off limits to Louvre security," Fache said. "My team from Police Technique et Scientifique has just finished their investigation." He pointed to the opening. "Please slide under." Langdon stared at the narrow crawl space at his feet and then up at the massive iron grate. He's kidding, right? The barrier looked like a guillotine waiting to crush anyone who tried to get through. Fache grumbled something in French and checked his watch. Then he dropped to his knees and crawled his large frame underneath the grate. On the other side, he stood up and looked back through the bars at Langdon.

Langdon sighed. Placing his palms flat on the polished parquet, he lay on his stomach and pulled himself forward. As he slid underneath, the back of his Harris tweed jacket snagged on the bottom of the grate, and he bumped the back of his head on the iron.

Very smooth, Robert, he thought, struggling and then finally pulling himself through. As he stood up, Langdon was beginning to think it was going to be a very long night.

CHAPTER 5

Murray Hill Place—the new Opus Dei World Headquarters and conference center—is at 243 Lexington Avenue in New York City. Costing just over $47 million, the 133,000 square-foot building is covered in red brick and Indiana limestone. Designed by May & Pinska, it has over one hundred bedrooms, six dining rooms, libraries, living rooms, meeting rooms, and offices. The second, eighth, and sixteenth floors have chapels, decorated with woodwork and marble. The seventeenth floor is entirely for living. Men enter the building through the main doors on Lexington Avenue. Women enter through a side street and are kept "acoustically and visually separated" from the men at all times inside the building. Earlier this evening, in the privacy of his penthouse apartment, Bishop Manuel Aringarosa had packed a small travel bag and put on a traditional black robe. Usually, he would have worn a purple belt around his waist, but tonight he would be traveling in public, and he didn't want to draw attention to his position. Only those who looked closely would notice his 14-karat gold bishop's ring with purple amethyst, large diamonds, and a handmade mitrecrozier design. Throwing the travel bag over his shoulder, he said a silent prayer and left his apartment, going down to the lobby where his driver was waiting to take him to the airport. Now, on a commercial plane headed for Rome, Aringarosa looked out the window at the dark Atlantic. The sun had already set, but Aringarosa knew his own success was coming. Tonight the battle will be won, he thought, amazed that only months ago he had felt helpless against those who threatened to destroy his organization.

As president-general of Opus Dei, Bishop Aringarosa had spent the last ten years spreading the message of "God's Work"—literally, Opus Dei. The group, started in 1928 by the Spanish priest Josemaría Escrivá, encouraged a return to traditional Catholic values and told its members to make big sacrifices in their own lives to do God's Work.

Opus Dei's traditional ideas first took hold in Spain before Franco's rule, but with the 1934 publication of Josemaría Escrivá's spiritual book The Way —999 points to think about for doing God's Work in your own life—Escrivá's message spread rapidly around the world. Now, with over four million copies of The Way in circulation in forty-two languages, Opus Dei was a global force. Its dorms, teaching centers, and even universities could be found in almost every major city on earth. Opus Dei was the fastest-growing and most financially stable Catholic organization in the world. Unfortunately, Aringarosa had learned that in a time of religious doubt, cults, and TV preachers, Opus Dei's growing wealth and power made people suspicious.

"Many call Opus Dei a brainwashing cult," reporters often challenged. "Others call you a very conservative Christian secret society. Which are you?"

"Opus Dei is neither," the bishop would patiently answer. "We are a Catholic Church. We are a group of Catholics who have chosen to follow Catholic teachings as closely as we can in our daily lives."

"Does God's Work always include vows of chastity, tithing, and making up for sins through self-whipping and the cilice?"

"You are describing only a small part of the Opus Dei population," Aringarosa said. "There are many levels of involvement. Thousands of Opus Dei members are married, have families, and do God's Work in their own communities. Others choose lives of strict self-discipline within our private residence halls. These choices are personal, but everyone in Opus Dei shares the goal of making the world better by doing the Work of God. Surely this is a good goal." But reason seldom worked. The media always looked for scandal, and Opus Dei, like most large organizations, had a few members who made mistakes that cast a bad light on the entire group.

Two months ago, an Opus Dei group at a midwestern university had been caught giving new recruits mescaline to create a feeling of intense happiness that the new members would think was a religious experience. Another university student had used his barbed cilice belt more often than the recommended two hours a day and had gotten a near-fatal infection. In Boston not long ago, a disappointed young investment banker had signed over his entire life savings to Opus Dei before trying to kill himself.

Misguided sheep, Aringarosa thought, feeling sorry for them. Of course the biggest embarrassment had been the widely publicized trial of FBI spy Robert Hanssen, who, besides being a prominent member of Opus Dei, had turned out to be a sexual pervert. His trial revealed that he had secretly installed hidden video cameras in his own bedroom so his friends could watch him having sex with his wife. "Hardly the activity of a devout Catholic," the judge had said.

Sadly, all these events helped create a new watch group called the Opus Dei Awareness Network (ODAN). The group's popular website—www.odan.org—shared alarming stories from former Opus Dei members who warned about the dangers of joining. The media was now calling Opus Dei "God's Mafia" and "the Cult of Christ." We fear what we do not understand, Aringarosa thought, wondering if these critics knew how many lives Opus Dei had improved. The group had the full support and blessing of the Vatican. Opus Dei is a personal prelature of the Pope himself.

Recently, however, Opus Dei had been threatened by a force far stronger than the media... an unexpected enemy Aringarosa couldn't possibly hide from. Five months ago, the balance of power had shifted dramatically, and Aringarosa was still struggling with the impact.

"They don't know the war they've started," Aringarosa whispered, staring out the plane's window at the dark ocean below. For a moment, his eyes focused on the reflection of his own face—dark and long, with a flat, crooked nose broken by a fist in Spain when he was a young missionary. The physical flaw barely mattered now. Aringarosa lived in a world of the soul, not of the body.

As the jet flew over the coast of Portugal, the cell phone in Aringarosa's cassock vibrated silently. Despite airline rules against using cell phones during flights, Aringarosa knew he had to answer this call. Only one man had this number, the man who had sent Aringarosa the phone.

Excited, the bishop answered quietly. "Yes?"

"Silas has found the keystone," the caller said. "It's in Paris, inside the Church of Saint-Sulpice." Bishop Aringarosa smiled. "Then we're close."

"We can get it immediately, but we need your help."

"Of course. Tell me what to do."

When Aringarosa turned off the phone, his heart was racing. He looked again into the darkness, feeling small compared to the events he had set in motion.

Five hundred miles away, the albino named Silas stood over a small basin of water, dabbing blood from his back and watching the red patterns swirl. Purge me with hyssop, and I will be clean, he prayed, quoting Psalms. Wash me, and I will be whiter than snow. Silas felt a thrilling anticipation he hadn't felt since his old life. It surprised and energized him. For the last ten years, he had been following The Way, cleansing himself of sins, rebuilding his life, and erasing the violence of his past. But tonight, it had all come rushing back. The hatred he had fought so hard to bury had been awakened. He was surprised how quickly his past had returned. And with it, of course, had come his skills—rusty but still useful.

Jesus' message is one of peace... of nonviolence... of love. This was the message Silas had been taught from the start, and the message he held dear. And yet this was the message the enemies of Christ now threatened to destroy. Those who threaten God with force will be met with force. Immovable and steadfast.

For two millennia, Christian soldiers had defended their faith against those who tried to replace it. Tonight, Silas had been called to battle.

Drying his wounds, he put on his ankle-length, hooded robe. It was simple, made of dark wool, making his white skin and hair stand out. Tightening the rope around his waist, he pulled the hood over his head and let his red eyes admire his reflection in the mirror. Things are set in motion.

CHAPTER 6

After squeezing under the security gate, Robert Langdon found himself just inside the Grand Gallery. He stared into what seemed like a long, deep canyon. On each side of the gallery, bare walls rose thirty feet, disappearing into the darkness above. The reddish light from the service lights shone upward, creating an odd glow across an impressive collection of Da Vincis, Titians, and Caravaggios hanging from ceiling cables. Still lifes, religious scenes, and landscapes were displayed alongside portraits of nobles and politicians. While the Grand Gallery held the Louvre's most famous Italian art, many visitors felt the wing's most amazing feature was its famous parquet floor. Made of a striking geometric design of diagonal oak slats, the floor created a temporary optical illusion—a complex network that made visitors feel like they were floating through the gallery on a surface that changed with each step.

As Langdon's eyes started to follow the floor's design, they stopped suddenly on something unexpected lying on the floor a few feet to his left, surrounded by police tape. He turned to Fache. "Is that... a Caravaggio on the floor?"

Fache nodded without looking.

Langdon guessed the painting was worth over two million dollars, yet it was lying on the floor like a discarded poster. "What on earth is it doing on the floor!" Fache frowned, clearly unaffected. "This is a crime scene, Mr. Langdon. We haven't touched anything. The curator pulled that canvas from the wall. That's how he activated the security system." Langdon looked back at the gate, trying to imagine what had happened.

"The curator was attacked in his office, ran into the Grand Gallery, and activated the security gate by pulling that painting from the wall. The gate fell immediately, blocking all access. This is the only way in or out of this gallery."

Langdon felt confused. "So the curator trapped his attacker inside the Grand Gallery?" Fache shook his head. "The security gate separated Saunière from his attacker. The killer was locked out in the hallway and shot Saunière through this gate." Fache pointed to an orange tag hanging from one of the bars on the gate they had just passed under. "The PTS team found traces of gunfire. He fired through the bars. Saunière died in here alone." Langdon pictured the photograph of Saunière's body. They said he did that himself. Langdon looked out at the huge corridor before them. "So where is his body?" Fache straightened his cross-shaped tie clip and began to walk. "As you probably know, the Grand Gallery is quite long."

Langdon thought the exact length was about fifteen hundred feet, the length of three Washington Monuments placed end to end. Equally impressive was the corridor's width, which could easily fit two passenger trains side by side. The center of the hallway had statues or large porcelain urns, which acted as dividers and kept people moving along one wall and back up the other.

Fache was silent now, walking quickly up the right side of the corridor with his eyes straight ahead. Langdon felt almost rude for rushing past so many masterpieces without even a glance.

Not that I could see anything in this lighting, he thought.

The dim red lighting unfortunately brought back memories of Langdon's last experience with similar lighting in the Vatican Secret Archives. This was the second unsettling similarity tonight to his near-death in Rome. He thought of Vittoria again. She had been out of his dreams for months. Langdon couldn't believe Rome had been only a year ago; it felt like decades. Another life. His last message from Vittoria had been in December—a postcard saying she was going to the Java Sea to continue her research in entanglement physics... something about using satellites to track manta ray migrations. Langdon never thought a woman like Vittoria Vetra could be happy living with him at a college, but their time in Rome had awakened a longing he never knew he could feel. His lifelong love of being a bachelor and the simple freedoms it allowed had been shaken somehow... replaced by an unexpected emptiness that seemed to have grown over the past year.

They continued walking quickly, but Langdon still didn't see a body. "Jacques Saunière went this far?"

"Mr. Saunière was shot in the stomach. He died very slowly, maybe over fifteen or twenty minutes. He was clearly a very strong man." Langdon turned, shocked. "It took security fifteen minutes to get here?"

"Of course not. Louvre security responded to the alarm right away and found the Grand Gallery sealed. Through the gate, they could hear someone moving around at the far end of the hall, but they couldn't see who it was. They shouted, but there was no answer. Thinking it was a criminal, they followed procedure and called the Judicial Police. We were in position within fifteen minutes. When we arrived, we lifted the barricade enough to crawl underneath, and I sent a dozen armed officers inside. They searched the length of the gallery to trap the intruder."

"And?"

"They found no one inside. Except..." He pointed farther down the hall. "Him." Langdon looked up and followed Fache's pointing finger. At first, he thought Fache was pointing to a large marble statue in the middle of the hallway. As they walked on, though, Langdon began to see past the statue. Thirty yards down the hall, a single spotlight on a portable stand shone down on the floor, creating a bright white spot in the dark red gallery. In the center of the light, like an insect under a microscope, the curator's body lay naked on the floor.

"You saw the photograph," Fache said, "so this shouldn't be a surprise." Langdon felt a deep chill as they approached the body. Before him was one of the strangest things he had ever seen.

The pale body of Jacques Saunière lay on the floor exactly as it appeared in the photograph. As Langdon stood over the body and squinted in the bright light, he reminded himself with amazement that Saunière had spent his last minutes arranging his own body in this strange way.

Saunière looked remarkably fit for a man of his age... and all of his muscles were visible. He had taken off all of his clothes, placed them neatly on the floor, and lay down on his back in the center of the wide corridor, perfectly aligned with the length of the room. His arms and legs were spread out wide, like a child making a snow angel... or, perhaps more accurately, like a man being torn apart by some invisible force.

Just below Saunière's chest, a bloody mark showed where the bullet went in. The wound hadn't bled much, leaving only a small spot of dark blood. Saunière's left index finger was also bloody, as if dipped in the wound to make his death scene even stranger. Using his own blood as ink on his bare stomach, Saunière had drawn a simple symbol: five straight lines forming a five-pointed star. The pentacle.

The bloody star, centered on Saunière's belly button, made his body look eerie. The photo Langdon saw was scary enough, but seeing it in person made Langdon feel even more uneasy.

He did this himself.

"Mr. Langdon?" Fache's dark eyes looked at him again.

"It's a pentacle," Langdon said, his voice sounding empty in the large room. "One of the oldest symbols on earth. Used over four thousand years before Christ."

"And what does it mean?"

Langdon always hesitated when asked this. Telling someone what a symbol "meant" was like telling them how a song should make them feel—it's different for everyone. A white Ku Klux Klan hood brings up images of hate and racism in the United States, but the same costume means religious faith in Spain.

"Symbols have different meanings in different situations," Langdon said. "Mostly, the pentacle is a pagan religious symbol."

Fache nodded. "Devil worship."

"No," Langdon corrected, realizing his words could have been clearer. Now, the word pagan almost meant devil worship, which is wrong. The word comes from the Latin paganus, meaning country people. "Pagans" were simply country people who kept the old, rural religions of nature worship. The Church was so afraid of those who lived in the rural villes that the harmless word for "villager"— villain —came to mean an evil person.

"The pentacle," Langdon explained, "is an ancient symbol from before Christianity that connects to the worship of Nature. People long ago saw their world as having two parts—male and female. Their gods and goddesses worked to keep these parts balanced. Like yin and yang. When male and female were in balance, the world was peaceful. When they were not, there was chaos." Langdon pointed to Saunière's stomach. "This pentacle stands for the female part of everything—an idea religious historians call the 'sacred feminine' or the 'divine goddess.' Saunière, of all people, would know this."

"Saunière drew a goddess symbol on his stomach?"

Langdon had to admit, it seemed strange. "More specifically, the pentacle represents Venus—the goddess of love, sex, and beauty."

Fache looked at the naked man and grunted.

"Early religion was based on the natural order of Nature. The goddess Venus and the planet Venus were the same. The goddess was in the night sky and was known by many names—Venus, the Eastern Star, Ishtar, Astarte—all powerful female ideas connected to Nature and Mother Earth."

Fache looked more worried now, as if he preferred the idea of devil worship. Langdon decided not to mention the pentacle's most amazing feature—the visual origin of its link to Venus. When he was a young astronomy student, Langdon was shocked to learn that the planet Venus makes a perfect pentacle in the sky every four years. The ancients were so amazed by this that Venus and her pentacle became symbols of perfection, beauty, and the repeating nature of love and sex. To honor the magic of Venus, the Greeks used her four-year cycle to plan their Olympic Games. Today, few people know that the four-year schedule of the modern Olympics still follows the cycles of Venus. Even fewer know that the five-pointed star almost became the official Olympic symbol but was changed at the last minute—its five points replaced with five interlocking rings to better show the games' spirit of togetherness and harmony.

"Mr. Langdon," Fache said suddenly. "The pentacle must also be connected to the devil. Your American horror movies make that clear."

Langdon frowned. Thanks, Hollywood. The five-pointed star was now a common image in movies about Satanic killers, usually drawn on the wall of some Satanist's apartment along with other supposed devil symbols. Langdon was always annoyed when he saw the symbol used this way; the pentacle's true beginnings were actually quite holy.

"I assure you," Langdon said, "despite what you see in the movies, the pentacle's devil meaning is historically wrong. The original female meaning is correct, but the pentacle's symbolism has been twisted over time. In this case, through violence."

"I'm not sure I understand."

Langdon glanced at Fache's crucifix, unsure how to put it. "The Church, sir. Symbols are very strong, but the early Roman Catholic Church changed the pentacle. To get rid of pagan religions and convert people to Christianity, the Church made the pagan gods and goddesses look evil, turning their holy symbols into something bad."

"Go on."

"This is common when things are changing," Langdon continued. "A new power will take old symbols and make them look bad to erase their meaning. The pagan symbols lost to Christian symbols. Poseidon's trident became the devil's pitchfork, the wise woman's hat became a witch's hat, and Venus's pentacle became a sign of the devil." Langdon paused. "Sadly, the U.S. military has also twisted the pentacle. Now it's our main symbol of war. We paint it on our fighter jets and put it on our generals' shoulders." So much for the goddess of love and beauty.

"Interesting." Fache nodded at the spread-eagle body. "And how the body is positioned? What do you think of that?"

Langdon shrugged. "The position just makes the reference to the pentacle and the sacred feminine stronger."

Fache looked confused. "I'm sorry?"

"Repeating. Showing a symbol again is the easiest way to make its meaning stronger. Jacques Saunière put himself in the shape of a five-pointed star." If one pentacle is good, two is better. Fache looked at the five points of Saunière's arms, legs, and head as he ran a hand through his slick hair again. "Interesting analysis." He paused. "And the nudity?" He seemed disgusted as he said the word, repulsed by the sight of an old man's body. "Why did he take off his clothes?"

Good question, Langdon thought. He'd been wondering the same thing since he saw the Polaroid. His best guess was that a naked body was another sign of Venus—the goddess of sex. Modern culture has erased much of Venus's link to physical relationships, but you can still see a bit of Venus's original meaning in the word "venereal." Langdon decided not to go there.

"Mr. Fache, I can't tell you why Mr. Saunière drew that symbol on himself or put himself in this position, but I can tell you that a man like Jacques Saunière would see the pentacle as a sign of the female deity. Art historians and symbologists know that this symbol is connected to the sacred feminine."

"Fine. And the use of his own blood as ink?"

"Obviously he had nothing else to write with."

Fache was silent a moment. "Actually, I think he used blood so the police would follow certain forensic steps."

"I'm sorry?"

"Look at his left hand."

Langdon's eyes followed the curator's pale arm to his left hand but saw nothing. Unsure, he walked around the body and crouched down, now noticing in surprise that the curator was holding a large, felt-tipped marker.

"Saunière was holding it when we found him," Fache said, leaving Langdon and walking to a portable table covered with investigation tools, cables, and electronic gear. "As I told you," he said, searching the table, "we haven't touched anything. Do you know this kind of pen?"

Langdon knelt down to see the pen's label.

STYLO DE LUMIERE NOIRE.

He looked up in surprise.

The black-light pen, also called a watermark pen, was a special marker originally made for museums, art restorers, and forgery experts to put invisible marks on objects. The pen used a noncorrosive, alcohol-based ink that glowed only under black light. Now, museum workers used these pens daily to mark paintings needing repair with invisible "tick marks" on their frames.

As Langdon stood, Fache turned off the spotlight. The gallery went dark.

Briefly blinded, Langdon felt unsure. Fache's dark shape appeared, lit in bright purple. He came closer with a portable light, surrounded by a violet glow.

"As you know," Fache said, his eyes glowing in the violet light, "police use black lights to find blood and other evidence at crime scenes. So you can imagine our surprise..." He quickly pointed the light at the body.

Langdon looked down and jumped back, shocked.

His heart raced as he saw the strange sight glowing on the floor. The curator's last words were written in glowing handwriting, shining purple beside his body. As Langdon stared at the shimmering words, he felt even more lost in the mystery of the night.

Langdon read the message again and looked at Fache. "What does this mean?" Fache's eyes shone white. "That, sir, is what you are here to figure out."

Nearby, in Saunière's office, Lieutenant Collet was back at the Louvre. He was sitting at the curator's large desk, working with an audio console. Except for a strange, robot-like doll of a medieval knight staring at him from the corner of the desk, Collet felt at ease. He adjusted his headphones and checked the recording system. Everything was ready. The microphones worked perfectly, and the sound was clear.

The moment of truth, he thought.

Smiling, he closed his eyes and got comfortable, ready to enjoy the rest of the conversation being recorded in the Grand Gallery.

CHAPTER 7

The simple living space inside the Church of Saint-Sulpice was on the second floor, to the left of the choir balcony. It was a two-room place with a stone floor and just a few things in it. Sister Sandrine Bieil had lived there for over ten years. The convent nearby was officially where she lived, but she liked the quiet of the church better and had made herself comfortable upstairs with a bed, phone, and hot plate.

As the church's caretaker, Sister Sandrine was in charge of all the nonreligious stuff—general upkeep, hiring staff and guides, locking up the building at night, and ordering things like communion wine and wafers. Tonight, she was asleep in her small bed when her phone rang loudly. Tired, she picked it up.

"Sister Sandrine. Church of Saint-Sulpice."

"Hello, Sister," the man said in French.

Sister Sandrine sat up. What time is it? She knew her boss's voice, but he had never woken her up in fifteen years. The priest was very religious and always went home to bed right after mass.

"I'm sorry if I woke you, Sister," the priest said, sounding tired and worried. "I need you to do me a favor. I just got a call from an important American bishop. Maybe you know him? Manuel Aringarosa?"

"The head of Opus Dei?" Of course I know him. Everyone in the Church does. Aringarosa's conservative group had become very powerful lately. They became important quickly in 1982 when Pope John Paul II surprisingly made them a "personal prelature of the Pope," officially approving all their practices. People thought it was strange that Opus Dei's rise happened the same year the rich group supposedly put almost a billion dollars into the Vatican's bank—officially called the Institute for Religious Works—saving it from a bad bankruptcy. In another move that made people suspicious, the Pope put the founder of Opus Dei on the "fast track" to becoming a saint, speeding up the usual long waiting period for sainthood to only twenty years. Sister Sandrine couldn't help but think that Opus Dei's good standing in Rome was questionable, but you didn't argue with the Holy See.

"Bishop Aringarosa called to ask me a favor," the priest told her, sounding anxious. "One of his members is in Paris tonight...."

As Sister Sandrine listened to the strange request, she became more and more confused. "I'm sorry, are you saying this visiting Opus Dei member can't wait until morning?"

"I'm afraid not. His plane leaves very early. He's always dreamed of seeing Saint-Sulpice."

"But the church is much more interesting during the day. The sunlight through the opening, the changing shadows on the sundial—this is what makes Saint-Sulpice special."

"Sister, I agree, but I would really appreciate it if you could let him in tonight. He can be there at… let's say one o'clock? That's in twenty minutes."

Sister Sandrine frowned. "Of course. I'd be happy to." The abbé thanked her and hung up.

Confused, Sister Sandrine stayed in her warm bed for a moment, trying to wake up fully. Her sixty-year-old body didn't wake up as quickly as it used to, though tonight's phone call had certainly gotten her attention. Opus Dei had always made her uncomfortable. Besides the group's commitment to the strange practice of physical self-punishment, their views on women were very outdated. She was shocked to hear that female members were forced to clean the men's dorms for free while the men were at mass; women slept on hard floors, while the men had straw mats; and women had to do extra acts of self-punishment… all as extra punishment for original sin. It seemed like Eve's bite of the apple of knowledge was a debt women were destined to pay forever. Sadly, while most of the Catholic Church was slowly improving when it came to women's rights, Opus Dei threatened to undo that progress. Even so, Sister Sandrine had her orders. Swinging her legs off the bed, she stood slowly, feeling the cold stone floor on her bare feet. As the cold spread through her body, she felt a sudden worry. Women's intuition?

As a follower of God, Sister Sandrine had learned to find peace in the quiet voices of her own soul. Tonight, however, those voices were as silent as the empty church around her.

CHAPTER 8

Langdon stared at the glowing purple text on the floor. Jacques Saunière's last words seemed like the strangest message he could imagine.

The message read:

13-3-2-21-1-1-8-5

O, Draconian devil!

Oh, lame saint!

Langdon had no idea what it meant, but he understood why Fache thought the pentacle was related to devil worship.

O, Draconian devil!

Saunière had directly mentioned the devil. The numbers were just as odd. "Part of it looks like a number code."

"Yes," Fache said. "Our code experts are working on it. We think these numbers might lead us to his killer. Maybe it's a phone number or some kind of ID. Do the numbers mean anything to you?"

Langdon looked again at the numbers, feeling it would take him hours to find any symbolic meaning. If Saunière had even meant any. To Langdon, the numbers seemed completely random. He was used to symbolic patterns that made some kind of sense, but everything here—the pentacle, the text, the numbers—seemed disconnected at the most basic level.

"You said earlier," Fache said, "that Saunière did all this to send some kind of message... goddess worship or something like that? How does this message fit in?" Langdon knew the question was just for show. This weird message clearly didn't fit Langdon's idea of goddess worship at all.

O, Draconian devil? Oh, lame saint?

Fache said, "This text looks like some kind of accusation. Wouldn't you agree?" Langdon tried to picture the curator's last minutes trapped alone in the Grand Gallery, knowing he was about to die. It seemed to make sense. "An accusation against his killer makes sense, I guess."

"My job, of course, is to find out who that is. Let me ask you this, Mr. Langdon. Besides the numbers, what about this message seems the strangest to you?"

Most strange? A dying man had locked himself in the gallery, drawn a pentacle on himself, and written a mysterious accusation on the floor. What about this situation wasn't strange?

"The word 'Draconian'?" he offered, saying the first thing that came to mind. Langdon was pretty sure that a reference to Draco—the harsh politician from the seventh century B.C.—wasn't something someone would think of as they were dying. " 'Draconian devil' seems like a weird choice of words."

"Draconian?" Fache sounded a little impatient now. "Saunière's choice of words doesn't seem like the main problem here."

Langdon wasn't sure what problem Fache was thinking of, but he was starting to think that Draco and Fache would have gotten along well.

"Saunière was French," Fache said plainly. "He lived in Paris. And yet he chose to write this message..."

"In English," Langdon said, now understanding what the captain meant. Fache nodded. "Exactly. Any idea why?"

Langdon knew Saunière spoke perfect English, but he didn't know why he would choose English for his last words. He shrugged. Fache pointed back to the pentacle on Saunière's stomach. "Nothing to do with devil worship? Are you still sure?"

Langdon wasn't sure of anything anymore. "The symbols and text don't seem to match. I'm sorry I can't help more."

"Maybe this will make it clearer." Fache stepped away from the body and raised the black light higher, making the beam wider. "And now?"

Langdon was shocked to see a simple circle glowing around the curator's body. Saunière had apparently laid down and swung the pen around himself in long, sweeping lines, drawing a circle around himself.

Suddenly, the meaning was clear.

"The Vitruvian Man," Langdon exclaimed. Saunière had made a life-sized copy of Leonardo da Vinci's most famous drawing.

Da Vinci's The Vitruvian Man, considered the most accurate anatomical drawing of its time, had become a well-known cultural symbol, appearing on posters, mouse pads, and T-shirts everywhere. The famous drawing showed a perfect circle with a naked man inside, his arms and legs stretched out wide.

Da Vinci. Langdon felt a shiver of awe. Saunière's message was now undeniable. In his last moments, the curator had taken off his clothes and arranged his body to clearly resemble Leonardo da Vinci's Vitruvian Man.

The circle had been the key piece missing. A feminine symbol of protection, the circle around the naked man's body made Da Vinci's message complete—male and female balance. The question now, though, was why Saunière would copy a famous drawing.

"Mr. Langdon," Fache said, "surely a man like you knows that Leonardo da Vinci was interested in the dark arts."

Langdon was surprised Fache knew about Da Vinci, and it helped explain the captain's suspicions about devil worship. Da Vinci had always been a difficult topic for historians, especially in the Christian world. Despite his genius, he was openly homosexual and worshipped Nature's divine order, both of which made him a sinner in the eyes of God. Also, the artist's strange habits gave him a demonic image: Da Vinci dug up bodies to study human anatomy; he kept secret journals in unreadable backward writing; he believed he could turn lead into gold and even trick God by creating a potion to prevent death; and he invented terrible, new weapons of war and torture.

Misunderstanding creates distrust, Langdon thought.

Even Da Vinci's many beautiful Christian artworks only made his reputation for spiritual hypocrisy worse. Da Vinci accepted many well-paying jobs from the Vatican, painting Christian themes not because he believed in them, but as a business deal—a way to pay for his fancy lifestyle. Sadly, Da Vinci was a prankster who often secretly bit the hand that fed him. He included hidden symbols in many of his Christian paintings that were not Christian at all—honoring his own beliefs and subtly mocking the Church. Langdon had even given a lecture at the National Gallery in London called: "The Secret Life of Leonardo: Pagan Symbolism in Christian Art."

"I understand your concerns," Langdon now said, "but Da Vinci never really practiced any dark arts. He was a very spiritual man, but he was always in conflict with the Church." As Langdon said this, a strange thought came to him. He looked down at the message on the floor again. O, Draconian devil! Oh, lame saint!

"Yes?" Fache said.

Langdon chose his words carefully. "I was just thinking that Saunière shared many spiritual ideas with Da Vinci, including a concern about the Church removing the sacred feminine from modern religion. Maybe, by copying a famous Da Vinci drawing, Saunière was simply repeating some of their shared frustrations with the modern Church's demonization of the goddess." Fache's eyes narrowed. "You think Saunière is calling the Church a lame saint and a Draconian devil?"

Langdon had to admit it seemed unlikely, but the pentacle seemed to support the idea somewhat. "All I'm saying is that Mr. Saunière dedicated his life to studying the history of the goddess, and nothing has done more to erase that history than the Catholic Church. It seems possible that Saunière might have chosen to express his disappointment in his final message."

"Disappointment?" Fache asked, sounding angry now. "This message sounds more angry than disappointed, wouldn't you say?"

Langdon was losing patience. "Captain, you asked for my gut feeling about what Saunière is trying to say, and that's what I'm giving you."

"That this is a criticism of the Church?" Fache's jaw tightened as he spoke through clenched teeth. "Mr. Langdon, I've seen a lot of death, and let me tell you something. When someone is murdered, I don't think their final thoughts are to write a confusing spiritual message that no one will understand. I think they're thinking of one thing only." Fache's whispery voice cut through the air. "La vengeance. I believe Saunière wrote this note to tell us who killed him." Langdon stared. "But that doesn't make any sense."

"No?"

"No," he snapped, tired and frustrated. "You told me Saunière was attacked in his office by someone he had apparently invited in."

"Yes."

"So it seems fair to say that the curator knew his attacker." Fache nodded. "Go on."

"So if Saunière knew the person who killed him, what kind of accusation is this?" He pointed at the floor. "Number codes? Weak saints? Harsh devils? Pentacles on his stomach? It's all too mysterious."

Fache frowned as if he'd never thought of that. "You're right."
