"Given the situation," Langdon said, "I'd guess that if Saunière wanted to tell you who killed him, he would have written down a name." As Langdon said this, a smug smile appeared on Fache's face for the first time all night.

"Précisément," Fache said. "Précisément."

I am witnessing the work of a master, Lieutenant Collet thought as he adjusted his audio equipment and listened to Fache's voice through the headphones. The senior agent knew it was moments like these that had elevated the captain to the top of French law enforcement. Fache will do what no one else dares.

The delicate art of persuasion was a lost skill in modern law enforcement, one that demanded exceptional composure under pressure. Few men had the necessary calm for this kind of operation, but Fache seemed born for it. His control and patience were almost robotic. Fache's only emotion this evening seemed to be intense determination, as if this arrest were somehow personal. Fache's briefing of his agents an hour ago had been unusually short and confident. I know who murdered Jacques Saunière, Fache had said. You know what to do. No mistakes tonight.

And so far, no mistakes had been made.

Collet wasn't yet aware of the evidence that had solidified Fache's certainty of their suspect's guilt, but he knew better than to question the Bull's instincts. Fache's intuition seemed almost supernatural at times. God whispers in his ear, one agent had insisted after Fache displayed an especially impressive hunch. Collet had to admit, if there was a God, Bezu Fache would be on His A-list. The captain attended mass and confession with zealous regularity—far more than the required holiday attendance fulfilled by other officials for good public relations. When the Pope visited Paris a few years back, Fache had used all his influence to get an audience. A photo of Fache with the Pope now hung in his office. The Papal Bull, the agents secretly called him.

Collet found it ironic that one of Fache's rare popular public stances in recent years had been his outspoken reaction to the Catholic pedophilia scandal. These priests should be hanged twice! Fache had declared. Once for their crimes against children. And once for shaming the good name of the Catholic Church. Collet had the odd sense it was the latter that angered Fache more. Turning now to his laptop, Collet attended to the other half of his responsibilities tonight—the GPS tracking system. The image on screen revealed a detailed floor plan of the Denon Wing, a structural schematic uploaded from the Louvre Security Office. Letting his eyes trace the maze of galleries and hallways, Collet found what he was looking for.

Deep in the heart of the Grand Gallery blinked a tiny red dot.

The mark.

Fache was keeping his prey on a very tight leash tonight. Wisely so. Robert Langdon had proven himself one cool customer.

CHAPTER 9

To make sure no one bothered him while he talked to Mr. Langdon, Bezu Fache had turned off his cell phone. But it was a fancy model with a two-way radio feature, and one of his agents was using it to call him, even though he had said not to.

"Capitaine?" The phone buzzed like a walkie-talkie. Fache was furious. He couldn't think of anything important enough for Collet to interrupt this secret surveillance —especially now. He gave Langdon a calm, apologetic look. "Just a moment." He took the phone from his belt and pushed the radio button. "Oui?"

"Capitaine, an agent from the Cryptography Department has arrived." Fache's anger paused. A cryptographer? Even though the timing was bad, this was probably good news. After finding Saunière's strange message on the floor, Fache had sent photos of the whole crime scene to the Cryptography Department, hoping someone there could figure out what Saunière was trying to say. If a code breaker was here, it probably meant someone had decoded Saunière's message.

"I'm busy right now," Fache radioed back, making it clear he was not happy. "Tell the cryptographer to wait at the command post. I'll talk to him when I'm done."

"Her," the voice corrected. "It's Agent Neveu." Fache was getting more annoyed with this call every second. Sophie Neveu was one of the DCPJ's worst mistakes. A young Parisian code breaker who had studied cryptography in England at the Royal Holloway, Sophie Neveu had been forced on Fache two years ago as part of the ministry's attempt to get more women on the police force. Fache argued that the ministry's focus on being politically correct was making the department weaker. He thought women didn't have the physical strength for police work and that their presence was a distraction for the men. And, as Fache had feared, Sophie Neveu was proving to be more of a distraction than most.

At thirty-two, she was very determined, almost stubborn. She was always pushing Britain's new code-breaking methods, which frustrated the older French cryptographers who worked above her. But the thing that bothered Fache the most was that, in an office full of middle-aged men, an attractive young woman always took attention away from the work.

The man on the radio said, "Agent Neveu insisted on speaking to you right away, Captain. I tried to stop her, but she's coming into the gallery."

Fache was shocked. "That's not okay! I made it very clear—"

Suddenly, Robert Langdon thought Bezu Fache was having a stroke. The captain stopped talking, his eyes wide. He seemed to be staring at something behind Langdon. Before Langdon could turn around, he heard a woman's voice behind him.

"Excuse me, gentlemen."

Langdon turned to see a young woman approaching. She moved down the corridor toward them with long, smooth steps... a haunting confidence in her walk. Dressed casually in a knee-length, cream-colored Irish sweater over black leggings, she was attractive and looked to be about thirty. Her thick burgundy hair fell naturally to her shoulders, framing her warm face. Unlike the thin, identical blondes that filled Harvard dorm rooms, this woman was healthy with a simple beauty and honesty that showed a strong self-assurance. To Langdon's surprise, the woman walked right up to him and offered a polite hand.

"Mr. Langdon, I am Agent Neveu from DCPJ's Cryptology Department." Her words rolled smoothly with her soft mix of English and French accent. "It is a pleasure to meet you." Langdon took her soft hand in his and felt himself briefly caught in her strong gaze. Her eyes were olive-green—sharp and clear.

Fache took a sharp breath, clearly ready to scold her.

"Captain," she said, turning quickly and speaking first, "please excuse the interruption, but—"

"This is not the time!" Fache snapped.

"I tried to call you." Sophie continued in English, as if being polite to Langdon. "But your cell phone was turned off."

"I turned it off for a reason," Fache hissed. "I am speaking to Mr. Langdon."

"I've solved the numeric code," she said simply.

Langdon felt a rush of excitement. She broke the code?

Fache seemed unsure how to respond.

"Before I explain," Sophie said, "I have an urgent message for Mr. Langdon." Fache's expression grew more concerned. "For Mr. Langdon?" She nodded, turning back to Langdon. "You need to contact the U.S. Embassy, Mr. Langdon. They have a message for you from the States."

Langdon reacted with surprise, his excitement about the code fading into sudden worry. A message from the States? He wondered who could be trying to reach him. Only a few of his colleagues knew he was in Paris.

Fache's jaw tightened at the news. "The U.S. Embassy?" he asked, sounding suspicious. "How would they know to find Mr. Langdon here?" Sophie shrugged. "Apparently, they called Mr. Langdon's hotel, and the concierge told them Mr. Langdon had been picked up by a DCPJ agent."

Fache looked troubled. "And the embassy contacted DCPJ Cryptography?"

"No, sir," Sophie said, her voice firm. "When I called the DCPJ switchboard to contact you, they had a message waiting for Mr. Langdon and asked me to pass it along if I got through to you."

Fache's brow furrowed, looking confused. He started to speak, but Sophie had already turned back to Langdon.

"Mr. Langdon," she said, pulling a small slip of paper from her pocket, "this is the number for your embassy's messaging service. They asked that you call them as soon as possible." She handed him the paper, looking intently at him. "While I explain the code to Captain Fache, you need to make this call."

Langdon looked at the slip. It had a Paris phone number and extension on it. "Thank you," he said, feeling worried now. "Where can I find a phone?"

Sophie started to pull a cell phone from her sweater pocket, but Fache waved her away. He now looked like he was about to explode. Without taking his eyes off Sophie, he took out his own cell phone and held it out. "This line is secure, Mr. Langdon. You may use it." Langdon was puzzled by Fache's anger at the young woman. Feeling uneasy, he took the captain's phone. Fache immediately led Sophie away and began scolding her in hushed tones. Disliking the captain more and more, Langdon turned away from the strange scene and switched on the cell phone. Checking the slip of paper Sophie had given him, Langdon dialed the number.

The line started ringing.

One ring... two rings... three rings...

Finally, the call connected.

Langdon expected to hear someone from the embassy, but instead, he heard an answering machine. Strangely, the voice on the recording sounded familiar. It was Sophie Neveu.

"Bonjour, vous êtes bien chez Sophie Neveu," the woman's voice said. "Je suis absenle pour le moment, mais... "

Confused, Langdon turned to Sophie. "I'm sorry, Ms. Neveu? I think you might have given me—"

"No, that's the right number," Sophie said quickly, as if she knew Langdon would be confused.

"The embassy uses an automated message system. You need to dial a code to get your messages."

Langdon stared. "But—"

"It's the three-digit code on the paper I gave you."

Langdon started to explain the odd mistake, but Sophie quickly silenced him with a brief glare. Her green eyes sent a clear message: Don't ask questions. Just do it.

Confused, Langdon entered the extension on the paper: 454. Sophie's outgoing message stopped right away, and Langdon heard a computerized voice say in French: "You have one new message." It seemed 454 was Sophie's code to check her messages from another location.

I'm checking this woman's messages?

Langdon could hear the tape rewinding. Then it stopped, and the machine started playing. Langdon listened as the message began. Again, it was Sophie's voice.

"Mr. Langdon," the message began in a worried whisper. "Do not react to this message. Just listen calmly. You are in danger right now. Follow my directions very carefully."

CHAPTER 10

Silas sat in the black Audi the Teacher had gotten for him and looked at the large Church of Saint-Sulpice. Lights below made the church's two bell towers look like strong guards above the building. On each side, dark rows of supports stuck out like the ribs of a beautiful animal.

The ungodly used a house of God to hide their keystone. Once again, the brotherhood had shown their famous ability to trick and lie. Silas was eager to find the keystone and give it to the Teacher so they could get back what the brotherhood had taken from the believers a long time ago.

That will make Opus Dei so powerful.

Silas parked the Audi on the empty Place Saint-Sulpice and sighed, telling himself to focus on what he needed to do. His back still hurt from the physical pain he had put himself through earlier, but the pain was nothing compared to the suffering he felt before Opus Dei had saved him.

Still, the memories bothered him.

Let go of your hate, Silas told himself. Forgive those who hurt you. Looking up at the stone towers of Saint-Sulpice, Silas fought the feeling... the force that often pulled his mind back to the past, trapping him in the prison that had been his world as a young man. The memories of suffering came as they always did, like a storm to his senses... the smell of rotting cabbage, the stink of death, human urine and feces. The cries of hopelessness against the strong wind of the Pyrenees and the quiet sobs of forgotten men. Andorra, he thought, feeling his muscles tense.

It was in that empty and lonely place between Spain and France, shivering in his stone cell, wanting to die, that Silas had been saved.

He didn't know it then.

The light came long after the storm.

His name wasn't Silas then, though he couldn't remember the name his parents had given him. He'd left home at seven. His father, a big, drunk dockworker, would beat his mother because he was angry about having a son with albinism, blaming her for the boy's shameful condition. When the boy tried to protect her, he was beaten badly too.

One night, they had a terrible fight, and his mother never woke up. The boy stood over her body and felt crushing guilt for letting it happen. This is my fault!

As if controlled by a demon, the boy walked to the kitchen and grabbed a butcher knife. Like in a trance, he went to the bedroom where his father lay passed out drunk. Without a word, the boy stabbed him in the back. His father cried out and tried to turn over, but his son stabbed him again and again until the apartment was silent. The boy ran from home but found the streets of Marseilles just as cruel. His strange looks made him an outcast among the other young runaways, and he had to live alone in the basement of a crumbling factory, eating stolen fruit and raw fish from the docks. His only friends were old, torn magazines he found in the trash, and he taught himself to read. He grew stronger over time. When he was twelve, another runaway—a girl twice his age—made fun of him in the street and tried to steal his food. The girl was beaten almost to death. When the police pulled the boy off her, they told him he had to leave Marseilles or go to juvenile prison.

The boy moved down the coast to Toulon. After a while, the pitying looks on the streets turned to fear. The boy had grown into a strong young man. When people walked by, he could hear them whispering to each other. A ghost, they'd say, eyes wide with fear as they stared at his white skin. A ghost with the devil's eyes!

And he felt like a ghost... see-through... drifting from port to port. People seemed to look right through him.

At eighteen, in a port town, he was caught by two crewmen while trying to steal a case of cured ham from a cargo ship. The sailors who started hitting him smelled of beer, just like his father had. The memories of fear and hate rose up like a monster from the deep. The young man broke the first sailor's neck with his bare hands, and only the arrival of the police saved the second sailor from the same fate.

Two months later, in chains, he arrived at a prison in Andorra.

You're as white as a ghost, the inmates mocked as the guards led him in, naked and cold. Look at the ghost! Maybe the ghost will float right through these walls!

Over twelve years, his body and soul wasted away until he knew he'd become see-through.

I am a ghost.

I am weightless.

Yo soy un espectro... palido coma una fantasma... caminando este mundo a solas. One night the ghost woke up to the screams of other prisoners. He didn't know what unseen force was shaking the floor he slept on, or what powerful hand was trembling the stone walls of his cell, but as he jumped up, a large rock fell onto the spot where he had been sleeping. Looking up to see where the stone came from, he saw a hole in the shaking wall, and beyond it, a sight he had not seen in over ten years: the moon. Even while the earth still shook, the ghost found himself crawling through a narrow tunnel, stumbling out into a wide open view, and falling down a bare mountainside into the woods. He ran all night, always downward, lightheaded with hunger and exhaustion. Barely conscious, he found himself at dawn in a clearing where train tracks cut across the forest. Following the rails, he moved on as if in a dream. Seeing an empty freight car, he crawled in for shelter and rest. When he woke up, the train was moving. How long? How far?

A pain was growing in his stomach. Am I dying? He slept again. This time he woke up to someone yelling, hitting him, throwing him out of the freight car. Bloody, he wandered the edge of a small village, looking for food but finding none. Finally, his body too weak to take another step, he lay down by the side of the road and lost consciousness.

The light came slowly, and the ghost wondered how long he had been dead. A day? Three days? It didn't matter. His bed was soft like a cloud, and the air around him smelled sweetly of candles. Jesus was there, staring down at him. I am here, Jesus said. The stone has been rolled away, and you are born again.

He slept and woke up. Confusion clouded his thoughts. He had never believed in heaven, and yet Jesus was watching over him. Food appeared beside his bed, and the ghost ate it, almost able to feel his body getting stronger. He slept again. When he woke up, Jesus was still smiling down, speaking. You are saved, my son. Blessed are those who follow my path. Again, he slept.

It was a scream of pain that startled the ghost from his sleep. His body jumped out of bed, stumbled down a hallway toward the sounds of shouting. He went into a kitchen and saw a large man hitting a smaller man. Without knowing why, the ghost grabbed the large man and threw him backward against a wall. The man ran away, leaving the ghost standing over the body of a young man in priest's robes. The priest had a badly broken nose. Lifting the bloody priest, the ghost carried him to a couch.

"Thank you, my friend," the priest said in clumsy French. "The collection money is tempting for thieves. You speak French in your sleep. Do you also speak Spanish?" The ghost shook his head.

"What is your name?" he continued in broken French.

The ghost could not remember the name his parents had given him. All he heard were the mocking words of the prison guards.

The priest smiled. "No hay problema. My name is Manuel Aringarosa. I am a missionary from Madrid. I was sent here to build a church for the Obra de Dios."

"Where am I?" His voice sounded empty.

"Oviedo. In northern Spain."

"How did I get here?"

"Someone left you at my door. You were sick. I fed you. You've been here many days." The ghost looked closely at the young man who cared for him. It had been years since anyone had been kind to him.

"Thank you, Father."

The priest touched his bleeding lip. "I am the one who is thankful, my friend." When the ghost woke up the next morning, his mind felt clearer. He looked up at the cross on the wall above his bed. Even though it no longer spoke to him, he felt a peaceful feeling near it. He sat up and was surprised to see a newspaper clipping on his bedside table. The article was in French and a week old. As he read the story, he became scared. It said that an earthquake in the mountains had destroyed a prison and let many dangerous criminals escape. His heart started to race. The priest knows who I am! The feeling he had was one he hadn't felt in a long time: shame, guilt, and the fear of being caught. He jumped out of bed. Where can I run?

"The Book of Acts," a voice said from the doorway.

The ghost turned, startled.

The young priest smiled as he came in. His nose was clumsily bandaged, and he held out an old Bible. "I found one in French for you. The chapter is marked." Hesitantly, the ghost took the Bible and looked at the chapter the priest had marked: Acts 16.

The verses told of a prisoner named Silas, who was naked and beaten in his cell, singing hymns to God. When the ghost reached Verse 26, he gasped in surprise.

"... And suddenly, there was a great earthquake, so that the prison's base shook, and all the doors opened."

His eyes shot up at the priest.

The priest smiled warmly. "From now on, my friend, if you have no other name, I shall call you Silas."

The ghost nodded blankly. Silas. He had been given flesh. My name is Silas.

"It's time for breakfast," the priest said. "You will need your strength if you are to help me build this church."

Twenty thousand feet above the Mediterranean, Alitalia flight 1618 bounced in turbulence, causing passengers to shift nervously. Bishop Aringarosa barely noticed. His thoughts were with the future of Opus Dei. Eager to know how plans in Paris were progressing, he wished he could phone Silas. But he could not. The Teacher had seen to that.

"It is for your own safety," the Teacher had explained, speaking in English with a French accent. "I know enough about electronic communications to know they can be intercepted. The results could be disastrous for you."

Aringarosa knew he was right. The Teacher seemed an exceptionally careful man. He had not revealed his own identity to Aringarosa, and yet he had proven himself a man well worth obeying. After all, he had somehow obtained very secret information. The names of the brotherhood's four top members! This had been one of the coups that convinced the bishop the Teacher was truly capable of delivering the astonishing prize he claimed he could unearth.

"Bishop," the Teacher had told him, "I have made all the arrangements. For my plan to succeed, you must allow Silas to answer only to me for several days. The two of you will not speak. I will communicate with him through secure channels."

"You will treat him with respect?"

"A man of faith deserves the best."

"Excellent. Then I understand. Silas and I will not speak until this is over."

"I do this to protect who you are, who Silas is, and what I've put into this."

"What you've put into this?"

"Bishop, if your own eagerness to know what's happening gets you in jail, then you won't be able to pay me my fee."

The bishop smiled. "A good point. We want the same thing. Godspeed." Twenty million euro, the bishop thought, now looking out the plane's window. The sum was about the same number of U.S. dollars. A small price for something so powerful. He felt sure again that the Teacher and Silas would succeed. Money and faith were strong motivators.

CHAPTER 11

"Une plaisanterie numérique?" Bezu Fache was furious, staring at Sophie Neveu in disbelief. A numeric joke? "Are you saying Saunière's code is just a silly math joke?"

Fache couldn't believe her nerve. Not only had she barged in on him without permission, but now she was trying to tell him that Saunière, in his last moments, decided to leave a mathematical gag?

"This code," Sophie explained quickly in French, "is so simple it's ridiculous. Jacques Saunière must have known we'd figure it out right away." She took a piece of paper from her sweater pocket and gave it to Fache. "Here's the solution." Fache looked at the paper.

1-1-2-3-5-8-13-21

"That's it?" he snapped. "You just put the numbers in order!" Sophie actually had the nerve to smile. "Exactly." Fache's voice dropped to a low growl. "Agent Neveu, I have no idea what you're getting at, but you better get there quickly." He glanced nervously at Langdon, who was standing nearby with the phone to his ear, still listening to his message from the U.S. Embassy. Langdon looked pale, and Fache guessed the news was bad.

"Captain," Sophie said, sounding almost defiant, "the numbers you're holding are part of one of the most famous mathematical sequences in history." Fache didn't know there was such a thing as a famous mathematical sequence, and he didn't like Sophie's casual tone.

"This is the Fibonacci sequence," she said, nodding at the paper in Fache's hand.

"A sequence where each number is the sum of the two before it." Fache looked at the numbers. Each one was indeed the sum of the previous two, but he couldn't see how any of this related to Saunière's death.

"The mathematician Leonardo Fibonacci created this sequence in the thirteenth century. It can't be a coincidence that all the numbers Saunière wrote on the floor are part of Fibonacci's famous sequence."

Fache stared at the young woman for several moments. "Fine, if it's not a coincidence, can you tell me why Jacques Saunière did this? What's he trying to say? What does this mean?"

She shrugged. "It means nothing. That's the point. It's a simple puzzle. Like taking a famous poem and mixing up the words to see if anyone recognizes the connection."

Fache took a threatening step forward, his face close to Sophie's. "I hope you have a better explanation than that."

Sophie's gentle face hardened as she leaned in. "Captain, considering what's at risk for you tonight, I thought you'd want to know that Jacques Saunière might be playing games with you. Apparently not. I'll tell the director of Cryptography we're no longer needed."

She turned and walked away. Fache watched her disappear into the darkness, stunned. Is she crazy? Sophie Neveu had just committed career suicide.

Fache turned to Langdon, who was still on the phone, looking worried, listening to his message. The U.S. Embassy. Bezu Fache hated many things... but few angered him more than the U.S. Embassy.

Fache and the ambassador often fought over shared issues, especially law enforcement for visiting Americans. Almost daily, DCPJ arrested American students with drugs, U.S. businessmen for hiring underage prostitutes, and American tourists for shoplifting or vandalism. Legally, the U.S. Embassy could take them back to the United States, where they would get off easy.

And the embassy always did.

The emasculation of the Police Judiciaire, Fache called it. Paris Match had recently drawn a cartoon of Fache as a police dog, trying to bite an American criminal, but chained to the U.S. Embassy.

Not tonight, Fache thought. Too much is at stake. When Robert Langdon hung up, he looked sick.

"Is everything okay?" Fache asked.

Langdon shook his head weakly.

Bad news from home, Fache guessed, noticing Langdon sweating slightly as Fache took his cell phone back.

"An accident," Langdon stammered, looking at Fache with a strange expression. "A friend..." He paused. "I'll need to fly home first thing in the morning." Fache had no doubt the shock on Langdon's face was real, and yet he sensed another feeling there too, as if a distant fear were suddenly growing in the American's eyes. "I'm sorry to hear that," Fache said, watching Langdon closely. "Would you like to sit down?" He pointed toward one of the benches in the gallery.

Langdon nodded blankly and took a few steps toward the bench. He paused, looking more confused with every moment. "Actually, I think I'd like to use the restroom." Fache frowned inwardly at the delay. "The restroom. Of course. Let's take a break for a few minutes." He pointed back down the long hallway in the direction they had come from. "The restrooms are back toward the curator's office."

Langdon hesitated, pointing in the other direction toward the far end of the Grand Gallery corridor.

"I believe there's a much closer restroom at the end."

Fache realized Langdon was right. They were two thirds of the way down, and the Grand Gallery ended at a pair of restrooms. "Shall I come with you?"

Langdon shook his head, already moving deeper into the gallery. "Not necessary. I think I'd like a few minutes alone."

Fache didn't like the idea of Langdon wandering alone down the remaining length of corridor, but he felt better knowing the Grand Gallery was a dead end whose only exit was at the other end—the gate under which they had entered. Although French fire rules required several emergency stairwells for a space this large, those stairwells had been sealed automatically when Saunière tripped the security system. That system had now been reset, unlocking the stairwells, but it didn't matter—the outside doors, if opened, would set off fire alarms and were guarded outside by DCPJ agents. Langdon could not possibly leave without Fache knowing about it.

"I need to return to Mr. Saunière's office for a moment," Fache said. "Please come find me directly, Mr. Langdon. There is more we need to discuss."

Langdon waved quietly as he vanished into the darkness.

Turning, Fache marched angrily the other way. Reaching the gate, he slid under, left the Grand Gallery, walked down the hall, and stormed into the command center in Saunière's office.

"Who approved letting Sophie Neveu into this building!" Fache yelled. Collet answered first. "She told the guards outside she'd cracked the code." Fache looked around. "Is she gone?"

"She's not with you?"

"She left." Fache glanced at the darkened hallway. Sophie clearly wasn't interested in stopping to chat with the other officers on her way out.

For a moment, Fache considered radioing the guards in the mezzanine and telling them to stop Sophie and drag her back before she could leave. He reconsidered. It was just his pride talking... wanting the last word. He'd had enough distractions tonight. Deal with Agent Neveu later, he told himself, already looking forward to firing her. Pushing Sophie from his mind, Fache stared for a moment at the small knight on Saunière's desk. Then he turned back to Collet. "Do you have him?" Collet nodded briefly and turned the laptop toward Fache. The red dot was clearly visible on the floor plan, blinking steadily in a room marked PUBLIC RESTROOMS.

"Good," Fache said, lighting a cigarette and walking into the hall. "I need to make a call. Make sure the restroom is the only place Langdon goes."

CHAPTER 12

Robert Langdon felt dizzy as he walked toward the end of the Grand Gallery. Sophie's phone message kept playing in his mind. At the end of the hall, signs with the international stick-figure symbols for restrooms guided him through a maze of dividers showing Italian drawings that hid the restrooms. He found the men's room door, went in, and turned on the lights.

The room was empty.

He walked to the sink, splashed cold water on his face, and tried to wake up. Bright fluorescent lights shone on the plain tile, and the room smelled like ammonia. As he dried his face, the restroom door creaked open behind him. He turned around quickly.

Sophie Neveu came in, her green eyes showing fear. "Thank God you came. We don't have much time."

Langdon stood by the sinks, staring in surprise at DCPJ cryptographer Sophie Neveu. Just minutes ago, he had heard her phone message and thought she must be crazy. But the more he listened, the more he felt she was being serious. Do not react to this message. Just listen calmly. You are in danger right now. Follow my directions very closely. Feeling uncertain, Langdon had decided to do as she said. He told Fache that the phone message was about a friend back home who was hurt. Then he had asked to use the restroom at the end of the Grand Gallery. Sophie stood before him now, still catching her breath from rushing back to the restroom. In the fluorescent lights, Langdon was surprised to see that her strong presence came from surprisingly soft features. Only her eyes were sharp, and the contrast made him think of a layered Renoir painting... hidden but clear, with a boldness that still felt mysterious.

"I wanted to warn you, Mr. Langdon..." Sophie began, still catching her breath, "that you are sous surveillance cachée. Under secret observation." As she spoke, her accented English echoed off the tile walls, making her voice sound hollow.

"But... why?" Langdon asked. Sophie had already explained it on the phone, but he wanted to hear it from her directly.

"Because," she said, stepping toward him, "Fache thinks you are the main suspect in this murder." Langdon was ready for the words, but they still sounded absurd. According to Sophie, Langdon had been called to the Louvre not as a symbologist but as a suspect and was now the unknowing target of one of DCPJ's favorite interrogation methods— surveillance cachée —a clever trick where the police calmly invite a suspect to a crime scene and interview him, hoping he will get nervous and accidentally admit his guilt.

"Look in the left pocket of your jacket," Sophie said. "You'll find proof they're watching you." Langdon felt his worry growing. Look in my pocket? It sounded like a cheap magic trick.

"Just look."

Confused, Langdon reached into the left pocket of his tweed jacket—a pocket he never used. He felt around inside but found nothing. What did you expect? He wondered if Sophie might be crazy after all. Then his fingers touched something unexpected. Small and hard. He pinched the tiny object and pulled it out, staring in surprise. It was a metallic disk, shaped like a button, about the size of a watch battery. He had never seen it before. "What the...?"

"GPS tracking dot," Sophie said. "It constantly sends its location to a GPS satellite that DCPJ can watch. We use them to track people. It's accurate within two feet anywhere in the world. They're watching you electronically. The agent who picked you up at the hotel slipped it into your pocket before you left your room." Langdon remembered the hotel room... his quick shower, getting dressed, the DCPJ agent politely holding his tweed coat as they left. It's cool outside, Mr. Langdon, the agent had said. Paris in spring isn't always what you expect. Langdon had thanked him and put on the jacket.

Sophie's sharp gaze was intense. "I didn't tell you about the tracking dot earlier because I didn't want you checking your pocket in front of Fache. He can't know you've found it." Langdon didn't know what to say.

"They put the GPS on you because they thought you might run." She paused. "Actually, they hoped you would run; it would make their case stronger."

"Why would I run!" Langdon demanded. "I'm innocent!"

"Fache doesn't think so."

Angrily, Langdon walked toward the trash can to throw away the tracking dot.

"No!" Sophie grabbed his arm and stopped him. "Leave it in your pocket. If you throw it away, the signal will stop, and they'll know you found it. Fache is only leaving you alone because he can see where you are. If he thinks you've found out what he's doing..." Sophie didn't finish. Instead, she took the metallic disk from Langdon's hand and put it back in his coat pocket. "The dot stays with you. For now, anyway." Langdon felt lost. "How could Fache really think I killed Jacques Saunière!"

"He has some pretty good reasons to suspect you." Sophie looked serious. "There's a piece of evidence here that you haven't seen yet. Fache has kept it hidden from you." Langdon could only stare.

"Do you remember the three lines Saunière wrote on the floor?" Langdon nodded. The numbers and words were stuck in his mind. Sophie whispered, "What you saw wasn't the whole message. There was a fourth line Fache photographed and then erased before you got there."

Langdon knew the special ink could be easily wiped away, but he couldn't understand why Fache would erase evidence.

"The last line," Sophie said, "was something Fache didn't want you to know." She paused. "At least, not until he was done with you." Sophie pulled a computer printout from her sweater pocket and unfolded it.

"Fache sent images of the crime scene to the Cryptology Department earlier so we could try to figure out Saunière's message. This is a photo of the complete message." She handed the page to Langdon.

Confused, Langdon looked at the image. The close-up photo showed the glowing message on the floor. The final line hit Langdon hard.

13-3-2-21-1-1-8-5

O, Draconian devil!

Oh, lame saint!

P.S. Find Robert Langdon

CHAPTER 13

For a few seconds, Langdon stared in amazement at the photo of Saunière's postscript. P.S. Find Robert Langdon. He felt like he was losing his balance. Saunière left a postscript with my name on it? He couldn't imagine why, even in his most far-fetched thoughts.

"Now do you understand," Sophie said, her eyes pleading, "why Fache asked you to come here tonight, and why he suspects you most of all?"

The only thing Langdon understood right now was why Fache had looked so pleased when Langdon said Saunière would have named his killer.

Find Robert Langdon.

"Why would Saunière write this?" Langdon asked, his confusion turning to anger.

"Why would I want to kill Jacques Saunière?"

"Fache hasn't found a reason yet, but he's been recording everything you've said tonight, hoping you'll give him one."

Langdon opened his mouth, but still couldn't speak.

"He's wearing a tiny microphone," Sophie explained. "It's connected to a transmitter in his pocket that sends the signal back to the command post."

"This is impossible," Langdon stammered. "I have an alibi. I went straight back to my hotel after my lecture. You can ask the hotel desk."

"Fache already did. His report shows you getting your room key from the front desk at about ten-thirty. Unfortunately, the murder happened closer to eleven. You could have easily left your hotel room without being seen."

"This is crazy! Fache has no proof!"

Sophie's eyes widened, as if to say: No proof? "Mr. Langdon, your name is written on the floor next to the body, and Saunière's date book says you were with him around the time of the murder." She paused. "Fache has more than enough proof to arrest you for questioning."

Langdon suddenly felt he needed a lawyer. "I didn't do this." Sophie sighed. "This isn't American television, Mr. Langdon. In France, the laws protect the police, not criminals. Unfortunately, there's also the media to consider. Jacques Saunière was a very important and well-loved person in Paris, and his murder will be news in the morning. Fache will be under immediate pressure to make a statement, and it looks much better for him to already have a suspect in custody. Whether you're guilty or not, you will definitely be held by DCPJ until they can figure out what really happened."

Langdon felt trapped. "Why are you telling me all this?"

"Because, Mr. Langdon, I believe you are innocent." Sophie looked away for a moment and then back into his eyes. "And also because it's partly my fault that you're in trouble."

"I'm sorry? Is it your fault Saunière is trying to frame me?"

"Saunière wasn't trying to frame you. It was a mistake. That message on the floor was meant for me."

Langdon needed a minute to understand that. "I'm sorry, what?"

That message wasn't for the police. He wrote it for me. I think he was forced to do everything so quickly that he didn't realize how it would look to the police." She paused. "The numbered code is pointless. Saunière wrote it to make sure the investigation involved code experts, making sure that I would know as soon as possible what had happened to him." Langdon felt himself losing touch quickly. Whether Sophie Neveu was crazy was now uncertain, but at least Langdon understood why she was trying to help him. P.S. Find Robert Langdon. She apparently believed the curator had left her a secret note telling her to find Langdon. "But why do you think his message was for you?"

"The Vitruvian Man," she said plainly. "That sketch has always been my favorite Da Vinci piece. Tonight he used it to get my attention."

"Wait. You're saying the curator knew your favorite piece of art?" She nodded. "I'm sorry. This is all out of order. Jacques Saunière and I..."

Sophie's voice broke, and Langdon heard a sudden sadness there, a painful past, hidden just below the surface. Sophie and Jacques Saunière apparently had some kind of special relationship. Langdon studied the beautiful young woman, knowing that older men in France often had young mistresses. Even so, Sophie Neveu as a "kept woman" didn't seem right.

"We had a fight ten years ago," Sophie said, her voice a whisper now. "We've barely spoken since. Tonight, when Crypto got the call that he had been murdered, and I saw the pictures of his body and text on the floor, I realized he was trying to send me a message."

"Because of The Vitruvian Man?"

"Yes. And the letters P.S."

"Post Script?"

She shook her head. "P.S. are my initials."

"But your name is Sophie Neveu."

She looked away. "P.S. is the nickname he used for me when I lived with him." She blushed. "It meant Princesse Sophie"

Langdon didn't reply.

"Silly, I know," she said. "But that was years ago. When I was a little girl."

"You knew him when you were a little girl?"

"Very well," she said, her eyes filling with emotion. "Jacques Saunière was my grandfather."

CHAPTER 14

"Where's Langdon?" Fache demanded, breathing out the last of a cigarette as he walked back into the command post.

"Still in the men's room, sir." Lieutenant Collet had been expecting the question. Fache grumbled, "Taking his time, I see."

The captain looked at the GPS dot over Collet's shoulder, and Collet could almost see what he was thinking. Fache was trying not to go check on Langdon. Ideally, someone being watched should have as much time and freedom as possible, so they feel safe. Langdon needed to come back on his own. Still, it had been almost ten minutes. Too long.

"Any chance Langdon suspects us?" Fache asked.

Collet shook his head. "We're still seeing small movements inside the men's room, so the GPS dot is clearly still on him. Maybe he feels sick? If he had found the dot, he would have removed it and tried to run."

Fache checked his watch. "Fine."

Still, Fache seemed worried. All evening, Collet had noticed a strange intensity in his captain. Usually calm and cool under pressure, Fache tonight seemed emotionally involved, as if this were somehow a personal matter for him.

Not surprising, Collet thought. Fache really needs this arrest. Lately, the Board of Ministers and the media had become more openly critical of Fache's aggressive methods, his issues with powerful foreign embassies, and his huge overspending on new technologies. Tonight, a high-tech, high-profile arrest of an American would do a lot to silence Fache's critics, helping him keep his job a few more years until he could retire with a good pension. God knows he needs the pension, Collet thought. Fache's love for technology had hurt him both professionally and personally. It was rumored that Fache had invested all his savings in the technology boom a few years back and lost everything. And Fache is a man who wears only the finest shirts.

Tonight, there was still plenty of time. Sophie Neveu's strange interruption, though unfortunate, had been only a small problem. She was gone now, and Fache still had moves to make. He had yet to tell Langdon that his name had been written on the floor by the victim. P.S. Find Robert Langdon. The American's reaction to that little bit of evidence would be very telling.

"Captain?" one of the DCPJ agents called from across the office. "You need to take this." He held out a phone, looking worried.

"Who is it?" Fache asked.

The agent frowned. "It's the head of our Cryptology Department."

"So?"

"It's about Sophie Neveu, sir. Something's wrong."

CHAPTER 15

It was time.

Silas felt strong as he stepped from the black Audi, the nighttime breeze rustling his loose robe. The winds of change are in the air. He knew the task before him would need care, not force, and he left his handgun in the car. The thirteen-round Heckler Koch USP 40 had been given to him by the Teacher.

A weapon of death has no place in a house of God.

The plaza before the great church was empty at this hour. The only people visible on the far side of Place Saint-Sulpice were a couple of teenage hookers showing what they had to offer to the late night tourists. Their young bodies stirred a familiar longing in Silas. His thigh flexed without him thinking, causing the barbed cilice belt to cut painfully into his flesh.

The lust disappeared instantly. For ten years now, Silas had faithfully denied himself all sexual pleasure, even masturbation. It was The Way. He knew he had given up much to follow Opus Dei, but he had gotten much more in return. A vow of celibacy and giving up all personal wealth hardly seemed like a sacrifice. Considering the poverty he had come from and the sexual horrors he had suffered in prison, celibacy was a welcome change. Now, having returned to France for the first time since being arrested and sent to prison in Andorra, Silas could feel his homeland testing him, dragging violent memories from his saved soul. You have been reborn, he reminded himself. His service to God today had required the sin of murder, and it was a sacrifice Silas knew he would have to hold silently in his heart forever. The measure of your faith is the measure of the pain you can endure, the Teacher had told him. Silas knew pain well and felt eager to prove himself to the Teacher, the one who had assured him his actions were ordered by a higher power.

"Hago la obra de Dios," Silas whispered, moving now toward the church entrance. Pausing in the shadow of the massive doorway, he took a deep breath. It was not until this moment that he truly realized what he was about to do, and what awaited him inside. The keystone. It will lead us to our final goal.

He raised his ghost-white fist and banged three times on the door.

Moments later, the bolts of the enormous wooden door began to move.

CHAPTER 16

Sophie wondered how long it would take Fache to realize she was still inside. Langdon seemed lost, and Sophie wondered if she had done the right thing by bringing him to the men's room.

What else could I do?

She thought of her grandfather's body, naked and spread out on the floor. He had once been everything to her, but tonight, Sophie was surprised that she felt almost no sadness for him. Jacques Saunière was now a stranger. Their relationship had vanished one night in March when she was twenty-two. Ten years ago. Sophie had returned early from graduate school in England and accidentally saw her grandfather doing something she shouldn't have. Even now, she could hardly believe what she had seen.

If I hadn't seen it myself...

Too ashamed and shocked to listen to her grandfather's awkward attempts to explain, Sophie immediately moved out, using her savings to rent a small apartment with roommates. She promised herself never to tell anyone what she had seen. Her grandfather tried hard to reach her, sending cards and letters, begging her to meet him so he could explain. Explain what!? Sophie only responded once—to tell him never to call or try to meet her in public. She feared his explanation would be worse than what she had seen.

Surprisingly, Saunière had never given up, and Sophie now had ten years of unopened letters in a drawer. To his credit, her grandfather had always respected her wish and never called her.

Until this afternoon.

"Sophie?" His voice sounded surprisingly old on her answering machine. "I have respected your wishes for so long... and it hurts me to call, but I must speak to you. Something awful has happened."

Standing in her Paris apartment, Sophie felt a chill hearing him again after all this time. His kind voice brought back many happy childhood memories.

"Sophie, please listen." He was speaking English to her, as he always did when she was a little girl. Practice French at school. Practice English at home. "You can't be angry forever. Haven't you read the letters I've sent all these years? Don't you understand yet?" He paused. "We must talk right away. Please grant your grandfather this one wish. Call me at the Louvre. Immediately. I think you and I are in great danger." Sophie stared at the answering machine. Danger? What was he talking about?

"Princess..." Her grandfather's voice broke with an emotion Sophie couldn't understand. "I know I've kept things from you, and I know it has cost me your love. But it was for your own safety. Now you must know the truth. Please, I must tell you the truth about your family." Sophie suddenly could hear her own heart. My family? Sophie's parents had died when she was only four. Their car went off a bridge into fast-moving water. Her grandmother and younger brother had also been in the car, and Sophie's entire family had disappeared in an instant. She had a box of newspaper clippings to prove it.

His words sent a sudden rush of longing through her. My family! In that brief moment, Sophie saw images from the dream that had woken her countless times when she was a little girl: My family is alive! They are coming home! But, as in her dream, the pictures vanished.

Your family is dead, Sophie. They are not coming home.

"Sophie..." her grandfather said on the machine. "I have been waiting for years to tell you. Waiting for the right moment, but now time is running out. Call me at the Louvre. As soon as you get this. I'll wait here all night. I'm afraid we both may be in danger. There's so much you need to know." The message ended.

In the silence, Sophie stood trembling for what felt like minutes. As she thought about her grandfather's message, only one idea made sense, and his real plan became clear. It was a trick.

Clearly, her grandfather really wanted to see her. He was trying anything. Her dislike for the man grew stronger. Sophie wondered if maybe he had become terminally ill and had decided to try any scheme he could think of to get Sophie to visit him one last time. If so, he had chosen well.

My family.

Now, standing in the darkness of the Louvre men's room, Sophie could hear the echoes of this afternoon's phone message. Sophie, we both may be in danger. Call me. She had not called him. Nor had she planned to. Now, however, her doubt had been greatly shaken. Her grandfather lay murdered inside his own museum. And he had written a code on the floor.

A code for her. Of this, she was sure.

Even though she didn't understand his message, Sophie felt sure its mysterious nature meant the words were for her. Sophie's love and skill for cryptography came from growing up with Jacques Saunière—a man obsessed with codes, word games, and puzzles. How many Sundays did we spend doing the cryptograms and crosswords in the newspaper?

By twelve, Sophie could finish the Le Monde crossword by herself, and her grandfather moved her on to English crosswords, math puzzles, and substitution ciphers. Sophie loved them all. Eventually, she made her passion a career, becoming a codebreaker for the Judicial Police.

Tonight, the codebreaker in Sophie had to admire how efficiently her grandfather had used a simple code to bring together two complete strangers—Sophie Neveu and Robert Langdon.

The question was why?

Sadly, from Langdon's confused look, Sophie guessed the American had no more idea than she did why her grandfather had brought them together.

She asked again, "You and my grandfather planned to meet tonight. What about?" Langdon looked truly puzzled. "His secretary set up the meeting and didn't give a specific reason, and I didn't ask. I thought he'd heard I was lecturing on the pagan symbols in French cathedrals, was interested, and thought it would be nice to meet for drinks after the talk."

Sophie didn't believe it. The connection was weak. Her grandfather knew more about pagan symbols than anyone. Plus, he was very private, not someone who would chat with random American professors without a good reason. Sophie took a deep breath and asked more. "My grandfather called me this afternoon and said he and I were in great danger. Does that mean anything to you?" Langdon's blue eyes now showed worry. "No, but given what just happened..." Sophie nodded. After tonight's events, she would be foolish not to be scared. Feeling tired, she walked to the small glass window at the end of the bathroom and looked out silently through the alarm tape in the glass. They were high up—at least forty feet.

Sighing, she looked up at the beautiful view of Paris. To her left, across the Seine, was the lit-up Eiffel Tower. Straight ahead, the Arc de Triomphe. And to the right, on top of Montmartre, was the elegant dome of Sacré-Coeur, its white stone glowing like a bright sanctuary.

Here at the far west end of the Denon Wing, the north-south road of Place du Carrousel was right next to the building, with only a small sidewalk separating it from the Louvre's outer wall. Far below, the usual delivery trucks were waiting, their lights seeming to wink at Sophie.

"I don't know what to say," Langdon said, coming up behind her. "Your grandfather is clearly trying to tell us something. I'm sorry I'm not more helpful."

Sophie turned from the window, sensing Langdon's genuine regret. Even with his own problems, he clearly wanted to help her. The teacher in him, she thought, remembering DCPJ's report on him. He was an academic who hated not understanding. We're alike in that way, she thought.

As a codebreaker, Sophie deciphered meaning from what seemed like nonsense. Tonight, she believed Langdon had information she desperately needed, whether he knew it or not. Princesse Sophie, Find Robert Langdon. Her grandfather's message couldn't be clearer. Sophie needed more time with Langdon. Time to think. Time to solve this mystery together. But time was running out.

Looking at Langdon, Sophie made her only possible move. "Bezu Fache will arrest you any minute. I can get you out of this museum. But we have to go now." Langdon's eyes widened. "You want me to run?"

"It's the best thing to do. If Fache arrests you, you'll be in a French jail for weeks while DCPJ and the U.S. Embassy argue over who gets to try you. If we escape and get to your embassy, your government will protect you while we prove you're innocent." Langdon looked unconvinced. "No way! Fache has guards at every exit! Even if we get out without being shot, running makes me look guilty. Tell Fache the message was for you, and my name isn't an accusation."

"I will," Sophie said quickly, "but after you're safe at the U.S. Embassy. It's only a mile away, and my car is just outside the museum. Arguing with Fache here is too risky. Don't you see? Fache wants to prove you're guilty. He only delayed your arrest to see if you'd do something to help his case."

"Exactly. Like running!"

Sophie's cell phone rang in her pocket. Probably Fache. She reached in and turned it off.

"Mr. Langdon," she said urgently, "I need to ask you one last question." Your future depends on it. "The writing on the floor doesn't prove you're guilty, yet Fache told our team he's certain you are. Can you think of any reason he'd think that?"

Langdon was silent for a moment. "None at all."

Sophie sighed. Which means Fache is lying. She couldn't guess why, but that didn't matter now. Fache was determined to imprison Robert Langdon tonight, no matter what. Sophie needed Langdon for herself, and this left her with only one choice.

I need to get Langdon to the U.S. Embassy.

Turning to the window, Sophie looked through the alarm mesh in the glass, down the steep forty feet to the ground. A jump from this height would likely break Langdon's legs. At best.

Still, Sophie made up her mind.

Robert Langdon was going to escape the Louvre, whether he liked it or not.

CHAPTER 17

"What do you mean she's not answering?" Fache looked like he couldn't believe it. "You're calling her cell phone, right? I know she has it with her."

Collet had been trying to reach Sophie for several minutes. "Maybe her battery died. Or she turned off the ringer."

Fache had looked worried since talking to the head of Cryptology on the phone. After hanging up, he had walked quickly to Collet and told him to get Agent Neveu on the phone. Now Collet had failed, and Fache was pacing like a caged lion.

"Why did Crypto call?" Collet asked.

Fache turned. "To tell us they found nothing about Draconian devils and lame saints."

"That's it?"

"No, also to say they figured out the numbers were Fibonacci numbers, but they thought the series didn't mean anything."

Collet was confused. "But Agent Neveu already told us that." Fache shook his head. "They didn't send Neveu."

"What?"

"According to the director, I told him to page his whole team to look at the images I'd sent. When Agent Neveu arrived, she glanced at the photos of Saunière and the code and left without a word. The director said he didn't ask why because she was understandably upset by the photos."

"Upset? Has she never seen a dead body before?"

Fache paused. "I didn't know this, and apparently neither did the director until someone told him, but Sophie Neveu is Jacques Saunière's granddaughter." Collet was stunned.

"The director said she never mentioned Saunière, and he thought she didn't want special treatment for having a famous grandfather." No wonder she was upset by the pictures. Collet could hardly believe the coincidence of calling in a young woman to decode a message written by her dead relative. Still, her actions didn't make sense. "But she knew they were Fibonacci numbers because she came here and told us. I don't get why she'd leave without saying she'd figured it out."

Collet could only think of one explanation: Saunière had written a number code hoping Fache would get cryptographers involved, and thus his granddaughter. As for the rest, was Saunière talking to his granddaughter? If so, what did the message say? And how did Langdon fit in?

Before Collet could think more, the silence of the empty museum was broken by an alarm. It seemed to be coming from the Grand Gallery.

"Alarme!" one of the agents yelled, looking at his feed from the Louvre security center. "Grande Galerie! Toilettes Messieurs!"

Fache turned to Collet. "Where's Langdon?"

"Still in the men's room!" Collet pointed to the blinking red dot on his laptop. "He must have broken the window!" Collet knew Langdon wouldn't get far. Paris fire codes required windows above fifteen meters to be breakable in case of fire, but jumping from a second-story window without a ladder would be suicide. Also, there were no trees or grass on the west side of the Denon Wing to soften a fall. The Place du Carrousel ran close to the outer wall under the restroom window. "My God," Collet said, looking at the screen. "Langdon's moving to the window ledge!" But Fache was already moving. He pulled his Manurhin MR-93 revolver from its holster and ran out.

Collet watched the screen, confused as the dot reached the ledge and did something unexpected. It moved outside the building. What's going on? he wondered. Is Langdon on a ledge or —

"Jesus!" Collet jumped up as the dot moved farther outside the wall. The signal seemed to shake for a moment, and then the blinking dot stopped suddenly about ten yards outside the building.

Fumbling with the controls, Collet pulled up a Paris street map and reset the GPS. Zooming in, he could now see exactly where the signal was.

It was no longer moving.

It was stopped in the middle of Place du Carrousel.

Langdon had jumped.

CHAPTER 18

Fache raced down the Grand Gallery as Collet's radio shouted over the loud alarm.

"He jumped!" Collet yelled. "The signal's showing on Place du Carrousel! Outside the bathroom window! And it's not moving! Jesus, I think Langdon killed himself!" Fache heard the words, but they didn't register. He kept running. The hallway seemed endless. As he ran past Saunière's body, he focused on the partitions at the far end of the Denon Wing. The alarm grew louder.

"Wait!" Collet's voice blared again. "He's moving! My God, he's alive. Langdon's moving!"

Fache kept running, cursing the long hallway with every step.

"Langdon's moving faster!" Collet yelled into the radio. "He's running down Carrousel. Wait... he's speeding up. He's moving too fast!"

Reaching the partitions, Fache weaved through them, saw the restroom door, and ran for it.

The walkie-talkie was almost drowned out by the alarm. "He must be in a car! I think he's in a car! I can't—"

Collet's words were lost in the alarm as Fache finally burst into the men's room, gun in hand. Wincing at the piercing noise, he looked around.

The stalls were empty. The bathroom was deserted. Fache's eyes went straight to the shattered window at the far end of the room. He ran to the opening and looked over the edge. Langdon was gone. Fache couldn't believe anyone would risk such a jump. If he had fallen that far, he would be badly hurt.

The alarm stopped, and Collet's voice came through the walkie-talkie again.

"...moving south... faster... crossing the Seine on Pont du Carrousel!" Fache turned. The only vehicle on Pont du Carrousel was a huge Trailor delivery truck heading south away from the Louvre. Its open bed was covered with a vinyl tarp, like a giant hammock. Fache felt a chill. That truck had probably been stopped at a red light right under the restroom window moments ago.

A crazy risk, Fache thought. Langdon couldn't know what was under the tarp. What if the truck was carrying steel? Or cement? Or garbage? A forty-foot jump? It was insane.

"The dot is turning!" Collet said. "He's turning right on Pont des Saints-Peres!" The Trailor truck that had crossed the bridge was slowing down and turning right onto Pont des Saints-Peres. So be it, Fache thought. He watched, amazed, as the truck disappeared. Collet was already radioing the agents outside, pulling them from the Louvre and sending them to their cars to follow, announcing the truck's location like a strange sports commentator.

It's over, Fache knew. His men would surround the truck in minutes. Langdon wasn't getting away.

Putting away his gun, Fache left the restroom and radioed Collet. "Bring my car around. I want to be there when we arrest him."

As Fache walked quickly back down the Grand Gallery, he wondered if Langdon had survived the fall.

Not that it mattered.

Langdon ran. Guilty.

Just fifteen yards from the restroom, Langdon and Sophie stood in the dark Grand Gallery, their backs against one of the large walls that hid the bathrooms from the gallery. They had barely hidden before Fache had run past them, gun out, and disappeared into the bathroom.

The last sixty seconds had been a blur.

Langdon stood in the men's room, refusing to run from a crime he didn't commit. Sophie was looking at the large glass window, checking out the alarm wires running through it. Then she looked down at the street, as if judging the distance.

"With some luck, you can get out of here," she said.

Luck? Feeling worried, he looked out the restroom window.

Up the street, a huge truck was approaching the stoplight beneath the window. A blue tarp was spread over the truck's large cargo area, loosely covering what it was carrying. Langdon hoped Sophie wasn't thinking what he thought she was.

"Sophie, I'm not jump—"

"Get rid of the tracker."

Confused, Langdon reached into his pocket and found the small metal disk. Sophie took it and went straight to the sink. She grabbed a big bar of soap, put the tracker on top, and pressed it down hard with her thumb. As the disk sank into the soap, she closed the hole, trapping the device inside. She gave the soap to Langdon and grabbed a heavy, round trash can from under the sinks. Before Langdon could say anything, Sophie ran at the window, holding the can like a weapon. She smashed the bottom of the trash can into the center of the window, breaking the glass.

Loud alarms went off above them.

"Give me the soap!" Sophie shouted, barely loud enough to hear over the alarm. Langdon pushed the bar into her hand.

Holding the soap, she looked out the broken window at the big rig idling below. The target was plenty big—a large, still tarp—and it was less than ten feet from the building. As the traffic lights were about to change, Sophie took a deep breath and threw the soap out into the night.

The soap fell toward the truck, landing on the edge of the tarp, and sliding down into the cargo bay as the traffic light turned green.

"Congratulations," Sophie said, pulling him toward the door. "You just escaped from the Louvre."

Leaving the men's room, they moved into the shadows as Fache rushed past.

Now that the fire alarm was off, Langdon could hear the sounds of police sirens leaving the Louvre. The police were leaving. Fache had hurried off, too, leaving the Grand Gallery empty.

"There's an emergency stairwell about fifty meters back in the Grand Gallery," Sophie said.

"Now that the guards are leaving, we can get out of here." Langdon decided to be quiet for the rest of the night. Sophie Neveu was clearly much smarter than he was.

CHAPTER 19

The Church of Saint-Sulpice is known to have the strangest story of any building in Paris. Built on the remains of an old temple to the Egyptian goddess Isis, the church is almost exactly the same size as Notre Dame. The church has been the site of baptisms for the Marquis de Sade and Baudelaire, and the wedding of Victor Hugo. The connected seminary has a history of being different and was once a secret meeting place for many hidden groups.

Tonight, the huge main area of Saint-Sulpice was as quiet as a grave. The only sign of life was the light scent of incense from the evening mass. Silas noticed Sister Sandrine seemed worried as she guided him into the church. He wasn't surprised. Silas was used to people feeling uneasy around him.

"You're an American," she said.

"French by birth," Silas replied. "I found my purpose in Spain, and now I study in the United States."

Sister Sandrine nodded. She was a small woman with calm eyes. "And you have never seen SaintSulpice?"

"I know it's almost a sin to have not seen it."

"It's more beautiful during the day."

"I'm sure. Still, I appreciate you giving me this chance tonight."

"The priest asked for it. You must have important friends." You have no idea, Silas thought.

As he followed Sister Sandrine down the main aisle, Silas was surprised by the plainness of the church. Unlike Notre Dame with its colorful paintings, gold decorations, and warm wood, Saint-Sulpice was stark and cold, feeling almost empty like the simple churches of Spain. The lack of decoration made the inside seem even bigger, and as Silas looked up at the high, arched ceiling, he felt like he was standing under the bottom of a giant, flipped-over ship.

A good image, he thought. The brotherhood's ship was about to be destroyed forever. Wanting to start, Silas wished Sister Sandrine would leave him. She was a small woman he could easily overpower, but he had promised not to use force unless he had to. She is a nun, and it is not her fault the brotherhood chose her church to hide their keystone. She shouldn't be punished for the sins of others.

"I'm sorry, Sister, that you were woken up for me."

"Not at all. You're only in Paris for a short time. You shouldn't miss Saint-Sulpice. Are you interested in the church's architecture or its history?"

"Actually, Sister, I'm interested in its spiritual side."

She laughed kindly. "That goes without saying. I just wondered where to start your tour."

Silas looked at the altar. "A tour isn't needed. You've been very kind. I can look around myself."

"It's no trouble," she said. "After all, I'm awake." Silas stopped walking. They had reached the front pew, and the altar was only fifteen yards away. He turned his large body to face the small woman, and he could feel her pull back as she looked up into his red eyes. "If you don't mind, Sister, I'm not used to just walking into a house of God and taking a tour. Would you mind if I spent some time alone praying before I look around?"

Sister Sandrine hesitated. "Oh, of course. I'll wait at the back of the church for you." Silas put a gentle but heavy hand on her shoulder and looked down. "Sister, I already feel bad for waking you up. Asking you to stay awake is too much. Please, go back to bed. I can enjoy your church and then let myself out."

She looked worried. "Are you sure you won't feel lonely?"

"Not at all. Prayer is a personal joy."

"As you wish."

Silas took his hand from her shoulder. "Sleep well, Sister. May the peace of the Lord be with you."

"And also with you." Sister Sandrine headed for the stairs. "Please make sure the door closes tightly when you leave."

"I will make sure of it." Silas watched her disappear up the stairs. Then he turned and knelt in the front pew, feeling the cilice cut into his leg.

Dear God, I offer you the work I do today....

Hidden in the shadows of the choir balcony high above the altar, Sister Sandrine quietly watched the cloaked monk kneeling alone through the railing. The sudden fear in her soul made it hard to stay still. For a moment, she wondered if this mysterious visitor could be the enemy they had warned her about, and if tonight she would have to carry out the orders she had been holding for years. She decided to stay there in the darkness and watch his every move.

CHAPTER 20

Leaving the shadows, Langdon and Sophie quietly made their way up the empty Grand Gallery corridor toward the emergency exit stairs.

As he moved, Langdon felt like he was trying to piece together a puzzle in the dark. The latest part of this mystery was very worrying: The head of the police is trying to make it look like I committed murder.

"Do you think," he whispered, "that maybe Fache wrote that message on the floor?" Sophie didn't even turn. "Impossible."

Langdon wasn't so sure. "He seems very determined to make me look guilty. Maybe he thought writing my name on the floor would help his case?"

"The Fibonacci sequence? The P.S.? All the Da Vinci and goddess symbols? That had to be my grandfather."

Langdon knew she was right. The symbols in the clues fit together too perfectly—the pentacle, The Vitruvian Man, Da Vinci, the goddess, and even the Fibonacci sequence. A clear set of symbols, as experts would call it. All closely connected.

"And his phone call to me this afternoon," Sophie added. "He said he had to tell me something. I'm sure his message at the Louvre was his last attempt to tell me something important, something he thought you could help me understand."

Langdon frowned. O, Draconian devil! Oh, lame saint! He wished he could understand the message, both for Sophie's sake and for his own. Things had definitely become more complicated since he first saw the mysterious words. His fake escape out the bathroom window was not going to make Fache like Langdon any more. He doubted the head of the French police would find it funny that they had chased down and arrested a bar of soap.

"The doorway isn't much farther," Sophie said.

"Do you think the numbers in your grandfather's message might help us understand the other lines?" Langdon had once worked on old papers that contained hidden codes, where some lines were clues to unlock the others.

"I've been thinking about the numbers all night. Adding, dividing, multiplying. I don't see anything. They seem random, like nonsense."

"But they're all part of the Fibonacci sequence. That can't be a coincidence."

"It's not. Using Fibonacci numbers was my grandfather's way of getting my attention—like writing the message in English, or posing like my favorite artwork, or drawing a pentacle on himself. He was trying to make me notice."

"The pentacle means something to you?"

"Yes. I didn't tell you, but the pentacle was a special symbol for my grandfather and me when I was a kid. We played Tarot cards for fun, and my card always came from the suit of pentacles. He probably cheated, but pentacles became our little joke."

Langdon felt a chill. They played Tarot? The old Italian card game was full of hidden, forbidden symbols, so Langdon had written a whole chapter about Tarot in his new book. The game's cards had names like The Female Pope, The Empress, and The Star. Tarot was first created as a secret way to share ideas that the Church didn't allow. Now, fortune-tellers use Tarot's mystical meanings. The Tarot suit for feminine power is pentacles, Langdon thought, realizing that if Saunière had been fixing his granddaughter's deck for fun, pentacles was a fitting inside joke. They got to the emergency stairs, and Sophie carefully opened the door. No alarm went off. Only the outside doors had alarms. Sophie led Langdon down a narrow set of stairs toward the ground floor, walking faster as they went.

"Your grandfather," Langdon said, hurrying after her, "when he told you about the pentacle, did he talk about goddess worship or disliking the Catholic Church?" Sophie shook her head. "I was more interested in the math—the Divine Proportion, PHI, Fibonacci sequences, things like that."

Langdon was surprised. "Your grandfather taught you about the number PHI?"

"Of course. The Divine Proportion." She looked embarrassed. "He even joked that I was half divine... because of the letters in my name."

Langdon thought about it for a moment and then groaned.

s-o-PHI-e.

Still going down, Langdon looked again at PHI. He was beginning to see that Saunière's clues fit together even better than he first thought.

Da Vinci... Fibonacci numbers... the pentacle.

Amazingly, all these things were linked by one idea so basic to art history that Langdon often spent several classes on it.

PHI.

He suddenly felt like he was back at Harvard, standing before his "Symbolism in Art" class, writing his favorite number on the board.

1.618

Langdon turned to his many eager students. "Who knows what this number is?" A tall math student in the back raised his hand. "That's the number PHI." He said it fee.

"Good job, Stettner," Langdon said. "Everyone, this is PHI."

"Not to be confused with PI," Stettner added, grinning. "As we mathematicians like to say: PHI is way cooler than PI!"

Langdon laughed, but nobody else seemed to get the joke.

Stettner slumped.

"This number PHI," Langdon continued, "one-point-six-one-eight, is very important in art. Who can tell me why?"

Stettner tried to recover. "Because it's so pretty?"

Everyone laughed.

"Actually," Langdon said, "Stettner's right again. PHI is generally considered the most beautiful number in the universe."

The laughter stopped, and Stettner gloated.

As Langdon loaded his slide projector, he explained that the number PHI came from the Fibonacci sequence—a series famous because adding two numbers next to each other gives you the next number, but also because dividing numbers next to each other gets closer and closer to 1.618—PHI!

Despite PHI's seemingly mysterious math beginnings, Langdon explained, the amazing thing about PHI was that it was a basic part of nature. Plants, animals, and even people all have sizes and shapes that closely follow the ratio of PHI to 1.

"PHI's commonness in nature," Langdon said, turning off the lights, "is clearly beyond chance, so the ancients thought the number PHI must have been planned by the universe's Creator. Early scientists called one-point-six-one-eight the Divine Proportion."

"Wait," said a young woman in the front row. "I study biology and I've never seen this Divine Proportion in nature."

"Really?" Langdon grinned. "Ever look at the ratio of females to males in a honeybee group?"

"Sure. There are always more female bees than male bees."

"Right. And did you know that if you divide the number of female bees by the number of male bees in any beehive, you always get the same number?"

"You do?"

"Yup. PHI."

The girl stared. "NO WAY!"

"Way!" Langdon replied, smiling as he showed a slide of a spiral seashell. "Know what this is?"

"It's a nautilus," the bio major said. "A mollusk that pumps gas into its shell to control its buoyancy."

"Correct. And can you guess the ratio of each spiral's width to the next?" The girl seemed unsure as she looked at the nautilus shell's circles. Langdon nodded. "PHI. The Divine Proportion. One-point-six-one-eight to one." The girl looked surprised.

Langdon moved to the next slide—a close-up of a sunflower's center. "Sunflower seeds grow in opposite spirals. Can you guess the ratio of each turn's width to the next?"

"PHI?" everyone said.

"Exactly." Langdon started quickly showing slides—spiraled pinecone petals, leaf arrangement on plant stems, insect sections—all showing amazing agreement with the Divine Proportion.

"This is amazing!" someone shouted.

"Yeah," someone else said, "but what does it have to do with art?"

"Aha!" Langdon said. "I'm glad you asked." He brought up another slide—a light yellow paper showing Leonardo da Vinci's famous naked man— The Vitruvian Man —named after Marcus Vitruvius, the smart Roman architect who praised the Divine Proportion in his book De Architectura.

"Da Vinci understood the human body's perfect structure better than anyone. Da Vinci even dug up dead bodies to measure the exact sizes of human bones. He was the first to show that the human body is made of parts whose size ratios always equal PHI."

Everyone in class looked doubtful.

"Don't believe me?" Langdon asked. "Next time you're in the shower, grab a tape measure." A couple of football players chuckled.

"Not just you insecure jocks," Langdon said. "All of you. Guys and girls. Try it. Measure from the top of your head to the floor. Then divide that by the distance from your belly button to the floor. Guess what number you get."

"Not PHI!" one of the jocks said, surprised.

"Yes, PHI," Langdon replied. "One-point-six-one-eight. Want another example? Measure from your shoulder to your fingertips, then divide it by the distance from your elbow to your fingertips. PHI again. Another? Hip to floor divided by knee to floor. PHI again. Finger joints. Toes. Spinal divisions. PHI. PHI. PHI. My friends, each of you is a walking example of the Divine Proportion."

Even in the darkness, Langdon could see they were all amazed. He felt a familiar warmth inside. This is why he taught. "My friends, as you can see, the world's chaos has an order underneath. When people long ago found PHI, they thought they had found God's building block for the world, and they worshipped Nature because of it. And it's easy to see why. God's hand is clear in Nature, and even today there are religions that worship nature and Mother Earth. Many of us celebrate nature like those people did, without even knowing it. May Day is a perfect example, celebrating spring... the earth coming back to life to produce its bounty. The mysterious magic in the Divine Proportion was there from the beginning. People are just following Nature's rules, and because art is how people try to copy the beauty of God's work, you can imagine we'll see the Divine Proportion a lot in art this semester."

Over the next half hour, Langdon showed them slides of artwork by Michelangelo, Albrecht Dürer, Da Vinci, and many others, showing how each artist carefully used the Divine Proportion in their compositions. Langdon pointed out PHI in the sizes of the Greek Parthenon, the pyramids of Egypt, and even the United Nations Building in New York. PHI appeared in the structures of Mozart's sonatas, Beethoven's Fifth Symphony, as well as the works of Bartók, Debussy, and Schubert. Langdon told them that the number PHI was even used by Stradivarius to figure out where to put the f-holes on his famous violins.

"In closing," Langdon said, walking to the chalkboard, "we go back to symbols." He drew five lines crossing to make a five-pointed star. "This symbol is one of the most important images you'll see this term. It's called a pentagram—or pentacle, as people long ago called it—and many cultures see it as divine and magical. Can anyone tell me why?"

Stettner, the math major, raised his hand. "Because if you draw a pentagram, the lines automatically split into sections that follow the Divine Proportion." Langdon nodded at the kid, proud. "Nice job. Yes, the ratios of line sections in a pentacle all equal PHI, making this symbol the best example of the Divine Proportion. That's why the five-pointed star has always been the symbol for beauty and perfection linked to the goddess and the sacred feminine."

The girls in class smiled.

"One thing to note, folks. We've only talked about Da Vinci a little today, but we'll see a lot more of him this semester. Leonardo really followed the old ways of the goddess.
