Tomorrow, I'll show you his painting The Last Supper, which is one of the most amazing examples of the sacred feminine you'll ever see."

"You're kidding, right?" someone said. "I thought The Last Supper was about Jesus!" Langdon winked. "There are symbols hidden in places you'd never expect."

"Come on," Sophie whispered. "What's wrong? We're almost there. Hurry!" Langdon glanced up, feeling himself return from his thoughts. He realized he was standing still on the stairs, frozen by a sudden realization.

O, Draconian devil! Oh, lame saint!

Sophie was looking back at him.

It can't be that simple, Langdon thought.

But he knew it was.

There in the depths of the Louvre... with images of PHI and Da Vinci swirling in his mind, Robert Langdon suddenly and unexpectedly solved Saunière's code.

"O, Draconian devil!" he said. "Oh, lame saint! It's the simplest kind of code!"

Sophie was stopped on the stairs below him, staring up in confusion. A code? She had been thinking about the words all night and hadn't seen a code, especially a simple one.

"You said it yourself." Langdon's voice echoed with excitement. "Fibonacci numbers only make sense in the right order. Otherwise, they're just mathematical nonsense." Sophie had no idea what he was talking about. The Fibonacci numbers? She was sure they were just meant to get the Cryptography Department involved tonight. Do they have another purpose? She reached into her pocket and pulled out the printout, studying her grandfather's message again.

13-3-2-21-1-1-8-5

O, cruel devil!

Oh, weak saint!

What about the numbers?

"The mixed-up Fibonacci sequence is a clue," Langdon said, taking the paper. "The numbers hint at how to figure out the rest of the message. He wrote the sequence out of order to tell us to do the same with the text. O, cruel devil? Oh, weak saint? Those lines mean nothing. They are just letters written out of order."

Sophie quickly understood what Langdon meant, and it seemed very simple.

"You think this message is... an anagram?" She stared at him. "Like a word puzzle from a newspaper?"

Langdon saw the doubt on Sophie's face and understood it. Few people knew that anagrams, though a simple modern game, had a long history of sacred meaning. The Kabbala's mystical teachings used anagrams a lot—rearranging Hebrew letters to find new meanings. French kings in the Renaissance thought anagrams had magic power, so they hired royal anagram experts to help them make better choices by studying important words. The Romans called the study of anagrams ars magna —"the great art." Langdon looked at Sophie, meeting her eyes. "Your grandfather's meaning was right in front of us, and he gave us plenty of clues to see it." Without another word, Langdon took a pen from his pocket and rearranged the letters in each line.

O, cruel devil! Oh, weak saint!

was a perfect anagram of...

Leonardo da Vinci! The Mona Lisa!

CHAPTER 21

The Mona Lisa.

For a moment, standing in the exit stairwell, Sophie forgot about leaving the Louvre. Her shock at the anagram was as strong as her embarrassment at not solving it herself. Sophie's skill with complicated codes had made her miss simple word games, but she knew she should have seen it. She knew anagrams well, especially in English.

When she was young, her grandfather often used anagrams to improve her English spelling. Once, he wrote the word "planets" and told Sophie that sixty-two other English words of different lengths could be made using those same letters. Sophie spent three days with a dictionary until she found them all.

"I can't believe," Langdon said, looking at the printout, "how your grandfather made such a complex anagram so quickly before he died."

Sophie knew the answer, and it made her feel even worse. I should have seen this!

She remembered that her grandfather—a wordplay fan and art lover—had enjoyed making anagrams of famous artworks when he was young. One of his anagrams even caused trouble when Sophie was little. While being interviewed by an American art magazine, Saunière had shared his dislike for the modern Cubist movement by saying that Picasso's painting Les Demoiselles d'Avignon was a perfect anagram of vile meaningless doodles. Picasso's fans were not happy.

"My grandfather probably made this Mona Lisa anagram a long time ago," Sophie said, looking at Langdon. And tonight he had to use it as a quick code. Her grandfather's message was clear and unsettling.

Leonardo da Vinci!

The Mona Lisa!

Why his last words to her mentioned the famous painting, Sophie didn't know, but she could only imagine one reason. A worrying one.

Those weren't his last words....

Was she meant to see the Mona Lisa? Had her grandfather left her a message there? It seemed quite possible. After all, the famous painting was in the Salle des Etats—a private room only reached from the Grand Gallery. Sophie now saw that the doors to this room were only twenty meters from where her grandfather had died.

He could easily have seen the Mona Lisa before he died.

Sophie looked back at the emergency stairs, feeling unsure. She knew she should get Langdon out of the museum at once, but something told her not to. Remembering her first visit to the Denon Wing as a child, Sophie thought that if her grandfather had a secret for her, Da Vinci's Mona Lisa was the perfect place to share it.

"She's just a little further," her grandfather had whispered, holding Sophie's small hand as he led her through the empty museum after hours.

Sophie was six. She felt small as she looked up at the huge ceilings and down at the dizzying floor. The empty museum scared her, but she didn't want her grandfather to know. She set her jaw and let go of his hand.

"Up ahead is the Salle des Etats," her grandfather said as they neared the Louvre's most famous room. Despite his excitement, Sophie wanted to go home. She had seen pictures of the Mona Lisa and didn't like it. She didn't get why everyone cared so much.

"C'est ennuyeux," Sophie mumbled.

"Boring," he corrected. "French at school. English at home."

"The Louvre is not my home!" she argued.

He chuckled wearily. "You're right. Let's speak English just for kicks." Sophie frowned and kept walking. As they entered the Salle des Etats, her eyes darted around the small room and stopped on the main attraction—the center of the right wall, where a single painting hung behind a protective Plexiglas screen. Her grandfather stopped at the entrance and pointed to the painting.

"Go ahead, Sophie. Not many get to see her alone." Trying to calm her nerves, Sophie slowly walked across the room. After all she'd heard about the Mona Lisa, she felt like she was approaching someone royal. Reaching the Plexiglas, Sophie held her breath and looked up, taking it all in. Sophie wasn't sure what she expected to feel, but it definitely wasn't this. No sudden shock of amazement. No moment of awe. The famous face looked just like it did in books. She stood silently for what seemed like forever, waiting for something to happen.

"So, what do you think?" her grandfather whispered, coming up behind her. "Beautiful, right?"

"She's too small."

Saunière smiled. "You're small, and you're beautiful."

I am not beautiful, she thought. Sophie hated her red hair and freckles, and she was taller than all the boys in her class. She looked back at the Mona Lisa and shook her head. "She's even worse than in the books. Her face is... brumeux."

"Foggy," her grandfather corrected.

"Foggy," Sophie repeated, knowing the conversation wouldn't go on until she said the new word.

"That's called the sfumato style," he told her, "and it's very difficult to do. Leonardo da Vinci was better at it than anyone."

Sophie still didn't like the painting. "She looks like she knows something... like when kids at school have a secret."

Her grandfather laughed. "That's part of why she is so famous. People like to guess why she is smiling."

"Do you know why she's smiling?"

"Maybe." Her grandfather winked. "Someday I'll tell you all about it." Sophie stamped her foot. "I told you I don't like secrets!"

"Princess," he smiled. "Life is full of secrets. You can't learn them all at once."

"I'm going back up," Sophie said, her voice echoing in the stairwell.

"To the Mona Lisa?" Langdon stepped back. "Now?" Sophie thought about the risk. "I'm not a murder suspect. I'll take my chances. I need to understand what my grandfather was trying to tell me."

"What about the embassy?"

Sophie felt bad for making Langdon a fugitive and then leaving him, but she didn't see any other way. She pointed down the stairs to a metal door. "Go through that door, and follow the lit exit signs. My grandfather used to bring me down here. The signs will lead you to a security gate. It only lets you go one way and opens outward." She handed Langdon her car keys. "Mine is the red SmartCar in the employee lot, right outside this wall. Do you know how to get to the embassy?"

Langdon nodded, looking at the keys in his hand.

"Listen," Sophie said, her voice softening. "I think my grandfather may have left me a message at the Mona Lisa—some kind of clue about who killed him, or why I'm in danger." Or what happened to my family. "I have to go see."

"But if he wanted to tell you why you were in danger, why wouldn't he just write it on the floor where he died? Why this complicated game?"

"Whatever my grandfather was trying to tell me, I don't think he wanted anyone else to hear it. Not even the police." Clearly, her grandfather had done everything he could to send a private message directly to her. He had written it in code, included her secret initials, and told her to find Robert Langdon—a smart move, considering the American symbologist had figured out his code. "As strange as it may sound," Sophie said, "I think he wants me to get to the Mona Lisa before anyone else does."

"I'll come."

"No! We don't know how long the Grand Gallery will stay empty. You have to go." Langdon seemed unsure, as if his own curiosity was about to overpower his good sense and pull him back into Fache's hands.

"Go. Now." Sophie gave him a grateful smile. "I'll see you at the embassy, Mr. Langdon." Langdon looked unhappy. "I'll meet you there on one condition," he replied, his voice firm. She paused, surprised. "What's that?"

"That you stop calling me Mr. Langdon."

Sophie saw the faint hint of a smile forming on Langdon's face, and she smiled back. "Good luck, Robert."

When Langdon reached the bottom of the stairs, he smelled linseed oil and plaster dust. Ahead, a lit SORTIE/EXIT sign showed an arrow pointing down a long corridor.

Langdon stepped into the hallway.

To the right was a dark restoration studio filled with statues being fixed. To the left, Langdon saw studios like Harvard art classrooms—rows of easels, paintings, palettes, framing tools—an art production line. Walking down the hall, Langdon felt like he might wake up in his bed in Cambridge. The whole night felt like a strange dream. I'm about to run out of the Louvre... as a fugitive.

Saunière's clever anagram was still in his thoughts, and Langdon wondered what Sophie would find at the Mona Lisa... if anything. She seemed sure her grandfather wanted her to see the famous painting again. Although this made sense, Langdon was bothered by a confusing problem.

P.S. Find Robert Langdon.

Saunière had written Langdon's name on the floor, telling Sophie to find him. But why?

Just so Langdon could help her solve an anagram?

That seemed unlikely.

Saunière had no reason to think Langdon was good at anagrams. We've never even met. More importantly, Sophie said she should have solved the anagram herself. She found the Fibonacci sequence, and she probably would have figured out the message with a little more time, without Langdon's help. Sophie was supposed to solve that anagram on her own. Langdon was now more sure of this, but the conclusion left a big hole in Saunière's actions.

Why me? Langdon wondered, walking down the hall. Why did Saunière want his granddaughter to find me before he died? What does Saunière think I know?

Suddenly, Langdon stopped. Eyes wide, he took out the computer printout from his pocket. He looked at the last line of Saunière's message. P.S. Find Robert Langdon.

He stared at two letters.

P.S.

In that instant, Langdon felt Saunière's puzzling symbolism become clear. Like a clap of thunder, all his knowledge of symbols and history suddenly made sense. Everything Jacques Saunière had done tonight was suddenly perfectly clear.

Langdon's thoughts raced as he tried to understand what it all meant. Turning quickly, he stared back the way he had come.

Is there time?

He knew it didn't matter.

Without hesitation, Langdon sprinted back toward the stairs.

CHAPTER 22

Kneeling in the first pew, Silas pretended to pray as he looked around the sanctuary. Saint-Sulpice, like most churches, was built like a giant Roman cross. Its long middle part—the nave—led right to the main altar, where it was crossed by a shorter section, called the transept. The place where the nave and transept met was right under the main dome and was seen as the heart of the church... its most holy and mysterious point. Not tonight, Silas thought. Saint-Sulpice hides its secrets elsewhere. Turning his head to the right, he looked into the south transept, toward the open floor beyond the pews, at what his victims had described.

There it is.

Set into the gray granite floor was a thin, shiny strip of brass... a golden line running across the church floor. The line had markings like a ruler. It was a gnomon, Silas had been told, a pagan tool like a sundial used for astronomy. Tourists, scientists, historians, and pagans from all over came to Saint-Sulpice to see this famous line. The Rose Line.

Slowly, Silas followed the brass strip as it went across the floor from his right to left, slanting in front of him at an odd angle, not matching the church's symmetry at all. Cutting across the main altar, the line looked to Silas like a cut on a beautiful face. The strip split the communion rail in two and then crossed the whole width of the church, ending at the corner of the north transept, where it met a very unexpected thing.

A huge Egyptian obelisk.

Here, the shiny Rose Line turned ninety degrees upward and went straight up the obelisk itself, climbing thirty-three feet to the very top of the pyramid-shaped point, where it stopped.

The Rose Line, Silas thought. The brotherhood hid the keystone at the Rose Line. Earlier, when Silas told the Teacher that the Priory keystone was hidden in Saint-Sulpice, the Teacher seemed unsure. But when Silas added that the brothers had given him a precise location, related to a brass line in Saint-Sulpice, the Teacher gasped in realization. "You speak of the Rose Line!"

The Teacher quickly told Silas about Saint-Sulpice's famous oddity—a strip of brass that divided the sanctuary on a perfect north-south line. It was an old sundial, a leftover from the pagan temple that had once stood there. The sun's rays, shining through the opening on the south wall, moved down the line each day, marking the passage of time from one solstice to the next.

The north-south stripe was known as the Rose Line. For centuries, the Rose symbol had been linked to maps and guiding people in the right direction. The Compass Rose—on almost every map—showed North, East, South, and West. Originally called the Wind Rose, it showed the directions of the thirty-two winds, blowing from the directions of eight major winds, eight half-winds, and sixteen quarter-winds. When drawn inside a circle, these thirty-two points looked just like a traditional thirty-two petal rose. Even now, the main tool for navigation is still called a Compass Rose, with its northern direction marked by an arrowhead... or, more often, the fleur-de-lis symbol. On a globe, a Rose Line—also called a meridian or longitude—was any imaginary line from the North Pole to the South Pole. Of course, there were countless Rose Lines because any point on the globe could have a longitude line connecting the north and south poles. The question for early navigators was which of these lines would be the Rose Line—the zero longitude—the line from which all other longitudes on earth would be measured. Today that line is in Greenwich, England.

But it had not always been.

Before Greenwich became the world's prime meridian, the zero longitude went right through Paris, and through the Church of Saint-Sulpice. The brass marker in Saint-Sulpice was a reminder of the world's first prime meridian. Even though Greenwich took the honor from Paris in 1888, the original Rose Line was still there.

"So the legend is true," the Teacher had told Silas. "The Priory keystone is said to lie 'beneath the Sign of the Rose.'"

Still on his knees, Silas looked around the church to be sure he was alone. For a moment, he thought he heard a noise in the choir balcony. He looked up for a few seconds. Nothing.

I am alone.

He stood, faced the altar, and bowed three times. Then he turned left and followed the brass line north toward the obelisk.

At that moment, at Leonardo da Vinci International Airport in Rome, the bump of the tires on the runway woke Bishop Aringarosa.

I fell asleep, he thought, surprised he was relaxed enough to do so.

"Benvenuto a Roma," the intercom announced.

Sitting up, Aringarosa straightened his black cassock and allowed himself a rare smile. He had been happy to make this trip. I have been on the defensive for too long. Tonight, however, things had changed. Only five months ago, Aringarosa had feared for the future of the Church. Now, as if by God's will, the solution had appeared.

Divine intervention.

If all went as planned tonight in Paris, Aringarosa would soon have something that would make him the most powerful man in the Christian world.

CHAPTER 23

Sophie arrived breathless outside the large wooden doors of the Salle des Etats—the room that held the Mona Lisa. Before going in, she sadly looked farther down the hall, about twenty yards, to where her grandfather's body still lay under the spotlight. She suddenly felt a strong sense of regret, a deep sadness mixed with guilt. The man had tried to connect with her many times over the past ten years, but Sophie had been unyielding—leaving his letters and packages unopened in a drawer and refusing to see him. He lied to me! Kept terrible secrets! What else could I do? So she had shut him out. Completely.

Now her grandfather was dead, and he was speaking to her from beyond the grave. The Mona Lisa.

She reached for the huge wooden doors and pushed. The entrance opened wide. Sophie paused, looking at the large rectangular room beyond. It was also bathed in a soft red light. The Salle des Etats was one of the museum's rare dead ends—the only room off the middle of the Grand Gallery. This door, the room's only entrance, faced a large fifteen-foot Botticelli painting on the far wall. Under it, in the center of the floor, a large octagonal bench offered a welcome rest for the thousands of visitors admiring the Louvre's most valuable piece.

Even before Sophie entered, she knew something was missing. A black light. She looked down the hall at her grandfather under the lights, surrounded by equipment. If he had written anything in here, he almost certainly would have used the watermark pen.

Taking a deep breath, Sophie hurried to the brightly lit crime scene. Unable to look at her grandfather, she focused on the tools. Finding a small ultraviolet penlight, she slipped it into her sweater pocket and hurried back toward the open doors of the Salle des Etats.

Sophie turned the corner and stepped inside. But she was met by the unexpected sound of muffled footsteps racing toward her from inside the room. Someone's in here! A ghostly figure suddenly appeared from the reddish haze. Sophie jumped back.

"There you are!" Langdon's hoarse whisper cut the air as his silhouette stopped in front of her. Her relief was brief. "Robert, I told you to get out of here! If Fache—"

"Where were you?"

"I had to get the black light," she whispered, holding it up. "If my grandfather left me a message—"

"Sophie, listen." Langdon paused, his blue eyes fixed on hers. "The letters P.S.... do they mean anything else to you? Anything at all?"

Afraid someone might hear them, Sophie pulled him into the Salle des Etats and quietly closed the huge doors, trapping them inside. "I told you, the initials mean Princess Sophie."

"I know, but have you ever seen them anywhere else? Did your grandfather ever use P.S. in any other way? Like on a monogram, or maybe on his stationery or something personal?"

The question surprised her. How would Robert know that? Sophie had seen the initials P.S. once before, as a monogram. It was the day before her ninth birthday. She was secretly searching the house for hidden birthday presents. Even then, she couldn't stand secrets. What did Grand-père get me this year? She looked through cupboards and drawers. Did he get me the doll I wanted? Where would he hide it?

Finding nothing, Sophie decided to sneak into her grandfather's bedroom. She wasn't allowed in there, but her grandfather was downstairs asleep on the couch. I'll just take a quick look!

Walking quietly across the creaky wood floor to his closet, Sophie peeked on the shelves behind his clothes. Nothing. Next, she looked under the bed. Still nothing. Moving to his bureau, she opened the drawers and carefully looked through them. There must be something for me here! As she reached the bottom drawer, she still hadn't found any sign of a doll. Disappointed, she opened the last drawer and moved aside some black clothes she had never seen him wear. She was about to close the drawer when she saw something gold in the back. It looked like a pocket watch chain, but she knew he didn't wear one. Her heart beat faster as she realized what it must be.

A necklace!

Sophie carefully pulled the chain from the drawer. To her surprise, there was a shiny gold key on the end. Heavy and bright. Fascinated, she held it up. It looked like no key she had ever seen. Most keys were flat with jagged teeth, but this one had a triangular shape with little marks all over it. Its large golden head was shaped like a cross, but not a normal cross. This was an even one, like a plus sign. In the middle of the cross was a strange symbol—two letters connected with a flowery design.

"P.S.," she whispered, frowning as she read the letters. What could this be?

"Sophie?" her grandfather said from the doorway.

Startled, she spun, dropping the key on the floor with a loud clang. She stared down at the key, afraid to look up at her grandfather's face. "I... was looking for my birthday present," she said, hanging her head, knowing she had betrayed his trust.

For what seemed like forever, her grandfather stood silently in the doorway. Finally, he let out a long, troubled breath. "Pick up the key, Sophie."

Sophie picked up the key.

Her grandfather walked in. "Sophie, you need to respect other people's privacy." Gently, he knelt down and took the key from her. "This key is very special. If you had lost it..." Her grandfather's quiet voice made Sophie feel even worse. "I'm sorry, Grand-père. I really am." She paused. "I thought it was a necklace for my birthday."

He gazed at her for several seconds. "I'll say this again, Sophie, because it's important. You need to learn to respect other people's privacy."

"Yes, Grand-père."

"We'll talk about this later. Right now, the garden needs to be weeded." Sophie hurried outside to do her chores.

The next morning, Sophie received no birthday present from her grandfather. She hadn't expected one, not after what she had done. But he didn't even wish her happy birthday all day. Sadly, she trudged up to bed that night. As she climbed in, though, she found a note card lying on her pillow. On the card was written a simple riddle. Even before she solved the riddle, she was smiling. I know what this is! Her grandfather had done this for her last Christmas morning. A treasure hunt!

Eagerly, she studied the riddle until she solved it. The solution pointed her to another part of the house, where she found another card and another riddle. She solved this one too, racing on to the next card. Running wildly, she darted back and forth across the house, from clue to clue, until at last she found a clue that directed her back to her own bedroom. Sophie dashed up the stairs, rushed into her room, and stopped in her tracks. There in the middle of the room sat a shining red bicycle with a ribbon tied to the handlebars. Sophie shrieked with delight.

"I know you asked for a doll," her grandfather said, smiling in the corner. "I thought you might like this even better."

The next day, her grandfather taught her to ride, running beside her down the walkway. When Sophie steered out over the thick lawn and lost her balance, they both went tumbling onto the grass, rolling and laughing.

"Grand-père," Sophie said, hugging him. "I'm really sorry about the key."

"I know, sweetie. You're forgiven. I can't stay mad at you. Grandfathers and granddaughters always forgive each other."

Sophie knew she shouldn't ask, but she couldn't help it. "What does it open? I never saw a key like that. It was very pretty."

Her grandfather was silent a long moment, and Sophie could see he wasn't sure how to answer. Grand-père never lies. "It opens a box," he finally said. "Where I keep many secrets." Sophie pouted. "I hate secrets!"

"I know, but these are important secrets. And someday, you'll learn to like them as much as I do."

"I saw letters on the key, and a flower."

"Yes, that's my favorite flower. It's called a fleur-de-lis. We have them in the garden. The white ones. In English we call that kind of flower a lily."

"I know those! They're my favorite too!"

"Then I'll make a deal with you." Her grandfather's eyebrows raised the way they always did when he was about to give her a challenge. "If you can keep my key a secret, and never talk about it ever again, to me or anybody, then someday I will give it to you."

Sophie couldn't believe her ears. "You will?"

"I promise. When the time comes, the key will be yours. It has your name on it." Sophie frowned. "No, it doesn't. It says P.S. My name isn't P.S.!" Her grandfather lowered his voice and looked around as if to make sure no one was listening.

"Okay, Sophie, if you really want to know, P.S. is a code. It's your secret initials." Her eyes went wide. "I have secret initials?"

"Of course. Granddaughters always have secret initials that only their grandfathers know."

"P.S.?"

He tickled her. "Princess Sophie."

She giggled. "I'm not a princess!"

He winked. "You are to me."

From that day on, they never spoke of the key again. And she became his Princess Sophie.

Inside the Salle des Etats, Sophie stood in silence and felt the sharp sting of loss.

"The initials," Langdon whispered, looking at her strangely. "Have you seen them?" Sophie felt her grandfather's voice whisper in the museum halls. Never speak of this key, Sophie. Not to me or anyone. She knew she hadn't forgiven him, and she wondered if she could break his trust again. P.S. Find Robert Langdon. Her grandfather wanted Langdon to help. Sophie nodded. "Yes, I saw the initials P.S. once. When I was very young."

"Where?"

Sophie hesitated. "On something very important to him."

Langdon looked straight at her. "Sophie, this is important. Can you tell me if the initials appeared with a symbol? A fleur-de-lis?"

Sophie felt herself step back in surprise. "But... how could you know that!" Langdon sighed and lowered his voice. "I'm pretty sure your grandfather was a member of a secret society. A very old hidden group."

Sophie felt her stomach tighten. She was sure of it too. For ten years she had tried to forget the event that had confirmed that terrible fact for her. She had seen something impossible to accept. Unforgivable.

"The fleur-de-lis," Langdon said, "with the initials P.S., that is the group's official symbol. Their coat of arms. Their logo."

"How do you know this?" Sophie hoped Langdon wasn't going to tell her that he himself was a member.

"I've written about this group," he said, his voice shaking with excitement. "Studying the symbols of secret societies is something I specialize in. They call themselves the Prieuré de Sion — the Priory of Sion. They're based here in France and have powerful members from all over Europe. They are one of the oldest secret societies on earth." Sophie had never heard of them.

Langdon was talking quickly now. "The Priory's members have included some of history's most educated people: men like Botticelli, Sir Isaac Newton, Victor Hugo." He paused, his voice full of academic interest. "And, Leonardo da Vinci." Sophie stared. "Da Vinci was in a secret society?"

Da Vinci led the Priory from 1510 to 1519 as its Grand Master, which might explain your grandfather's love for Leonardo's work. The two men share a historical connection. And it all fits with their interest in goddess symbols, paganism, female deities, and dislike for the Church. The Priory is known to have always honored the sacred feminine.

"You're saying this group is a pagan goddess worship cult?"

"More like the pagan goddess worship cult. But more important, they are known as the guardians of an ancient secret. One that made them incredibly powerful."

Despite Langdon's confidence, Sophie's first thought was disbelief. A secret pagan cult? Once led by Leonardo da Vinci? It all seemed ridiculous. And yet, even as she rejected it, she remembered a night ten years ago when she had surprised her grandfather and seen something she still couldn't accept. Could that explain —?

"The identities of living Priory members are closely guarded," Langdon said, "but the P.S. and fleur-de-lis that you saw as a child are proof. It could only have been related to the Priory." Sophie realized Langdon knew more about her grandfather than she thought. This American clearly had a lot to tell her, but not here. "I can't let them catch you, Robert. We need to talk. You need to leave!"

Langdon barely heard her. He wasn't going anywhere. He was lost in another world now. A world where old secrets came to light. A world where forgotten histories emerged.

Slowly, as if underwater, Langdon turned and looked through the reddish light toward the Mona Lisa.

The fleur-de-lis... the flower of Lisa... the Mona Lisa.

It was all connected, a silent song echoing the deepest secrets of the Priory of Sion and Leonardo da Vinci.

A few miles away, on the riverbank beyond Les Invalides, the confused driver of a twin-bed truck stood at gunpoint and watched as the captain of the Judicial Police roared in anger and threw a bar of soap into the swollen waters of the Seine.

CHAPTER 24

Silas looked up at the Saint-Sulpice obelisk, taking in the tall marble pillar. He felt excited and tense. He looked around the church again to be sure he was alone. Then he knelt at the base of the pillar, not to worship, but because he had to. The keystone is hidden under the Rose Line.

At the base of the Sulpice obelisk.

All the brothers had agreed.

On his knees, Silas felt the stone floor. He saw no cracks or marks to show a tile that could be moved, so he started tapping lightly with his knuckles on the floor. Following the brass line closer to the obelisk, he knocked on each tile next to the brass line. Finally, one of them sounded odd.

There's an empty space under the floor!

Silas smiled. His victims had told the truth.

He stood up and looked around for something to break the floor tile.

High above Silas, in the balcony, Sister Sandrine held back a gasp. Her worst fears had come true. This visitor was not who he appeared to be. The mysterious Opus Dei monk had come to Saint-Sulpice for another reason. A secret reason.

You are not the only one with secrets, she thought.

Sister Sandrine Bieil was more than just the keeper of this church. She was a guard. And tonight, the old wheels had started turning. The arrival of this stranger at the foot of the obelisk was a signal from the brotherhood.

It was a quiet cry for help.

CHAPTER 25

The U.S. Embassy in Paris is a small area on Avenue Gabriel, just north of the Champs-Elysées. The three-acre space is considered U.S. land, meaning anyone on it has the same laws and protections as in the United States. The embassy's night operator was reading Time magazine's International Edition when her phone rang.

"U.S. Embassy," she answered.

"Good evening." The caller spoke English with a French accent. "I need some help." Although the man was polite, he sounded stern and formal. "I was told you had a phone message for me on your automated system. The name is Langdon. I've forgotten my three-digit code. If you could help me, I'd appreciate it." The operator paused, confused. "I'm sorry, sir. Your message must be old. That system was removed two years ago for security reasons. Also, all the codes were five-digit. Who told you we had a message for you?"

"You have no automated phone system?"

"No, sir. Any message for you would be written by hand in our services department. What was your name again?"

But the man had hung up.

Bezu Fache was stunned as he walked along the Seine. He was sure he had seen Langdon dial a local number, enter a three-digit code, and then listen to a recording. But if Langdon didn't call the embassy, then who did he call?

Just then, looking at his cell phone, Fache realized the answer was in his hand. Langdon used my phone to make that call.

Using the cell phone's menu, Fache found the list of recent calls and saw the one Langdon had made.

An exchange in Paris, then the code 454.

Fache dialed again, waiting as the phone rang.

Finally, a woman answered. "Bonjour, vous êtes bien chez Sophie Neveu," the recording said. "Je suis absente pour le moment, mais..." Fache's blood boiled as he typed 4... 5... 4.

CHAPTER 26

Despite her huge fame, the Mona Lisa was only thirty-one inches by twenty-one inches—smaller than the posters of her sold in the Louvre gift shop. She hung on the northwest wall of the Salle des Etats behind a two-inch-thick piece of protective Plexiglas. Painted on a poplar wood panel, her dreamy, misty look was due to Da Vinci's skill with the sfumato style, in which shapes seem to fade into each other. Since coming to the Louvre, the Mona Lisa —or La Jaconde as she is called in France—had been stolen twice, most recently in 1911, when she disappeared from the Louvre's

"satte impénétrable" —Le Salon Carre. Parisians cried in the streets and wrote newspaper articles begging the thieves to return the painting. Two years later, the Mona Lisa was found hidden in the false bottom of a trunk in a Florence hotel room.

Langdon, now making it clear to Sophie that he wasn't going to leave, moved with her across the Salle des Etats. The Mona Lisa was still twenty yards ahead when Sophie turned on the black light, and the bluish light spread out on the floor in front of them. She swung the light back and forth across the floor like a minesweeper, looking for any sign of glowing ink.

Walking beside her, Langdon was already feeling excited about seeing the great artwork up close. He tried to see beyond the purple light from the black light in Sophie's hand. To the left, the room's octagonal viewing couch appeared, looking like a dark island on the empty wooden floor.

Langdon could now see the dark glass on the wall. Behind it, he knew, inside her own private space, hung the most famous painting in the world. The Mona Lisa's fame, Langdon knew, had nothing to do with her mysterious smile. It also wasn't because of the strange interpretations given to her by many art historians and conspiracy theorists. Simply put, the Mona Lisa was famous because Leonardo da Vinci said she was his best work. He carried the painting with him when he traveled and, when asked why, would say that he found it hard to be apart from his most beautiful expression of female beauty.

Even so, many art historians thought Da Vinci's love for the Mona Lisa had nothing to do with its artistic quality. Actually, the painting was a surprisingly normal sfumato portrait. Da Vinci's admiration for this work, many claimed, came from something much deeper: a hidden message in the layers of paint. The Mona Lisa was, in fact, one of the world's most well-known inside jokes. The painting's many double meanings and playful references had been revealed in most art history books, and yet, surprisingly, the public still thought her smile was a great mystery.

No mystery at all, Langdon thought, moving forward and watching as the faint outline of the painting began to appear. No mystery at all.

Most recently Langdon had shared the Mona Lisa's secret with an unlikely group—a dozen inmates at the Essex County Penitentiary. Langdon's jail seminar was part of a Harvard program trying to bring education into the prison system— Culture for Convicts, as Langdon's colleagues liked to call it.

Standing at an overhead projector in a darkened prison library, Langdon had shared the Mona Lisa's secret with the prisoners in the class, men whom he found surprisingly interested—rough, but smart. "You may notice," Langdon told them, walking up to the projected image of the Mona Lisa on the library wall, "that the background behind her face is uneven." Langdon pointed to the obvious difference. "Da Vinci painted the horizon line on the left much lower than the right."

"He messed it up?" one of the inmates asked.

Langdon chuckled. "No. Da Vinci didn't do that very often. Actually, this is a little trick Da Vinci used. By making the land lower on the left, Da Vinci made Mona Lisa look much bigger from the left side than from the right side. A little Da Vinci inside joke. In the past, male and female ideas have been linked to sides—left is female, and right is male. Because Da Vinci really liked feminine ideas, he made Mona Lisa look more impressive from the left than the right."

"I heard he was gay," said a small man with a goatee.

Langdon winced. "Historians don't usually say it like that, but yes, Da Vinci was gay."

"Is that why he liked that whole feminine thing?"

"Actually, Da Vinci understood the balance between male and female. He believed that a person could not be truly enlightened unless they had both male and female parts."

"You mean like girls with dicks?" someone called.

This got a lot of laughs. Langdon thought about explaining where the word hermaphrodite comes from and how it's related to Hermes and Aphrodite, but he figured this group wouldn't understand.

"Hey, Mr. Langford," a muscle-bound man said. "Is it true that the Mona Lisa is a picture of Da Vinci dressed as a woman? I heard that was true."

"It's very possible," Langdon said. "Da Vinci liked to play jokes, and computer analysis of the Mona Lisa and Da Vinci's self-portraits shows some surprising similarities in their faces. Whatever Da Vinci was doing," Langdon said, "his Mona Lisa is neither male nor female. It has a subtle message of being both. It is a combination of both."

"You sure that's not just a fancy way of saying the Mona Lisa is ugly?" Langdon laughed. "You might be right. But Da Vinci actually left a big hint that the painting was meant to be both male and female. Has anyone here heard of an Egyptian god named Amon?"

"Of course!" the big guy said. "God of male fertility!" Langdon was surprised.

"It's on every box of Amon condoms." The muscular man grinned. "It has a guy with a ram's head on the front and says he's the Egyptian god of fertility." Langdon didn't know the brand, but he was glad the condom makers had gotten their symbols right. "Good. Amon is shown as a man with a ram's head, and his love life and curved horns are related to the word 'horny.'"

"Really!"

"Really," Langdon said. "And do you know who Amon's female partner was? The Egyptian goddess of fertility?"

The question was met with silence.

"It was Isis," Langdon told them, grabbing a marker. "So we have the male god, Amon." He wrote it down. "And the female goddess, Isis, whose old symbol was once called L'ISA." Langdon finished writing and stepped back from the projector.

AMON L'ISA

"Sound familiar?" he asked.

"Mona Lisa... wow," someone gasped.

Langdon nodded. "Gentlemen, the Mona Lisa's face not only looks androgynous, but her name is an anagram for the divine union of male and female. And that, my friends, is Da Vinci's little secret, and the reason for Mona Lisa's knowing smile."

"My grandfather was here," Sophie said, suddenly kneeling, only ten feet from the Mona Lisa. She pointed the black light at a spot on the floor. At first, Langdon saw nothing. Then, kneeling beside her, he saw a tiny drop of dried liquid glowing. Ink? He suddenly remembered what black lights were really used for. Blood. His senses sharpened. Sophie was right. Jacques Saunière had visited the Mona Lisa before he died.

"He had a reason to come here," Sophie whispered, standing up. "I know he left a message for me here." She quickly walked to the Mona Lisa, shining the light on the floor in front of the painting. She waved the light across the bare floor.

"There's nothing here!"

Langdon then saw a faint purple glimmer on the protective glass in front of the Mona Lisa. Reaching down, he took Sophie's wrist and slowly moved the light up to the painting. They both froze.

Six purple words glowed on the glass, scrawled across the Mona Lisa's face.

CHAPTER 27

Seated at Saunière's desk, Lieutenant Collet held the phone to his ear in disbelief. Did I hear Fache right? "A bar of soap? But how could Langdon have known about the GPS dot?"

"Sophie Neveu," Fache replied. "She told him."

"What! Why?"

"Good question, but I just heard a recording that confirms she warned him." Collet was speechless. What was Neveu thinking? Fache had proof that Sophie had messed with a DCPJ operation? Sophie Neveu was not only going to be fired, she was also going to jail.

"But, Captain... where is Langdon now?"

"Have any fire alarms gone off there?"

"No, sir."

"And no one has come out under the Grand Gallery gate?"

"No. We've got a Louvre security officer on the gate. Just as you asked."

"Okay, Langdon must still be inside the Grand Gallery."

"Inside? But what is he doing?"

"Is the Louvre security guard armed?"

"Yes, sir. He's a senior warden."

"Send him in," Fache ordered. "I can't get my men back to the outside for a few minutes, and I don't want Langdon running for an exit." Fache paused. "And you should tell the guard Agent Neveu is probably in there with him."

"Agent Neveu left, I thought."

"Did you actually see her leave?"

"No, sir, but—"

"Well, nobody outside saw her leave either. They only saw her go in." Collet was stunned by Sophie Neveu's boldness. She's still inside the building?

"Handle it," Fache ordered. "I want Langdon and Neveu held at gunpoint by the time I get back."

As the truck drove off, Captain Fache gathered his men. Robert Langdon had been hard to catch tonight, and with Agent Neveu now helping him, he might be even harder to trap than expected.

Fache decided to be careful.

Playing it safe, he ordered half of his men back to the Louvre area. The other half he sent to guard the only place in Paris where Robert Langdon could find safety.

CHAPTER 28

Inside the Salle des Etats, Langdon stared in amazement at the six words glowing on the Plexiglas. The text seemed to float in space, casting a sharp shadow across Mona Lisa's mysterious smile.

"The Priory," Langdon whispered. "This proves your grandfather was a member!" Sophie looked at him, confused. "You understand this?"

"It's perfect," Langdon said, nodding as he thought hard. "It's a statement of one of the Priory's most basic beliefs!"

Sophie looked puzzled in the light of the message written across the Mona Lisa's face.

SO DARK THE CON OF MAN

"Sophie," Langdon said, "the Priory's tradition of continuing goddess worship is based on a belief that powerful men in the early Christian church 'conned' the world by spreading lies that made women seem less important and favored men instead." Sophie stayed quiet, staring at the words.

"The Priory believes that Constantine and the men who came after him successfully changed the world from paganism that honored women to Christianity that honored men by using propaganda that made the sacred feminine seem evil, erasing the goddess from modern religion forever." Sophie still looked unsure. "My grandfather sent me to this spot to find this. He must be trying to tell me more than that."

Langdon understood what she meant. She thinks this is another code. Whether there was a hidden meaning here or not, Langdon couldn't tell right away. He was still trying to understand the clear message Saunière had left.

So dark the con of man, he thought. So dark indeed.

No one could deny the great good the modern Church did in today's troubled world, yet the Church had a deceitful and violent history. Their brutal crusade to "reeducate" the pagan and feminine-worshipping religions lasted three centuries, using methods as clever as they were horrific.

The Catholic Inquisition published what could be called the most blood-soaked book in human history. Malleus Maleficarum —or The Witches' Hammer —taught the world about "the dangers of freethinking women" and told the clergy how to find, torture, and kill them. Those labeled "witches" by the Church included all female scholars, priestesses, gypsies, mystics, nature lovers, herb gatherers, and any women "suspiciously connected to the natural world." Midwives were also killed for using medical knowledge to ease childbirth pain—a suffering, the Church claimed, was God's rightful punishment for Eve eating the Apple of Knowledge, creating the idea of Original Sin. During three hundred years of witch hunts, the Church burned an astounding five million women at the stake.

The propaganda and bloodshed had worked.

Today's world was living proof.

Women, once honored as a vital part of spiritual enlightenment, had been removed from the world's temples. There were no female Orthodox rabbis, Catholic priests, or Islamic clerics. The once sacred act of Hieros Gamos—the natural sexual union between man and woman through which each became spiritually whole—had been changed into a shameful act. Holy men who once needed sexual union with their female partners to connect with God now feared their natural sexual urges as the work of the devil, working with his favorite helper... woman.

Even the feminine link with the left-hand side was defamed by the Church. In France and Italy, the words for "left"— gauche and sinistra —became very negative, while their right-hand counterparts sounded right eous, skillful, and correct. To this day, radical thought is considered left wing, irrational thought is left brain, and anything evil, sinister.

The days of the goddess were over. The pendulum had swung. Mother Earth had become a man's world, and the gods of destruction and war were taking their toll. The male ego had spent two millennia unchecked by its female counterpart. The Priory of Sion believed that this destruction of the sacred feminine in modern life had caused what the Hopi Native Americans called koyanisquatsi —"life out of balance"—an unstable situation marked by testosterone-fueled wars, many misogynistic societies, and a growing disrespect for Mother Earth.

"Robert!" Sophie whispered, pulling him back. "Someone's coming!" He heard footsteps approaching in the hallway.

"Over here!" Sophie turned off the black light and seemed to vanish before Langdon's eyes. For a moment he felt completely blind. Over where! As his vision cleared he saw Sophie running toward the center of the room and hiding behind the octagonal viewing bench. He was about to run after her when a loud voice stopped him.

"Arrêtez!" a man commanded from the doorway.

The Louvre security agent moved quickly into the Salle des Etats, his pistol aimed at Langdon's chest.

Langdon's arms went up without him thinking.

"Couchez-vous!" the guard yelled. "Lie down!" Langdon was on the floor in seconds. The guard rushed over and kicked his legs apart, making him stretch out.

"Mauvaise idée, Monsieur Langdon," he said, pushing the gun into Langdon's back.

"Mauvaise idée."

Lying face down on the floor with his arms and legs spread wide, Langdon didn't find the situation funny. The Vitruvian Man, he thought. Face down.

CHAPTER 29

Inside Saint-Sulpice, Silas carried the heavy iron candle holder from the altar back to the obelisk. The shaft would work well as a battering ram. He looked at the gray marble panel covering the hollow in the floor and knew he couldn't break it without making a lot of noise.

Iron on marble would echo loudly in the high ceilings.

Would the nun hear him? She should be asleep. Still, Silas didn't want to risk it. He looked for something to wrap around the tip of the iron pole, but saw only the altar's linen cloth, which he didn't want to ruin. My cloak, he thought. Knowing he was alone in the church, Silas untied his cloak and took it off. As he did, he felt a sting as the wool stuck to the fresh wounds on his back.

Now naked except for his loincloth, Silas wrapped his cloak around the end of the iron rod. Then, aiming at the center of the floor tile, he hit it hard. A muffled thud. The stone didn't break. He hit it again. Again, a dull thud, but this time with a crack. On the third try, the covering finally shattered, and stone pieces fell into a hollow space below.

A compartment!

Silas quickly pulled the remaining pieces from the opening and looked into the empty space. His heart raced as he knelt down. He raised his pale, bare arm and reached inside. At first, he felt nothing. The compartment floor was bare, smooth stone. Then, reaching deeper under the Rose Line, he touched something! A thick stone tablet. He got his fingers around the edge, gripped it, and gently lifted the tablet out. As he stood and looked at his find, he saw it was a rough stone slab with words carved into it. For a moment, he felt like Moses.

As Silas read the words on the tablet, he was surprised. He had expected the keystone to be a map, or a set of complex instructions, maybe even in code. But the keystone had a very simple inscription.

Job 38:11

A Bible verse? Silas was shocked by the devilish simplicity. Was the secret location of what they sought hidden in a Bible verse? The brotherhood would stop at nothing to make fun of the righteous!

Job. Chapter thirty-eight. Verse eleven.

Silas didn't remember the exact words of verse eleven, but he knew the Book of Job was about a man whose faith in God lasted through many trials. Fitting, he thought, barely able to hide his eagerness.

Looking back, he stared down the shining Rose Line and smiled. There on the main altar, open on a fancy book stand, was a huge leather Bible.

Up in the balcony, Sister Sandrine was shaking. She had almost run away to do what she was told, but then the man below took off his cloak. When she saw his pale skin, she was shocked and confused. His wide, pale back was covered in fresh, bloody cuts. Even from far away, she could see the wounds were new.

This man has been whipped terribly!

She also saw the bloody cilice around his leg, with a dripping wound under it. What kind of God would want someone hurt like this? Sister Sandrine knew she would never understand the ways of Opus Dei. But that didn't matter right now. Opus Dei is looking for the keystone. She didn't know how they knew about it, but she didn't have time to wonder.

The bloody monk was now putting his cloak back on, holding his treasure as he walked toward the altar, toward the Bible.

Holding her breath, Sister Sandrine left the balcony and hurried down the hall to her room. She got on her hands and knees, reached under her wooden bed, and pulled out a sealed envelope she had hidden there years ago.

She ripped it open and found four Paris phone numbers.

Shaking, she began to dial.

Downstairs, Silas put the stone tablet on the altar and eagerly reached for the leather Bible. His long white fingers were sweating as he turned the pages. He flipped through the Old Testament and found the Book of Job. He found chapter thirty-eight. As he ran his finger down the text, he looked forward to the words he was about to read.

They will lead the way!

Finding verse eleven, Silas read the text. It was only seven words. Confused, he read it again, feeling that something had gone terribly wrong. The verse simply read: HITHERTO SHALT THOU COME, BUT NO FURTHER.

CHAPTER 30

Security warden Claude Grouard fumed as he stood over his captive in front of the Mona Lisa. This bastard killed Jacques Saunière! Saunière had been like a father to Grouard and his security team.

Grouard wanted nothing more than to pull the trigger and shoot Robert Langdon in the back. As senior warden, Grouard was one of the few guards who carried a loaded weapon. He reminded himself that killing Langdon would be a generous fate compared to the misery Bezu Fache and the French prison system would inflict.

Grouard yanked his walkie-talkie off his belt and tried to radio for backup. All he heard was static. The extra security in this room always messed with the guards' communications. I have to move to the doorway. Still aiming his weapon at Langdon, Grouard began backing slowly toward the entrance. On his third step, he saw something that made him stop.

What the hell is that!

An odd mirage was appearing near the center of the room. A silhouette. Was there someone else in the room? A woman was moving through the darkness, walking quickly toward the far left wall. In front of her, a purplish beam of light swung back and forth across the floor, as if she were searching for something with a colored flashlight.

"Qui est là?" Grouard demanded, his adrenaline spiking again. He suddenly didn't know where to aim his gun or which way to move.

"PTS," the woman replied calmly, still scanning the floor with her light. Police Technique et Scientifique. Grouard was sweating now. I thought all the agents were gone!

He now recognized the purple light as ultraviolet, like a PTS team's, but he couldn't understand why DCPJ would be looking for evidence in here.

"Your name!" Grouard yelled, his gut telling him something was wrong. "Answer!"

"It's me," the voice answered calmly in French. "Sophie Neveu." Somewhere in the back of Grouard's mind, the name clicked. Sophie Neveu? That was Saunière's granddaughter, wasn't it? She used to visit as a kid, but that was years ago. It couldn't be her! And even if it was Sophie Neveu, that wasn't a reason to trust her; Grouard had heard about the bad falling-out between Saunière and his granddaughter.

"You know me," the woman said. "And Robert Langdon didn't kill my grandfather. Trust me."

Warden Grouard wasn't buying that. I need help! He tried his walkie-talkie again but only got static. The entrance was still twenty yards behind him, and Grouard started backing up slowly, keeping his gun aimed at the man on the floor. As Grouard moved back, he saw the woman across the room raising her UV light and looking closely at a large painting on the far side of the Salle des Etats, right across from the Mona Lisa. Grouard gasped, realizing which painting it was.

What is she doing?

Across the room, Sophie Neveu felt cold sweat on her forehead. Langdon was still spread out on the floor. Hang on, Robert. Almost there. Knowing the guard wouldn't shoot them, Sophie focused on the task, scanning the area around one masterpiece—another Da Vinci. But the UV light showed nothing. Not on the floor, walls, or the canvas. There has to be something here!

Sophie was sure she had understood her grandfather's intentions. What else could he mean?

The painting was five feet tall. Da Vinci's scene showed a strangely posed Virgin Mary with Baby Jesus, John the Baptist, and the Angel Uriel on rocks. When Sophie was a kid, she always visited this painting with her grandfather after seeing the Mona Lisa.

Grandfather, I'm here! But I don't see it!

Behind her, Sophie heard the guard trying to radio for help again. Think!

She pictured the message scrawled on the protective glass of the Mona Lisa. How dark the tricks of man. The painting before her had no protective glass to write on, and Sophie knew her grandfather would never have ruined this masterpiece by writing on the painting itself. She paused. At least not on the front. Her eyes shot upward, following the long cables hanging from the ceiling that held the canvas.

Could that be it? Grabbing the left side of the carved wood frame, she pulled it toward her. The painting was large, and the backing bent as she swung it away from the wall. Sophie squeezed her head and shoulders in behind the painting and raised the black light to check the back. It only took seconds to know she was wrong. The back of the painting was pale and blank. There was no purple text here, only the faded brown backside of old canvas and—

Wait.

Sophie's eyes stopped on a strange glint of shiny metal stuck near the bottom edge of the frame. The object was small, partly stuck in the crack where the canvas met the frame. A shimmering gold chain hung from it.

To Sophie's surprise, the chain was attached to a familiar gold key. The large, carved head was in the shape of a cross and had an engraved seal she had not seen since she was nine years old. A fleur-de-lis with the initials P.S. Right then, Sophie felt like her grandfather was whispering in her ear. When the time comes, the key will be yours. Her throat tightened as she realized that her grandfather, even in death, had kept his promise. This key opens a box, his voice was saying, where I keep many secrets.

Sophie now understood that the whole point of tonight's word game had been this key. Her grandfather had it with him when he was killed. Not wanting it to fall into the hands of the police, he hid it behind this painting. Then he created a clever treasure hunt to make sure only Sophie would find it.

"Au secours!" the guard yelled.

Sophie grabbed the key from behind the painting and slipped it deep in her pocket with the UV penlight. Peeking out from behind the canvas, she could see the guard was still trying hard to reach someone on the walkie-talkie. He was moving backward toward the entrance, still pointing the gun at Langdon.

"Au secours!" he shouted again into his radio.

Static.

He can't transmit, Sophie realized, remembering that tourists with cell phones often got frustrated in here when they tried to call home to brag about seeing the Mona Lisa. The extra surveillance wiring in the walls made it nearly impossible to get a signal unless you stepped out into the hall. The guard was backing quickly toward the exit now, and Sophie knew she had to act immediately. Gazing up at the large painting behind which she was partially hidden, Sophie realized that Leonardo da Vinci, for the second time tonight, was there to help.

Another few meters, Grouard told himself, keeping his gun pointed.

"Stop! Or I'll destroy it!" the woman's voice echoed across the room. Grouard glanced over and stopped in his tracks. "My God, no!" Through the reddish haze, he could see that the woman had actually lifted the large painting off its cables and propped it on the floor in front of her. At five feet tall, the canvas almost entirely hid her body. Grouard's first thought was to wonder why the painting's trip wires hadn't set off alarms, but of course the artwork cable sensors had yet to be reset tonight. What is she doing!

When he saw it, his blood went cold.

The canvas started to bulge in the middle, the fragile outlines of the Virgin Mary, Baby Jesus, and John the Baptist beginning to distort.

"No!" Grouard screamed, frozen in horror as he watched the priceless Da Vinci stretching. The woman was pushing her knee into the center of the canvas from behind! "NO!" Grouard wheeled and aimed his gun at her but instantly realized it was an empty threat. The canvas was only fabric, but it was utterly impenetrable—a six-million-dollar piece of body armor. I can't put a bullet through a Da Vinci!

"Put down your gun and radio," the woman said in calm French, "or I'll put my knee through this painting. I think you know how my grandfather would feel about that." Grouard felt dizzy. "Please... no. That's Madonna of the Rocks!" He dropped his gun and radio, raising his hands over his head.

"Thank you," the woman said. "Now do exactly as I tell you, and everything will work out fine."

Moments later, Langdon's pulse was still pounding as he ran beside Sophie down the emergency stairwell toward the ground level. Neither of them had said a word since leaving the trembling Louvre guard lying in the Salle des Etats. The guard's pistol was now clutched tightly in Langdon's hands, and he couldn't wait to get rid of it. The weapon felt heavy and dangerously foreign. Taking the stairs two at a time, Langdon wondered if Sophie had any idea how valuable a painting she had almost ruined. Her choice in art seemed strangely relevant to tonight's adventure. The Da Vinci she had grabbed, much like the Mona Lisa, was famous among art historians for its many hidden pagan symbols.

"You chose a valuable hostage," he said as they ran.

"Madonna of the Rocks," she replied. "But I didn't pick it; my grandfather did. He left me a little something behind the painting."

Langdon looked surprised. "What!? But how did you know which painting? Why Madonna of the Rocks?"

"So dark the con of man." She smiled, pleased with herself. "I missed the first two anagrams, Robert. I wasn't going to miss the third."

CHAPTER 31

"They're dead!" Sister Sandrine stammered into the phone at her home. She was leaving a message on a machine. "Please answer! They're all dead!" The first three numbers on the list had brought awful news—a distraught widow, a detective at a murder scene, and a sad priest comforting a grieving family. All three contacts were dead. And now, as she called the fourth and last number—the number she was only to call if the first three didn't answer—she got a machine. The message just asked the caller to leave a message.

"The floor panel is broken!" she begged as she left the message. "The other three are dead!" Sister Sandrine didn't know who the four men were, but the private numbers hidden under her bed were only for one reason.

If that floor panel is ever broken, the messenger had told her, it means the highest level has been broken into. One of us is in danger and has had to lie. Call the numbers. Warn the others. Don't fail us.

It was a silent alarm. Simple and reliable. The plan had amazed her when she first heard it. If one brother's identity was discovered, he could lie to warn the others. But tonight, it seemed more than one had been discovered.

"Please answer," she whispered in fear. "Where are you?"

"Hang up the phone," a deep voice said from the doorway.

Turning in fear, she saw the large monk. He was holding the heavy iron candle holder. Shaking, she put the phone back.

"They are dead," the monk said. "All four of them. And they have tricked me. Tell me where the keystone is."

"I don't know!" Sister Sandrine said truthfully. "That secret is kept by others." Others who are dead!

The man stepped forward, his white fists tight on the iron stand. "You are a sister of the Church, yet you serve them?"

"Jesus had only one real message," Sister Sandrine said, standing firm. "I don't see that message in Opus Dei."

A sudden burst of anger flashed in the monk's eyes. He attacked, swinging the candle stand like a club. As Sister Sandrine fell, her last feeling was a deep sense of dread.

All four are dead.

The valuable truth is lost forever.

CHAPTER 32

The security alarm on the west side of the Denon Wing startled the pigeons in the nearby Tuileries Gardens as Langdon and Sophie hurried from the doorway into the Paris night. As they ran across the plaza to Sophie's car, Langdon heard police sirens in the distance.

"It's there," Sophie said, pointing to a red, short, two-seater parked on the plaza. Is she serious? It was the smallest car Langdon had ever seen.

"SmartCar," she said. "A hundred kilometers per liter." Langdon had barely gotten into the passenger seat before Sophie drove the SmartCar over a curb onto a gravel divider. He held onto the dash as the car sped across a sidewalk and back down into the small roundabout at Carrousel du Louvre.

For a moment, Sophie seemed to think about cutting across the roundabout by driving straight ahead, through the hedge, and across the large circle of grass in the center.

"No!" Langdon shouted, knowing the hedges hid the dangerous pit in the center— La Pyramide Inversée —the upside-down pyramid skylight he had seen earlier. It was big enough to swallow their SmartCar whole. Luckily, Sophie took the normal route, turning the wheel hard to the right, circling until she exited, cut left, and swung into the northbound lane, heading toward Rue de Rivoli.

The police sirens grew louder behind them, and Langdon could now see the lights in his side mirror. The SmartCar engine struggled as Sophie sped away from the Louvre. Fifty yards ahead, the traffic light at Rivoli turned red. Sophie cursed quietly and kept going. Langdon tensed up.

"Sophie?"

Slowing a little as they reached the intersection, Sophie flashed her headlights and quickly looked both ways before speeding up again and making a sharp left turn through the empty intersection onto Rivoli. Speeding west for a quarter of a mile, Sophie turned right around a wide roundabout. Soon they were heading out the other side onto the wide avenue of Champs-Elysées.

As they straightened out, Langdon turned to look out the rear window toward the Louvre. The police didn't seem to be following them. The blue lights were gathering at the museum.

His heartbeat finally slowing, Langdon turned back around. "That was interesting." Sophie didn't seem to hear. Her eyes remained fixed ahead down the long street of Champs-Elysées, the two-mile stretch of fancy stores that was often called the Fifth Avenue of Paris. The embassy was only about a mile away, and Langdon settled into his seat. So dark the con of man. Sophie's quick thinking had been impressive. Madonna of the Rocks. Sophie had said her grandfather left her something behind the painting. A final message? Langdon could not help but marvel over Saunière's clever hiding place; Madonna of the Rocks was yet another fitting link in the evening's chain of interconnected symbolism. Saunière, it seemed, at every turn, was reinforcing his fondness for the dark and mischievous side of Leonardo da Vinci. Da Vinci's original commission for Madonna of the Rocks had come from an organization known as the Confraternity of the Immaculate Conception, which needed a painting for the centerpiece of an altar triptych in their church of San Francesco in Milan. The nuns gave Leonardo specific dimensions, and the desired theme for the painting—the Virgin Mary, baby John the Baptist, Uriel, and Baby Jesus sheltering in a cave. Although Da Vinci did as they requested, when he delivered the work, the group reacted with horror. He had filled the painting with shocking and disturbing details.

The painting showed a blue-robed Virgin Mary sitting with her arm around an infant child, presumably Baby Jesus. Opposite Mary sat Uriel, also with an infant, presumably baby John the Baptist. Oddly, though, rather than the usual Jesus-blessing-John scene, it was baby John who was blessing Jesus... and Jesus was submitting to his authority! More troubling still, Mary was holding one hand high above the head of infant John and making a decidedly threatening gesture—her fingers looking like eagle's talons, gripping an invisible head. Finally, the most obvious and frightening image: Just below Mary's curled fingers, Uriel was making a cutting gesture with his hand—as if slicing the neck of the invisible head gripped by Mary's claw-like hand.

Langdon's students were always amused to learn that Da Vinci eventually satisfied the confraternity by painting them a second, "toned-down" version of Madonna of the Rocks in which everyone was arranged in a more traditional manner. The second version now hung in London's National Gallery under the name Virgin of the Rocks, although Langdon still preferred the Louvre's more intriguing original.

As Sophie sped the car up Champs-Elysées, Langdon said, "The painting. What was behind it?" Her eyes remained on the road. "I'll show you once we're safely inside the embassy."

"You'll show it to me?" Langdon was surprised. "He left you a physical object?" Sophie gave a quick nod. "Embossed with a fleur-de-lis and the initials P.S." Langdon couldn't believe his ears.

We're going to make it, Sophie thought as she swung the SmartCar's wheel to the right, cutting sharply past the luxurious Hôtel de Crillon into Paris's tree-lined diplomatic neighborhood. The embassy was less than a mile away now. She was finally feeling like she could breathe normally again.

Even as she drove, Sophie's mind remained locked on the key in her pocket, her memories of seeing it many years ago, the gold head shaped as an equal-armed cross, the triangular shaft, the indentations, the embossed flowery seal, and the letters P.S.

Although the key barely had entered Sophie's thoughts through the years, her work in the intelligence community had taught her plenty about security, and now the key's peculiar tooling no longer looked so mysterious. A laser-tooled varying matrix. Impossible to duplicate. Rather than teeth that moved tumblers, this key's complex series of laser-burned marks was examined by an electric eye. If the eye determined that the hexagonal marks were correctly spaced, arranged, and rotated, then the lock would open.

Sophie could not begin to imagine what a key like this opened, but she sensed Robert would be able to tell her. After all, he had described the key's embossed seal without ever seeing it. The cross shape on top implied the key belonged to some kind of Christian organization, and yet Sophie knew of no churches that used laser-tooled varying matrix keys.

Besides, my grandfather was no Christian....

Sophie had seen proof of that ten years ago. Ironically, another key—a more ordinary one—had shown her what he was really like.

It was a warm afternoon when she arrived at Charles de Gaulle Airport and took a taxi home. Grand-père will be so surprised to see me, she thought. Sophie was back from graduate school in Britain for spring break a few days early, and she couldn't wait to see him and tell him about the encryption methods she was studying.

But when she got to their Paris home, her grandfather wasn't there. Disappointed, she knew he hadn't been expecting her and was probably at the Louvre. But it's Saturday afternoon, she thought. He rarely worked on weekends. Weekends were usually for—

Smiling, Sophie ran to the garage. His car was gone, just as she suspected. It was the weekend, and Jacques Saunière hated driving in the city. He only had a car to go to one place—his vacation home in Normandy, north of Paris. Sophie was eager to be out in nature after being in busy London for months, and she wanted to start her vacation right away. It was still early evening, so she decided to leave immediately and surprise him. After borrowing a friend's car, Sophie drove north, up into the quiet, moonlit hills near Creully. She arrived just after ten o'clock and turned onto the long driveway leading to her grandfather's home. The driveway was over a mile long, and she could only see the house through the trees halfway there—a huge, old stone château nestled in the woods on the side of a hill.

Sophie had almost expected to find her grandfather asleep at this hour, so she was surprised to see the house lit up. But her delight turned to surprise when she saw the driveway full of parked cars—Mercedeses, BMWs, Audis, and a Rolls-Royce. Sophie stared for a moment and then laughed. My grand-père, the famous recluse! It seemed Jacques Saunière was much less of a recluse than he pretended to be. He was clearly having a party while Sophie was away, and judging by the cars, some of Paris's most important people were there.

Excited to surprise him, she hurried to the front door. But it was locked. She knocked, but no one answered. Confused, she walked around and tried the back door. It was also locked, and no one answered.

Puzzled, she stood for a moment and listened. The only sound was the cool Normandy air moaning softly as it moved through the valley.

No music.

No voices.

Nothing.

In the quiet woods, Sophie rushed to the side of the house and climbed onto a woodpile, pressing her face against the living room window. What she saw inside didn't make sense.

"Nobody's here!"

The whole first floor looked empty.

Where is everyone?

Heart racing, Sophie ran to the woodshed and grabbed the spare key her grandfather hid under the kindling box. She ran to the front door and let herself in. As she stepped into the empty entrance hall, the security system's control panel began flashing red—warning that whoever entered had ten seconds to type in the right code before the alarms went off.

He has the alarm on during a party?

Sophie quickly typed the code and turned off the system.

Going inside, she found the whole house empty. Upstairs too. As she came back down to the deserted living room, she paused in the silence, wondering what could be happening.

That's when Sophie heard it.

Quiet voices. And they seemed to be coming from below her. Sophie couldn't believe it. Crouching, she put her ear to the floor and listened. Yes, the sound was definitely coming from underneath. The voices seemed to be singing, or... chanting? She was scared. Almost more unsettling than the sound was the thought that this house didn't even have a basement. At least none I've ever seen.

Turning and looking around the living room, Sophie noticed the only thing that seemed odd—her grandfather's favorite antique, a large Aubusson tapestry. It usually hung on the east wall by the fireplace, but tonight it had been moved aside on its brass rod, showing the wall behind it.

Walking to the bare wooden wall, Sophie felt the chanting getting louder. Unsure, she put her ear against the wood. The voices were clearer now. People were definitely chanting... saying words Sophie couldn't understand.

The space behind this wall is empty!

Feeling around the edge of the panels, Sophie found a hidden fingerhold. It was carefully made. A sliding door. Her heart pounding, she put her finger in the slot and pulled. The heavy wall slid sideways quietly. From the darkness beyond, the voices echoed up.

Sophie slipped through the door and found herself on a rough stone staircase that went down in a spiral. She'd been coming to this house since she was a child but had no idea this staircase was even here!

As she went down, the air got cooler. The voices clearer. She heard men and women now. She couldn't see far because of the spiral of the staircase, but the last step was now coming into view. Beyond it, she could see a small bit of the basement floor—stone, lit by the flickering orange light of a fire.

Holding her breath, Sophie moved down a few more steps and crouched to look. It took her a few seconds to understand what she was seeing.

The room was a grotto—a rough chamber that seemed to have been carved from the granite of the hillside. The only light came from torches on the walls. In the light of the flames, about thirty people stood in a circle in the center of the room.

I'm dreaming, Sophie thought. A dream. What else could this be?

Everyone in the room was wearing a mask. The women were dressed in white, see-through gowns and golden shoes. Their masks were white, and they held golden balls in their hands. The men wore long black robes, and their masks were black. They looked like pieces in a giant chess set. Everyone in the circle rocked back and forth and chanted in respect to something on the floor before them... something Sophie couldn't see.

The chanting grew steady again. Faster. Louder now. Even faster. The participants stepped inward and knelt. At that moment, Sophie could finally see what they all were seeing. Though she stumbled back in horror, she felt the image burning into her memory forever. Overcome with nausea, Sophie spun, grabbing the stone walls as she hurried back up the stairs. Shutting the door, she fled the empty house, and drove back to Paris in a tearful daze. That night, her life ruined by disappointment and betrayal, she packed her things and left her home. On the dining room table, she left a note.

I WAS THERE. DON'T TRY TO FIND ME.

Beside the note, she put the old spare key from the château's woodshed.

"Sophie!" Langdon's voice broke in. "Stop! Stop!"

Coming out of the memory, Sophie slammed on the brakes, screeching to a halt. "What? What is it?!"

Langdon pointed down the long street ahead.

When she saw it, Sophie's blood ran cold. A hundred yards ahead, the intersection was blocked by a couple of DCPJ police cars, parked at angles, their purpose clear. They've blocked off Avenue Gabriel!

Langdon sighed heavily. "I guess we can't get to the embassy tonight?" Down the street, the two DCPJ officers standing by their cars were now staring at them, curious about the headlights that had stopped so suddenly up the street from them.

Okay, Sophie, turn around very slowly.

Putting the SmartCar in reverse, she did a smooth three-point turn and went the other way. As she drove off, she heard tires screeching behind them. Sirens began to wail.

Sophie cursed and floored the accelerator.

CHAPTER 33

Sophie's SmartCar sped through the diplomatic area, dodging embassies and consulates, before shooting down a side street and turning right onto the Champs-Elysées.

Langdon, tense and gripping the seat, twisted around to watch for the police. He suddenly regretted running. You didn't, he reminded himself. Sophie decided when she threw the GPS tracker out the window. Now, speeding away from the embassy through the light traffic on the Champs-Elysées, Langdon felt his options shrinking. Sophie seemed to have lost the police, for now, but Langdon doubted their luck would last.

Sophie, driving, reached into her sweater pocket. She pulled out a small metal object and held it out. "Robert, look at this. My grandfather left it behind Madonna of the Rocks."

Langdon, excited, took the object and examined it. It was heavy and cross-shaped. He thought it was a small funeral marker, like the ones stuck in the ground at graves. But then he saw that the shaft was angled and triangular. It also had hundreds of tiny hexagons, precisely made and scattered randomly.

"It's a laser-cut key," Sophie said. "An electric eye reads the hexagons." A key? Langdon had never seen anything like it.

"Look at the other side," she said, changing lanes and speeding through an intersection. Langdon turned the key and was amazed. There, on the center of the cross, was a fleur-de-lis with the initials P.S.! "Sophie," he said, "it's the seal I told you about! The Priory of Sion's official symbol."

She nodded. "I saw the key a long time ago. He told me never to mention it." Langdon stared at the key. Its advanced design and ancient symbol felt like a strange mix of old and new.

"He said the key opened a box with many secrets." Langdon felt uneasy thinking about what secrets Jacques Saunière might have kept.
