He couldn't imagine what an ancient group would do with a futuristic key. The Priory existed only to protect a secret. A very powerful secret. Could this key be related? The thought was overwhelming. "Do you know what it opens?" Sophie seemed disappointed. "I hoped you did."

Langdon turned the cross in his hand, examining it, and remained silent.

"It looks Christian," Sophie insisted.

Langdon wasn't so sure. The head of this key wasn't the usual long Christian cross, but a square cross—with four equal arms—that was around fifteen hundred years older than Christianity. This kind of cross didn't have the Christian ideas of crucifixion that went with the longer Latin Cross, which Romans created as a way to torture people. Langdon was always surprised that many Christians who looked at "the crucifix" didn't realize its violent history was right there in its name: "cross" and "crucifix" came from the Latin word cruciare —to torture.

"Sophie," he said, "all I know is that equal-armed crosses like this are seen as peaceful crosses. Their square shape makes them hard to use for crucifixion, and the balanced up and down and side to side parts show a natural joining of male and female, which fits with the Priory's beliefs."

She looked tired. "You don't get it, do you?"

Langdon frowned. "Not at all."

"Okay, we have to get off the road." Sophie looked in her rearview mirror. "We need a safe place to figure out what this key unlocks."

Langdon wished he was back in his cozy room at the Ritz. But that was out of the question.

"What about my friends at the American University of Paris?"

"Too easy. Fache will check there."

"You must know people. You live here."

Fache will check my phone and e-mail records, talk to my coworkers. My contacts are at risk, and a hotel won't work because they all need ID." Langdon wondered again if he should have just let Fache arrest him at the Louvre. "Let's call the embassy. I can explain and have them send someone to meet us."

"Meet us?" Sophie turned and stared like he was crazy. "Robert, you're dreaming. Your embassy only has power on their own property. Sending someone to get us would be helping a fugitive of the French government. It won't happen. If you go to your embassy and ask for temporary safety, that's one thing, but asking them to act against French police in the field?" She shook her head. "Call your embassy now, and they'll tell you to avoid making things worse and turn yourself in to Fache. Then they'll promise to use diplomatic channels to get you a fair trial." She looked up at the fancy storefronts on the Champs-Elysées. "How much cash do you have?"

Langdon checked his wallet. "A hundred dollars. Some euros. Why?"

"Credit cards?"

"Of course."

As Sophie sped up, Langdon felt she was making a plan. Straight ahead, at the end of the Champs-Elysées, was the Arc de Triomphe—Napoleon's 164-foot-tall tribute to his military strength—surrounded by France's biggest roundabout, a nine-lane monster.

Sophie's eyes were on the rearview mirror again as they neared the roundabout. "We lost them for now," she said, "but we won't last another five minutes if we stay in this car." So steal another one, Langdon thought, now that we're criminals. "What are you going to do?" Sophie gunned the SmartCar into the roundabout. "Trust me."

Langdon didn't answer. Trust hadn't helped him much tonight. Pulling back his jacket sleeve, he checked his watch—a vintage, collector's-edition Mickey Mouse wristwatch his parents gave him on his tenth birthday. Although its childish face often got strange looks, Langdon had never owned another watch; Disney cartoons were his first look into the magic of form and color, and Mickey was now Langdon's daily reminder to stay young at heart. But right now, Mickey's hands were at an awkward angle, showing an equally awkward hour.

2:51 A.M.

"Interesting watch," Sophie said, glancing at his wrist and steering the SmartCar around the wide, counterclockwise roundabout.

"It's a long story," he said, pulling his sleeve back down.

"I'm sure it is." She smiled quickly and left the roundabout, heading straight north, away from the city center. Barely catching two green lights, she reached the third intersection and turned sharply right onto Boulevard Malesherbes. They'd left the wealthy, tree-lined streets of the embassy area and entered a darker, industrial neighborhood. Sophie turned quickly left, and Langdon immediately knew where they were.

Gare Saint-Lazare.

The glass-roofed train terminal ahead looked like a strange mix of an airplane hangar and a greenhouse. European train stations never rested. Even at this hour, several taxis waited near the main entrance. Vendors were selling sandwiches and water, while tired-looking kids with backpacks came out of the station, rubbing their eyes and looking around as if trying to remember where they were. Up ahead, two city policemen stood on the sidewalk, giving directions to some lost tourists.

Sophie pulled her SmartCar in behind the taxis and parked illegally, even though there was plenty of legal parking across the street. Before Langdon could ask what was happening, she was out of the car. She rushed to the window of the taxi in front of them and started talking to the driver. As Langdon got out of the SmartCar, he saw Sophie give the taxi driver a large stack of money. The taxi driver nodded and then, surprisingly, drove off without them.

"What was that about?" Langdon asked, joining Sophie on the sidewalk as the taxi disappeared. Sophie was already heading for the train station entrance. "Come on. We're buying two tickets on the next train leaving Paris."

Langdon hurried after her. What started as a short trip to the U.S. Embassy had become a complete escape from Paris. Langdon liked this idea less and less.

CHAPTER 34

The driver who picked up Bishop Aringarosa from Leonardo da Vinci International Airport arrived in a small, plain black Fiat sedan. Aringarosa remembered when all Vatican vehicles were large luxury cars with medallions and flags showing the Holy See's symbol. Those days are gone. Now, Vatican cars were less showy and almost always unmarked. The Vatican said this was to save money to better help their dioceses, but Aringarosa thought it was more for security. The world had become crazy, and in many parts of Europe, showing you loved Jesus Christ was like putting a target on your car. Pulling his black cassock around him, Aringarosa got into the back seat and prepared for the long drive to Castel Gandolfo. It would be the same trip he had taken five months before. Last year's trip to Rome, he sighed. The longest night of my life. Five months ago, the Vatican had called and asked Aringarosa to come to Rome immediately. They gave no reason. Your tickets are at the airport. The Holy See tried hard to keep an air of mystery, even for its highest clergy.

Aringarosa thought the mysterious summons was probably a chance for the Pope and other Vatican officials to take advantage of Opus Dei's recent public success—the completion of their World Headquarters in New York City. Architectural Digest had called Opus Dei's building "a shining beacon of Catholicism beautifully combined with the modern landscape," and lately the Vatican seemed interested in anything that included the word "modern." Aringarosa had to accept the invitation, though he didn't want to. Not a fan of the current papal administration, Aringarosa, like most conservative clergy, had watched with worry as the new Pope began his first year in office. An unusually liberal man, His Holiness had become Pope through one of the most debated and strange conclaves in Vatican history. Now, instead of being humbled by his unexpected rise to power, the Holy Father had quickly used all the power of his high position in Christendom. With strong liberal support within the College of Cardinals, the Pope was now declaring his papal mission to be "rejuvenation of Vatican doctrine and updating Catholicism into the third millennium."

Aringarosa feared that this meant the man was arrogant enough to think he could rewrite God's laws and win back those who felt true Catholicism was too inconvenient in the modern world.

Aringarosa had been using all his political influence—which was significant given the size of Opus Dei's following and their wealth—to convince the Pope and his advisers that weakening the Church's laws was not only unfaithful and cowardly, but also a bad political move. He reminded them that previous changes to Church law—the Vatican II disaster—had had a terrible result: Church attendance was now lower than ever, donations were decreasing, and there were not enough Catholic priests to manage their churches.

People need structure and guidance from the Church, Aringarosa insisted, not pampering and indulgence!

On that night, months ago, as the Fiat left the airport, Aringarosa was surprised to find they were heading not toward Vatican City but east up a winding mountain road. "Where are we going?" he had asked his driver.

"Alban Hills," the man replied. "Your meeting is at Castel Gandolfo." The Pope's summer residence? Aringarosa had never been there, nor had he wanted to see it. Besides being the Pope's summer home, the sixteenth-century fortress housed the Specula Vaticana—the Vatican Observatory—one of the most advanced astronomical observatories in Europe. Aringarosa had never been comfortable with the Vatican's historical interest in science. Why combine science and faith? Unbiased science could not be done by someone who had faith in God. And faith did not need physical proof of its beliefs.

Nonetheless, there it is, he thought as Castel Gandolfo came into view, rising against a star-filled November sky. From the access road, Gandolfo looked like a large stone monster considering a suicidal jump. Perched on the edge of a cliff, the castle leaned out over the valley where the Curiazi and Orazi clans fought long before Rome was founded.

Even in silhouette, Gandolfo was a sight—an impressive, layered, defensive building, reflecting the power of the dramatic cliffside setting. Sadly, Aringarosa now saw, the Vatican had ruined the building by putting two huge aluminum telescope domes on the roof, making this once dignified building look like a proud warrior wearing party hats. When Aringarosa got out of the car, a young Jesuit priest hurried out and greeted him. "Bishop, welcome. I am Father Mangano. I'm an astronomer here."

Good for you. Aringarosa mumbled his hello and followed his host into the castle's foyer—a wide-open space with a clumsy mix of Renaissance art and astronomy images. Following his escort up the wide marble staircase, Aringarosa saw signs for conference centers, science lecture halls, and tourist information. It amazed him that the Vatican couldn't provide clear, strong guidance for spiritual growth, but somehow still found time to give astrophysics lectures to tourists.

"Tell me," Aringarosa said to the young priest, "when did things get so mixed up?" The priest gave him an odd look. "Sir?"

Aringarosa waved it off, deciding not to start that argument again this evening. The Vatican has gone mad. Like a lazy parent who found it easier to give in to a spoiled child's demands than to stand firm and teach values, the Church kept softening, trying to change itself to fit a culture that had gone wrong.

The top floor's corridor was wide, richly decorated, and led in only one direction—toward huge oak doors with a brass sign.

BIBLIOTECA ASTRONOMICA

Aringarosa had heard of this place—the Vatican's Astronomy Library—said to contain more than twenty-five thousand books, including rare works of Copernicus, Galileo, Kepler, Newton, and Secchi. It was also supposedly the place where the Pope's top officials held private meetings... meetings they preferred not to hold inside Vatican City. Approaching the door, Bishop Aringarosa would never have imagined the shocking news he was about to receive, or the deadly chain of events it would set off. It was only an hour later, as he stumbled from the meeting, that the devastating implications sank in. Six months from now! he had thought. God help us!

Now, seated in the Fiat, Bishop Aringarosa realized his fists were clenched just thinking about that first meeting. He released his grip and took a slow breath, relaxing his muscles. Everything will be fine, he told himself as the Fiat climbed higher into the mountains. Still, he wished his cell phone would ring. Why hasn't the Teacher called me? Silas should have the keystone by now.

Trying to calm his nerves, the bishop focused on the purple amethyst in his ring. Feeling the textures of the miter-crozier design and the facets of the diamonds, he reminded himself that this ring was a symbol of power, but far less than what he would soon gain.

CHAPTER 35

Gare Saint-Lazare's interior resembled any European train station, a vast indoor space filled with familiar sights—homeless people with cardboard signs, groups of tired students asleep on backpacks and lost in their music, and clusters of baggage handlers in blue uniforms smoking.

Sophie looked up at the large departure board. The black and white panels flipped, updating the information. When it stopped, Langdon studied the options. The first listing read: LYON—RAPIDE—3:06

"I wish it left earlier," Sophie said, "but Lyon will work." Earlier? Langdon checked his watch 2:59 A.M. The train left in seven minutes, and they didn't have tickets yet. Sophie led Langdon to the ticket window and said, "Buy two tickets with your credit card."

"I thought using a credit card could be traced by—"

"Exactly."

Langdon stopped trying to anticipate Sophie Neveu's actions. He used his Visa card to buy two second-class tickets to Lyon and gave them to Sophie.

Sophie led him toward the tracks. A familiar chime sounded, and an announcer made the final boarding call for Lyon. Sixteen tracks stretched before them. In the distance to the right, at platform three, the Lyon train was hissing, ready to leave. But Sophie pulled Langdon in the opposite direction. They hurried through a side lobby, past an all-night cafe, and out a side door onto a quiet street on the station's west side.

A single taxi waited by the door, engine running.

The driver saw Sophie and flashed his lights.

Sophie jumped in the back seat. Langdon got in after her.

As the taxi pulled away from the station, Sophie took out their new train tickets and tore them up.

Langdon sighed. Seventy dollars well spent.

It wasn't until their taxi settled into a steady northbound hum on Rue de Clichy that Langdon felt they had truly escaped. Out the window to his right, he could see Montmartre and the beautiful dome of Sacré-Coeur. The image was interrupted by the flash of police lights speeding past them in the opposite direction.

Langdon and Sophie ducked down as the sirens faded.

Sophie had simply told the cab driver to head out of the city, and from her determined jaw, Langdon sensed she was trying to figure out their next move.

Langdon examined the cross-shaped key again, holding it to the window and close to his eyes, trying to find any markings on it that might show where it had been made. In the brief light of passing streetlights, he saw no markings except the Priory seal.

"It doesn't make sense," he finally said.

"Which part?"

"That your grandfather would go to so much trouble to give you a key that you wouldn't know what to do with."

"I agree."

"Are you sure he didn't write anything else on the back of the painting?"

"I looked everywhere. This is all there was. This key, stuck behind the painting. I saw the Priory seal, put the key in my pocket, then we left."

Langdon frowned, looking closely at the flat end of the triangular shaft. Nothing. Squinting, he brought the key close to his eyes and checked the edge of the head. Nothing there either. "I think this key was cleaned recently."

"Why?"

"It smells like rubbing alcohol."

She turned. "I'm sorry?"

"It smells like someone polished it with a cleaner." Langdon held the key to his nose and sniffed.

"It's stronger on the other side." He flipped it over. "Yes, it's alcohol-based, like it's been shined with a cleaner or—" Langdon stopped.

"What?"

He tilted the key toward the light and examined the smooth surface of the cross's wide arm. It seemed to shimmer in spots, as if damp. "Did you check the back of this key carefully before putting it in your pocket?"

"What? Not really. I was rushing."

Langdon faced her. "Do you still have the black light?"

Sophie reached into her pocket and pulled out the UV penlight. Langdon took it, turned it on, and shone the light on the key's back.

The back glowed immediately. There was writing there, in handwriting that was quick but clear.

"Well," Langdon said, smiling. "I guess we know what the alcohol smell was."

Sophie stared in wonder at the purple writing on the key's back. 24 Rue Haxo

An address! My grandfather wrote down an address!

"Where is this?" Langdon asked.

Sophie had no idea. Turning forward, she leaned in and eagerly asked the driver,

"Do you know Rue Haxo?"

The driver paused, then nodded. He said it was near the tennis stadium on the west side of Paris. She asked him to take them there right away.

"The fastest way is through the Bois de Boulogne," he said in French. "Is that all right?" Sophie frowned. She could think of less risky routes, but tonight she wouldn't be choosy. "Yes." We can surprise the American.

Sophie looked at the key again, wondering what they'd find at 24 Rue Haxo. A church? Some kind of Priory center?

Her mind filled with images of the secret ritual she'd seen in the basement grotto ten years ago, and she sighed. "Robert, I have a lot to tell you." She stopped, looking him in the eye as the taxi sped west. "But first, tell me everything you know about this Priory of Sion."

CHAPTER 36

Outside the Salle des Etats, Bezu Fache was angry as Louvre warden Grouard explained how Sophie and Langdon had disarmed him. Why didn't you just shoot the damn painting!

"Captain?" Lieutenant Collet hurried toward them from the command post. "Captain, I just heard. They found Agent Neveu's car."

"Did she get to the embassy?"

"No. Train station. Bought two tickets. Train just left."

Fache waved off warden Grouard and led Collet to a nearby alcove, speaking quietly. "What was the destination?"

"Lyon."

"Probably a trick." Fache sighed, making a plan. "Okay, tell the next station to stop and search the train, just in case. Leave her car and have people in normal clothes watch it, in case they come back. Send men to search the streets around the station in case they ran. Are buses running from the station?"

"Not now, sir. Only the taxi line."

"Good. Ask the drivers if they saw anything. Then call the taxi company and give them descriptions. I'm calling Interpol."

Collet looked surprised. "You're putting this on the wire?" Fache regretted the potential embarrassment, but he saw no other choice. Close the net fast, and close it tight.

The first hour was critical. Fugitives were predictable the first hour after escape. They always needed the same thing. Travel. Lodging. Cash. The Holy Trinity. Interpol had the power to make all three disappear in the blink of an eye. By sending photos of Langdon and Sophie to Paris travel authorities, hotels, and banks, Interpol would leave no options—no way to leave the city, no place to hide, and no way to withdraw cash without being recognized. Usually, fugitives panicked on the street and did something stupid. Stole a car. Robbed a store. Used a bank card in desperation. Whatever mistake they committed, they quickly made their whereabouts known to local authorities.

"Only Langdon, right?" Collet said. "You're not flagging Sophie Neveu. She's our own agent."

"Of course I'm flagging her!" Fache snapped. "What good is flagging Langdon if she can do all his dirty work? I plan to check Neveu's employment file—friends, family, personal contacts—anyone she might turn to for help. I don't know what she thinks she's doing out there, but it's going to cost her one hell of a lot more than her job!"

"Do you want me on the phones or in the field?"

"Field. Get over to the train station and coordinate the team. You're in charge, but don't make a move without talking to me."

"Yes, sir." Collet ran out.

Fache felt stiff as he stood in the alcove. Outside the window, the glass pyramid shone, its reflection rippling in the windswept pools. They slipped through my fingers. He told himself to relax.

Even a trained field agent would be lucky to withstand the pressure that Interpol was about to apply.

A female cryptologist and a schoolteacher?

They wouldn't make it to morning.

CHAPTER 37

The Bois de Boulogne, a park thick with trees, was known by many names, but those in Paris who knew it well called it "the Garden of Earthly Delights." This nickname, though it sounded nice, was not meant as a compliment. Anyone familiar with Bosch's strange and disturbing painting of the same name understood the comparison; the painting, like the forest, was dark and twisted, a kind of hell for oddballs and those with unusual desires. At night, the forest's winding paths were filled with people offering their bodies for money, earthly pleasures to satisfy hidden yearnings—men, women, and everything in between.

As Langdon prepared to tell Sophie about the Priory of Sion, their taxi drove through the park's wooded entrance and started west on the cobblestone road. Langdon found it hard to focus as some of the park's nighttime visitors were already stepping out of the shadows, showing off what they had to offer in the bright headlights. Up ahead, two teenage girls with bare chests stared intensely at the taxi. Beyond them, a muscular Black man wearing only a G-string turned and flexed his rear. Next to him, a beautiful blond woman lifted her short skirt to reveal that she was not, in fact, a woman.

Heaven help me! Langdon looked back inside the taxi and took a deep breath.

"Tell me about the Priory of Sion," Sophie said.

Langdon nodded, unable to imagine a less suitable setting for the story he was about to tell. He wondered where to start. The brotherhood's history stretched back over a thousand years... an incredible story of secrets, blackmail, betrayal, and even cruel torture at the hands of an angry Pope.

"The Priory of Sion," he began, "was started in Jerusalem in 1099 by a French king named Godefroi de Bouillon, right after he had captured the city." Sophie nodded, her eyes fixed on him.

"King Godefroi supposedly possessed a powerful secret—a secret that had been in his family since the time of Christ. Afraid that his secret might be lost when he died, he founded a secret brotherhood—the Priory of Sion—and tasked them with protecting his secret by quietly passing it down from generation to generation. During their time in Jerusalem, the Priory learned about a collection of hidden documents buried under the ruins of Herod's temple, which had been built on top of the older ruins of Solomon's Temple. They believed these documents confirmed Godefroi's powerful secret and were so explosive that the Church would do anything to get them." Sophie looked unsure.

"The Priory vowed that no matter how long it took, these documents had to be recovered from the rubble beneath the temple and protected forever, so the truth would never die. To retrieve the documents from within the ruins, the Priory created a military division—a group of nine knights called the Order of the Poor Knights of Christ and the Temple of Solomon." Langdon paused.

"More commonly known as the Knights Templar."

Sophie glanced up, surprised. Langdon had lectured on the Knights Templar so often that he knew almost everyone had heard of them. For academics, the Templars' history was a tricky mix of fact, legend, and lies, making it almost impossible to find the truth. Now, Langdon hesitated to even mention the Knights Templar in lectures because it always led to confusing questions about conspiracy theories.

Sophie looked worried. "You're saying the Knights Templar were started by the Priory of Sion to find secret documents? I thought the Templars were created to protect the Holy Land."

"That's a common misunderstanding. Protecting pilgrims was the cover for the Templars' real mission. Their true goal in the Holy Land was to retrieve the documents from under the temple ruins."

"And did they find them?"

Langdon grinned. "Nobody knows for sure, but all academics agree on one thing: The Knights discovered something down there... something that made them incredibly rich and powerful."

Langdon quickly gave Sophie the usual academic explanation of the Knights Templar history, explaining how the Knights were in the Holy Land during the Second Crusade and told King Baldwin II they were there to protect Christian pilgrims. Although unpaid and poor, the Knights asked the king for shelter and permission to live in the stables under the temple ruins. King Baldwin agreed, and the Knights moved into the ruined shrine. Langdon explained that this odd choice of lodging was not random. The Knights believed the documents the Priory sought were buried deep under the ruins—beneath the Holy of Holies, a sacred chamber where God Himself was believed to reside. It was the very center of the Jewish faith. For almost a decade, the nine Knights lived in the ruins, secretly digging through solid rock.

Sophie looked over. "And you said they discovered something?"

"They certainly did," Langdon said, explaining that after nine years, the Knights finally found what they were looking for. They took the treasure from the temple and went to Europe, where their influence quickly grew. No one was sure if the Knights had blackmailed the Vatican or if the Church simply tried to buy their silence, but Pope Innocent II immediately issued a special order giving the Knights Templar unlimited power and declaring them "a law unto themselves"—an independent army free from interference from kings and religious leaders.

With this new freedom from the Vatican, the Knights Templar grew rapidly, both in numbers and political power, gaining large estates in many countries. They began lending money to bankrupt royals and charging interest, creating modern banking and increasing their wealth and influence.

By the 1300s, the Vatican's approval had helped the Knights become so powerful that Pope Clement V decided something had to be done. Working with France's King Philippe IV, the Pope planned a clever operation to crush the Templars, seize their treasure, and take control of the secrets held over the Vatican. In a military move like something from the CIA, Pope Clement issued secret orders to be opened simultaneously by his soldiers across Europe on Friday, October 13, 1307.

At dawn on the thirteenth, the documents were opened, and their terrible contents revealed. Clement's letter said that God had appeared to him and warned him that the Knights Templar were heretics guilty of worshiping the devil, homosexuality, disrespecting the cross, sodomy, and other sinful acts. God had asked Pope Clement to cleanse the earth by rounding up all the Knights and torturing them until they confessed their sins against God. Clement's cunning plan worked perfectly. On that day, many Knights were captured, tortured without mercy, and finally burned as heretics. The tragedy still affects modern culture; even today, Friday the thirteenth is considered unlucky.

Sophie looked confused. "The Knights Templar were destroyed? I thought groups of Templars still exist today?"

"They do, under different names. Despite Clement's false accusations and attempts to wipe them out, the Knights had powerful friends, and some managed to escape the Vatican's attacks. The Templars' valuable collection of documents, which seemed to be their source of power, was Clement's real target, but he didn't get it. The documents had long been entrusted to the Templars' secret protectors, the Priory of Sion, whose secrecy kept them safe from the Vatican's assault. As the Vatican closed in, the Priory secretly moved their documents from a Paris center at night onto Templar ships in La Rochelle."

"Where did the documents go?"

Langdon shrugged. "Only the Priory of Sion knows the answer to that mystery. Because the documents are still being investigated and debated today, they are believed to have been moved and hidden again several times. It is now thought that the documents are somewhere in the United Kingdom."

Sophie looked worried.

"For a thousand years," Langdon continued, "stories of this secret have been passed down. The entire collection of documents, its power, and the secret it reveals have become known by a single name—Sangreal. Hundreds of books have been written about it, and few mysteries have caused as much interest among historians as the Sangreal."

"The Sangreal? Does the word have anything to do with the French word sang or Spanish sangre —meaning 'blood'?"

Langdon nodded. Blood was central to the Sangreal, but not in the way Sophie probably thought. "The story is complicated, but the important thing to remember is that the Priory guards the proof, and is supposedly waiting for the right moment in history to reveal the truth."

"What truth? What secret could be that powerful?"

Langdon took a deep breath and looked out at the hidden parts of Paris lurking in the shadows.

"Sophie, the word Sangreal is an old word. It has changed over time to become something else... a more common name." He paused. "When I tell you its common name, you'll realize you already know a lot about it. Almost everyone on earth has heard the story of the Sangreal." Sophie looked unsure. "I've never heard of it."

"Yes, you have." Langdon smiled. "You just know it by the name 'Holy Grail.' "

CHAPTER 38

Sophie looked closely at Langdon in the back of the taxi. He's joking. "The Holy Grail?" Langdon nodded, looking serious. "Holy Grail is what Sangreal literally means. The word comes from the French Sangraal, which became Sangreal, and then was split into two words, San Greal."

Holy Grail. Sophie was surprised she hadn't seen the connection in the words right away. Even so, Langdon's idea still didn't make sense to her. "I thought the Holy Grail was a cup. You just told me the Sangreal is a set of papers that tells some big secret."

"Yes, but the Sangreal papers are only half of what the Holy Grail really is. They are buried with the Grail itself... and tell what it really means. The papers gave the Knights Templar so much power because they revealed the true nature of the Grail."

The true nature of the Grail? Sophie felt even more confused. She had always thought the Holy Grail was the cup Jesus drank from at the Last Supper, and that Joseph of Arimathea later used to catch His blood when He died on the cross. "The Holy Grail is the Cup of Christ," she said. "It couldn't be simpler, right?"

"Sophie," Langdon whispered, leaning closer, "according to the Priory of Sion, the Holy Grail is not a cup at all. They say the Grail story—the one about a chalice —is actually a clever way of saying something else. That is, the Grail story uses the chalice as a symbol for something else, something much more powerful." He paused. "Something that fits perfectly with everything your grandfather has been trying to tell us tonight, including all his symbolic references to the sacred feminine."

Still not sure, Sophie could tell from Langdon's patient smile that he understood her confusion, but his eyes were still serious. "But if the Holy Grail isn't a cup," she asked, "what is it?" Langdon knew this question was coming, but he still wasn't sure how to answer it. If he didn't explain the history behind it, Sophie would just be confused—the same way his editor looked a few months ago when Langdon gave him a draft of the book he was working on.

"This book says what?" his editor had asked, surprised, putting down his wineglass and looking at him over his lunch. "You can't be serious."

"Serious enough to have spent a year researching it."

Jonas Faukman, a well-known editor in New York, tugged nervously at his goatee. Faukman had probably heard a lot of crazy book ideas in his career, but this one seemed to have shocked him.

"Robert," Faukman finally said, "don't misunderstand. I love your work, and we've had a great time together. But if I agree to publish an idea like this, people will be protesting outside my office for months. Besides, it will ruin your reputation. You're a Harvard historian, for God's sake, not someone just trying to make a quick buck. Where could you possibly find enough solid evidence to support a theory like this?"

With a quiet smile Langdon pulled a piece of paper from the pocket of his tweed coat and handed it to Faukman. The page listed a bibliography of over fifty titles—books by well-known historians, some modern, some hundreds of years old—many of them academic bestsellers. All the book titles suggested the same idea Langdon had just proposed. As Faukman read down the list, he looked like a man who had just found out the earth was actually flat. "I know some of these authors. They're... real historians!"

Langdon grinned. "As you can see, Jonas, this isn't just my theory. It's been around for a long time. I'm simply adding to it. No book has yet looked at the legend of the Holy Grail from a symbolic point of view. The visual evidence I'm finding to support the theory is, well, incredibly convincing."

Faukman was still staring at the list. "My God, one of these books was written by Sir Leigh Teabing—a British Royal Historian."

"Teabing has spent much of his life studying the Holy Grail. I've met with him. He was actually a big inspiration to me. He believes it, Jonas, along with all of the others on that list."

"You're telling me all of these historians actually believe..." Faukman swallowed, apparently unable to say the words.

Langdon grinned again. "The Holy Grail is probably the most sought-after treasure in human history. The Grail has created legends, wars, and lifelong searches. Does it make sense that it's just a cup? If so, then surely other relics should create similar or greater interest—the Crown of Thorns, the True Cross of the Crucifixion, the Titulus—and yet, they don't. Throughout history, the Holy Grail has been the most special." Langdon grinned. "Now you know why." Faukman was still shaking his head. "But with all these books written about it, why isn't this theory more well-known?"

"These books can't possibly compete with hundreds of years of accepted history, especially when that history is supported by the biggest bestseller of all time."

Faukman's eyes went wide. "Don't tell me Harry Potter is actually about the Holy Grail."

"I was referring to the Bible."

Faukman cringed. "I knew that."

"Laissez-le!" Sophie's shouts sliced through the air inside the taxi. "Put it down!" Langdon jumped as Sophie leaned over the seat and yelled at the driver. Langdon saw the driver clutching his radio and speaking into it. Sophie turned and plunged her hand into Langdon's tweed jacket. Before Langdon knew it, she yanked out the pistol, swung it around, and pressed it to the back of the driver's head. The driver instantly dropped his radio, raising his one free hand.

"Sophie!" Langdon choked. "What the hell—"

"Arrêtez!" Sophie commanded the driver.

Trembling, the driver obeyed, stopping the car and putting it in park.

Just then, Langdon heard the metallic voice of the taxi company's dispatcher coming from the dashboard. "... qui s'appette Agent Sophie Neveu... " the radio crackled. "Et un Américain, Robert Langdon..."

Langdon's muscles turned rigid. They found us already?

"Descendez," Sophie demanded.

The trembling driver kept his arms over his head as he got out of his taxi and took a few steps backward.

Sophie had rolled down her window and now aimed the gun outside at the bewildered cabbie.

"Robert," she said quietly, "take the wheel. You're driving." Langdon wasn't going to argue with a woman holding a gun. He got out of the car and back in behind the wheel. The driver was shouting curses, his arms still up in the air.

"Robert," Sophie said from the back seat, "I think you've seen enough of our magic forest?" He nodded. More than enough.

"Good. Drive us out of here."

Langdon looked at the car's controls and hesitated. Damn. He reached for the stick shift and clutch. "Sophie? Maybe you—"

"Go!" she yelled.

Outside, some prostitutes were walking over to see what was happening. One woman was making a call on her cell phone. Langdon pressed the clutch and moved the stick into what he hoped was first gear. He touched the accelerator, testing the gas.

Langdon released the clutch. The tires screeched as the taxi shot forward, swerving wildly and sending the gathering crowd diving for cover. The woman with the cell phone jumped into the woods, barely avoiding being hit.

"Doucement!" Sophie said, as the car jerked down the road. "What are you doing?"

"I tried to warn you," he shouted over the sound of grinding gears. "I drive an automatic!"

CHAPTER 39

Even though the plain room in the brownstone on Rue La Bruyère had seen a lot of suffering, Silas doubted anything could compare to the pain now gripping his pale body. I was tricked. Everything is lost.

Silas had been deceived. The brothers had lied, choosing death rather than revealing their secret. Silas did not have the strength to call the Teacher. Not only had Silas killed the only four people who knew where the keystone was hidden, he had killed a nun inside Saint-Sulpice. She was working against God! She scorned the work of Opus Dei!

A crime of impulse, the woman's death made things much worse. Bishop Aringarosa had made the phone call that got Silas into Saint-Sulpice; what would the abbé think when he found out the nun was dead? Even though Silas had placed her back in her bed, the wound on her head was obvious. Silas had tried to put the broken tiles back in the floor, but that damage was also obvious. They would know someone had been there.

Silas had planned to hide within Opus Dei when his task here was done. Bishop Aringarosa will protect me. Silas could imagine nothing better than a life of meditation and prayer deep inside Opus Dei's headquarters in New York City. He would never go outside again. Everything he needed was within that sanctuary. Nobody will miss me. Unfortunately, Silas knew, a prominent man like Bishop Aringarosa could not disappear so easily. I have endangered the bishop. Silas stared blankly at the floor and thought about taking his own life. After all, it was Aringarosa who gave Silas life in the first place... in that small rectory in Spain, educating him, giving him purpose.

"My friend," Aringarosa had told him, "you were born an albino. Don't let others shame you for this. Don't you understand how special this makes you? Didn't you know that Noah himself was an albino?"

"Noah of the Ark?" Silas had never heard this.

Aringarosa was smiling. "Indeed, Noah of the Ark. An albino. Like you, he had skin as white as an angel. Think about this. Noah saved all of life on the planet. You are destined for great things, Silas. The Lord has freed you for a reason. You have your calling. The Lord needs your help to do His work."

Over time, Silas learned to see himself in a new way. I am pure. White. Beautiful. Like an angel. At the moment, though, in his room at the residence hall, it was his father's disappointed voice that whispered to him from the past.

You are a disaster. A ghost.

Kneeling on the wooden floor, Silas prayed to be forgiven. Then, taking off his robe, he reached again for the whip.

CHAPTER 40

Fumbling with the gears, Langdon managed to steer the stolen taxi to the edge of the Bois de Boulogne, only stalling twice. But the funny side of it was lost as the taxi dispatcher kept calling their cab on the radio.

"Car five-six-three. Where are you? Answer!"

When Langdon reached the park exit, he gave in and slammed on the brakes. "You drive."

Sophie looked glad as she got behind the wheel. Soon she had the car running smoothly west on Allée de Longchamp, leaving the Garden of Earthly Delights behind.

"Which way to Rue Haxo?" Langdon asked, watching Sophie push the speedometer past a hundred kilometers an hour.

Sophie kept her eyes on the road. "The cab driver said it's next to the Roland Garros tennis stadium. I know that area."

Langdon took the heavy key from his pocket again, feeling its weight. He knew it was very important, maybe the key to his freedom. Earlier, telling Sophie about the Knights Templar, Langdon saw that the key, besides having the Priory seal, had another link to the Priory of Sion. The equal-armed cross stood for balance and harmony, but also for the Knights Templar. Everyone had seen paintings of Knights Templar in white robes with red equal-armed crosses. The Templar cross arms were slightly flared, but still equal.

A square cross. Like the one on this key.

Langdon's mind raced as he thought about what they might find. The Holy Grail. He almost laughed at how crazy that sounded. The Grail was thought to be in England, buried under a Templar church, hidden since at least 1500.

The era of Grand Master Da Vinci.

To keep their important documents safe, the Priory had to move them many times in the early centuries. Historians now think the Grail was moved perhaps six times after arriving in Europe from Jerusalem. The last time the Grail was "seen" was in 1447 when many people said they saw a fire that almost destroyed the documents before they were moved to safety in four large chests that each needed six men to carry. After that, no one claimed to see the Grail again. All that was left were rumors that it was hidden in Great Britain, the land of King Arthur and the Knights of the Round Table. Wherever it was, two things were still true:

Leonardo knew where the Grail was during his life.

That hiding place probably hasn't changed.

Because of this, Grail fans still studied Da Vinci's art and diaries, hoping to find a hidden clue to where the Grail is now. Some said the mountains in the background of Madonna of the Rocks looked like the hills with caves in Scotland. Others insisted that the way the disciples were placed in The Last Supper was a kind of code. Still others claimed that X-rays of the Mona Lisa showed she was first painted wearing a lapis lazuli pendant of Isis—a detail Da Vinci supposedly decided to paint over later. Langdon had never seen any proof of the pendant, and he couldn't imagine how it could reveal the Holy Grail, but Grail lovers still talked about it endlessly on Internet forums and chat rooms.

Everyone loves a conspiracy.

And the conspiracies kept coming. Most recently, there was the shocking discovery that Da Vinci's famous Adoration of the Magi was hiding a dark secret under its layers of paint. Italian art expert Maurizio Seracini revealed the disturbing truth, which the New York Times Magazine featured in a story called "The Leonardo Cover-Up." Seracini proved that while the gray-green sketched underdrawing of the Adoration was indeed Da Vinci's work, the painting itself was not. The truth was that an unknown painter had filled in Da Vinci's sketch like a paint-by-numbers picture years after Da Vinci died. Even more troubling was what was under the fake paint. Photos taken with infrared reflectography and X-rays suggested that this unknown painter, while filling in Da Vinci's sketch, had made strange changes to the underdrawing... as if to change Da Vinci's real intention. Whatever the true nature of the underdrawing, it hadn't been made public yet. Even so, embarrassed officials at Florence's Uffizi Gallery quickly moved the painting to a warehouse across the street. Visitors to the gallery's Leonardo Room now saw a misleading and unapologetic sign where the Adoration used to hang.

THIS WORK IS UNDERGOING

DIAGNOSTIC TESTS IN PREPARATION

FOR RESTORATION.

In the strange world of modern Grail seekers, Leonardo da Vinci was still the quest's big puzzle. His art seemed ready to tell a secret, but it stayed hidden, maybe under some paint, maybe coded in plain sight, or maybe nowhere at all. Perhaps Da Vinci's many tempting clues were just a trick to frustrate those who were curious and bring a smile to the face of his knowing Mona Lisa.

"Is it possible," Sophie asked, pulling Langdon back, "that the key you have unlocks the hiding place of the Holy Grail?"

Langdon's laugh sounded fake, even to him. "I can't really believe that. Besides, the Grail is thought to be hidden in the United Kingdom, not France." He gave her the short version of the history.

"But the Grail seems like the only logical answer," she insisted. "We have a very secure key, marked with the Priory of Sion seal, given to us by a member of the Priory of Sion—a group which, you just told me, guards the Holy Grail." Langdon knew she made sense, but he still couldn't accept it. There were rumors that the Priory had promised to bring the Grail back to France someday to its final resting place, but there was no real proof that this had happened. Even if the Priory had brought the Grail back to France, the address 24 Rue Haxo near a tennis stadium didn't sound like a proper final resting place. "Sophie, I really don't see how this key could have anything to do with the Grail."

"Because the Grail is supposed to be in England?"

"Not only that. The location of the Holy Grail is one of the best-kept secrets in history. Priory members wait years to prove themselves trustworthy before being allowed to know where the Grail is. That secret is protected by a careful system of limited knowledge, and even though the Priory is very large, only four members know where the Grail is hidden—the Grand Master and his three sénéchaux. The chance of your grandfather being one of those four top people is very small." My grandfather was one of them, Sophie thought, pressing down on the gas. She had a clear memory that proved her grandfather's high position in the brotherhood beyond any doubt.

"And even if your grandfather were in that top group, he would never be allowed to tell anyone outside the brotherhood anything. It's impossible that he would bring you into their inner circle."

I've already been there, Sophie thought, picturing the ritual in the basement. She wondered if she should tell Langdon what she had seen that night in the Normandy château. For ten years, simple shame had kept her from telling anyone. Just thinking about it made her shiver. Sirens wailed somewhere in the distance, and she felt very tired.

"There!" Langdon said, excited to see the huge Roland Garros tennis stadium ahead.

Sophie carefully drove toward the stadium. After driving around a few times, they found the intersection of Rue Haxo and turned onto it, heading toward the lower numbers. The road became more industrial, with businesses lining the street.

We need number twenty-four, Langdon thought, noticing he was subconsciously searching for church spires. Don't be absurd. A lost Templar church around here?

"There it is," Sophie said, pointing.

Langdon looked to where she was pointing.

What on earth?

The building was modern. A short, strong building with a huge, neon cross with equal arms shining on the front. Under the cross were the words:

DEPOSITORY BANK OF ZURICH

Langdon was glad he hadn't told Sophie about the Templar church. A common problem for symbologists was seeing hidden meanings where there were none. He had simply forgotten that the simple cross with equal arms was the symbol for neutral Switzerland.

At least the puzzle was solved.

Sophie and Langdon had the key to a Swiss bank safe deposit box.

CHAPTER 41

Outside Castel Gandolfo, a rush of mountain air blew over the cliff and across the high edge, making Bishop Aringarosa feel cold as he got out of the Fiat. I should have worn something warmer than this, he thought, trying not to shiver. He didn't want to look weak or scared tonight.

The castle was dark except for the windows at the very top, which glowed in a threatening way. The library, Aringarosa thought. They're awake and waiting. He lowered his head against the wind and walked on without looking at the observatory domes. The priest who met him at the door seemed sleepy. It was the same priest who had met Aringarosa five months ago, but tonight he was much less welcoming. "We were worried about you, Bishop," the priest said, checking his watch and looking more annoyed than worried.

"I'm sorry. The airlines are so unpredictable these days."

The priest mumbled something and then said, "They're waiting upstairs. I'll take you up."

The library was a large square room with dark wood from floor to ceiling. Tall bookcases full of books lined all the walls. The floor was amber marble with black basalt trim, a beautiful reminder that this building had once been a palace.

"Welcome, Bishop," a man's voice said from across the room.

Aringarosa tried to see who was speaking, but the lights were very dim—much dimmer than during his first visit, when everything was brightly lit. The night I learned the truth. Tonight, these men sat in the shadows, as if they were ashamed of what was about to happen.

Aringarosa walked in slowly, even with dignity. He could see the shapes of three men at a long table on the other side of the room. He immediately recognized the figure in the middle—the large Secretary of the Vatican, in charge of all legal matters in Vatican City. The other two were high-ranking Italian cardinals.

Aringarosa walked across the library toward them. "I'm very sorry for the late hour. We're in different time zones. You must be tired."

"Not at all," the secretary said, his hands folded on his large belly. "We appreciate you coming so far. The least we can do is be awake to meet you. Can we offer you some coffee or something to drink?"

"I'd prefer we don't act like this is a friendly visit. I have another plane to catch. Shall we get started?"

"Of course," the secretary said. "You have acted faster than we expected."

"Have I?"

"You still have a month."

"You told us your concerns five months ago," Aringarosa said. "Why should I wait?"

"Indeed. We are very pleased with your speed."

Aringarosa's eyes went down the long table to a large black briefcase. "Is that what I asked for?"

"It is." The secretary sounded worried. "Although, I must admit, we are concerned about the request. It seems quite..."

"Dangerous," one of the cardinals finished. "Are you sure we can't send it to you somewhere?"

"The amount is outrageous."

Freedom is expensive. "I'm not worried about my safety. God is with me." The men looked unsure.

"Is the money exactly as I asked?"

The secretary nodded. "Large bonds from the Vatican Bank, payable to whoever has them. They can be used like cash anywhere."

Aringarosa walked to the end of the table and opened the briefcase. Inside were two thick stacks of bonds, each marked with the Vatican seal and the word PORTATORE, meaning they could be cashed in by whoever held them.

The secretary looked nervous. "I must say, Bishop, we'd all feel better if this money was in cash."

I couldn't carry that much cash, Aringarosa thought, closing the case. "Bonds can be used like cash. You said so yourself."

The cardinals exchanged uneasy glances, and finally one said, "Yes, but these bonds can be traced back to the Vatican Bank."

Aringarosa smiled to himself. That was exactly why the Teacher told Aringarosa to get the money in Vatican Bank bonds. It was a safety measure. We're all in this together now. "This is a completely legal transaction," Aringarosa defended. "Opus Dei is a part of Vatican City, and the Pope can spend money however he wants. No law has been broken."

"True, and yet..." The secretary leaned forward and his chair creaked under his weight. "We don't know what you plan to do with this money, and if it's in any way illegal..."

"Considering what you're asking," Aringarosa replied, "what I do with this money isn't your business."

There was a long silence.

They know I'm right, Aringarosa thought. "Now, I guess you have something for me to sign?" They all jumped, quickly pushing the paper toward him, as if they wanted him to just leave. Aringarosa looked at the sheet in front of him. It had the papal seal. "This is the same as the copy you sent me?"

"Exactly."

Aringarosa was surprised he felt so little as he signed the document. But the three men there seemed to sigh in relief.

"Thank you, Bishop," the secretary said. "Your service to the Church will never be forgotten." Aringarosa picked up the briefcase, feeling the promise and power in its weight. The four men looked at each other for a moment as if there was something more to say, but apparently there wasn't. Aringarosa turned and went to the door.

"Bishop?" one of the cardinals called as Aringarosa reached the doorway. Aringarosa stopped and turned. "Yes?"

"Where will you go from here?"

Aringarosa felt the question was more about his soul than his location, but he didn't want to talk about right and wrong at this time. "Paris," he said, and walked out the door.

CHAPTER 42

The Depository Bank of Zurich was a twenty-four-hour vault bank offering a full range of modern, anonymous services in the tradition of Swiss numbered accounts. With offices in Zurich, Kuala Lumpur, New York, and Paris, the bank had recently expanded to offer anonymous computer code protection and faceless digital backup. But the main part of their business was their oldest and simplest service—the anonymous storage—blind drop services, also known as anonymous safe-deposit boxes. Clients could store anything from stock certificates to valuable paintings anonymously, through layers of high-tech privacy, and withdraw items at any time, also anonymously.

As Sophie parked the taxi, Langdon looked at the building's harsh design and felt the Depository Bank of Zurich was not a fun place. The building was a windowless rectangle made entirely of dull steel. Like a huge metal brick, it sat back from the road with a fifteen-foot-tall, neon cross glowing over the front.

Switzerland's reputation for secret banking had become one of its most profitable exports. Places like this had become controversial in the art world because they were perfect for hiding stolen art, sometimes for years, until things cooled down. Because deposits were protected from police searches by privacy laws and were linked to numbered accounts instead of names, thieves could relax knowing their stolen goods were safe and untraceable.

Sophie stopped the taxi at a large gate blocking the bank's driveway—a concrete ramp that went under the building. A video camera pointed at them, and Langdon felt this camera was real, unlike the ones at the Louvre. Sophie rolled down the window and looked at the electronic stand on the driver's side. An LCD screen had instructions in seven languages, with English at the top: INSERT KEY.

Sophie took the gold, laser-etched key from her pocket and looked back at the stand. Below the screen was a triangular hole.

"I bet that fits," Langdon said.

Sophie lined up the key's triangular part with the hole and slid it in until it disappeared. The key didn't need to be turned. The gate immediately began to open. Sophie took her foot off the brake and rolled down to a second gate and stand. The first gate closed behind them, trapping them like a ship in a lock.

Langdon didn't like feeling trapped. Hopefully this second gate works too. This second stand had the same instructions.

INSERT KEY.

When Sophie put in the key, the second gate opened right away. Soon after, they were driving down the ramp into the building's depths.

The private garage was small and dark, with room for about twelve cars. At the far end, Langdon saw the building's main entrance. A red carpet ran across the concrete floor, inviting people to a huge door that looked like it was made of solid metal.

That's confusing, Langdon thought. Welcome, but stay away. Sophie parked the taxi near the entrance and turned off the engine. "You should leave the gun here."

Gladly, Langdon thought, sliding the gun under the seat. Sophie and Langdon got out and walked up the red carpet toward the metal door. The door had no handle, but on the wall next to it was another triangular keyhole. There were no instructions this time.

"Keeps out the dummies," Langdon said.

Sophie laughed, looking worried. "Here we go." She put the key in the hole, and the door swung open with a low hum. Sophie and Langdon looked at each other and went inside. The door slammed shut behind them.

The entrance hall of the Depository Bank of Zurich had decorations as grand as any Langdon had ever seen. While most banks were happy with regular shiny marble and granite, this one had chosen metal and rivets all over the walls.

Who decorated this place? Langdon wondered. A steel company?

Sophie looked just as scared as she looked around the lobby.

The gray metal was everywhere—the floor, walls, counters, doors, even the lobby chairs seemed to be made of molded iron. Still, the effect was impressive. The message was clear: You are walking into a vault.

A large man behind the counter looked up as they entered. He turned off the small television he was watching and greeted them with a pleasant smile. Despite his huge muscles and visible gun, his speech was as polite as a Swiss bellhop.

"Bonsoir," he said. "How may I help you?"

The greeting in two languages was the latest hospitality trick of European hosts. It assumed nothing and allowed the guest to reply in whichever language was easier. Sophie replied in neither. She simply laid the gold key on the counter in front of the man. The man glanced down and immediately stood up straighter. "Of course. Your elevator is at the end of the hall. I will let someone know you are on your way."

Sophie nodded and took her key back. "Which floor?"

The man gave her an odd look. "Your key tells the elevator which floor." She smiled. "Ah, yes."

The guard watched as the two newcomers walked to the elevators, put in their key, got on the lift, and disappeared. As soon as the door had closed, he grabbed the phone. He was not calling to let anyone know they were here; there was no need for that. A vault greeter had already been alerted automatically when the client's key was inserted outside at the entry gate. Instead, the guard was calling the bank's night manager. As the line rang, the guard switched the television back on and stared at it. The news story he had been watching was just ending. It didn't matter. He got another look at the two faces on the television.

The manager answered. "Oui?"

"We have a situation down here."

"What's happening?" the manager demanded.

"The French police are chasing two people tonight."

"So?"

"They just walked into our bank."

The manager cursed softly. "Okay. I'll call Monsieur Vernet now." The guard hung up and made another call. This one to Interpol.

Langdon was surprised the elevator was going down, not up. He didn't know how many floors they'd gone below the Zurich Depository Bank before the door opened. He didn't care. He was glad to be out of the elevator.

A host was already there to meet them, moving quickly. He was old and kind, wearing a neat suit that looked strange—an old-fashioned banker in a modern world.

"Bonsoir," the man said. "Good evening. Please, follow me." He turned and walked quickly down a narrow metal hall without waiting for an answer.

Langdon and Sophie walked down halls, past large rooms full of flashing computers.

"Voici," their host said, stopping at a steel door and opening it. "Here you are." Langdon and Sophie entered a different world. The small room looked like a fancy hotel lounge. The metal was gone, replaced by rugs, dark wood furniture, and soft chairs. On the large desk, two glasses sat next to an open bottle of Perrier, still bubbling. A pewter coffee pot steamed nearby.

Typical, Langdon thought. Only the Swiss.

The man smiled knowingly. "I guess this is your first time visiting us?" Sophie hesitated and then nodded.

"I understand. Keys are often passed down in families, and first-time users are always unsure what to do." He pointed to the table of drinks. "This room is yours to use as long as you like."

"You said keys are sometimes inherited?" Sophie asked.

"Yes. Your key is like a Swiss bank account, often passed down through generations. For our gold accounts, the shortest safe-deposit box rental is fifty years. Paid in advance. So, we see a lot of family changes."

Langdon stared. "Did you say fifty years?"

"At least," their host replied. "Of course, you can rent for much longer, but unless other arrangements are made, if there's no activity on an account for fifty years, the contents of the safe-deposit box are automatically destroyed. Should I explain how to access your box?" Sophie nodded. "Please."

Their host gestured around the fancy room. "This is your private viewing room. Once I leave, you can spend as much time as you need in here to look at and change the contents of your safe-deposit box, which arrives... over here." He led them to the far wall where a wide conveyor belt curved into the room, looking a bit like a baggage claim carousel.

"You put your key in that slot there...." The man pointed to a large electronic stand facing the conveyor belt. The stand had a familiar triangular hole. "Once the computer confirms your key, you enter your account number, and your safe-deposit box will be brought up robotically from the vault below for you to examine. When you're done, you place the box back on the conveyor belt, insert your key again, and the process is reversed. Because it's all automated, your privacy is guaranteed, even from the bank staff. If you need anything, just press the call button on the table in the center of the room." Sophie was about to ask a question when a telephone rang. The man looked surprised and a little embarrassed. "Excuse me, please." He walked over to the phone, which was on the table next to the coffee and Perrier.

"Oui?" he answered.

He frowned as he listened. "Oui... oui... d'accord." He hung up and gave them an uneasy smile. "I'm sorry, I have to leave now. Make yourselves at home." He hurried toward the door.

"Excuse me," Sophie called. "Could you explain something before you go? You said we need an account number?"

The man stopped at the door, looking pale. "Of course. Like most Swiss banks, our safe-deposit boxes are linked to a number, not a name. You have a key and a personal account number that only you know. Your key is only half of your identification. Your personal account number is the other half. Otherwise, if you lost your key, anyone could use it."

Sophie hesitated. "And if my benefactor didn't give me an account number?" The banker's heart pounded. Then you shouldn't be here! He gave them a calm smile. "I'll have someone help you. He'll be here soon." Leaving, the banker closed the door behind him and turned a heavy lock, trapping them inside.

Across town, Collet was standing in the Gare du Nord train terminal when his phone rang. It was Fache. "Interpol got a tip," he said. "Forget the train. Langdon and Neveu just went into the Paris branch of the Depository Bank of Zurich. I want your men there immediately."

"Any clues yet on what Saunière was trying to tell Agent Neveu and Robert Langdon?" Fache's tone was cold. "If you arrest them, Lieutenant Collet, then I can ask them myself." Collet understood. "Twenty-four Rue Haxo. Right away, Captain." He hung up and radioed his men.

CHAPTER 43

André Vernet—president of the Paris branch of the Depository Bank of Zurich—lived in a fancy apartment above the bank. Despite his luxurious home, he had always dreamed of owning an apartment by the river on L'lle Saint-Louis, where he could mingle with the truly cultured, instead of just meeting the very rich.

When I retire, Vernet thought, I will fill my cellar with fine Bordeaux wines, decorate my living room with a Fragonard and maybe a Boucher painting, and spend my days looking for antique furniture and rare books in the Quartier Latin.

Tonight, Vernet had been awake for only six and a half minutes. Still, as he hurried through the bank's underground corridor, he looked as if his personal tailor and hairdresser had made him perfectly polished. Dressed in a silk suit, Vernet sprayed breath freshener in his mouth and tightened his tie as he walked. Used to being woken up to help his international clients arriving from different time zones, Vernet based his sleep habits on the Maasai warriors—the African tribe known for their ability to wake from deep sleep to full readiness in seconds.

Ready for battle, Vernet thought, worried the comparison might be fitting tonight. The arrival of a gold key client always needed extra attention, but the arrival of a gold key client who was wanted by the police would be very tricky. The bank had enough problems with law enforcement over client privacy without proof that some clients were criminals.

Five minutes, Vernet thought. I need these people out of my bank before the police get here. If he acted fast, he could avoid this disaster. Vernet could tell the police that the people they were looking for had come into his bank as reported, but because they weren't clients and didn't have an account number, they were turned away. He wished the watchman hadn't called Interpol. Being discreet was clearly not something a 15-euro-per-hour watchman knew about. Stopping at the doorway, he took a deep breath and relaxed his muscles. Then, forcing a friendly smile, he unlocked the door and entered the room like a warm breeze.

"Good evening," he said, his eyes finding his clients. "I am André Vernet. How can I help y—" The rest of the sentence caught in his throat. The woman before him was as unexpected a visitor as Vernet had ever had.

"I'm sorry, do we know each other?" Sophie asked. She didn't recognize the banker, but he looked for a moment as if he'd seen a ghost.

"No...," the bank president stammered. "I don't... think so. Our services are private." He breathed out and forced a calm smile. "My assistant tells me you have a gold key but no account number? May I ask how you got this key?"

"My grandfather gave it to me," Sophie replied, watching the man closely. His nervousness seemed even more obvious now.

"Really? Your grandfather gave you the key but didn't give you the account number?"

"I don't think he had time," Sophie said. "He was murdered tonight." Her words made the man step back, shocked. "Jacques Saunière is dead?" he asked, his eyes wide with horror. "But... how?!"

Now Sophie was the one who felt stunned, shocked and numb. "You knew my grandfather?" Banker André Vernet looked just as surprised, holding himself up by leaning on a small table.

"Jacques and I were close friends. When did this happen?"

"Earlier tonight. Inside the Louvre."

Vernet walked to a big leather chair and sat down heavily. "I need to ask you both something very important." He looked at Langdon and then back at Sophie. "Did either of you have anything to do with his death?"

"No!" Sophie said. "Absolutely not."

Vernet's face was serious, and he stopped, thinking. "Your pictures are being sent around by Interpol. That's how I knew who you were. You're wanted for murder."

Sophie felt weak. Fache sent an Interpol message already? It seemed the captain was more determined than Sophie thought. She quickly told Vernet who Langdon was and what had happened inside the Louvre tonight.

Vernet looked astonished. "And as your grandfather was dying, he left you a message telling you to find Mr. Langdon?"

Yes. And this key." Sophie put the gold key on the coffee table in front of Vernet, with the Priory seal face down.

Vernet looked at the key but didn't touch it. "He only left you this key? Nothing else? No note?"

Sophie knew she had been in a hurry inside the Louvre, but she was sure she had seen nothing else behind Madonna of the Rocks. "No. Just the key." Vernet sighed, sounding helpless. "I'm afraid every key needs a ten-digit account number to work, like a password. Without that number, your key is useless." Ten digits. Sophie did a quick calculation. Over ten billion possible choices. Even with DCPJ's best computers, it would still take weeks to break the code. "Surely, monsieur, considering what's happening, you can help us."

"I'm sorry. I really can't do anything. Clients pick their own account numbers on a secure terminal, so only the client and the computer know them. This is how we keep things private. And keep our employees safe."

Sophie understood. Stores did the same thing. EMPLOYEES DO NOT HAVE KEYS TO THE SAFE. This bank clearly didn't want someone stealing a key and forcing an employee to give up the account number.

Sophie sat next to Langdon, looked at the key, and then at Vernet. "Do you have any idea what my grandfather is keeping in your bank?"

"None at all. That's what a Geldschrank bank is."

"Monsieur Vernet," she said urgently, "we don't have much time tonight. I'm going to be very direct, if I may." She reached for the gold key and turned it over, watching his eyes as she showed the Priory of Sion seal. "Does this symbol mean anything to you?" Vernet looked at the fleur-de-lis seal and didn't react. "No, but many of our clients put company logos or initials on their keys."

Sophie sighed, still watching him closely. "This seal is the symbol of a secret group called the Priory of Sion."

Vernet still didn't react. "I don't know anything about that. Your grandfather was a friend, but we talked mostly about business." He adjusted his tie, now looking nervous.

"Monsieur Vernet," Sophie said firmly. "My grandfather called me tonight and said he and I were in serious danger. He said he had to give me something. He gave me a key to your bank. Now he's dead. Anything you can tell us would help."

Vernet started sweating. "We need to leave the building. I'm afraid the police will be here soon. My guard felt he had to call Interpol."

Sophie had expected this. She tried one last thing. "My grandfather said he needed to tell me the truth about my family. Does that mean anything to you?"

"Mademoiselle, your family died in a car accident when you were young. I'm sorry. I know your grandfather loved you very much. He often told me how sad he was that you two had lost touch."

Sophie didn't know what to say.

Langdon asked, "Does what's in this account have anything to do with the Sangreal?" Vernet looked at him strangely. "I have no idea what that is." Just then, Vernet's cell phone rang, and he grabbed it from his belt. "Oui?" He listened, looking surprised and worried. "La police? Si rapidement?" He swore, gave some quick directions in French, and said he'd be in the lobby in a minute.

Hanging up, he turned back to Sophie. "The police are here much faster than normal. They're arriving right now."

Sophie didn't want to leave with nothing. "Tell them we already came and left. If they want to search the bank, make them get a warrant. That will take time."

"Listen," Vernet said, "Jacques was a friend, and my bank doesn't need this kind of attention, so for those two reasons, I won't let this arrest happen here. Give me a minute, and I'll see how to help you leave the bank without being seen. After that, I can't get involved." He stood and rushed to the door. "Stay here. I'll set things up and be right back."

"But the safe-deposit box," Sophie said. "We can't just leave it."

"There's nothing I can do," Vernet said, hurrying out. "I'm sorry." Sophie watched him go, wondering if the account number was hidden in one of the many letters and packages her grandfather had sent her that she hadn't opened.

Langdon stood suddenly, and Sophie saw a hint of happiness in his eyes.

"Robert? You're smiling."

"Your grandfather was a genius."

"What?"

"Ten digits?"

Sophie didn't know what he meant.

"The account number," he said, a grin spreading across his face. "I think he did leave it for us after all."

"Where?"

Langdon took out the crime scene photo and laid it on the coffee table. Sophie only had to read the first line to know Langdon was right.

13-3-2-21-1-1-8-5

O, harsh devil!

Oh, weak saint!

P.S. Find Robert Langdon

CHAPTER 44

"Ten digits," Sophie said, her code-breaking instincts kicking in as she looked at the printout. 13-3-2-21-1-1-8-5

Grand-père wrote his account number on the Louvre floor!

When Sophie first saw the mixed-up Fibonacci sequence on the floor, she thought it was just to get DCPJ to call in their code experts and get Sophie involved. Later, she understood the numbers also helped decode the other lines—a sequence mixed up... a number puzzle. Now, completely surprised, she saw the numbers meant even more. They were almost definitely the final key to opening her grandfather's secret safe-deposit box.

"He loved hidden meanings," Sophie said, turning to Langdon. "He loved things with many layers. Codes inside codes."

Langdon was already heading to the electronic stand near the conveyor belt. Sophie took the computer printout and followed.

The stand had a keypad like a bank ATM. The screen showed the bank's cross-shaped logo. Next to the keypad was a triangle-shaped hole. Sophie quickly put her key into the hole.

The screen changed right away.

ACCOUNT NUMBER: _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _

The cursor blinked. Waiting.

Ten digits. Sophie read the numbers from the printout, and Langdon typed them in. ACCOUNT NUMBER: 1332211185

When he had typed the last digit, the screen refreshed again. A message in several languages appeared. English was at the top.

CAUTION:

Before you press the enter key, please check that your account number is correct.

For your own security, if the computer does not recognize your account number, this system will automatically shut down.

"Fonction terminer," Sophie said, frowning. "Looks like we only get one try." Standard ATM machines allowed users three attempts to type a PIN before taking their bank card. This was obviously no ordinary cash machine.

"The number looks right," Langdon confirmed, carefully checking what they had typed and comparing it to the printout. He pointed to the ENTER key. "Go ahead." Sophie reached her index finger toward the keypad, but hesitated, a strange thought now occurring to her.

"Go ahead," Langdon urged. "Vernet will be back soon."

"No." She pulled her hand away. "This isn't the right account number."

"Of course it is! Ten digits. What else would it be?"

"It's too random."

Too random? Langdon couldn't disagree more. Every bank told its customers to pick random PINs so nobody could guess them. Surely clients here would be told to pick their account numbers at random.

Sophie deleted what she had typed and looked up at Langdon, her gaze confident.

"It's too much of a coincidence that this supposedly random account number could be rearranged to make the Fibonacci sequence."

Langdon saw her point. Earlier, Sophie had rearranged this account number into the Fibonacci sequence. What were the chances of that?

Sophie was at the keypad again, entering a different number, as if she remembered it. "Plus, with my grandfather's love of symbols and codes, it makes sense he would have picked an account number that meant something to him, something easy to remember." She finished typing and smiled slyly. "Something that seemed random... but wasn't." Langdon looked at the screen.

ACCOUNT NUMBER: 1123581321

It took him a second, but when Langdon saw it, he knew she was right. The Fibonacci sequence.

1-1-2-3-5-8-13-21

When the Fibonacci sequence was made into one ten-digit number, it was almost impossible to recognize. Easy to remember, but seemingly random. A smart ten-digit code that Saunière would never forget. Also, it perfectly explained why the mixed-up numbers on the Louvre floor could be put back together to make the famous sequence.

Sophie reached down and pressed the ENTER key.

Nothing happened.

At least, nothing they could see.

At that moment, below them, in the bank's huge underground vault, a robotic claw started moving. Sliding on a track attached to the ceiling, the claw went to find the right spot. On the cement floor below, hundreds of identical plastic crates were lined up on a large grid... like rows of small coffins in an underground tomb. Stopping over the right spot on the floor, the claw reached down, an electric eye checking the bar code on the box. Then, with computer precision, the claw grabbed the heavy handle and lifted the crate straight up. New gears started, and the claw moved the box to the other side of the vault, stopping over a still conveyor belt. Carefully, the retrieval arm set the crate down and pulled back.

Once the arm was out of the way, the conveyor belt started moving....

Upstairs, Sophie and Langdon let out a breath of relief when they saw the conveyor belt move. Standing by the belt, they felt like tired travelers at baggage claim, waiting for a mysterious piece of luggage with unknown contents.

The conveyor belt came into the room on their right through a narrow opening under a door that could slide up. The metal door slid up, and a huge plastic box appeared, coming out of the depths on the rising conveyor belt. The box was black, heavy plastic, and much bigger than she thought. It looked like an air-freight pet carrier without any airholes.

The box rolled to a stop right in front of them.

Langdon and Sophie stood there, quiet, staring at the strange container. Like everything else about this bank, this crate was industrial—metal clasps, a bar code sticker on top, and a strong, molded handle. Sophie thought it looked like a giant toolbox. Without delay, Sophie unhooked the two buckles facing her. Then she looked over at Langdon. Together, they raised the heavy lid and let it drop back.

Stepping forward, they looked down into the crate.

At first, Sophie thought the crate was empty. Then she saw something. Sitting at the bottom of the crate. A single item.

The polished wooden box was about the size of a shoebox and had fancy hinges. The wood was a shiny, deep purple with a strong grain. Rosewood, Sophie realized. Her grandfather's favorite. The lid had a beautiful design of a rose inlaid in it. She and Langdon exchanged puzzled looks. Sophie leaned in and grabbed the box, lifting it out.

My God, it's heavy!

She carried it carefully to a large table and set it down. Langdon stood beside her, both of them staring at the small treasure chest her grandfather had apparently sent them to get. Langdon stared in wonder at the lid's hand-carved inlay—a five-petal rose. He had seen this type of rose many times. "The five-petal rose," he whispered, "is a Priory symbol for the Holy Grail."

Sophie turned and looked at him. Langdon could see what she was thinking, and he was thinking it too. The size of the box, the obvious weight of its contents, and a Priory symbol for the Grail all seemed to point to one unbelievable idea. The Cup of Christ is in this wooden box. Langdon again told himself it was impossible.

"It's a perfect size," Sophie whispered, "to hold... a chalice." It can't be a chalice.

Sophie pulled the box toward her across the table, getting ready to open it. As she moved it, though, something unexpected happened. The box made a strange gurgling sound.

Langdon looked again, surprised. Is there liquid inside?

Sophie looked just as confused. "Did you hear that too...?"

Langdon nodded, puzzled. "Liquid."

Reaching out, Sophie carefully unfastened the clasp and lifted the lid.
