The thing inside was like nothing Langdon had ever seen. But one thing was obvious to both of them right away. This was definitely not the Cup of Christ.

CHAPTER 45

"The police are blocking the street," André Vernet said, walking into the waiting room. "It will be hard to get you out." As he closed the door, Vernet saw the strong plastic case on the belt and stopped. Oh no! They got into Saunière's account?

Sophie and Langdon were at the table, gathered around what looked like a big wooden jewelry box. Sophie quickly closed the lid and looked up. "We did have the account number," she said.

Vernet was stunned. This changed everything. He politely looked away from the box and tried to plan. I have to get them out of the bank! But with the police blocking the street, Vernet could think of only one way. "Mademoiselle Neveu, if I can get you out safely, will you take the item with you or put it back in the vault before you leave?"

Sophie looked at Langdon, then back to Vernet. "We need to take it." Vernet nodded. "Okay. Then, whatever it is, hide it in your jacket as we walk. I don't want anyone else to see it."

As Langdon took off his jacket, Vernet rushed to the belt, closed the empty crate, and typed in a few simple commands. The belt started moving, taking the plastic container back to the vault. Taking the gold key from the stand, he gave it to Sophie.

"This way, please. Hurry."

At the back loading dock, Vernet saw police lights flashing in the underground garage. He frowned. They were likely blocking the ramp. Am I really going to do this? He was sweating now.

Vernet pointed to one of the bank's small armored trucks. Safe Transport was another service from the Depository Bank of Zurich.

"Get in the back," he said, pulling open the heavy rear door and pointing to the shiny steel area. "I'll be right back."

As Sophie and Langdon climbed in, Vernet hurried across the loading dock to the dock overseer's office, let himself in, got the truck keys, and found a driver's uniform jacket and cap. Taking off his suit coat and tie, he started to put on the driver's jacket. Then he reconsidered and put on a shoulder holster under the uniform. On his way out, he grabbed a driver's pistol from the rack, loaded it, and stuck it in the holster, buttoning his uniform over it. Returning to the truck, Vernet pulled the driver's cap down low and looked in at Sophie and Langdon, who were standing inside the empty steel box.

"You'll want this on," Vernet said, reaching inside and flipping a wall switch to turn on the single light on the hold's ceiling. "And you'd better sit down. Not a sound while we go out the gate."

Sophie and Langdon sat down on the metal floor. Langdon held the treasure wrapped in his tweed jacket. Swinging the heavy doors closed, Vernet locked them inside. Then he got behind the wheel and started the engine.

As the armored truck slowly moved toward the top of the ramp, Vernet could already feel sweat under his driver's cap. He saw many more police lights in front than he had expected. As the truck went up the ramp, the inner gate swung open to let him pass. Vernet moved forward and waited for the gate behind him to close before driving ahead and setting off the next sensor. The second gate opened, and the exit was ahead.

Except for the police car blocking the top of the ramp.

Vernet wiped his forehead and drove forward.

A tall officer stepped out and waved him to a stop a few meters from the roadblock. Four patrol cars were parked in front.

Vernet stopped. Pulling his driver's cap down further, he tried to look as rough as his refined upbringing would allow. Without getting out, he opened the door and looked down at the agent, whose face was stern and pale.

"Qu'est-ce qui se passe?" Vernet asked, his voice rough.

"Je suis Jérome Collet," the agent said. "Lieutenant Police Judiciaire." He pointed to the truck's cargo hold. "Qu'est-ce qu'ily a là dedans?"

"Hell if I know," Vernet replied in crude French. "I'm just a driver." Collet looked unimpressed. "We're looking for two criminals." Vernet laughed. "Then you came to the right place. Some of these bastards I drive for have so much money they must be criminals."

The agent held up a passport picture of Robert Langdon. "Was this man in your bank tonight?" Vernet shrugged. "No idea. I'm a dock rat. They don't let us anywhere near the clients. You need to go in and ask the front desk."

"Your bank is demanding a search warrant before we can enter." Vernet put on a disgusted look. "Administrators. Don't even get me started."

"Open your truck, please." Collet motioned toward the cargo hold. Vernet stared at the agent and forced an obnoxious laugh. "Open the truck? You think I have keys?

You think they trust us? You should see the crap wages I get." The agent's head tilted to one side, his skepticism obvious. "You're telling me you don't have keys to your own truck?"

Vernet shook his head. "Not the cargo area. Only the ignition. These trucks get sealed by supervisors on the loading dock. Then the truck sits there while someone drives the cargo keys to the drop-off. Once we get the call that the cargo keys are with the receiver, then I get the okay to drive. Not a second before. I never know what I'm hauling."

"When was this truck sealed?"

"Must have been hours ago. I'm driving all the way up to St. Thurial tonight. Cargo keys are already up there."

The agent made no response, his eyes searching as if trying to read Vernet's mind. A drop of sweat was about to slide down Vernet's nose. "You mind?" he said, wiping his nose with his sleeve and motioning to the police car blocking his way. "I'm on a tight schedule."

"Do all the drivers wear Rolexes?" the agent asked, pointing to Vernet's wrist. Vernet glanced down and saw the shiny band of his ridiculously expensive watch peeking out from beneath his jacket sleeve. Merde. "This piece of junk? Bought it for twenty euro from a Taiwanese street vendor in St. Germain des Prés. I'll sell it to you for forty." The agent paused and finally stepped aside. "No thanks. Have a safe trip." Vernet didn't breathe again until the truck was a good fifty meters down the street. And now he had another problem. His cargo. Where do I take them?

CHAPTER 46

Silas lay still on the canvas mat in his room, letting the whip marks on his back dry in the air. Tonight's second session with the Discipline had left him lightheaded and weak. He still wore the cilice belt, and he could feel blood running down his inner thigh. But he couldn't justify taking the strap off.

I have failed the Church.

Even worse, I have failed the bishop.

Tonight was meant to be Bishop Aringarosa's salvation. Five months ago, the bishop had returned from a meeting at the Vatican Observatory, where he had learned something that changed him deeply. Aringarosa was depressed for weeks before finally sharing the news with Silas.

"But this is impossible!" Silas had exclaimed. "I can't believe it!"

"It is true," Aringarosa said. "Unbelievable, but true. In only six months." The bishop's words scared Silas. He prayed for help, and even then, his trust in God and The Way never faded. Only a month later, things cleared up miraculously, and hope appeared.

Divine intervention, Aringarosa had called it.

The bishop seemed hopeful for the first time. "Silas," he whispered, "God has given us a chance to protect The Way. Our fight, like all fights, will require sacrifice. Will you be a soldier of God?"

Silas knelt before Bishop Aringarosa—the man who had given him a new life—and said, "I am a lamb of God. Guide me as you wish." When Aringarosa explained the opportunity, Silas knew it could only be God's work. Amazing fate! Aringarosa put Silas in touch with the man who had suggested the plan—a man who called himself the Teacher. Although the Teacher and Silas never met in person, each time they spoke on the phone, Silas was amazed by the Teacher's deep faith and great power. The Teacher seemed to know everything, with eyes and ears everywhere. Silas didn't know how the Teacher got his information, but Aringarosa trusted the Teacher greatly and told Silas to do the same. "Do as the Teacher tells you," the bishop told Silas. "And we will win." Win. Silas now stared at the bare floor, fearing they had lost. The Teacher had been tricked. The keystone was a clever trap. And with the trick, all hope was gone. Silas wished he could call Bishop Aringarosa and warn him, but the Teacher had cut off all direct contact tonight. For our safety.

Finally, overcoming great fear, Silas crawled to his feet and found his robe on the floor. He took his cell phone from the pocket. Hanging his head in shame, he dialed.

"Teacher," he whispered, "we've lost everything." Silas truthfully told the man how he had been tricked.

"You lose your faith too easily," the Teacher replied. "I have just received news. Very unexpected and welcome. The secret lives. Jacques Saunière passed on information before he died. I will call you soon. Our work tonight is not yet finished."

CHAPTER 47

Riding in the dim cargo hold of the armored truck felt like being in solitary confinement. Langdon fought the familiar anxiety he felt in small spaces. Vernet said he would take us far from the city. Where? How far?

Langdon's legs were stiff from sitting on the metal floor, and he shifted, wincing as blood flowed back into them. He still held the strange treasure they had taken from the bank.

"I think we're on the highway now," Sophie whispered.

Langdon felt it too. The truck had paused at the bank ramp, then turned left and right for a few minutes, and now was speeding up. Below them, the tires hummed on the smooth road. He looked at the rosewood box in his arms, placed it on the floor, took off his jacket, and pulled the box closer. Sophie moved so they were sitting side by side. Langdon felt like they were two kids with a Christmas present. The inlaid rose on the warm-colored rosewood box was made of light wood, probably ash, and stood out in the dim light. The Rose. Armies, religions, and secret societies had been built on this symbol. The Rosicrucians. The Knights of the Rosy Cross.

"Go ahead," Sophie said. "Open it."

Langdon took a deep breath. He reached for the lid, admired the woodwork, and then unhooked the clasp and opened it. He had imagined many things they might find inside, but he was wrong. Inside the padded crimson silk lining was something Langdon didn't understand.

It was a polished white marble cylinder, about the size of a tennis ball can. But it was more complex than a simple stone column, and seemed to be made of many pieces. Six doughnut-sized marble disks were stacked and attached to each other within a delicate brass frame. It looked like a tubular kaleidoscope with many wheels. Each end of the cylinder had a marble cap, so you couldn't see inside. Langdon heard liquid inside, so he thought the cylinder was hollow. The engravings around the tube were as puzzling as its construction. Each of the six disks had the same series of letters—the entire alphabet. The lettered cylinder reminded Langdon of a childhood toy—a rod with lettered pieces that could be turned to spell words.

"Amazing, isn't it?" Sophie whispered.

Langdon looked up. "I don't know. What is it?"

Now there was a spark in Sophie's eye. "My grandfather used to make these as a hobby. They were invented by Leonardo da Vinci."

Even in the dim light, Sophie could see Langdon's surprise.

"Da Vinci?" he murmured, looking again at the canister.

"Yes. It's called a cryptex. According to my grandfather, the plans come from one of Da Vinci's secret notebooks."

"What is it for?"

Considering what was happening tonight, Sophie knew the answer could be important.

"It's a vault," she said. "For keeping secret information." Langdon's eyes widened even more.

Sophie explained that making models of Da Vinci's inventions was one of her grandfather's favorite hobbies. A skilled craftsman who spent hours in his workshop, Jacques Saunière enjoyed copying master craftsmen—Fabergé, various enamel artists, and the less artistic, but more useful, Leonardo da Vinci.

Even a quick look through Da Vinci's journals showed why he was known for not finishing things as much as he was known for his genius. Da Vinci had drawn plans for hundreds of inventions he never built. One of Jacques Saunière's favorite things to do was to bring Da Vinci's more unusual ideas to life—clocks, water pumps, cryptexes, and even a fully movable model of a medieval French knight, which now stood proudly on the desk in his office. Designed by Da Vinci in 1495 as a result of his early studies of the body and movement, the robot knight's inner workings had accurate joints and tendons, and was designed to sit up, wave its arms, and move its head with a flexible neck while opening and closing a realistic jaw. Sophie had always thought this armored knight was the most beautiful thing her grandfather had ever built... until she saw the cryptex in this rosewood box.

"He made me one of these when I was little," Sophie said. "But I've never seen one so fancy and big."

Langdon's eyes had never left the box. "I've never heard of a cryptex." Sophie was not surprised. Most of Leonardo's unbuilt inventions had never been studied or even named. The term cryptex might have been her grandfather's creation, a fitting name for this device that used the science of cryptology to protect information written on the scroll or codex inside. Da Vinci had been a cryptology pioneer, Sophie knew, though he was rarely given credit. Sophie's university teachers, when presenting computer encryption methods for protecting data, praised modern cryptologists like Zimmerman and Schneier but didn't mention that Leonardo had invented one of the first basic forms of public key encryption centuries ago. Sophie's grandfather, of course, had told her all about that. As their armored truck sped down the highway, Sophie explained to Langdon that the cryptex had been Da Vinci's solution to sending secure messages over long distances. In a time without telephones or e-mail, anyone wanting to send private information to someone far away had to write it down and trust a messenger to carry the letter. Unfortunately, if a messenger thought the letter might contain valuable information, he could make more money selling the information to enemies than delivering the letter properly.

Many great minds in history had invented cryptologic solutions to protect data: Julius Caesar created a code-writing system called the Caesar Box; Mary, Queen of Scots created a transposition cipher and sent secret messages from prison; and the brilliant Arab scientist Abu Yusuf Ismail al-Kindi protected his secrets with a clever polyalphabetic substitution cipher.

Da Vinci, however, avoided mathematics and cryptology for a mechanical solution. The cryptex. A portable container that could protect letters, maps, diagrams, anything at all. Once information was sealed inside the cryptex, only the person with the correct password could access it.

"We need a password," Sophie said, pointing to the lettered dials. "A cryptex works like a bicycle's combination lock. If you line up the dials in the right position, the lock slides open. This cryptex has five lettered dials. When you turn them to the right sequence, the tumblers inside line up, and the entire cylinder slides apart."

"And inside?"

"Once the cylinder slides apart, you can access a hollow center, which can hold a scroll of paper with the information you want to keep private." Langdon looked doubtful. "And you're saying your grandfather built these for you when you were younger?"

"Some smaller ones, yes. A couple of times for my birthday, he gave me a cryptex and told me a riddle. The answer to the riddle was the password to the cryptex, and once I solved it, I could open it and find my birthday card."

"A lot of work for a card."

"No, the cards always had another riddle or clue. My grandfather loved creating elaborate treasure hunts around our house, a series of clues that eventually led to my real gift. Each treasure hunt was a test of character and worth, to make sure I earned my rewards. And the tests were never easy."

Langdon looked at the device again, still skeptical. "But why not just pry it open? Or smash it?"

The metal looks fragile, and marble is a soft stone."

Sophie smiled. "Because Da Vinci is too clever for that. He made the cryptex so that if you try to break it open, the information destroys itself. Watch." Sophie reached into the box and carefully lifted out the cylinder. "Any information to be put inside is first written on a papyrus scroll."

"Not vellum?"

Sophie shook her head. "Papyrus. I know sheep's vellum lasted longer and was more common then, but it had to be papyrus. The thinner it is, the better."

"Okay."

"Before the papyrus was put into the cryptex, it was rolled around a thin glass vial." She tipped the cryptex, and the liquid inside made a gurgling sound. "A vial of liquid."

"Liquid what?"

Sophie smiled. "Vinegar."

Langdon paused for a moment and then started nodding. "Brilliant." Vinegar and papyrus, Sophie thought. If someone tried to force the cryptex open, the glass vial would break, and the vinegar would quickly dissolve the papyrus. By the time anyone got the secret message out, it would be a blob of unreadable mush.

"As you can see," Sophie told him, "the only way to get the information inside is to know the right five-letter password. And with five dials, each with twenty-six letters, that's twenty-six to the fifth power." She quickly figured out the combinations. "About twelve million possibilities."

"If you say so," Langdon said, looking like he had a million questions in his head. "What do you think is inside?"

"Whatever it is, my grandfather really wanted to keep it secret." She paused, closing the box and looking at the five-petal Rose on it. Something bothered her. "Did you say the Rose is a symbol for the Grail?"

"Exactly. In Priory symbols, the Rose and the Grail mean the same thing." Sophie frowned. "That's strange, because my grandfather always said the Rose meant secrecy. He'd hang a rose on his office door when he was on a private call and didn't want me to bother him. He told me to do the same." Sweetie, her grandfather said, instead of locking each other out, we can hang a rose — la fleur des secrets — on our door when we need privacy. This way we learn to respect and trust each other. Hanging a rose is an old Roman custom.

"Sub rosa," Langdon said. "The Romans hung a rose over meetings to show they were private. People knew that whatever was said under the rose — or sub rosa — had to stay secret."

Langdon quickly explained that secrecy wasn't the only reason the Priory used the Rose as a symbol for the Grail. Rosa rugosa, one of the oldest types of rose, had five petals and a five-sided shape, like the star of Venus, so the Rose was strongly linked to women. Also, the Rose was closely tied to "true direction" and finding your way. The Compass Rose helped travelers navigate, as did Rose Lines, the lines on maps. So, the Rose symbolized the Grail in many ways—secrecy, women, and guidance—the feminine cup and guiding star that led to secret truth.

As Langdon finished, his face suddenly looked tense.

"Robert? Are you okay?"

He stared at the rosewood box. "Sub... rosa," he gasped, looking scared and confused. "It can't be."

"What?"

Langdon slowly looked up. "Under the sign of the Rose," he whispered. "This cryptex... I think I know what it is."

CHAPTER 48

Langdon could hardly believe his idea, but considering who gave them this stone cylinder, how he gave it to them, and now, the inlaid Rose on the container, Langdon could only reach one conclusion.

I am holding the Priory keystone.

The legend was clear.

The keystone is a coded stone that lies under the sign of the Rose.

"Robert?" Sophie was watching him. "What's going on?" Langdon needed a moment to collect his thoughts. "Did your grandfather ever mention something called la clef de voûte?"

"The key to the vault?" Sophie translated.

"No, that's the direct translation. Clef de voûte is a common term in architecture. Voûte doesn't refer to a bank vault, but to a vault in an archway. Like a vaulted ceiling."

"But vaulted ceilings don't have keys."

"Actually, they do. Every stone archway needs a central, wedge-shaped stone at the top that locks the pieces together and supports all the weight. This stone is, in architectural terms, the key to the vault. In English, we call it a keystone." Langdon watched her eyes for any sign of understanding.

Sophie shrugged, glancing down at the cryptex. "But this obviously is not a keystone." Langdon didn't know where to begin. Keystones, used in stone archways, were a closely guarded secret of the early Masons. The Royal Arch Degree. Architecture. Keystones. It was all connected. Knowing how to use a keystone to build an archway was part of what made the Masons wealthy, and they kept it secret. Keystones had always been linked to secrecy. Yet, the stone cylinder in the rosewood box was clearly something different. The Priory keystone—if that's what they had—was not what Langdon had expected.

"The Priory keystone is not my specialty," Langdon admitted. "I study the Holy Grail through symbols, so I don't focus on how to actually find it." Sophie's eyebrows went up. "Find the Holy Grail?"

Langdon nodded slightly, choosing his words carefully. "Sophie, according to Priory stories, the keystone is a coded map... a map to the Holy Grail's hiding place." Sophie's face went blank. "And you think this is it?"

Langdon didn't know what to say. It sounded unbelievable even to him, but the keystone was the only logical explanation he could think of. A coded stone, hidden under the sign of the Rose.

The idea that Leonardo da Vinci—a former leader of the Priory of Sion—had designed the cryptex made it even more likely that this was the Priory keystone. A former leader's design... brought to life centuries later by another Priory member. The connection was too strong to ignore.

For the past ten years, historians had looked for the keystone in French churches. Grail searchers, knowing the Priory used confusing language, thought la clef de voûte was a real keystone—a stone wedge—an engraved, coded stone in a church archway. Beneath the sign of the Rose. Churches had many roses in their architecture. Rose windows. Rosette reliefs. And many cinquefoils —the five-petaled flowers often found above archways, over the keystone. The hiding place seemed deceptively simple. The map to the Holy Grail was part of a church archway, mocking the unaware churchgoers below.

"This cryptex can't be the keystone," Sophie argued. "It's not old enough. My grandfather made it. It can't be part of an ancient Grail legend."

"Actually," Langdon replied, feeling excited, "the keystone is thought to have been made by the Priory in recent decades." Sophie looked disbelieving. "But if this cryptex shows where the Holy Grail is hidden, why would my grandfather give it to me? I don't know how to open it or what to do with it. I don't even know what the Holy Grail is!"

Langdon realized she was right. He hadn't explained the Holy Grail to Sophie yet. That would have to wait. Right now, they were focused on the keystone.

If that is what this is....

Against the hum of the bulletproof wheels beneath them, Langdon quickly explained to Sophie everything he had heard about the keystone. Supposedly, for centuries, the Priory's biggest secret—the location of the Holy Grail—was never written down. For safety, it was verbally passed to each new rising sénéchal at a secret ceremony. However, at some point during the last century, rumors began to spread that the Priory policy had changed. Maybe it was because of new electronic listening devices, but the Priory promised never again to even speak the location of the sacred hiding place.

"But then how could they pass on the secret?" Sophie asked.

"That's where the keystone comes in," Langdon explained. "When one of the top four members died, the remaining three would choose the next candidate to become sénéchal from the lower ranks. Instead of telling the new sénéchal where the Grail was hidden, they gave him a test to prove he was worthy."

Sophie seemed uneasy, and Langdon suddenly remembered her mentioning how her grandfather used to make treasure hunts for her— preuves de mérite. The keystone was like that in some ways. Then again, tests like this were very common in secret societies. The most famous was the Masons', where members moved up to higher levels by proving they could keep a secret and by performing rituals and various tests of merit over many years. The tasks became harder and harder until a successful candidate was made a thirty-second-degree Mason.

"So the keystone is a preuve de mérite," Sophie said. "If a rising Priory sénéchal can open it, he proves he deserves the information it holds."

Langdon nodded. "I forgot you'd had experience with this sort of thing."

"Not only with my grandfather. In cryptology, that's called a 'self-authorizing language.' If you're smart enough to read it, you're allowed to know what it says." Langdon hesitated a moment. "Sophie, you realize that if this is the keystone, your grandfather's access to it means he was very powerful within the Priory of Sion. He would have had to have been one of the top four members."

Sophie sighed. "He was powerful in a secret society. I'm sure of it. I can only guess it was the Priory."

Langdon was surprised. "You knew he was in a secret society?"

"I saw some things I wasn't supposed to see ten years ago. We haven't spoken since." She paused.

"My grandfather was not only a high-ranking member of the group... I believe he was the highest."

Langdon could not believe what she had just said. "Grand Master? But... there's no way you could know that!"

"I'd rather not talk about it." Sophie looked away, her expression determined and pained. Langdon sat in stunned silence. Jacques Saunière? Grand Master? Despite the shocking implications if it were true, Langdon felt it almost made perfect sense. After all, previous Priory Grand Masters had also been well-known public figures with artistic spirits. Proof of that had been found years ago in Paris's Bibliothèque Nationale in papers known as Les Dossiers Secrets.

Every Priory historian and Grail enthusiast had read the Dossiers. Listed under Number 4° lm1 249, the Dossiers Secrets had been verified by many experts and undeniably confirmed what historians had long suspected: Priory Grand Masters included Leonardo da Vinci, Botticelli, Sir Isaac Newton, Victor Hugo, and, more recently, Jean Cocteau, the famous Parisian artist.

Why not Jacques Saunière?

Langdon's disbelief grew as he realized he was supposed to meet Saunière tonight. The Priory Grand Master called a meeting with me. Why? To chat about art? It suddenly seemed unlikely. After all, if Langdon's instincts were right, the Grand Master of the Priory of Sion had just given the brotherhood's legendary keystone to his granddaughter and ordered her to find Robert Langdon.

Unbelievable!

Langdon couldn't imagine any situation that would explain Saunière's actions. Even if Saunière feared for his life, there were three sénéchaux who also knew the secret and ensured the Priory's safety. Why would Saunière risk so much by giving his granddaughter the keystone, especially since they didn't get along? And why involve Langdon... a complete stranger?

Something is missing, Langdon thought.

The answers would have to wait. The sound of the slowing engine made them both look up. Gravel crunched under the tires. Why is he stopping already? Langdon wondered. Vernet had said he would take them far outside the city to safety. The truck slowed to a crawl and moved over unexpectedly rough ground. Sophie gave Langdon a worried look, quickly closing and latching the cryptex box. Langdon put his jacket back on. When the truck stopped, the engine kept running as the locks on the rear doors began to turn. When the doors opened, Langdon was surprised to see they were parked in a wooded area, far from the road. Vernet stepped into view, looking strained. He held a pistol in his hand.

"I'm sorry about this," he said. "I really can't do anything else."

CHAPTER 49

André Vernet looked clumsy with a pistol, but his eyes showed a determination that Langdon knew better than to challenge.

"I'm afraid I have to insist," Vernet said, pointing the gun at them in the back of the idling truck. "Put the box down."

Sophie held the box tightly. "You said you and my grandfather were friends."

"I have a duty to protect your grandfather's things," Vernet replied. "And that's what I'm doing. Now put the box on the floor."

"My grandfather trusted me with this!" Sophie said.

"Do it," Vernet ordered, raising the gun.

Sophie set the box down.

Langdon watched the gun point toward him.

"Mr. Langdon," Vernet said, "you will bring the box to me. And know that I'm asking you because I would not hesitate to shoot you."

Langdon stared at the banker in disbelief. "Why are you doing this?"

"Why do you think?" Vernet snapped, his accented English now sharp. "To protect my client's assets."

"We are your clients now," Sophie said.

Vernet's face turned ice-cold, an eerie change. "Mademoiselle Neveu, I don't know how you got that key and account number tonight, but it seems clear that something bad happened. If I had known how bad your crimes were, I would never have helped you leave the bank."

"I told you," Sophie said, "we had nothing to do with my grandfather's death!" Vernet looked at Langdon. "And yet the radio says you are wanted not only for the murder of Jacques Saunière but for those of three other men as well?"

"What!" Langdon was stunned. Three more murders? The matching number hit him harder than being the main suspect. It seemed too unlikely to be a coincidence. The three sénéchaux? Langdon's eyes dropped to the rosewood box. If the sénéchaux were murdered, Saunière had no choice. He had to give the keystone to someone.

"The police can figure that out when I turn you in," Vernet said. "I have already involved my bank too much."

Sophie glared at Vernet. "You clearly don't plan to turn us in. You would have driven us back to the bank. Instead, you bring us out here and hold us at gunpoint?"

"Your grandfather hired me for one reason—to keep his things safe and private. Whatever this box contains, I will not let it become evidence in a police investigation. Mr. Langdon, bring me the box."

Sophie shook her head. "Don't do it."

A gunshot blasted, and a bullet ripped into the wall above him. The echo shook the back of the truck as an empty shell pinged onto the floor.

Shit! Langdon froze.

Vernet sounded surer now. "Mr. Langdon, pick up the box." Langdon lifted the box.

"Now bring it to me." Vernet was aiming right at him, standing on the ground behind the bumper, his gun reaching into the truck.

Box in hand, Langdon moved toward the open door.

I've got to do something! Langdon thought. I'm about to hand over the Priory keystone! As Langdon moved toward the doorway, his higher position became clearer, and he wondered if he could use it to help himself. Vernet's gun, though raised, was at Langdon's knee. Could I kick him? But as Langdon got closer, Vernet seemed to notice the danger and stepped back, putting himself six feet away. Out of reach.

Vernet ordered, "Place the box beside the door."

Seeing no choice, Langdon knelt and set the box at the edge of the truck, right in front of the open doors.

"Now stand up."

Langdon started to stand but stopped, spotting the small, empty shell on the floor beside the truck's doorsill.

"Stand up, and step away from the box."

Langdon paused, looking at the metal edge. Then he stood. As he did, he quietly brushed the shell over the edge onto the narrow ledge at the bottom of the door. Now standing straight, Langdon stepped back.

"Return to the back wall and turn around."

Langdon did as he was told.

Vernet could feel his heart beating fast. Holding the gun with his right hand, he reached for the wooden box with his left. It was too heavy. I need two hands. He looked back at his prisoners, judging the risk. They were far away, at the other end of the cargo hold, facing away from him. Vernet decided quickly. He put the gun down on the bumper, lifted the box with both hands, and set it on the ground. Then he immediately grabbed the gun again and pointed it back into the hold. Neither prisoner had moved. Perfect. Now he just had to close and lock the door. Leaving the box on the ground, he grabbed the metal door and started to pull it shut. As the door swung past him, Vernet reached up to grab the bolt that needed to be slid into place. The door closed with a thud, and Vernet quickly grabbed the bolt, pulling it to the left. The bolt slid a little and then stopped suddenly, not lined up correctly. What's going on? Vernet pulled again, but the bolt wouldn't lock. The mechanism was not aligned. The door isn't fully closed! Panicked, Vernet pushed hard against the outside of the door, but it wouldn't move. Something is blocking it! Vernet turned to slam his shoulder into the door, but instead, the door burst open, hitting Vernet in the face and sending him staggering backward onto the ground, his nose breaking. The gun flew as Vernet reached for his face and felt the warm blood running from his nose.

Robert Langdon landed somewhere nearby, and Vernet tried to get up, but he couldn't see clearly. His vision blurred, and he fell backward again. Sophie Neveu was yelling. Moments later, Vernet felt dirt and exhaust blowing over him. He heard tires crunching on gravel and sat up just in time to see the truck's wide tires struggle to make a turn. There was a crash as the front bumper hit a tree. The engine roared, and the tree bent. Finally, the bumper broke, tearing half off. The armored car jerked away, its front bumper dragging. When the truck reached the paved road, sparks flew up into the night, following the truck as it sped away. Vernet looked back at the ground where the truck had been parked. Even in the dim moonlight, he could see that it was empty.

The wooden box was gone.

CHAPTER 50

The plain Fiat sedan leaving Castel Gandolfo wound down through the Alban Hills into the valley below. In the back seat, Bishop Aringarosa smiled, feeling the weight of the bonds in the briefcase on his lap and wondering how long before he and the Teacher could make the exchange.

Twenty million euro.

The sum would buy Aringarosa power worth far more than that.

As his car sped back toward Rome, Aringarosa again wondered why the Teacher had not yet contacted him. Pulling his cell phone from his cassock pocket, he checked the signal. Very weak.

"Cell service is spotty up here," the driver said, glancing at him in the rearview mirror. "In about five minutes, we'll be out of the mountains, and service gets better."

"Thank you." Aringarosa felt a sudden wave of worry. No service in the mountains? Maybe the Teacher had been trying to reach him all this time. Maybe something had gone very wrong. Quickly, Aringarosa checked the phone's voice mail. Nothing. Then again, he realized, the Teacher never would have left a recorded message; he was a man who was extremely careful with his communications. Nobody understood better than the Teacher the dangers of speaking openly in this modern world. Electronic spying had played a big role in how he had gathered his amazing amount of secret knowledge.

For this reason, he takes extra precautions.

Unfortunately, the Teacher's rules for caution included a refusal to give Aringarosa any kind of contact number. I alone will start contact, the Teacher had told him. So keep your phone close. Now that Aringarosa realized his phone might not have been working right, he feared what the Teacher might think if he had been calling repeatedly with no answer. He'll think something is wrong.

Or that I failed to get the bonds.

The bishop started to sweat a little.

Or worse... that I stole the money and ran away!

CHAPTER 51

Even at a slow sixty kilometers an hour, the armored truck's loose front bumper scraped against the empty suburban road with a loud, grinding noise, sending sparks flying onto the hood. We have to get off the road, Langdon thought.

He could hardly see where they were going. The truck's only working headlight was knocked to the side, shining a slanted beam into the woods next to the country highway. It seemed the armor in this "armored truck" only protected the cargo area, not the front.

Sophie sat in the passenger seat, staring blankly at the rosewood box on her lap.

"Are you okay?" Langdon asked.

Sophie looked upset. "Do you believe him?"

"About the three more murders? Definitely. It explains a lot—why your grandfather was so desperate to give away the keystone, and why Fache is so intensely hunting me."

"No, I mean about Vernet trying to protect his bank."

Langdon glanced over. "Instead of what?"

"Taking the keystone for himself."

Langdon hadn't even thought of it. "How could he know what's in this box?"

"His bank kept it. He knew my grandfather. Maybe he knew things. He might have wanted the Grail for himself."

Langdon shook his head. Vernet didn't seem like that kind of person. "I think people look for the Grail for only two reasons. Either they're innocent and think they're finding the Cup of Christ..."

"Or?"

"Or they know the truth and are scared of it. Many groups have tried to destroy the Grail."

The silence made the scraping sound louder. They had driven a few kilometers, and Langdon wondered if the sparks from the truck were dangerous. If another car passed, it would definitely notice. Langdon decided what to do.

"I'm going to try to bend the bumper back."

He pulled the truck onto the side of the road and stopped.

Finally, silence.

As Langdon walked to the front of the truck, he felt awake. Having a gun pointed at him again had given him energy. He took a deep breath of the night air and tried to think clearly. Besides being hunted, Langdon felt a heavy responsibility. He and Sophie might have a coded guide to one of history's biggest mysteries. And now, Langdon realized they couldn't return the keystone to the Priory. The news of the three murders was bad. The Priory has been infiltrated. They are not safe. Someone was watching the brotherhood, or there was a traitor. That explained why Saunière gave the keystone to Sophie and Langdon—people outside the brotherhood, people he knew were safe. We can't give the keystone back to the brotherhood. Even if Langdon knew how to find a Priory member, the person who came to take the keystone could be the enemy. For now, Sophie and Langdon were stuck with the keystone, whether they liked it or not. The front of the truck was worse than Langdon thought. The left headlight was gone, and the right one was hanging loose. Langdon straightened it, but it fell out again. The good news was that the bumper was almost completely torn off. Langdon kicked it hard and thought he could break it off. As he kicked the twisted metal, Langdon remembered his conversation with Sophie. My grandfather left me a phone message, Sophie had said. He said he needed to tell me the truth about my family. It hadn't meant anything then, but now that the Priory of Sion was involved, Langdon saw a new possibility.

The bumper broke off suddenly with a crash. Langdon paused to catch his breath. At least the truck would no longer look like a Fourth of July sparkler. He grabbed the bumper and began dragging it out of sight into the woods, wondering where they should go next. They had no idea how to open the cryptex, or why Saunière had given it to them. Sadly, their survival tonight seemed to depend on getting answers to those very questions.

We need help, Langdon decided. Expert help.

In the world of the Holy Grail and the Priory of Sion, that meant only one man. The problem, of course, would be convincing Sophie.

Inside the armored car, while Sophie waited for Langdon to return, she felt the weight of the rosewood box on her lap and disliked it. Why did my grandfather give this to me? She had no idea what to do with it.

Think, Sophie! Use your head. Grand-père is trying to tell you something!

Opening the box, she looked at the cryptex's dials. A proof of worth. She could feel her grandfather's influence. The keystone is a map that can be followed only by those who deserve it. It sounded just like her grandfather.

Lifting the cryptex out of the box, Sophie ran her fingers over the dials. Five letters. She rotated the dials one by one. The mechanism moved smoothly. She lined up the disks so her chosen letters were between the cryptex's two brass arrows on each end of the cylinder. The dials now spelled a five-letter word that Sophie knew was ridiculously obvious. G-R-A-I-L.

Gently, she held the two ends of the cylinder and pulled, applying pressure slowly. The cryptex didn't move. She heard the vinegar inside gurgle and stopped pulling. Then she tried again. V-I-N-C-I

Again, no movement.

V-O-U-T-E

Nothing. The cryptex stayed firmly locked.

Frowning, she put it back in the rosewood box and closed the lid. Looking at Langdon outside, Sophie was glad he was with her tonight. P.S. Find Robert Langdon. Her grandfather's reason for including him was now obvious. Sophie couldn't understand her grandfather's intentions, so he had chosen Robert Langdon to guide her. A teacher to help her learn. But tonight, Langdon had become much more than a teacher. He was now the target of Bezu Fache... and some hidden power wanting the Holy Grail. Whatever the Grail is.

Sophie wondered if finding out was worth her life.

As the armored truck sped up again, Langdon was happy with how smoothly it drove.

"Do you know how to get to Versailles?"

Sophie looked at him. "Sightseeing?"

"No, I have a plan. I know a religious historian who lives near Versailles. I can't remember exactly where, but we can find it. I've been to his place a few times. His name is Leigh Teabing. He used to be a British Royal Historian."

"And he lives in Paris?"

"Teabing is obsessed with the Grail. When rumors about the Priory keystone started about fifteen years ago, he moved to France to search churches, hoping to find it. He's written books about the keystone and the Grail. He might be able to help us figure out how to open it and what to do with it."

Sophie's eyes showed concern. "Can you trust him?"

"Trust him to do what? Not steal the information?"

"And not to turn us in."

"I don't plan to tell him the police want us. I hope he'll let us stay until we can figure things out."

"Robert, do you realize every TV in France is probably about to show our pictures? Bezu Fache always uses the media. He'll make it impossible for us to go anywhere without being recognized."

Great, Langdon thought. My first time on French TV will be on "Paris's Most Wanted." At least Jonas Faukman would be happy; Langdon's book sales always went up when he was on the news.

"Is this man a good enough friend?" Sophie asked.

Langdon doubted Teabing watched TV, especially this late, but the question was worth thinking about. Langdon's gut told him Teabing was completely trustworthy. A perfect safe place. Given the situation, Teabing would probably bend over backward to help. He not only owed Langdon a favor, but he was a Grail expert, and Sophie said her grandfather was the Grand Master of the Priory of Sion. If Teabing heard that, he would be thrilled to help them solve this.

"Teabing could be a strong ally," Langdon said. Depending on how much you want to tell him.

"Fache will probably offer a reward."

Langdon laughed. "Trust me, money is the last thing he needs." Leigh Teabing was rich like small countries are rich. A descendant of Britain's First Duke of Lancaster, Teabing had gotten his money the old-fashioned way—he'd inherited it. His estate outside Paris was a seventeenth-century palace with two private lakes. Langdon had met Teabing years ago through the British Broadcasting Corporation. Teabing had come to the BBC with an idea for a historical documentary where he would reveal the shocking history of the Holy Grail to a wide TV audience. The BBC

Producers loved Teabing's exciting idea, his research, and his qualifications, but they worried that the concept was so shocking and unbelievable that the network might damage its reputation for good journalism. At Teabing's suggestion, the BBC eased its worries about being credible by getting three respected historians from around the world to make brief appearances, all of whom confirmed the shocking nature of the Holy Grail secret with their own research.

Langdon had been one of those chosen.

The BBC had flown Langdon to Teabing's estate in Paris for the filming. He sat before cameras in Teabing's fancy living room and shared his story, admitting he was initially doubtful when he heard the alternate Holy Grail story, then explaining how years of research had convinced him it was true. Finally, Langdon offered some of his own research—a series of symbolic connections that strongly supported the seemingly controversial claims. When the program was broadcast in Britain, despite its large cast and well-documented evidence, the idea clashed so strongly with popular Christian beliefs that it immediately faced a storm of anger. It never aired in the States, but the effects were felt across the Atlantic. Shortly afterward, Langdon received a postcard from an old friend—the Catholic Bishop of Philadelphia. The card simply read: Et tu, Robert?

"Robert," Sophie asked, "are you sure we can trust this man?"

"Absolutely. We're colleagues, he doesn't need money, and I happen to know he hates the French authorities. The French government taxes him at ridiculous rates because he bought a historic building. He won't be quick to cooperate with Fache."

Sophie stared out at the dark road. "If we go to him, how much do you want to tell him?" Langdon looked calm. "Believe me, Leigh Teabing knows more about the Priory of Sion and the Holy Grail than anyone on earth."

Sophie watched him. "More than my grandfather?"

"I meant more than anyone outside the brotherhood."

"How do you know Teabing isn't a member of the brotherhood?"

"Teabing has spent his life trying to reveal the truth about the Holy Grail. The Priory's oath is to keep its true nature a secret."

"Sounds to me like a conflict of interest."

Langdon understood her worries. Saunière had given the cryptex right to Sophie, and even though she didn't know what was inside or what to do with it, she didn't want to involve a complete stranger. Considering what might be inside, that was probably smart. "We don't have to tell Teabing about the keystone right away. Or ever, even. His house will give us a place to hide and think, and maybe when we talk to him about the Grail, you'll start to understand why your grandfather gave this to you."

"Us," Sophie pointed out.

Langdon felt a quiet pride and wondered again why Saunière had included him.

"Do you know about where Mr. Teabing lives?" Sophie asked.

"His place is called Château Villette."

Sophie turned, looking surprised. "The Château Villette?"

"That's it."

"Nice friends."

"You know the place?"

"I've passed it. It's in the castle area. Twenty minutes from here." Langdon frowned. "That far?"

"Yes, which will give you enough time to tell me what the Holy Grail really is." Langdon paused. "I'll tell you at Teabing's. He and I know different parts of the legend, so together, we'll give you the whole story." Langdon smiled. "Besides, the Grail has been Teabing's life. Hearing about the Holy Grail from Leigh Teabing will be like hearing the theory of relativity from Einstein himself."

"Just so you know, it's Sir Leigh." Langdon had made that mistake only once. "Teabing is quite a guy. The Queen made him a knight a few years ago after he wrote a long history about the House of York."

Sophie looked over. "You're kidding, right? We're going to see a knight?" Langdon gave an awkward smile. "We're on a Grail quest, Sophie. Who better to help us than a knight?"

CHAPTER 52

The large, 185-acre estate of Château Villette was located twenty-five minutes northwest of Paris, near Versailles. Designed by François Mansart in 1668 for the Count of Aufflay, it was one of Paris's most important historical châteaux. With two rectangular lakes and gardens designed by Le Nôtre, Château Villette was more like a small castle than a large house. The estate had become known as la Petite Versailles.

Langdon stopped the armored truck at the start of the mile-long driveway. Beyond the large security gate, Sir Leigh Teabing's home stood in a field in the distance. The sign on the gate was in English: PRIVATE PROPERTY. NO TRESPASSING. As if to declare his home a small piece of Britain, Teabing had not only put up signs in English, but he had also installed the gate's intercom system on the right-hand side of the truck—the passenger's side everywhere in Europe except England.

Sophie looked strangely at the misplaced intercom. "What if someone comes alone?"

"Don't ask." Langdon had already talked about that with Teabing. "He likes things the way they are at home."

Sophie rolled down her window. "Robert, you should do the talking." Langdon moved, leaning over Sophie to press the intercom button. As he did, he smelled Sophie's perfume, and he realized how close they were. He waited there, awkwardly leaning, while a telephone began ringing over the small speaker. Finally, the intercom crackled and an annoyed French voice spoke. "Château Villette. Who is calling?"

"This is Robert Langdon," Langdon called out, leaning across Sophie's lap. "I'm a friend of Sir Leigh Teabing. I need his help."

"My master is sleeping. As was I. What do you want with him?"

"It is a private matter. One he would be very interested in."

"Like Sir Leigh's sleep. If you're a friend, you know he's not well." Sir Leigh Teabing had polio as a child and now wore leg braces and walked with crutches, but Langdon had found him so lively and interesting on his last visit that it barely seemed like a weakness. "If you could, please tell him I've found new information about the Grail. Information that can't wait until morning."

There was a long pause.

Langdon and Sophie waited, the truck idling loudly.

A full minute passed.

Finally, someone spoke. "My good man, I think you're still on Harvard time." The voice was clear and light.

Langdon grinned, recognizing the strong British accent. "Leigh, sorry for waking you so early."

"My servant tells me you're in Paris and talking about the Grail."

"I thought that might get you out of bed."

"And it has."

"Any chance you'd open the gate for an old friend?"

"Those who seek the truth are more than friends. They are brothers." Langdon rolled his eyes at Sophie, used to Teabing's love for dramatic actions.

"I will open the gate," Teabing announced, "but first I must know your heart is true. A test of your honor. You will answer three questions."

Langdon groaned, whispering to Sophie. "Just go along with it. Like I said, he's quite a character."

"Your first question," Teabing said, his voice strong. "Should I serve you coffee, or tea?" Langdon knew Teabing's feelings about Americans drinking coffee. "Tea," he replied.

"Earl Grey."

"Excellent. Your second question. Milk or sugar?"

Langdon paused.

"Milk," Sophie whispered in his ear. "I think the British like milk."

"Milk," Langdon said.

Silence.

"Sugar?"

Teabing didn't answer.

Wait! Langdon remembered the awful tea from before and knew this was a test. "Lemon!" he said. "Earl Grey with lemon."

"Indeed." Teabing seemed very amused. "And now, I must ask the most serious question." Teabing paused and then said solemnly, "When did a Harvard rower last beat an Oxford rower at Henley?"

Langdon had no idea, but he knew why he was being asked. "That has surely never happened."

The gate clicked open. "You are loyal, my friend. You may enter."

CHAPTER 53

"Monsieur Vernet!" The night manager of the Zurich Depository Bank was relieved to hear the bank president's voice. "Where are you? The police are here, everyone's waiting!"

"I have a small problem," the bank president said, sounding worried. "I need your help now."

You have a big problem, the manager thought. The police had surrounded the bank and were threatening to have the DCPJ captain come with the warrant the bank wanted. "How can I help?"

"Armored truck number three. I need to find it."

Confused, the manager checked his schedule. "It's here, downstairs at the loading dock."

"Actually, no. The truck was stolen by the two people the police are looking for."

"What? How did they get out?"

"I can't explain on the phone, but this could be very bad for the bank."

"What do you need me to do?"

"I'd like you to turn on the truck's emergency tracker." The night manager looked at the LoJack control box across the room. Like many armored cars, each of the bank's trucks had a radio-controlled tracking device that could be turned on from the bank. The manager had only used the emergency system once, after a hijacking, and it had worked perfectly, finding the truck and sending its location to the police automatically. But tonight, the manager felt the president wanted him to be more careful. "Sir, if I turn on the LoJack system, it will immediately tell the police we have a problem." Vernet didn't speak for a few seconds. "Yes, I know. Do it anyway. Truck number three. I'll wait. I need the truck's exact location as soon as you have it."

"Right away, sir."

Thirty seconds later, twenty-five miles away, hidden under the armored truck, a small tracker turned on.

CHAPTER 54

As Langdon and Sophie drove the armored truck up the curving, poplar-lined driveway toward the house, Sophie could feel her muscles start to relax. It felt good to be off the road, and she couldn't think of many safer places to catch their breath than this private, gated estate owned by a friendly foreigner.

They turned into the wide circular driveway, and Château Villette came into view on their right. The building was three stories tall and at least sixty meters long, with gray stone walls lit up by spotlights. The rough exterior was a sharp contrast to the perfectly kept gardens and smooth pond.

The lights inside were just turning on.

Instead of driving to the front door, Langdon pulled into a parking area hidden among the evergreens.

The butler led them through a grand marble entrance hall into a beautifully decorated drawing room, softly lit by Victorian lamps with tassels. Against the far wall, between two shining suits of armor, was a rough fireplace big enough to roast an ox. Walking to the fireplace, the butler knelt and lit a pile of oak logs and kindling. A fire quickly crackled to life. The man stood, straightening his jacket. "His master asks that you make yourselves at home." With that, he left, leaving Langdon and Sophie alone.

Sophie wondered which of the antiques by the fire she should sit on—the Renaissance velvet couch, the rustic rocker with eagle claws, or the pair of stone benches that looked like they came from some ancient temple.

Sophie nodded, smiling at the burping sound of the water going through their throats.

"They're gargling," her grandfather told her. "Gargariser! And that's where they get the silly name.

'gargoyles.' " Sophie had never been afraid since then.

Plump and red-faced, Sir Leigh Teabing had thick red hair and cheerful hazel eyes that seemed to sparkle as he spoke. He wore pleated pants and a loose silk shirt under a patterned vest. Teabing looked shocked. "She doesn't know?"

Teabing turned to Sophie, excited. "How much do you know, my dear?" Sophie quickly explained what Langdon had said earlier—the Priory of Sion, the Knights Templar, the Sangreal documents, and the Holy Grail, which many said wasn't a cup... but something much more powerful.

Teabing was already looking intently at Sophie. "You are new to the Grail, my dear. And trust me, you will never forget this."

CHAPTER 55

Sitting on the couch next to Langdon, Sophie drank her tea and ate a scone, feeling the caffeine and food help her feel better. Sir Leigh Teabing was smiling widely as he walked back and forth awkwardly in front of the fireplace, his leg braces clicking on the stone floor.

"The Holy Grail," Teabing said, sounding like he was giving a speech. "Most people only ask me where it is. I'm afraid that's a question I may never answer." He turned and looked right at Sophie. "However... the more important question is this: What is the Holy Grail?"

Sophie felt that both of her male companions were now very interested and eager to learn.

Many have made a business of lies

and fake miracles, fooling the foolish.

—LEONARDO DA VINCI

Oh! Sad humans, open your eyes!

—LEONARDO DA VINCI

The Bible is a product of man, my dear. Not of God. The Bible didn't magically fall from the sky. Man created it as a historical record of difficult times, and it has changed through many translations, additions, and revisions. History has never had one final version of the book."

"Jesus Christ was a historical figure with great influence, maybe the most mysterious and inspiring leader the world has ever seen. Teabing paused to sip his tea and then put the cup back on the mantel. "More than eighty gospels were considered for the New Testament, but only a few were chosen—Matthew, Mark, Luke, and John among them.

"Who chose which gospels to include?" Sophie asked.

"Aha!" Teabing said with enthusiasm. "The basic irony of Christianity! The Bible, as we know it today, was put together by the pagan Roman emperor Constantine the Great."

"I thought Constantine was a Christian," Sophie said.

"Hardly," Teabing scoffed. "He was a pagan his whole life and was only baptized on his deathbed, when he was too weak to argue. In Constantine's time, Rome's official religion was sun worship—the cult of Sol Invictus, or the Invincible Sun—and Constantine was its main priest. But a growing religious problem was taking over Rome. Three centuries after Jesus Christ's death, His followers had grown greatly in number. Christians and pagans started fighting, and the conflict became so big that it threatened to tear Rome apart. Constantine decided something had to be done. In 325 A.D., he decided to unite Rome under one religion: Christianity." Sophie was surprised. "Why would a pagan emperor choose Christianity as the official religion?" Teabing chuckled. "Constantine was a very good businessman. He could see that Christianity was becoming more popular, and he simply supported the winner. Historians still admire how cleverly Constantine converted the sun-worshipping pagans to Christianity. By mixing pagan symbols, dates, and rituals into the growing Christian tradition, he created a kind of combined religion that was acceptable to both groups."

"Transmogrification," Langdon said. "The signs of pagan religion in Christian symbols are clear. Egyptian sun disks became the halos of Catholic saints. Pictures of Isis nursing her miraculously conceived son Horus became the basis for our modern images of the Virgin Mary nursing Baby Jesus. And almost all the parts of the Catholic ritual—the miter, the altar, the doxology, and communion, the act of "God-eating"—were taken directly from earlier pagan mystery religions."

Teabing groaned. "Don't get a symbologist started on Christian icons. Nothing in Christianity is original. The pre-Christian God Mithras—called the Son of God and the Light of the World —was born on December 25, died, was buried in a rock tomb, and then came back to life in three days. By the way, December 25 is also the birthday of Osiris, Adonis, and Dionysus. The newborn Krishna was given gold, frankincense, and myrrh. Even Christianity's weekly holy day was taken from the pagans."

"Originally," Langdon said, "Christianity followed the Jewish Sabbath on Saturday, but Constantine changed it to match the pagans' day of worship for the sun." He paused, grinning. "Even today, most churchgoers go to services on Sunday morning without knowing they are there because of the pagan sun god's weekly tribute— Sun day."

Sophie's head was spinning. "And how does this relate to the Grail?"

"Indeed," Teabing said. "Follow me. During this blending of religions, Constantine needed to reinforce the new Christian tradition, and he held a famous meeting known as the Council of Nicaea."

Sophie had only heard of it as the origin of the Nicene Creed.

"At this meeting," Teabing said, "many parts of Christianity were discussed and voted on—the date of Easter, the role of bishops, the performance of sacraments, and, of course, the divinity of Jesus."

"I don't understand. His divinity?"

"My dear," Teabing said, "until that point in history, Jesus was seen by His followers as a human prophet... a great and powerful man, but still a man. A mortal."

"Not the Son of God?"

"Correct," Teabing said. "Jesus being declared 'the Son of God' was officially suggested and voted on by the Council of Nicaea."

"Wait. You're saying Jesus' divinity was decided by a vote?"

"It was a close vote," Teabing added. "However, declaring Christ divine was key to uniting the Roman empire and building the Vatican's power. By officially calling Jesus the Son of God, Constantine made Jesus a deity beyond human reach, with power that couldn't be questioned. This stopped pagan challenges to Christianity, and now Christ's followers could only be saved through the Church." Sophie looked at Langdon, who nodded slightly in agreement.

"It was all about power," Teabing continued. "Christ as Messiah was vital to the Church and state. Many experts say the early Church stole Jesus from His followers, twisting His human message, hiding it with a thick layer of divinity, and using it to gain power. I've written books about it."

"So, Christians send you hate mail every day?"

"Why would they?" Teabing asked. "Most educated Christians know their faith's history. Jesus was a great man. Constantine's sneaky politics don't lessen Christ's importance. No one says Christ was a fake or denies He lived and inspired people. We're just saying Constantine used Christ's influence. In doing so, he shaped Christianity as we know it."

Sophie glanced at the art book, eager to see the Da Vinci painting of the Holy Grail.

"Here's the twist," Teabing said, talking faster. "Because Constantine upgraded Jesus almost four centuries after His death, many documents already showed His life as a human. To rewrite history, Constantine needed a bold move. This led to the most important moment in Christian history." Teabing paused, looking at Sophie.

"Constantine ordered a new Bible, which left out gospels that showed Christ's human side and emphasized those that made Him seem godlike. The older gospels were banned, collected, and burned."

"An interesting point," Langdon added. "Anyone who chose the banned gospels was called a heretic. The word heretic comes from that time. The Latin word haereticus means 'choice.' Those who 'chose' the original history of Christ were the first heretics."

"Luckily for historians," Teabing said, "some gospels Constantine tried to destroy survived. The Dead Sea Scrolls were found in the 1950s in a cave near Qumran. And the Coptic Scrolls in 1945 at Nag Hammadi. Besides telling the true Grail story, these documents show Christ's ministry in human terms. Of course, the Vatican, as usual, tried to hide these scrolls. Why wouldn't they? The scrolls show historical errors and lies, proving the modern Bible was written and edited by men with a political goal—to promote Jesus' divinity and use His influence to strengthen their power."

"Yet," Langdon said, "it's important to remember that the Church's desire to hide these documents comes from their strong belief in their view of Christ. The Vatican is made of devoted men who think these opposing documents must be false."

"Much more than that. Magdalene's and Sarah's lives were carefully recorded by their Jewish protectors. Remember that Magdalene's child was from the line of Jewish kings—David and Solomon. Because of this, the Jews in France saw Magdalene as sacred royalty and honored her as the beginning of the royal line of kings. Many scholars of that time wrote about Mary Magdalene's life in France, including the birth of Sarah and the family tree that followed." Sophie was surprised. "There's a family tree of Jesus Christ?"

"Yes. And it's said to be a key part of the Sangreal documents. A full family history of Christ's early descendants."

"But what good is a written family history of Christ's bloodline?" Sophie asked. "It's not proof. Historians couldn't confirm if it's real."

Teabing chuckled. "No more than they can confirm if the Bible is real."

"Meaning?"

"Meaning that history is always written by the winners. When two cultures fight, the loser is destroyed, and the winner writes the history books—books that praise their own side and put down the defeated enemy. As Napoleon said, 'What is history, but a story we all agree on?'" He smiled. "History is always a one-sided story." Sophie had never thought of it that way.

"The Sangreal documents simply tell the other side of the Christ story. In the end, what you believe is a matter of faith and personal searching, but at least the information has survived. The Sangreal documents have tens of thousands of pages of information. People who saw the Sangreal treasure say it was carried in four huge trunks. Inside those trunks are said to be the Purist Documents —thousands of pages of original documents from before Constantine, written by Jesus' early followers, who respected Him as a human teacher and prophet. Also said to be part of the treasure is the famous "Q" Document —a manuscript that even the Vatican says they think exists. It's said to be a book of Jesus' teachings, maybe written in His own hand."

"Writings by Christ Himself?"

"Of course," Teabing said. "Why wouldn't Jesus have written about His ministry? Most people did back then. Another shocking document thought to be in the treasure is a manuscript called The Magdalene Diaries —Mary Magdalene's own story of her relationship with Christ, His crucifixion, and her time in France."

Sophie paused. "And these four chests of documents were the treasure that the Knights Templar found under Solomon's Temple?"

"Exactly. The documents that made the Knights so strong. The documents that people have been searching for in countless Grail quests throughout history."

"But you said the Holy Grail was Mary Magdalene. If people are searching for documents, why call it a search for the Holy Grail?"

Teabing looked at her, his face gentler. "Because the hiding place of the Holy Grail includes a sarcophagus."

Outside, the wind howled in the trees.

Teabing spoke more softly now. "The quest for the Holy Grail is really the quest to kneel before the bones of Mary Magdalene. A journey to pray at the feet of the rejected one, the lost sacred feminine."

Sophie felt a sudden sense of awe. "The hiding place of the Holy Grail is actually... a tomb?" Teabing's hazel eyes grew watery. "It is. A tomb holding the body of Mary Magdalene and the documents that tell the true story of her life. Really, the quest for the Holy Grail has always been a quest for the Magdalene—the Queen who was wronged, buried with proof that her family should rightfully be in power."

Sophie waited while Teabing composed himself. So much about her grandfather still didn't make sense. "Members of the Priory," she finally said, "all these years have been responsible for protecting the Sangreal documents and the tomb of Mary Magdalene?"

Yes, but the brotherhood had another, more important job—to protect the bloodline itself. Christ's family was always in danger. The early Church feared that if the family grew, the secret of Jesus and Magdalene would come out and challenge the Catholic belief—that of a divine Messiah who did not have relationships with women or have sex." He paused. "However, Christ's line grew quietly in France until the fifth century, when it married into French royalty and created the Merovingian bloodline."

This surprised Sophie. Every student in France learns about the Merovingians. "The Merovingians founded Paris."

"Yes. That's why the Grail legend is so strong in France. Many of the Vatican's Grail searches here were secret missions to kill members of the royal bloodline. Have you heard of King Dagobert?"

Sophie vaguely remembered the name from a horrible story in history class. "Dagobert was a Merovingian king, right? Stabbed in the eye while sleeping?"

"Exactly. The Vatican and Pepin d'Heristal killed him. Late seventh century. After Dagobert's murder, the Merovingian bloodline was almost wiped out. Luckily, Dagobert's son, Sigisbert, secretly escaped and continued the line, which later included Godefroi de Bouillon—founder of the Priory of Sion."

"The same man," Langdon said, "who told the Knights Templar to get the Sangreal documents from under Solomon's Temple to give the Merovingians proof that they were related to Jesus Christ."

Teabing nodded, sighing deeply. "The Priory of Sion has a very important job. They have three duties. They must protect the Sangreal documents. They must protect Mary Magdalene's tomb. And, of course, they must take care of and protect the bloodline of Christ—the few members of the royal Merovingian bloodline who have survived."

The words filled the room, and Sophie felt a strange feeling, as if her bones were shaking with a new truth. Descendants of Jesus who survived. Her grandfather's voice whispered in her ear again. Princess, I must tell you the truth about your family.

She felt a chill.

Royal blood.

She couldn't believe it.

Princess Sophie.

"Sir Leigh?" The servant's voice buzzed from the intercom on the wall, making Sophie jump. "Could you come to the kitchen for a moment?"

Teabing frowned at the bad timing. He walked to the intercom and pressed the button.

"Rémy, as you know, I'm busy with my guests. If we need anything else from the kitchen tonight, we'll get it ourselves. Thank you, and good night."

"A word before I go to bed, sir. If you would."

Teabing grunted and pressed the button. "Make it quick, Rémy."

"It's a house matter, sir, not for guests to hear." Teabing looked surprised. "And it can't wait until morning?"

"No, sir. My question will only take a minute."

Teabing rolled his eyes and looked at Langdon and Sophie. "Sometimes I wonder who's in charge here?" He pressed the button again. "I'll be right there, Rémy. Can I bring you anything?"

"Just freedom from being controlled, sir."

"Rémy, you know your pepper steak is the only reason I haven't fired you."

"You always say that, sir. You always say that."

CHAPTER 61

Princess Sophie.

Sophie felt empty as she heard Teabing's crutches clicking away down the hall. Numb, she turned to face Langdon in the empty ballroom. He was already shaking his head, as if he knew what she was thinking.

"No, Sophie," he whispered, his eyes gentle. "I thought the same thing when I learned your grandfather was in the Priory, and you said he wanted to tell you a family secret. But it can't be." Langdon paused. "Saunière isn't a Merovingian name." Sophie didn't know if she was relieved or disappointed. Earlier, Langdon had asked a strange question about Sophie's mother's maiden name: Chauvel. Now the question made sense.

"What about Chauvel?" she asked, worried.

He shook his head again. "I'm sorry. I know that would have answered some questions. Only two direct Merovingian lines are left. Their last names are Plantard and Saint-Clair. Both families are in hiding, likely protected by the Priory."

Sophie repeated the names to herself and then shook her head. No one in her family was named Plantard or Saint-Clair. A tired feeling washed over her. She realized she was no closer to understanding what her grandfather wanted to tell her than she had been at the Louvre. Sophie wished her grandfather hadn't mentioned her family. He had reopened old wounds that hurt as much as ever. They are dead, Sophie. They aren't coming back. She thought of her mother singing her to sleep, her father carrying her on his shoulders, and her grandmother and younger brother smiling at her with their bright green eyes. All of that was gone. And all she had left was her grandfather. And now he's gone too. I am alone.

Sophie turned quietly back to The Last Supper and looked at Mary Magdalene's long red hair and calm eyes. There was something in her face that showed the loss of someone loved. Sophie felt it too.

"Robert?" she said softly.

He came closer.

"I know Leigh said the Grail story is all around us, but tonight is the first time I've ever heard any of this."

Langdon looked like he wanted to comfort her with a hand on her shoulder, but he stopped himself.

"You've heard her story before, Sophie. Everyone has. We just don't realize it when we hear it."

"I don't understand."

"The Grail story is everywhere, but it's hidden. When the Church made it forbidden to talk about Mary Magdalene, who they didn't like, her story and importance had to be shared in secret ways... ways that used symbols and metaphors."

"Of course. The arts."

Langdon pointed to The Last Supper. "A perfect example. Some of today's most famous art, books, and music secretly tell the story of Mary Magdalene and Jesus." Langdon quickly told her about works by Da Vinci, Botticelli, Poussin, Bernini, Mozart, and Victor Hugo that all hinted at the quest to bring back respect for the sacred feminine. Popular stories like Sir Gawain and the Green Knight, King Arthur, and Sleeping Beauty were Grail stories in disguise. Victor Hugo's Hunchback of Notre Dame and Mozart's Magic Flute were full of Masonic symbols and Grail secrets.

"Once you understand the Holy Grail," Langdon said, "you see it everywhere. Paintings. Music. Books. Even in cartoons, theme parks, and popular movies."

Langdon held up his Mickey Mouse watch and told her that Walt Disney had quietly dedicated his life to passing on the Grail story to future generations. Throughout his life, Disney had been called "the Modern-Day Leonardo da Vinci." Both men were ahead of their time, uniquely talented artists, members of secret groups, and, most importantly, big jokers. Like Leonardo, Walt Disney loved putting hidden messages and symbols in his art. For someone who studies symbols, watching an early Disney movie was like being hit by a flood of hints and metaphors.

Most of Disney's hidden messages were about religion, old myths, and stories of the mistreated goddess. It was no accident that Disney retold tales like Cinderella, Sleeping Beauty, and Snow White — all of which were about the imprisonment of the sacred feminine. You didn't need to know a lot about symbols to see that Snow White—a princess who fell from grace after eating a poisoned apple—was a clear reference to Eve's downfall in the Garden of Eden. Or that Sleeping Beauty's Princess Aurora—called "Rose" and hidden in the forest to protect her from the evil witch—was the Grail story for kids. Despite its corporate image, Disney still had clever, playful employees, and their artists still enjoyed adding hidden symbols to Disney products. Langdon would never forget one of his students bringing in a Lion King DVD and pausing it to show a frame where the word SEX was clearly visible, spelled out by dust particles over Simba's head. Langdon thought this was probably just a childish prank by a cartoonist rather than a smart reference to pagan human sexuality, but he had learned not to underestimate Disney's understanding of symbols. The Little Mermaid was a beautiful collection of spiritual symbols so clearly related to the goddess that it couldn't be a coincidence. When Langdon first saw The Little Mermaid, he gasped when he noticed that the painting in Ariel's underwater home was Georges de la Tour's The Penitent Magdalene — a famous tribute to Mary Magdalene — fitting decor since the movie was a collection of obvious symbolic references to the lost importance of Isis, Eve, Pisces the fish goddess, and, often, Mary Magdalene. The Little Mermaid's name, Ariel, was strongly connected to the sacred feminine and, in the Book of Isaiah, meant "the Holy City under attack." And the Little Mermaid's flowing red hair was certainly not a coincidence.

The clicking of Teabing's crutches came closer in the hallway, his pace faster than usual. When their host entered the study, he looked stern.

"You need to explain, Robert," he said coldly. "You haven't been honest with me."

CHAPTER 62

"I'm being set up, Leigh," Langdon said, trying to stay calm. You know me. I wouldn't kill anyone. Teabing's tone did not soften. "Robert, you're on television, for God's sake. Did you know the police were looking for you?"

"Yes."

"Then you betrayed my trust. I'm shocked you would endanger me by coming here and asking me to talk about the Grail so you could hide in my house."

The butler stepped in now. "Leave, or I will call the police."

Teabing seemed to lose his balance slightly.

Rémy walked stiffly across the room. "Leave now! Or I will make you—"

After a moment of stunned silence, Rémy slunk out like a scolded dog. In the cool night air coming through the open doors, Teabing turned back to Sophie and Langdon, still looking cautious. "This better be good. What do you know about the keystone?"

In the thick bushes outside Teabing's study, Silas gripped his pistol and looked through the glass doors. Just moments before, he had walked around the house and seen Langdon and the woman talking in the large study. Before he could go in, a man on crutches came in, yelled at Langdon, threw open the doors, and demanded that his guests leave. Then the woman mentioned the keystone, and everything changed. Shouting turned to whispers. Tempers calmed. And the glass doors were quickly shut.

Now, as he hid in the shadows, Silas peered through the glass. The keystone is somewhere inside the house. Silas could feel it.

Staying hidden, he moved closer to the glass, wanting to hear what was being said. He would give them five minutes. If they didn't say where the keystone was, Silas would have to go in and force them to tell him.

Inside the study, Langdon could feel their host's confusion.

"Grand Master?" Teabing gasped, looking at Sophie. "Jacques Saunière?" Sophie nodded, seeing his shock.

"But you can't possibly know that!"

"Jacques Saunière was my grandfather."

Teabing stumbled back on his crutches, glancing at Langdon, who nodded. Teabing turned back to Sophie. "Miss Neveu, I'm speechless. If this is true, I'm very sorry for your loss. I must admit, for my research, I've kept lists of men in Paris who I thought might be good candidates for the Priory. Jacques Saunière was on that list with many others. But Grand Master, you say? Even if your grandfather was the Priory Grand Master and made the keystone himself, he would never tell you how to find it. The keystone reveals the way to the brotherhood's greatest treasure. Granddaughter or not, you're not allowed to have that knowledge."

"He didn't need choices," Teabing argued. "There are three sénéchaux who also know the secret. That's the beauty of their system. One will become Grand Master, and they'll choose a new sénéchal and share the keystone's secret."

Teabing's jaw dropped. "And you think they were..."
