CHAPTER 63

Lieutenant Collet stood alone at the bottom of Leigh Teabing's driveway, looking up at the huge house. Alone. Dark. Lots of hiding places. Collet watched his six agents quietly spread out along the fence. They could easily climb over it and surround the house in minutes. Langdon couldn't have picked a better place for Collet's men to make a surprise attack.

Collet was about to call Fache when his phone finally rang.

Fache didn't sound as happy about the news as Collet expected. "Why didn't anyone tell me we had a lead on Langdon?"

"You were on a call and—"

"Where are you exactly, Lieutenant Collet?"

Collet gave him the address. "The property belongs to a British man named Teabing. Langdon drove quite a distance to get here, and the car is inside the gate. There's no sign of a break-in, so Langdon probably knows the owner."

"I'm coming there," Fache said. "Don't do anything. I'll take care of this myself." Collet was shocked. "But Captain, you're twenty minutes away! We should move now. I have him surrounded. I'm here with eight men. Four have rifles, and the others have guns."

"Wait for me."

"Captain, what if Langdon has a hostage inside? What if he sees us and tries to escape on foot?"

"We need to move now! My men are ready and waiting."

"Lieutenant Collet, wait for me to get there before you do anything. That's an order." Fache hung up.

Stunned, Lieutenant Collet turned off his phone. Why does Fache want me to wait? Collet knew the answer. Fache was known for his instinct, but even more for his pride. Fache wants the credit for the arrest. He put the American's face on TV, so he wanted his own face to get attention too. Collet's job was just to hold things steady until the boss arrived to save the day.

As he stood there, Collet thought of another reason for the delay. Damage control. In law enforcement, the only time you wait to arrest a fugitive is when you're not sure if they're guilty. Is Fache wondering if Langdon is the right guy? The thought scared him. Captain Fache had gone all out to arrest Robert Langdon—secret surveillance, Interpol, and now TV. Even the great Bezu Fache wouldn't survive the political mess if he wrongly put a famous American's face on French TV, calling him a murderer. If Fache now realized he'd made a mistake, it made sense that he would tell Collet to wait. The last thing Fache needed was for Collet to storm an innocent Brit's property and arrest Langdon at gunpoint. Also, Collet realized, if Langdon was innocent, it explained one of the case's weirdest puzzles: Why had Sophie Neveu, the victim's granddaughter, helped the accused killer escape? Unless Sophie knew Langdon was wrongly accused. Fache had offered many reasons for Sophie's strange behavior, including that Sophie, as Saunière's only heir, had convinced her secret lover Robert Langdon to kill Saunière for the inheritance money. If Saunière suspected this, he might have left the police the message P.S. Find Robert Langdon. Collet was pretty sure something else was happening. Sophie Neveu seemed too decent to be involved in something so awful.

"Lieutenant?" A field agent ran over. "We found a car." Collet followed the agent about fifty yards past the driveway. The agent pointed to a wide shoulder on the other side of the road. There, parked in the bushes, almost hidden, was a black Audi. It had rental plates. Collet felt the hood. Still warm. Even hot.

"That must be how Langdon got here," Collet said. "Call the rental company. See if it's stolen."

"Yes, sir."

Another agent waved Collet back toward the fence. "Lieutenant, look at this." He handed Collet night vision binoculars. "The trees near the top of the driveway."

Collet aimed the binoculars up the hill and adjusted the focus. Slowly, the green shapes became clear. He found the curve of the driveway and followed it up to the trees. He could only stare. There, hidden in the trees, was an armored truck. A truck just like the one Collet had allowed to leave the Depository Bank of Zurich earlier that night. He hoped it was a strange coincidence, but he knew it wasn't.

"It seems clear," the agent said, "that Langdon and Neveu used this truck to escape from the bank."

Collet was speechless. He thought of the armored truck driver he had stopped at the roadblock. The Rolex. His eagerness to leave. I never checked the cargo hold.

In disbelief, Collet realized that someone in the bank had actually lied to DCPJ about where Langdon and Sophie were and then helped them escape. But who? And why? Collet wondered if maybe this was why Fache had told him not to act yet. Maybe Fache knew there were more people involved tonight than just Langdon and Sophie. And if Langdon and Neveu arrived in the armored truck, then who drove the Audi?

Hundreds of miles to the south, a private Beechcraft Baron 58 sped north over the Tyrrhenian Sea. Despite calm skies, Bishop Aringarosa held an airsickness bag, sure he would be sick soon. His conversation with Paris had not gone as he had hoped. Alone in the small cabin, Aringarosa twisted the gold ring on his finger and tried to calm his great fear and despair. Everything in Paris has gone terribly wrong. Closing his eyes, Aringarosa prayed that Bezu Fache would be able to fix it.

CHAPTER 64

Teabing sat on the divan, holding the wooden box on his lap and admiring the lid's detailed inlaid Rose. Tonight is the strangest and most magical night of my life.

"Lift the lid," Sophie whispered, standing over him, beside Langdon. Teabing smiled. Don't rush me. After searching for this keystone for over a decade, he wanted to savor every moment. He ran his palm across the wooden lid, feeling the texture of the inlaid flower.

"The Rose," he whispered. The Rose is Magdalene is the Holy Grail. The Rose is the compass that guides the way. Teabing felt foolish. For years he had traveled to cathedrals and churches all over France, paying for special access, examining hundreds of archways beneath rose windows, searching for an encrypted keystone. La clef de voûte — a stone key beneath the sign of the Rose. Teabing slowly unlatched the lid and raised it.

As his eyes finally saw the contents, he knew instantly it had to be the keystone. He was staring at a stone cylinder, made of interconnecting lettered dials. The device seemed surprisingly familiar to him.

"Designed from Da Vinci's diaries," Sophie said. "My grandfather made them as a hobby." Of course, Teabing realized. He had seen the sketches and blueprints. The key to finding the Holy Grail lies inside this stone. Teabing lifted the heavy cryptex from the box, holding it gently. Although he had no idea how to open the cylinder, he sensed his own destiny lay inside. In moments of failure, Teabing had questioned whether his life's quest would ever be rewarded. Now those doubts were gone forever. He could hear the ancient words... the foundation of the Grail legend:

Vous ne trouvez pas le Saint-Graal, c'est le Saint-Graal qui vous trouve. You do not find the Grail, the Grail finds you.

And tonight, incredibly, the key to finding the Holy Grail had walked right through his front door.

While Sophie and Teabing sat with the cryptex and talked about the vinegar, the dials, and what the password might be, Langdon carried the rosewood box across the room to a well-lit table to get a better look at it. Something Teabing had just said was now running through Langdon's mind. The key to the Grail is hidden beneath the sign of the Rose.

Langdon held the wooden box up to the light and examined the inlaid symbol of the Rose. Although his knowledge of art did not include woodworking or inlaid furniture, he had just recalled the famous tiled ceiling of the Spanish monastery outside of Madrid, where, three centuries after it was built, the ceiling tiles began to fall out, revealing sacred texts scrawled by monks on the plaster beneath.

Langdon looked again at the Rose.

Beneath the Rose.

Sub Rosa.

Secret.

A noise in the hall behind him made Langdon turn. He saw only shadows. It was likely Teabing's servant. Langdon turned back to the box. He ran his finger over the smooth edge, wondering if he could remove the Rose, but it was too well-made. He doubted even a razor could fit between the Rose and the carved space it sat in.

Opening the box, he looked at the inside of the lid. It was smooth. But as he moved it, he saw what looked like a small hole in the center of the lid. Langdon closed the lid and looked at the Rose from above. No hole. It doesn't go all the way through.

Setting the box on the table, he saw some papers with a paper clip. Taking the clip, he went back to the box, opened it, and looked at the hole again. Carefully, he straightened the paper clip and put one end in the hole. He pushed gently. It was easy. He heard something quietly fall onto the table. Langdon closed the lid to see. It was a small piece of wood, like a puzzle piece. The Rose had come out of the lid and fallen onto the desk.

Without a word, Langdon stared at the empty spot on the lid where the Rose had been. There, carved in the wood in neat writing, were four lines of text in a language he had never seen. The letters look a little like Semitic languages, Langdon thought, but I don't know the language!

A sudden movement behind him caught his attention. Unexpectedly, a hard hit to the head knocked Langdon to his knees.

As he fell, he thought he saw a pale figure standing over him, holding a gun. Then everything went black.

CHAPTER 65

Sophie Neveu, despite working in law enforcement, had never had a gun pointed at her until tonight. It was almost unbelievable that the gun she was now staring at was held in the pale hand of a huge albino with long white hair. He looked at her with red eyes that seemed scary and unreal. He was wearing a wool robe tied with a rope, like a priest from the Middle Ages. Sophie had no idea who he was, but she suddenly felt that Teabing's idea that the Church was behind this might be right.

"You know why I'm here," the monk said, his voice empty. Sophie and Teabing were sitting on the couch, arms raised as the attacker had told them. Langdon was on the floor, moaning. The monk's eyes went straight to the keystone on Teabing's lap.

Teabing's voice was strong. "You won't be able to open it."

"My Teacher is very smart," the monk replied, moving closer, the gun moving between Teabing and Sophie.

Sophie wondered where Teabing's servant was. Didn't he hear Robert fall?

"Who is your teacher?" Teabing asked. "Maybe we can pay you off."

"The Grail is worth more than money." He came closer.

"You're bleeding," Teabing said calmly, pointing to the monk's right ankle where blood was running down his leg. "And you're limping."

"So are you," the monk replied, pointing to the metal crutches next to Teabing. "Now, give me the keystone."

"You know about the keystone?" Teabing asked, sounding surprised.

"Never mind what I know. Stand up slowly, and give it to me."

"Standing is hard for me."

"Exactly. I don't want anyone making sudden moves."

Teabing slid his right hand through one of his crutches and grabbed the keystone in his left. Stumbling to his feet, he stood up straight, holding the heavy cylinder in his left hand, and leaning unsteadily on his crutch with his right.

The monk came within a few feet, keeping the gun aimed right at Teabing's head. Sophie watched, feeling helpless as the monk reached out to take the cylinder.

"You will not succeed," Teabing said. "Only the worthy can unlock this stone."

God alone judges who is worthy, Silas thought.

"It's quite heavy," the man on crutches said, his arm shaking now. "If you don't take it soon, I'm afraid I'll drop it!" He swayed dangerously.

Silas quickly stepped forward to take the stone, and as he did, the man on crutches lost his balance. The crutch slid out from under him, and he started to fall sideways to his right. No! Silas reached out to save the stone, lowering his weapon as he did. But the keystone was moving away from him now. As the man fell to his right, his left hand swung backward, and the cylinder fell from his palm onto the couch. At the same moment, the metal crutch that had been sliding out from under the man seemed to speed up, swinging in a wide arc through the air toward Silas's leg. Sharp pain shot through Silas's body as the crutch hit his cilice perfectly, crushing the barbs into his already raw flesh. Collapsing, Silas crumpled to his knees, causing the belt to cut even deeper. The pistol fired with a loud roar, the bullet hitting harmlessly in the floorboards as Silas fell. Before he could raise the gun and fire again, the woman's foot hit him right under the jaw.

At the bottom of the driveway, Collet heard the gunshot. The muffled pop sent panic through his veins. With Fache on the way, Collet had already given up any hope of getting personal praise for finding Langdon tonight. But Collet would be damned if Fache's ego got him in front of a Ministerial Review Board for careless police work.

A weapon was fired inside a private home! And you waited at the bottom of the driveway?

Collet knew the chance for a quiet approach was long gone. He also knew if he stood still for another second, his whole career would be over by morning. Looking at the estate's iron gate, he made his decision.

"Tie on, and pull it down."

In the distant corners of his foggy mind, Robert Langdon had heard the gunshot. He'd also heard a scream of pain. Was it his? A jackhammer was drilling a hole into the back of his head. Somewhere nearby, people were talking.

"Where the devil were you?" Teabing was yelling.

The manservant hurried in. "What happened? Oh my God! Who is that? I'll call the police!"

"Bloody hell! Don't call the police. Make yourself useful and get us something to tie up this monster."

"And some ice!" Sophie called after him.

Langdon drifted off again. More voices. Movement. Now he was sitting on the divan. Sophie was holding an ice pack to his head. His skull ached. As Langdon's vision finally started to clear, he found himself staring at a body on the floor. Am I imagining things? The large body of an albino monk lay tied up and gagged with duct tape. His chin was split open, and the robe over his right thigh was soaked with blood. He too seemed to be just now waking up.

Langdon turned to Sophie. "Who is that? What... happened?"

Teabing hobbled over. "You were rescued by a knight with an Excalibur made by Acme Orthopedic."

Huh? Langdon tried to sit up.

Sophie's touch was shaky but gentle. "Just take a moment, Robert."

"I'm afraid," Teabing said, "that I've just shown your friend the upside of my disability. It seems everyone underestimates you."

From the couch, Langdon looked down at the monk and tried to picture what had happened.

"He was wearing a cilice," Teabing explained.

"A what?"

Teabing pointed to a bloody strip of barbed leather on the floor. "A Discipline belt. He wore it on his thigh. I aimed carefully."

Langdon rubbed his head. He knew about Discipline belts. "But how... did you know?" Teabing grinned. "Christianity is what I study, Robert, and some groups are very open about their beliefs." He pointed his crutch at the blood soaking the monk's cloak.

"As it were."

"Opus Dei," Langdon whispered, remembering recent news stories about several well-known Boston businessmen who belonged to Opus Dei. Worried coworkers had wrongly and publicly claimed the men wore Discipline belts under their suits. But the three men did not. Like many in Opus Dei, these men were at the

"supernumerary" level and didn't practice any physical self-punishment. They were devoted Catholics, loving fathers, and active members of their community. The media quickly moved past their faith to focus on the shocking practices of the stricter "numerary" members... like the monk on the floor.

Teabing examined the bloody belt. "Why would Opus Dei want the Holy Grail?"

Langdon was too out of it to think about it.

"Robert," Sophie said, by the wooden box. "What's this?" She held the small Rose inlay he took from the lid.

"It hid writing on the box. It might tell us how to open the keystone." Before Sophie and Teabing could speak, a wave of blue police lights and sirens appeared at the bottom of the hill and moved up the long driveway.

Teabing frowned. "Friends, we have a choice to make, and quickly."

CHAPTER 66

Collet and his agents kicked open the front door of Sir Leigh Teabing's estate, guns ready. Spreading out, they searched every room on the first floor. They found a bullet hole in the living room floor, signs of a fight, a little blood, a strange, spiky leather belt, and a roll of duct tape that had been partly used. The whole floor seemed empty. Just as Collet was about to split up his men to check the basement and the land behind the house, he heard voices from upstairs.

"They're upstairs!"

Charging up the wide stairs, Collet and his men went from room to room in the huge house, making sure dark bedrooms and hallways were safe as they got closer to the voices. The sound seemed to be coming from the last bedroom at the end of a very long hallway. The agents crept down the hall, blocking off other ways out.

As they got close to the last bedroom, Collet saw the door was wide open. The voices had stopped without warning, replaced by a strange rumbling, like an engine. Gun raised, Collet gave the signal. Silently reaching around the door frame, he flipped on the light switch. He spun into the room with men right behind him, yelling and pointing his gun at... nothing.

An empty guest bedroom. Spotless.

The rumbling sound of a car engine came from a black electronic panel on the wall next to the bed. Collet had seen these in other parts of the house. It was some kind of intercom. He hurried over to it. The panel had about a dozen buttons with labels:

STUDY... KITCHEN... LAUNDRY... CELLAR...

So where the hell do I hear a car?

MASTER BEDROOM... SUN ROOM... BARN... LIBRARY...

Barn! Collet was downstairs in seconds, running to the back door, grabbing an agent on the way. They crossed the back lawn and arrived, out of breath, at the front of a weathered gray barn. Even before entering, Collet heard the fading sounds of a car engine. He drew his weapon, rushed in, and flicked on the lights.

The right side of the barn was a simple workshop—lawnmowers, car tools, gardening supplies. A familiar intercom panel hung on the nearby wall. One of its buttons was pressed down, transmitting.

GUEST BEDROOM II.

Collet turned, anger rising. They lured us upstairs with the intercom! Searching the other side of the barn, he found a long row of horse stalls. No horses. Apparently the owner preferred a different kind of horsepower; the stalls had been turned into an impressive car parking area. The collection was amazing—a black Ferrari, a perfect Rolls-Royce, an old Astin Martin sports car, a vintage Porsche 356.

The last stall was empty.

Collet ran over and saw oil stains on the stall floor. They can't leave the area. The driveway and gate were blocked by two patrol cars to prevent this.

"Sir?" The agent pointed down the stalls.

The barn's back slider was wide open, leading to a dark, muddy slope of rough fields stretching into the night behind the barn. Collet ran to the door, trying to see into the darkness. He could only make out the faint shadow of a forest in the distance. No headlights. The wooded valley was probably full of unmapped fire roads and hunting trails, but Collet was sure they wouldn't make it to the woods. "Get some men spread out down there. They're probably stuck nearby. These fancy sports cars can't handle that kind of ground."

"Um, sir?" The agent pointed to a pegboard with several sets of keys. The labels above the keys had familiar names.

DAIMLER... ROLLS-ROYCE... ASTIN MARTIN... PORSCHE...

The last hook was empty.

When Collet read the label above the empty hook, he knew he was in trouble.

CHAPTER 67

The Range Rover was Java Black Pearl, four-wheel drive, standard transmission, with strong plastic lamps, rear light fittings, and the steering wheel on the right. Langdon was glad he wasn't driving.

Teabing's servant Rémy, following his master's orders, was skillfully driving the vehicle across the moonlit fields behind Château Villette. Without headlights, he had crossed an open hill and was now going down a long slope, moving farther from the estate. He seemed to be going toward a rough outline of wooded land in the distance. Langdon, holding the keystone, turned in the passenger seat and looked at Teabing and Sophie in the back seat.

"How's your head, Robert?" Sophie asked, sounding worried.

Langdon forced a weak smile. "Better, thanks." It was really hurting. Beside her, Teabing glanced at the tied and gagged monk lying in the small luggage area behind the back seat. Teabing had the monk's gun on his lap and looked like an old photo of a British safari hunter posing with his kill.

"So glad you came by this evening, Robert," Teabing said, grinning as if he were enjoying himself for the first time in years.

"Sorry to get you involved in this, Leigh."

"Oh, please, I've waited my whole life to be involved." Teabing looked past Langdon out the windshield at the shadow of a long hedgerow. He tapped Rémy on the shoulder from behind.

"Remember, no brake lights. Use the emergency brake if you need it. I want to get into the woods a bit. No need to risk them seeing us from the house."

Rémy slowed to a crawl and guided the Range Rover through an opening in the hedge. As the vehicle bumped onto an overgrown path, the trees overhead quickly blocked out the moonlight.

I can't see a thing, Langdon thought, trying to make out any shapes at all in front of them. It was completely dark. Branches scraped against the left side of the car, and Rémy steered in the other direction. Keeping the wheel mostly straight, he moved forward about thirty yards.

"You're doing great, Rémy," Teabing said. "That should be far enough. Robert, if you could push that little blue button just below the vent there. See it?"

Langdon found the button and pushed it.

A soft yellow light spread across the path in front of them, showing thick bushes on either side of the trail. Fog lights, Langdon realized. They gave off just enough light to stay on the path, but they were far enough into the woods that the lights wouldn't give them away.

"Well, Rémy," Teabing said cheerfully. "The lights are on. Our lives are in your hands."

"Where are we going?" Sophie asked.

"This trail goes on for about three kilometers into the forest," Teabing said. "Cutting across the estate and then curving north. If we don't hit any puddles or fallen trees, we'll come out safely on the side of highway five."

Safely. Langdon's head disagreed. He looked down at his lap, where the keystone was safely stored in its wooden box. The inlaid Rose on the lid was back in place, and even though his head felt confused, Langdon was eager to remove the inlay again and look at the engraving underneath more closely. He unlatched the lid and started to lift it when Teabing put a hand on his shoulder from behind.

"Patience, Robert," Teabing said. "It's bumpy and dark. God help us if we break anything. If you didn't recognize the language in the light, you won't do any better in the dark. Let's focus on getting away safely, okay? There will be time for that very soon." Langdon knew Teabing was right. He nodded and relatched the box.

The monk in back was moaning now, struggling against his ropes. Suddenly, he began kicking wildly.

Teabing spun around and aimed the pistol over the seat. "I don't understand your complaint, sir. You broke into my home and left a nasty bump on a dear friend's head. I have every right to shoot you right now and leave you in the woods." The monk went silent.

"Are you sure we should have brought him?" Langdon asked.

"Absolutely!" Teabing exclaimed. "You're wanted for murder, Robert. This man is your way to freedom. The police clearly want you badly enough to have followed you to my home."

"My fault," Sophie said. "The armored car probably had a tracker."

"That's not the point," Teabing said. "I'm not surprised the police found you, but I am surprised that this Opus Dei man found you. From what you've told me, I don't see how this man could have followed you to my home unless he had someone on the inside, either in the Judicial Police or in the Zurich Depository."

Langdon thought about it. Bezu Fache seemed determined to find someone to blame for tonight's murders. And Vernet had turned on them quickly, although since Langdon was being accused of four murders, the banker's change of heart seemed understandable.

"This monk isn't working alone, Robert," Teabing said, "and until you know who is behind this, you're both in danger. The good news, my friend, is that you now have the upper hand. This monster behind me has that information, and whoever is controlling him must be quite nervous right now."

Rémy was speeding up, getting used to the trail. They splashed through some water, climbed a small hill, and started going down again.

"Robert, could you hand me that phone?" Teabing pointed to the car phone on the dash. Langdon gave it to him, and Teabing dialed a number. He waited a long time before someone answered. "Richard? Did I wake you? Of course, I did. Stupid question. I'm sorry. I have a small problem. I'm not feeling well. Rémy and I need to go to the Isles for my treatments. Right away, actually. Sorry for the short notice. Can you have Elizabeth ready in about twenty minutes? I know, just do your best. See you soon." He hung up.

"Elizabeth?" Langdon said.

"My plane. She cost me a fortune."

Langdon turned around and looked at him.

"What?" Teabing demanded. "You two can't expect to stay in France with the entire police force after you. London will be much safer."

Sophie had turned to Teabing as well. "You think we should leave the country?"

"My friends, I have more influence in the world than here in France. Besides, the Grail is thought to be in Great Britain. If we unlock the keystone, I'm sure we'll find a map that shows we're on the right track."

"You're taking a big risk," Sophie said, "by helping us. You won't make friends with the French police."

Teabing waved his hand in disgust. "I'm done with France. I moved here to find the keystone. That's finished now. I don't care if I ever see Château Villette again." Sophie sounded unsure. "How will we get through airport security?" Teabing chuckled. "I fly from Le Bourget—a private airfield near here. French doctors make me nervous, so every two weeks, I fly to England for my treatments. I pay for special treatment at both airports. Once we're in the air, you can decide if you want someone from the U.S. Embassy to meet us."

Langdon suddenly didn't want anything to do with the embassy. All he could think about was the keystone, the inscription, and whether it would all lead to the Grail. He wondered if Teabing was right about Britain. Most modern stories placed the Grail in the United Kingdom. Even King Arthur's mythical Isle of Avalon, full of Grail legends, was now thought to be Glastonbury, England. Wherever the Grail was, Langdon never thought he would be looking for it. The Sangreal documents. The real story of Jesus Christ. The tomb of Mary Magdalene. He felt like he was in some kind of dream tonight... a bubble where the real world couldn't reach him.

"Sir?" Rémy said. "Are you really thinking of moving back to England for good?"

"Rémy, don't worry," Teabing said. "Just because I'm returning to England doesn't mean I'm going to eat sausages and mashed potatoes every day. I expect you'll join me there permanently. I'm planning to buy a beautiful house in Devonshire, and we'll have all your things sent there right away. An adventure, Rémy. I say, an adventure!" Langdon smiled. As Teabing talked excitedly about his plans to return to Britain, Langdon felt caught up in the man's contagious enthusiasm.

Looking blankly out the window, Langdon watched the woods go by, pale and ghostly in the yellow glow of the fog lights. The side mirror was turned in, bumped out of place by branches, and Langdon saw Sophie's reflection in the back seat, sitting quietly. He watched her for a while and felt a surprising sense of peace. Despite his problems tonight, Langdon was grateful to have ended up with such good company.

After a few minutes, as if she felt him watching her, Sophie leaned forward and put her hands on his shoulders, quickly rubbing them. "You okay?"

"Yeah," Langdon said. "Somehow."

Sophie sat back in her seat, and Langdon saw a small smile appear on her face. He realized he was smiling too.

Squeezed into the back of the Range Rover, Silas could hardly breathe. His arms were pulled back and tightly tied to his ankles with kitchen twine and duct tape. Every bump in the road sent pain through his twisted shoulders. At least his captors had taken off the cilice. Unable to breathe in through the tape over his mouth, he could only breathe through his nose, which was slowly getting blocked by the dusty cargo area he was crammed into. He started coughing.

"I think he's choking," the French driver said, sounding worried. The British man who had hit Silas with his crutch turned and looked over the seat, frowning coldly at Silas. "Luckily for you, we British measure a man's decency not by how he treats his friends, but how he treats his enemies." The Brit reached down and grabbed the duct tape on Silas's mouth. In one quick motion, he ripped it off. Silas felt like his lips were on fire, but the air rushing into his lungs was a blessing.

"Who do you work for?" the British man asked.

"I do God's work," Silas replied, despite the pain in his jaw where the woman had kicked him.

"You belong to Opus Dei," the man said. It wasn't a question.

"You know nothing about me."

"Why does Opus Dei want the keystone?"

Silas didn't plan to answer. The keystone led to the Holy Grail, and the Holy Grail was vital for protecting the faith.

I do God's work. The Way is in danger.

Now, in the Range Rover, struggling against his restraints, Silas worried he had failed the Teacher and the bishop for good. He couldn't even reach them to tell them what had happened. My captors have the keystone! They'll find the Grail before us! In the stuffy darkness, Silas prayed. He used his physical pain to strengthen his prayers.

A miracle, Lord. I need a miracle. Silas didn't know that in a few hours, he would get one.

"Robert?" Sophie was still watching him. "You just had a strange look on your face." Langdon looked at her, realizing his jaw was tight and his heart was pounding. He had just had an amazing idea. Could the explanation really be that simple? "I need to use your cell phone, Sophie."

"Now?"

"I think I just figured something out."

"What?"

"I'll tell you in a minute. I need your phone."

Sophie seemed cautious. "I doubt Fache is tracking, but keep it under a minute just in case." She handed him her phone.

"How do I call the States?"

"You need to call collect. My plan doesn't cover overseas." Langdon dialed zero, knowing that the next minute might solve a mystery that had confused him all night.

CHAPTER 68

New York editor Jonas Faukman had just gotten into bed when the phone rang. A bit late for calls, he muttered, picking up the phone. An operator asked, "Will you accept a collect call from Robert Langdon?" Confused, Jonas turned on the light. "Uh... sure, okay."

The line clicked. "Jonas?"

"Robert? You wake me up and make me pay for it?"

"Jonas, I'm sorry," Langdon said. "I'll be quick. I need to know. The manuscript I gave you. Have you—"

"Robert, I know I said I'd send you the edits this week, but I'm too busy. Next Monday, I promise."

"I'm not worried about the edits. I need to know if you sent out any copies for reviews without telling me?"

Faukman hesitated. Langdon's latest manuscript—about the history of goddess worship—had some parts about Mary Magdalene that would cause a stir. Although it was well researched and others had written about it, Faukman didn't want to print early copies of Langdon's book without endorsements from respected historians and art experts. Jonas had picked ten famous people in the art world and sent them parts of the manuscript with a letter asking if they'd write a short endorsement for the book jacket. Usually, people were eager to have their name in print.

"Jonas?" Langdon asked. "You sent out my manuscript, didn't you?" Faukman frowned, sensing Langdon wasn't pleased. "The manuscript was good, Robert, and I wanted to surprise you with great reviews."

A pause. "Did you send one to the curator of the Paris Louvre?"

"What do you think? You mentioned his Louvre collection in your manuscript a bunch of times, his books are in your bibliography, and the guy's got serious pull for foreign sales. Saunière was an easy choice." The silence on the other end stretched out. "When did you send it?"

"About a month ago. I also said you'd be in Paris soon and suggested you two talk. Did he ever call you to meet?" Faukman paused, rubbing his eyes. "Wait, aren't you supposed to be in Paris this week?"

"I am in Paris."

Faukman sat up straight. "You called me collect from Paris?"

"Take it out of my earnings, Jonas. Did you ever hear back from Saunière? Did he like the manuscript?"

"I don't know. I haven't heard from him yet."

"Well, don't expect much. I've got to go, but this makes sense now. Thanks."

"Robert—"

But Langdon had already hung up.

Faukman put down the phone, shaking his head in disbelief. Authors, he thought. Even the normal ones are crazy.

Inside the Range Rover, Leigh Teabing laughed loudly. "Robert, you're saying you wrote a book about a secret society, and your editor sent a copy to that secret society?" Langdon slumped. "Apparently."

"A bad coincidence, my friend."

Coincidence had nothing to do with it, Langdon knew. Asking Jacques Saunière to endorse a book on goddess worship was as obvious as asking Tiger Woods to endorse a book on golf. Plus, any book on goddess worship would almost certainly have to mention the Priory of Sion.

"Here's the big question," Teabing said, still chuckling. "Did you say good or bad things about the Priory?"

Langdon understood Teabing's real question clearly. Many historians wondered why the Priory was still hiding the Sangreal documents. Some thought the information should have been shared with the world a long time ago. "I didn't say whether the Priory was right or wrong."

"You mean, didn't do anything."

Langdon shrugged. Teabing seemed to think the documents should be made public. "I just gave the history of the group and said they were a modern goddess worship society, keepers of the Grail, and protectors of old documents."

Sophie looked at him. "Did you mention the keystone?"

Langdon cringed. He had, many times. "I talked about the keystone as an example of how far the Priory would go to protect the Sangreal documents." Sophie looked surprised. "I guess that explains P.S. Find Robert Langdon." Langdon felt that something else in the book had really caught Saunière's attention, but he would talk about that with Sophie later when they were alone.

"So," Sophie said, "you lied to Captain Fache."

"What?" Langdon asked.

"You told him you had never written to my grandfather."

"I didn't! My editor sent him a book."

"Think about it, Robert. If Captain Fache didn't find the envelope your editor used to send the book, he would think you sent it." She paused. "Or worse, that you delivered it yourself and lied about it."

When the Range Rover arrived at Le Bourget Airfield, Rémy drove to a small hangar at the far end of the airstrip. As they got closer, a messy-haired man in wrinkled khakis rushed from the hangar, waved, and slid open the huge metal door to show a shiny white jet inside. Langdon stared at the gleaming body. "That's Elizabeth?" Teabing grinned. "Better than the Chunnel."

The man in khakis hurried toward them, squinting into the headlights. "Almost ready, sir," he called in a British accent. "Sorry for the delay, but you surprised me and—" He stopped as the group got out. He looked at Sophie and Langdon, and then Teabing. Teabing said, "My friends and I have important business in London. We have no time to waste. Please get ready to leave now." As he spoke, Teabing took the pistol out of the car and handed it to Langdon.

The pilot's eyes widened at the sight of the gun. He walked over to Teabing and whispered, "Sir, I'm very sorry, but my flight agreement only covers you and your servant. I can't take your guests."

"Richard," Teabing said, smiling kindly, "two thousand pounds and that loaded gun say you can take my guests." He pointed to the Range Rover. "And the unlucky man in the back."

CHAPTER 69

The Hawker 731's twin Garrett TFE-731 engines roared, lifting the plane with powerful force. Outside the window, Le Bourget Airfield shrank away quickly. I'm running away from the country, Sophie thought, her body pressed back into the leather seat. Until now, she had thought her game with Fache could be justified to the Ministry of Defense. I was trying to protect an innocent man. I was trying to fulfill my grandfather's last wishes. That chance, Sophie knew, was gone. She was leaving the country, without papers, with a wanted man, and transporting a tied-up hostage. If there was ever a "reasonable" course, she had crossed it. Almost at the speed of sound.

Sophie sat with Langdon and Teabing near the front of the cabin—the Fan Jet Executive Elite Design, according to the gold label on the door. Their comfortable chairs were bolted to tracks on the floor and could be moved and locked around a rectangular wooden table. A mini-boardroom. But the fancy surroundings didn't hide the undignified situation in the back of the plane where, in a separate area near the bathroom, Teabing's servant Rémy sat with the gun, reluctantly guarding the bloody monk who lay tied up at his feet like luggage.

"Before we focus on the keystone," Teabing said, "I was wondering if I could say a few words." He sounded uneasy, like a father about to have a serious talk with his children. "My friends, I know I am just a guest on this journey, and I appreciate that. But, as someone who has spent his life searching for the Grail, I feel I must warn you that you are about to start a journey with no turning back, no matter the dangers." He turned to Sophie. "Miss Neveu, your grandfather gave you this cryptex hoping you would keep the secret of the Holy Grail alive."

"Yes."

"You feel you must follow the trail wherever it leads." Sophie nodded, but she felt another reason burning within her. The truth about my family. Even though Langdon said the keystone had nothing to do with her past, Sophie still felt something personal connected to this mystery, as if this cryptex, made by her grandfather, was trying to speak to her and resolve the emptiness that had haunted her.

"Your grandfather and three others died tonight," Teabing continued, "to keep this keystone away from the Church. Opus Dei almost got it tonight. You understand, I hope, that this puts you in a very important position. You have been given a torch. A two-thousand-year-old flame that cannot be allowed to go out. This torch cannot fall into the wrong hands." He paused, looking at the rosewood box. "I know you didn't have a choice, Miss Neveu, but considering what is at stake, you must either fully accept this responsibility... or you must give it to someone else."

"My grandfather gave the cryptex to me. I'm sure he thought I could handle it." Teabing looked hopeful but unsure. "Good. A strong will is needed. But, I wonder if you understand that unlocking the keystone will bring a much bigger challenge."

"How?"

"My dear, imagine you suddenly have a map that shows the location of the Holy Grail. You will have a truth that can change history forever. You will be the keeper of a truth that people have searched for centuries. You will have to decide whether to reveal that truth to the world. The person who does will be admired by many and hated by many. The question is whether you will be strong enough to do it." Sophie paused. "I'm not sure that is my decision." Teabing raised his eyebrows. "No? If not the person with the keystone, then who?"

"The brotherhood that has kept the secret safe for so long."

"The Priory?" Teabing looked doubtful. "But how? The brotherhood was destroyed tonight. Killed off, as you said. Whether they were bugged or had a spy, we'll never know, but someone got to them and found out who their four leaders were. I wouldn't trust anyone who claims to be from the brotherhood now."

"So what do you suggest?" Langdon asked.

"Robert, you know the Priory hasn't protected the truth all these years just to let it sit around forever. They've been waiting for the right time in history to share their secret. A time when the world can handle it."

"And you think that time is now?" Langdon asked.

"Definitely. It's obvious. All the signs are there, and if the Priory wasn't planning to reveal their secret soon, why has the Church attacked now?" Sophie argued, "The monk hasn't told us why he's doing this yet."

"The monk's goal is the Church's goal," Teabing replied, "to destroy the documents that reveal the big lie. The Church almost succeeded tonight, and the Priory is trusting you, Miss Neveu. Saving the Holy Grail means carrying out the Priory's wish to share the truth with the world." Langdon interrupted. "Leigh, asking Sophie to make that decision is a lot to ask of someone who just learned about the Sangreal documents an hour ago." Teabing sighed. "I'm sorry if I'm pressuring you, Miss Neveu. I've always believed these documents should be public, but it's your decision. I just think you should start thinking about what happens if we open the keystone."

"Gentlemen," Sophie said, firmly. "To use your words, 'You don't find the Grail, the Grail finds you.' I'm going to trust that the Grail found me for a reason, and I'll know what to do when the time comes."

They both looked surprised.

"So then," she said, pointing to the rosewood box. "Let's continue."

CHAPTER 70

Standing in the drawing room of Château Villette, Lieutenant Collet watched the dying fire and felt sad. Captain Fache had arrived moments earlier and was now in the next room, yelling into the phone, trying to organize the failed search for the missing Range Rover. It could be anywhere by now, Collet thought.

Having disobeyed Fache's direct orders and lost Langdon again, Collet was thankful that the forensics team had found a bullet hole in the floor, which at least proved Collet's story that a shot had been fired. Still, Fache was in a bad mood, and Collet knew there would be serious consequences later.

Sadly, the clues they were finding here didn't seem to explain anything about what was happening or who was involved. The black Audi outside had been rented with a fake name and fake credit card numbers, and the fingerprints in the car didn't match anything in Interpol's records. Another agent rushed into the living room, looking urgent. "Where's Captain Fache?" Collet barely looked up from the burning embers. "He's on the phone."

"I'm off the phone," Fache snapped, walking into the room. "What is it?" The second agent said, "Sir, Central just heard from André Vernet at the Depository Bank of Zurich. He wants to talk to you alone. He's changing his story."

"Oh?" Fache said.

Now Collet looked up.

"Vernet is admitting that Langdon and Neveu were inside his bank tonight."

"We already knew that," Fache said. "Why did Vernet lie about it?"

"He said he'll only talk to you, but he's agreed to fully cooperate."

"In exchange for what?"

"For keeping his bank's name out of the news and helping him get back some stolen items. It seems Langdon and Neveu stole something from Saunière's account."

"What?" Collet exclaimed. "How?"

Fache didn't react, his eyes fixed on the second agent. "What did they steal?"

"Vernet didn't say, but he seems ready to do anything to get it back." Collet tried to picture how this could have happened. Maybe Langdon and Neveu had threatened a bank worker? Maybe they forced Vernet to open Saunière's account and help them escape in the armored truck. As possible as it seemed, Collet struggled to believe Sophie Neveu could be part of something like that.

From the kitchen, another agent called out to Fache. "Captain? I'm checking Mr. Teabing's speed dial numbers and I'm on the phone with Le Bourget Airfield. I have some bad news."

Thirty seconds later, Fache was gathering his things and getting ready to leave Château Villette. He had just found out that Teabing kept a private jet at Le Bourget Airfield and that the plane had taken off about thirty minutes ago.

The Bourget representative on the phone claimed not to know who was on the plane or where it was going. The takeoff wasn't planned, and no flight information was recorded. Very illegal, even for a small airfield. Fache was sure he could get the answers he needed by putting enough pressure on them.

"Lieutenant Collet," Fache ordered, heading for the door. "I have to leave you in charge of the PTS investigation here. Try to get something right for once."

CHAPTER 71

As the Hawker evened out, heading toward England, Langdon gently picked up the rosewood box from his lap, where he'd been keeping it safe during takeoff. Setting the box on the table, he felt Sophie and Teabing eagerly leaning closer. He unfastened the lid and opened the box, looking not at the lettered dials of the cryptex, but at the small hole on the underside of the lid. With the tip of a pen, he carefully took off the inlaid Rose on top, revealing the text underneath. Sub Rosa, he thought, hoping a new look at the text would make things clearer. Giving it his full attention, Langdon examined the

strange text.

After a few seconds, he started to feel frustrated again. "Leigh, I just can't seem to figure it out."

Sophie, sitting across the table, couldn't see the text yet, but Langdon's inability to name the language right away surprised her. Did my grandfather speak a language so rare that even a symbologist doesn't know it? She quickly remembered this shouldn't surprise her. It wasn't the first secret Jacques Saunière had kept from her.

Across from Sophie, Leigh Teabing was practically jumping out of his seat. Anxious to see the text for himself, he shook with excitement, leaning in, trying to peek around Langdon, who was still bent over the box.

"I'm not sure," Langdon said quietly. "I first thought it was Semitic, but now I doubt it. Most basic Semitic languages have nekkudot. This doesn't have any."

"Probably ancient," Teabing suggested.

"Nekkudot?" Sophie asked.

Teabing never took his eyes from the box. "Most modern Semitic alphabets don't have vowels and use nekkudot —small dots and dashes written below or inside the consonants—to show the vowel sound. Nekkudot are a newer addition to language."

Langdon was still looking at the script. "A Sephardic translation, maybe...?" Teabing couldn't take it anymore. "Maybe if I just..." Reaching over, he moved the box away from Langdon and pulled it closer. Langdon knew a lot about the usual ancient languages—Greek, Latin, the Romance languages—but from the quick look Teabing had of this language, he thought it seemed more specific, maybe a Rashi script or a STA'M with crowns. Taking a deep breath, Teabing stared at the engraving. He didn't say anything for a long time. With each second, Teabing felt his confidence drop. "I'm amazed," he said.

"This language is like nothing I've ever seen!"

Langdon sank down.

"Can I see it?" Sophie asked.

Teabing acted like he didn't hear her. "Robert, you said you thought you'd seen something like this before?"

Langdon looked annoyed. "I thought so. I'm not sure. The script looks familiar somehow."

"Leigh?" Sophie repeated, clearly not happy about being left out. "Can I look at the box my grandfather made?"

"Of course, dear," Teabing said, pushing it to her. He didn't mean to sound condescending, but Sophie Neveu was out of her depth. If a British Royal Historian and a Harvard symbologist couldn't even name the language—

"Aah," Sophie said, after looking at the box for a few seconds. "I should have known." Teabing and Langdon turned at the same time, staring at her.

"Guess what?" Teabing asked.

Sophie shrugged. "Guess that this would be the language my grandfather would have used."

"You're saying you can read this text?" Teabing exclaimed.

"Very easily," Sophie replied, clearly enjoying herself now. "My grandfather taught me this language when I was only six years old. I'm fluent." She leaned across the table and looked sternly at Teabing. "And frankly, sir, considering your loyalty to the Crown, I'm a little surprised you didn't recognize it."

Instantly, Langdon knew.

No wonder the script looks so familiar!

Years ago, Langdon had been to an event at Harvard's Fogg Museum. Harvard dropout Bill Gates had returned to his old school to loan the museum one of his valuable purchases—eighteen sheets of paper he had recently bought at an auction from the Armand Hammar Estate.

His winning offer—a staggering $30.8 million.

The author of the pages—Leonardo da Vinci.

The eighteen pages—now known as Leonardo's Codex Leicester after their famous owner, the Earl of Leicester—were all that was left of one of Leonardo's most interesting notebooks: essays and drawings describing Da Vinci's advanced ideas on astronomy, geology, archaeology, and hydrology.

Langdon would never forget his reaction after waiting in line to see the priceless parchment. He was completely let down. The pages made no sense. Although they were well-preserved and written in very neat handwriting—red ink on cream paper—the codex looked like nonsense. At first, Langdon thought he couldn't read it because Da Vinci wrote his notebooks in old Italian. But after looking closer, he realized he couldn't find a single Italian word, or even one letter.

"Try this, sir," whispered the female guide at the display case. She pointed to a hand mirror attached to the display with a chain. Langdon picked it up and looked at the text in the mirror.

Suddenly, it was clear.

Langdon had been so excited to read some of the great thinker's ideas that he had forgotten one of the man's many artistic skills was writing in a mirrored script that was almost impossible for anyone else to read. Historians still debated whether Da Vinci wrote this way just to amuse himself or to prevent people from looking over his shoulder and stealing his ideas, but it didn't matter. Da Vinci did what he wanted.

Sophie smiled to see that Robert understood her. "I can read the first few words," she said. "It's English."

Teabing was still complaining. "What's going on?"

"Reversed text," Langdon said. "We need a mirror."

"No, we don't," Sophie said. "I bet this wood is thin enough." She lifted the rosewood box up to a light on the wall and began looking at the underside of the lid. Her grandfather couldn't actually write in reverse, so he always cheated by writing normally, then flipping the paper over and tracing the reversed image. Sophie guessed that he had burned normal text into a block of wood and then sanded the back of the block until the wood was paper thin and the burning could be seen through the wood. Then he simply flipped the piece over and placed it in.

As Sophie moved the lid closer to the light, she saw she was right. The bright light shone through the thin layer of wood, and the writing appeared in reverse on the underside of the lid. It was instantly readable.

"English," Teabing groaned, hanging his head in shame. "My own language."

At the back of the plane, Rémy Legaludec struggled to hear over the loud engines, but he couldn't make out the conversation in front. Rémy didn't like how the night was going. Not one bit. He looked down at the tied-up monk at his feet. The man was perfectly still now, as if he had accepted his fate, or maybe, was silently praying to be saved.

CHAPTER 72

Fifteen thousand feet in the air, Robert Langdon felt the real world disappear as all his thoughts focused on Saunière's mirror-image poem, lit up by the box's lid.

Sophie quickly found paper and copied it down by hand. When she finished, the three of them read the text in turn. It was like an archaeological crossword puzzle... a riddle that promised to reveal how to open the cryptex. Langdon read the verse slowly. An ancient word of wisdom frees this scroll... and helps us keep her scatter'd family whole... a headstone praised by templars is the key... and atbash will reveal the truth to thee. Before Langdon could even think about what ancient password the verse was trying to reveal, he felt something more basic resonate within him—the rhythm of the poem. Iambic pentameter. Langdon had encountered this rhythm often over the years while researching secret societies across Europe, including just last year in the Vatican Secret Archives. For centuries, iambic pentameter had been a favorite poetic rhythm of outspoken writers around the world, from the ancient Greek writer Archilochus to Shakespeare, Milton, Chaucer, and Voltaire—brave people who chose to write their social commentaries in a rhythm that many at the time believed had mystical powers. The roots of iambic pentameter were deeply pagan.

Iambs. Two syllables with opposite emphasis. Stressed and unstressed. Yin yang. A balanced pair. Arranged in strings of five. Pentameter. Five for the pentacle of Venus and the sacred feminine.

"It's pentameter!" Teabing blurted, turning to Langdon. "And the verse is in English! La lingua pura!"

Langdon nodded. The Priory, like many European secret societies at odds with the Church, had considered English the only European pure language for centuries. Unlike French, Spanish, and Italian, which were rooted in Latin— the tongue of the Vatican —English was linguistically separate from Rome's propaganda machine, and therefore became a sacred, secret tongue for those brotherhoods educated enough to learn it.

"This poem," Teabing gushed, "references not only the Grail, but the Knights Templar and the scattered family of Mary Magdalene! What more could we ask for?"

"The password," Sophie said, looking again at the poem. "It sounds like we need some kind of ancient word of wisdom?"

"Abracadabra?" Teabing ventured, his eyes twinkling.

A word of five letters, Langdon thought, considering the huge number of old words that might be considered words of wisdom—quotes from mystic chants, astrological predictions, secret society ceremonies, Wicca spells, Egyptian magic spells, pagan prayers—the list went on and on.

"The password," Sophie said, "seems to be about the Templars." She read the words aloud. " 'A headstone praised by Templars is the key.' "

"Leigh," Langdon said, "you know the most about the Templars. Any ideas?" Teabing was quiet for a few seconds and then sighed. "Well, a headstone is obviously a grave marker. Maybe the poem means a gravestone the Templars praised at Magdalene's tomb, but that doesn't help much because we don't know where her tomb is."

"The last line," Sophie said, "says that Atbash will reveal the truth. I've heard that word. Atbash."

"That's not surprising," Langdon replied. "You probably heard it in Cryptology 101. The Atbash Cipher is one of the oldest codes ever."

Of course! Sophie thought. The famous Hebrew code. The Atbash Cipher had been part of Sophie's early training in codes. The code was created around 500 B.C. and is now used as an example of a simple substitution method. A common type of Jewish code, the Atbash Cipher is a simple substitution code based on the twenty-two-letter Hebrew alphabet. In Atbash, the first letter is replaced by the last letter, the second letter by the second to last letter, and so on.

"Atbash is perfect," Teabing said. "Text coded with Atbash is found in the Kabbala, the Dead Sea Scrolls, and even the Old Testament. Jewish scholars and mystics are still finding hidden meanings using Atbash. The Priory would definitely include the Atbash Cipher in their teachings."

"The only problem," Langdon said, "is that we don't have anything to use the cipher on." Teabing sighed. "There must be a code word on the headstone. We have to find this headstone praised by Templars."

Sophie could tell from Langdon's worried look that finding the Templar headstone would be very difficult.

Atbash is the key, Sophie thought. But we don't have a door. Three minutes later, Teabing sighed in frustration and shook his head. "My friends, I'm stuck. Let me think about this while I get us some snacks and check on Rémy and our guest." He stood up and walked to the back of the plane.

Sophie felt tired as she watched him leave.

Outside the window, the darkness before dawn was complete. Sophie felt as if she were being thrown through space, not knowing where she would end up. Having spent her childhood solving her grandfather's puzzles, she now had the unsettling feeling that this poem held information they still hadn't found.

There is more there, she thought. Cleverly hidden... but still there. She also worried that what they eventually found inside this cryptex would not be as simple as "a map to the Holy Grail." Despite Teabing's and Langdon's belief that the answer was inside the marble cylinder, Sophie had solved enough of her grandfather's treasure hunts to know that Jacques Saunière did not give up his secrets easily.

CHAPTER 73

The air traffic controller at Bourget Airfield's night shift was dozing in front of an empty radar screen when the captain of the Judicial Police almost broke down his door.

"Teabing's jet," Bezu Fache shouted, walking into the small tower, "where did it go?" The controller's first reaction was a rambling, weak attempt to protect the privacy of their British client—one of the airfield's most valued customers. It didn't work at all.

"Okay," Fache said, "I am arresting you for allowing a private plane to take off without filing a flight plan." Fache signaled to another officer, who came forward with handcuffs, and the traffic controller felt a wave of fear. He thought about the newspaper stories arguing whether the nation's police captain was a hero or a threat. That question had just been answered.

"Wait!" the controller heard himself beg at the sight of the handcuffs. "I can tell you this much. Sir Leigh Teabing often travels to London for medical treatments. He has a hangar at Biggin Hill Executive Airport in Kent. It's outside of London." Fache waved away the officer with the cuffs. "Is Biggin Hill where he's going tonight?"

"I don't know," the controller said truthfully. "The plane took off on its usual route, and his last radar contact pointed towards the United Kingdom. Biggin Hill is a very good guess."

"Were there others on the plane?"

"I swear, sir, I have no way of knowing that. Our clients can drive right to their hangars and load whatever they want. Who is on board is up to the customs officials at the airport where they land."

Fache checked his watch and looked out at the few jets parked in front of the terminal. "If they're going to Biggin Hill, how long until they land?"

The controller quickly looked through his records. "It's a short flight. His plane could be on the ground by... about six-thirty. In fifteen minutes."

Fache frowned and turned to one of his men. "Get a vehicle up here. I'm going to London. And get me the Kent police. Not British MI5. I want this low-key. Kent police. Tell them I want Teabing's plane to be allowed to land. Then I want it surrounded on the ground. Nobody gets off until I get there."

CHAPTER 74

"You're quiet," Langdon said, looking at Sophie across the Hawker's cabin.

"Just tired," she replied. "And the poem. I don't know." Langdon felt the same. The engine's hum and the plane's gentle rocking were hypnotic, and his head still hurt where the monk had hit him. Teabing was still at the back of the plane, and Langdon decided to use this moment alone with Sophie to tell her something he'd been thinking about. "I think I know part of why your grandfather planned to get us together. I think he wanted me to explain something to you."

"Isn't the story of the Holy Grail and Mary Magdalene enough?" Langdon wasn't sure how to continue. "The break between you. The reason you haven't spoken in ten years. I think maybe he hoped I could fix that by explaining what drove you apart."

Sophie shifted in her seat. "I haven't told you what drove us apart." Langdon watched her closely. "You saw a sex ritual. Didn't you?" Sophie flinched. "How do you know that?"

"Sophie, you told me you saw something that made you think your grandfather was in a secret society. And whatever you saw upset you so much that you haven't spoken to him since. I know a lot about secret societies. It doesn't take a genius to guess what you saw." Sophie stared.

"Was it in the spring?" Langdon asked. "Around the equinox? Mid-March?" Sophie looked out the window. "I was on spring break from university. I came home a few days early."

"Do you want to tell me about it?"

"I'd rather not." She turned back to Langdon suddenly, her eyes filling with emotion. "I don't know what I saw."

"Were there men and women there?"

After a pause, she nodded.

"Dressed in white and black?"

She wiped her eyes and then nodded, seeming to relax a little. "The women wore white, see-through dresses... with gold shoes. They held gold balls. The men wore black robes and black shoes."

Langdon tried to hide his feelings, but he couldn't believe what he was hearing. Sophie Neveu had unknowingly seen a sacred ceremony that was two thousand years old. "Masks?" he asked, trying to stay calm. "Neutral masks?"

"Yes. Everyone. The same masks. White on the women. Black on the men." Langdon had read about this ceremony and knew its ancient origins. "It's called Hieros Gamos," he said quietly. "It goes back over two thousand years. Egyptian priests and priestesses used to do it regularly to honor the female's ability to create life." He paused, leaning closer to her. "And if you saw Hieros Gamos without knowing what it meant, I can imagine it would be quite disturbing."

Sophie said nothing.

"Hieros Gamos is Greek," he continued. "It means sacred marriage."

"The ritual I saw was not a marriage."

"Marriage as in joining together, Sophie."

"You mean as in sex."

"No."

"No?" she said, her olive eyes testing him.

Langdon backed up a little. "Well... yes, in a way, but not as we think of it today." He explained that even though what she saw probably looked like a sex ritual, Hieros Gamos had nothing to do with being sexy. It was a spiritual act. In the past, sex was how men and women felt God. People long ago believed that a man wasn't spiritually complete until he had sex with a sacred woman. Physical connection with a woman was the only way a man could become spiritually whole and truly understand God. Since the time of Isis, sex rituals had been seen as man's only way to go from earth to heaven. "By being with a woman," Langdon said, "a man could reach a high point when his mind went completely blank and he could see God." Sophie looked doubtful. "Orgasm as prayer?"

Langdon shrugged, not really agreeing or disagreeing, even though Sophie was basically right. When a man climaxes, there's a split second when he's not thinking at all. A short mental blank. A moment of clarity when God might be seen. Meditation teachers reach similar thoughtless states without sex and often describe Nirvana as a never-ending spiritual orgasm.

"Sophie," Langdon said quietly, "it's important to remember that people in the past saw sex completely differently than we do today. Sex created new life—the biggest miracle—and only a god could perform miracles. A woman's ability to create life in her womb made her sacred. A god. Sex was the respected joining of the two halves of the human spirit—male and female—through which the man could find spiritual wholeness and connection with God. What you saw wasn't about sex, it was about spirituality. The Hieros Gamos ritual isn't something dirty. It's a very sacred ceremony."

His words seemed to affect her deeply. Sophie had been very calm all evening, but now, for the first time, Langdon saw her composure starting to break. Tears came to her eyes again, and she wiped them away with her sleeve.

He gave her a moment. He knew that the idea of sex as a way to God was hard to grasp at first. Langdon's Jewish students always looked shocked when he first told them that early Jewish tradition included ritualistic sex. In the Temple, no less. Early Jews believed that the most sacred part of Solomon's Temple held not only God but also His powerful female equal, Shekinah. Men seeking spiritual wholeness came to the Temple to visit priestesses—or hierodules —with whom they had sex and felt the divine through physical union. The Jewish name for God, YHWH—in fact came from Jehovah, a joining of the masculine Jah and the old name for Eve, Havah.

"For the early Church," Langdon explained softly, "mankind's use of sex to connect directly with God was a big threat to the Catholic Church's power. It cut the Church out, hurting their claim to be the only way to God. So, they worked hard to make sex seem bad and sinful. Other major religions did the same."

Sophie was quiet, but Langdon felt she was starting to understand her grandfather better. He had made this same point in a class earlier this semester. "Is it any wonder we feel confused about sex?" he asked his students. "Our history and our bodies tell us sex is natural—a valued path to spiritual fulfillment—but modern religion calls it shameful, teaching us to fear our sexual desire as evil." Langdon decided not to shock his students with the fact that many secret societies around the world—many of them very powerful—still practiced sex rituals and kept the old traditions alive. Tom Cruise's character in the movie Eyes Wide Shut found this out the hard way when he went to a private party of wealthy Manhattanites and saw Hieros Gamos. The filmmakers got some of the details wrong, but the main idea was there—a secret society celebrating the magic of sex.

"Professor Langdon?" A male student in the back raised his hand, sounding hopeful. "Are you saying that instead of going to chapel, we should have more sex?"

Langdon chuckled, not falling for the trick. From what he knew about Harvard parties, these kids were having plenty of sex. "Gentlemen," he said, knowing he was on thin ice,

"may I offer a suggestion? Without encouraging premarital sex, and without being foolish enough to think you're all innocent, I'll give you this advice about your sex lives."

All the men in the audience leaned forward, listening closely.

"The next time you're with a woman, look inside yourself and see if you can approach sex as a spiritual act. Challenge yourself to find that divine spark that humans can only achieve through connection with the sacred feminine."

The women smiled knowingly, nodding.

The men exchanged uncertain giggles and crude jokes.

Langdon sighed. College men were still boys.

Sophie's forehead felt cold as she pressed it against the plane's window and stared blankly into the darkness, trying to understand what Langdon had just told her. She felt a new regret growing inside her. Ten years. She pictured the stacks of unopened letters her grandfather had sent. I will tell Robert everything. Without turning from the window, Sophie began to speak. Quietly. Fearfully. As she began to recount what had happened that night, she felt herself drifting back... arriving in the woods outside her grandfather's Normandy château... searching the empty house in confusion... hearing the voices below her... and then finding the hidden door. She crept down the stone staircase, one step at a time, into that basement grotto. She could taste the earthy air. Cool and light. It was March. In the shadows of her hiding place on the staircase, she watched as the strangers swayed and chanted by flickering orange candles.

I'm dreaming, Sophie told herself. This is a dream. What else could it be?

The women and men were arranged alternately, black, white, black, white. The women's beautiful, flowing gowns billowed as they raised golden orbs in their right hands and called out in unison, "I was with you in the beginning, in the dawn of all that is holy, I bore you from the womb before the start of day."

The women lowered their eyes, and everyone rocked back and forth as if hypnotized. They were honoring something in the center of the circle.

What are they looking at?

The voices sped up now. Louder. Faster.

"The woman you see is love!" The women called, raising their eyes again. The men responded, "She lives in eternity!"

The chanting grew steady again. Accelerating. Thundering now. Faster. The participants stepped inward and knelt.

In that instant, Sophie could finally see what they were all watching.

On a low, fancy altar in the center of the circle lay a man. He was naked, lying on his back, and wearing a black mask. Sophie instantly recognized his body and the birthmark on his shoulder. She almost cried out. Grand-père! This image alone would have shocked Sophie beyond belief, and yet there was more.

Straddling her grandfather was a naked woman wearing a white mask, her long silver hair flowing out behind it. Her body was full, not perfect, and she was moving in rhythm to the chanting—making love to Sophie's grandfather.

Sophie wanted to turn and run, but she couldn't. The stone walls of the grotto trapped her as the chanting rose to a fever pitch. The circle of participants seemed almost to be singing now, the noise rising to a frenzy. With a sudden roar, the entire room seemed to erupt in climax. Sophie could not breathe. She suddenly realized she was quietly sobbing. She turned and stumbled silently up the stairs, out of the house, and drove trembling back to Paris.

CHAPTER 75

The chartered turboprop was flying over the sparkling lights of Monaco when Aringarosa hung up on Fache for the second time. He reached for the airsickness bag again but felt too tired to be sick.

Just let it end!

Fache's latest news seemed impossible, but almost nothing made sense tonight. What is happening? Everything had gone completely wrong. What have I gotten Silas into?

What have I gotten myself into!

On unsteady legs, Aringarosa walked to the cockpit. "I need to change where we're going." The pilot looked over his shoulder and laughed. "You're kidding, right?"

"No. I have to get to London right away."

"Father, this is a charter flight, not a taxi."

"I will pay you more, of course. How much? London is only one hour north and we barely have to change direction, so—"

"It's not about money, Father, there are other problems."

"Ten thousand euro. Right now."

The pilot turned, shocked. "How much? What priest has that kind of money?"

Aringarosa went to his black briefcase, opened it, and took out a bearer bond. He gave it to the pilot.

"What's this?" the pilot asked.

"A ten-thousand-euro bearer bond from the Vatican Bank."

The pilot looked unsure.

"It's like cash."

"Only cash is cash," the pilot said, giving the bond back.

Aringarosa felt weak and leaned on the cockpit door. "This is life or death. You have to help me. I need to get to London."

The pilot looked at the bishop's gold ring. "Real diamonds?"

Aringarosa looked at the ring. "I can't give this up." The pilot shrugged, turning to look out the windshield again.

Aringarosa felt sadder. He looked at the ring. The bishop would get everything it stood for anyway. After a long pause, he took the ring off his finger and set it carefully on the dashboard.

Aringarosa slipped out of the cockpit and sat down again. Fifteen seconds later, he felt the pilot turn a bit more to the north.

Even so, Aringarosa's great moment was falling apart.

It had started as a sacred mission. A clever plan. Now, like a house of cards, it was falling apart... and there was no end in sight.

CHAPTER 76

Langdon could tell Sophie was still disturbed from describing her experience of Hieros Gamos. He was amazed to have heard it. Not only had Sophie witnessed the ritual, but her own grandfather had been the leader... the Grand Master of the Priory of Sion. It was impressive. Da Vinci, Botticelli, Isaac Newton, Victor Hugo, Jean Cocteau... Jacques Saunière.

"I don't know what else I can tell you," Langdon said softly. Sophie's eyes were a deep green now, tearful. "He raised me like his own daughter." Langdon now understood the emotion that had been growing in her eyes as they spoke. It was regret. Distant and deep. Sophie Neveu had rejected her grandfather and was now seeing him in a completely different light.

Outside, dawn was coming fast, its red glow spreading from the right side of the plane. The earth was still black beneath them.

"Food, my dears?" Teabing rejoined them with a flourish, presenting several cans of Coke and a box of old crackers. He apologized for the limited food as he handed it out. "Our friend the monk isn't talking yet," he said, "but give him time." He bit into a cracker and eyed the poem. "So, my lovely, any progress?" He looked at Sophie. "What is your grandfather trying to tell us here? Where is this headstone? This headstone praised by Templars." Sophie shook her head and remained silent.

While Teabing again studied the verse, Langdon opened a Coke and turned to the window, his thoughts full of images of secret rituals and unbroken codes. A headstone praised by Templars is the key. He took a long sip from the can. A headstone praised by Templars. The cola was warm. The fading night seemed to disappear quickly, and as Langdon watched the change, he saw a shimmering ocean stretch out beneath them. The English Channel. It wouldn't be long now.

Langdon hoped the daylight would also bring understanding, but the brighter it became outside, the further he felt from the truth. He heard the rhythms of poetry and chanting, Hieros Gamos and sacred rites, blending with the rumble of the jet. A headstone praised by Templars.

The plane was over land again when an idea struck him. Langdon set down his empty can of Coke hard. "You won't believe this," he said, turning to the others. "The Templar headstone—I figured it out."

Teabing's eyes widened. "You know where the headstone is?" Langdon smiled. "Not where it is. What it is." Sophie leaned in to hear.

"I think the headstone refers to an actual stone head," Langdon explained, enjoying the excitement of academic discovery. "Not a grave marker."

"A stone head?" Teabing asked, surprised.

Sophie looked just as confused.

"Leigh," Langdon said, turning, "during the Inquisition, the Church accused the Knights Templar of many wrongdoings, right?"

"Correct. They made up all sorts of charges. Sexual sins, disrespecting the cross, devil worship—quite a list."

"And on that list was the worship of fake idols, right? Specifically, the Church said the Templars secretly held ceremonies where they prayed to a carved stone head... the pagan god—"

"Baphomet!" Teabing exclaimed. "My heavens, Robert, you're right! A headstone praised by Templars!"

Langdon quickly told Sophie that Baphomet was a pagan god of fertility, linked to the creative power of reproduction. Baphomet's head looked like a ram or goat, a common symbol of having children. The Templars honored Baphomet by circling a stone copy of his head and chanting prayers.

"Baphomet," Teabing chuckled. "The ceremony celebrated the creative magic of sex, but Pope Clement convinced everyone that Baphomet's head was actually the devil's. The Pope used the head of Baphomet as the key piece of evidence against the Templars." Langdon agreed. The modern idea of a horned devil called Satan likely came from Baphomet and the Church's attempts to turn the horned fertility god into a symbol of evil. The Church clearly succeeded, but not completely. Traditional American Thanksgiving tables still have pagan, horned fertility symbols. The cornucopia, or "horn of plenty," honors Baphomet's fertility and comes from the story of Zeus being fed by a goat whose horn broke off and magically filled with fruit. Baphomet also appears in group photos when someone jokingly puts two fingers behind a friend's head to make horns. Few of these pranksters know that they're actually suggesting their victim has a high sperm count.

"Yes, yes," Teabing said excitedly. "Baphomet must be what the poem is talking about. A headstone praised by Templars."

"Okay," Sophie said, "but if Baphomet is the headstone praised by Templars, we have a new problem." She pointed to the dials on the cryptex. "Baphomet has eight letters. We only have room for five."

Teabing grinned broadly. "My dear, this is where the Atbash code becomes useful."

CHAPTER 77

Langdon was impressed. Teabing had just written out the entire twenty-two-letter Hebrew alphabet— alef-beit —from memory. He used Roman letters instead of Hebrew, but still read them perfectly. A B G D H V Z Ch T Y K L M N S O P Tz Q R Sh Th

"Alef, Beit, Gimel, Dalet, Hei, Vav, Zayin, Chet, Tet, Yud, Kaf, Lamed, Mem, Nun, Samech, Ayin, Pei, Tzadik, Kuf, Reish, Shin, and Tav." Teabing wiped his forehead and continued. "In Hebrew, vowel sounds aren't written. So, when we write Baphomet in Hebrew, it loses its three vowels, leaving us—"

"Five letters," Sophie said quickly.

Teabing nodded and wrote again. "Here's Baphomet spelled in Hebrew letters. I'll add the missing vowels to make it clearer.

B a P V o M e Th

"Remember," he added, "Hebrew is usually written the other way, but we can use Atbash this way too. Now, we just make our substitution by writing the alphabet backward under the original."

"There's an easier way," Sophie said, taking the pen. "It works for all ciphers like Atbash. I learned it at the Royal Holloway." Sophie wrote the first half of the alphabet left to right, then the second half right to left underneath. "Cryptanalysts call it the fold-over. Less complicated, and cleaner." A

B

G

D

H

V

Z

Ch

T

Y
