Th

Sh

R

Q

Tz

P

O

S

N

M

L

Teabing looked at her work and chuckled. "You're exactly right. I'm glad those boys at the Holloway are doing their job."

Looking at Sophie's substitution matrix, Langdon felt a thrill that he thought must have been similar to the thrill felt by early scholars when they first used the Atbash Cipher to decode the famous Mystery of Sheshach. For years, religious scholars had been puzzled by biblical references to a city called Sheshach. The city was not on any map or in any other records, yet it was mentioned many times in the Book of Jeremiah—the king of Sheshach, the city of Sheshach, the people of Sheshach. Finally, a scholar used the Atbash Cipher on the word, and the results were amazing. The cipher showed that Sheshach was actually a code word for another very famous city. The decoding was simple.

Sheshach, in Hebrew, was spelled: Sh-Sh-K.

Sh-Sh-K, when put in the substitution matrix, became B-B-L.

B-B-L, in Hebrew, spelled Babel.

The mysterious city of Sheshach was revealed to be the city of Babel, and a lot of biblical study followed. In a few weeks, several more Atbash code words were found in the Old Testament, revealing many hidden meanings that scholars didn't know were there.

"We're getting close," Langdon whispered, unable to hide his excitement.

"Very close, Robert," Teabing said. He looked at Sophie and smiled. "You ready?" She nodded.

"Okay, Baphomet in Hebrew without the vowels is: B-P-V-M-Th. Now we just use your Atbash substitution matrix to change the letters into our five-letter password." Langdon's heart beat fast. B-P-V-M-Th. The sun was shining through the windows now. He looked at Sophie's substitution matrix and slowly began to make the change. B is Sh... P is V... Teabing was grinning like a child at Christmas. "And the Atbash Cipher reveals..." He stopped suddenly. "Good God!" His face turned pale.

Langdon looked up quickly.

"What's wrong?" Sophie asked.

"You won't believe this." Teabing glanced at Sophie. "Especially you."

"What do you mean?" she said.

"This is... brilliant," he whispered. "Absolutely brilliant!" Teabing wrote again on the paper.

"Drumroll, please. Here is your password." He showed them what he had written. Sh-V-P-Y-A

Sophie frowned. "What is it?"

Langdon didn't recognize it either.

Teabing's voice seemed to tremble with awe. "This, my friend, is an actual ancient word of wisdom."

Langdon read the letters again. An ancient word of wisdom frees this scroll. A moment later he understood. He never saw this coming. "An ancient word of wisdom!" Teabing was laughing. "Quite literally!"

Sophie looked at the word and then at the dial. Right away she realized Langdon and Teabing had missed a serious problem. "Hold on! This can't be the password," she argued. "The cryptex doesn't have an Sh on the dial. It uses a standard Roman alphabet."

"Read the word," Langdon urged. "Keep in mind two things. In Hebrew, the symbol for the sound Sh can also be pronounced as S, depending on the accent. Just as the letter P can be pronounced F." SVFYA? she thought, puzzled.

"Genius!" Teabing added. "The letter Vav often stands in for the vowel sound O!" Sophie looked at the letters again, trying to sound them out.

"S...o...f...y...a."

She heard her own voice and couldn't believe what she'd said. "Sophia? It spells Sophia?"

Langdon nodded eagerly. "Yes! Sophia means wisdom in Greek. Your name, Sophie, comes from a 'word of wisdom.' "

Sophie suddenly missed her grandfather a lot. He hid the Priory keystone with my name. Her throat tightened. It seemed so perfect. But as she looked at the five letter dials on the cryptex, she saw there was still a problem. "But wait... Sophia has six letters."

Teabing's smile didn't fade. "Look at the poem again. Your grandfather wrote, 'An ancient word of wisdom.' "

"Yes?"

Teabing winked. "In old Greek, wisdom is spelled S-O-F-I-A."

CHAPTER 78

Sophie felt a rush of excitement as she held the cryptex and began dialing the letters. An ancient word of wisdom frees this scroll. Langdon and Teabing seemed to hold their breath as they watched.

S... O... F...

"Carefully," Teabing said. "Very carefully."

...I... A.

Sophie lined up the last dial. "Okay," she whispered, looking up at the others. "I'm going to pull it open."

"Remember the vinegar," Langdon whispered, both scared and excited. "Be careful." Sophie knew that if this cryptex was like the ones she had opened as a child, she just needed to hold the cylinder at both ends, right past the dials, and pull, using slow, steady pressure in opposite directions. If the dials were set to the correct password, then one of the ends would slide off, like a lens cap, and she could reach in and take out the rolled papyrus document, which would be wrapped around the vial of vinegar. However, if the password they had entered was wrong, Sophie's pulling force would move a hinged lever inside, which would press down into the space and push on the glass vial, eventually breaking it if she pulled too hard.

Pull gently, she thought.

Teabing and Langdon both moved closer as Sophie put her hands around the ends of the cylinder. In the excitement of figuring out the code word, Sophie had almost forgotten what they expected to find inside. This is the Priory keystone. According to Teabing, it held a map to the Holy Grail, revealing the tomb of Mary Magdalene and the Sangreal treasure... the greatest collection of secret truth.

Now holding the stone tube, Sophie double-checked that all of the letters were lined up correctly with the marker. Then, slowly, she pulled. Nothing happened. She pulled a little harder. Suddenly, the stone slid apart like a smooth telescope. The heavy end piece came off in her hand. Langdon and Teabing almost jumped up. Sophie's heart beat faster as she put the end cap on the table and tilted the cylinder to look inside.

A scroll!

Looking into the hollow of the rolled paper, Sophie saw it was wrapped around something round—the vinegar vial, she thought. But the paper wasn't the usual thin papyrus. It was vellum. That's strange, she thought, vinegar can't dissolve lambskin vellum. She looked again and realized the object wasn't a vial of vinegar after all. It was something else.

"What's wrong?" Teabing asked. "Pull out the scroll." Sophie frowned, grabbed the rolled vellum and the object inside, pulling them out of the container.

"That's not papyrus," Teabing said. "It's too thick."

"I know. It's padding."

"For what? The vinegar vial?"

"No." Sophie unrolled the scroll, showing what was inside. "For this." When Langdon saw the object, his heart sank.

"God help us," Teabing said, slumping. "Your grandfather was cruel." Langdon was amazed. Saunière won't make this easy. On the table was a second cryptex. Smaller. Made of black onyx. It was inside the first one. Saunière loved opposites. Two cryptexes. Everything in pairs. Double meanings. Male female. Black inside white. Langdon felt the symbolism growing. White gives birth to black.

Every man came from woman.

White — female.

Black — male.

Reaching over, Langdon picked up the smaller cryptex. It looked just like the first, but half the size and black. He heard the familiar gurgle. It seemed the vial of vinegar they heard earlier was inside this smaller cryptex.

"Well, Robert," Teabing said, sliding the vellum page to him.

"You'll be glad to hear we're at least flying the right way." Langdon looked at the thick vellum sheet. Another four-line verse was written in fancy handwriting. Again, in iambic pentameter. The verse was puzzling, but Langdon only needed to read the first line to know that Teabing's plan to come to Britain would work. IN LONDON LIES A KNIGHT A POPE INTERRED.

The rest of the poem clearly meant that the password to open the second cryptex could be found by visiting this knight's tomb, somewhere in the city.

Langdon turned to Teabing, excited. "Do you have any idea which knight this poem means?"

Teabing grinned. "Not a clue. But I know exactly which crypt we should search."

At that moment, fifteen miles ahead, six Kent police cars sped down rainy streets toward Biggin Hill Executive Airport.

CHAPTER 79

Lieutenant Collet took a Perrier from Teabing's fridge and walked back through the drawing room. Instead of going with Fache to London where things were happening, he was now watching over the PTS team that had spread out through Château Villette. So far, the evidence they had found was not useful: one bullet in the floor; a paper with symbols and the words blade and chalice; and a bloody spiked belt that PTS said was connected to the Catholic group Opus Dei, which had been in the news for its recruiting in Paris.

Collet sighed. Good luck figuring this out.

Walking down a fancy hallway, Collet went into the large ballroom study, where the head PTS examiner was dusting for fingerprints. He was a large man in suspenders.

"Anything?" Collet asked, walking in.

The examiner shook his head. "Nothing new. Several sets matching those in the house."

"What about the prints on the cilice belt?"

"Interpol is still working on it. I sent them everything we found."

Collet pointed to two sealed evidence bags on the desk. "And this?" The man shrugged. "Just in case. I bag anything strange."

Collet walked over. Strange?

"This Brit's a weird one," the examiner said. "Take a look at this." He went through the evidence bags and picked one, giving it to Collet.

The photo showed the main entrance of a Gothic cathedral—the usual, set-back archway, getting smaller through many layers to a small doorway.

Collet looked at the photo and turned. "This is strange?"

"Turn it over."

On the back, Collet saw notes written in English, saying a cathedral's long empty nave was a hidden pagan gift to a woman's womb. This was odd. But the note about the cathedral's doorway was what shocked him. "Wait! He thinks a cathedral's entrance is like a woman's..."

The examiner nodded. "With ridges like lips and a cute little cinquefoil clitoris above the doorway." He sighed. "Makes you want to go back to church." Collet picked up the second evidence bag. Through the plastic, he saw a large shiny photo of what looked like an old paper. The title at the top said: Les Dossiers Secrets — Number 4° lm1 249

"What's this?" Collet asked.

"No clue. He's got copies of it everywhere, so I bagged it." Collet looked at the paper.

Prieuré de Sion? Collet wondered.

"Lieutenant?" Another agent poked his head in. "The switchboard has an urgent call for Captain Fache, but they can't find him. Can you take it?"

Collet went back to the kitchen and answered the call.

It was André Vernet.

The banker's polished accent barely hid the stress in his voice. "I thought Captain Fache would call me, but I haven't heard from him."

"The captain is very busy," Collet replied. "Can I help you?"

"I was promised I'd be updated on your progress tonight." For a moment, Collet thought he knew the man's voice, but he couldn't quite remember from where. "Monsieur Vernet, I'm currently in charge of the Paris investigation. My name is Lieutenant Collet."

For a few seconds, Collet held the phone. Then it hit him. I knew I knew that voice! The realization made him gasp.

The armored car driver.

With the fake Rolex.

Collet now understood why the banker had hung up so fast. Vernet had remembered the name Lieutenant Collet—the officer he plainly lied to earlier tonight.

Collet thought about what this strange twist meant. Vernet is involved. He knew right away he should call Fache. But he also knew this lucky chance was his opportunity to stand out.

He called Interpol right away and asked for every bit of information they had on the Depository Bank of Zurich and its president, André Vernet.

CHAPTER 80

"Seat belts, please," Teabing's pilot said as the Hawker 731 went down into a gray, drizzly morning. "We'll be landing in five minutes."

Teabing felt happy to be home when he saw the misty hills of Kent spread out below the plane. England was less than an hour from Paris, but it felt like a different world. This morning, the wet, green of his homeland looked especially welcoming. My time in France is over. I'm returning to England victorious. The keystone has been found. The question, of course, was where the keystone would lead. Somewhere in the United Kingdom. Teabing had no idea where, but he could already taste success. As Langdon and Sophie watched, Teabing got up and went to the other side of the cabin. He slid open a wall panel to show a hidden wall safe. He entered the combination, opened the safe, and took out two passports. "Passports for Rémy and me." Then he took out a thick stack of fifty-pound notes. "And something for you two."

Sophie looked unsure. "A bribe?"

"Creative diplomacy. Private airfields allow certain things. A British customs official will meet us at my hangar and ask to come on the plane. Instead of letting him on, I'll tell him I'm traveling with a French celebrity who doesn't want anyone to know she's in England—you know, for press reasons—and I'll offer the official this generous tip as thanks for his discretion."

Langdon looked amazed. "And the official will accept?"

"Not from just anyone, but these people all know me. I'm not an arms dealer. I was knighted." Teabing smiled. "Being a member has its benefits." Rémy came up the aisle, holding the Heckler Koch pistol. "Sir, what about me?" Teabing looked at his servant. "I want you to stay on board with our guest until we return. We can't very well drag him all over London with us."

Sophie looked worried. "Leigh, I meant what I said about the French police finding your plane before we return."

Teabing laughed. "Yes, imagine their surprise if they come aboard and find Rémy." Sophie was surprised by his casual attitude. "Leigh, you brought a tied-up hostage across international borders. This is serious."

"So are my lawyers." He glared at the monk in the back of the plane. "That animal broke into my home and almost killed me. That's a fact, and Rémy will back me up."

"But you tied him up and flew him to London!" Langdon said.

Teabing held up his right hand like he was taking an oath in court. "Forgive an odd old knight his silly preference for the British court system. I know I should have called the French police, but I'm a snob and don't trust the relaxed French to prosecute properly. This man almost killed me. Yes, I made a quick decision forcing my servant to help me bring him to England, but I was under a lot of stress. My fault. My fault."

Langdon looked shocked. "Coming from you, Leigh, that might just work."

"Sir?" the pilot called back. "The tower just radioed. They've got some kind of maintenance issue near your hangar, and they're asking me to bring the plane straight to the terminal instead."

Teabing had been flying to Biggin Hill for over ten years, and this was a first. "Did they say what the problem is?"

"The controller wasn't specific. Something about a gas leak at the pumping station? They asked me to park in front of the terminal and keep everyone on board until they say it's okay. Safety thing. We're not supposed to get off until we get the all clear from airport people." Teabing was doubtful. That must be a big gas leak. The pumping station was far from his hangar.

Rémy also looked worried. "Sir, this seems very strange." Teabing turned to Sophie and Langdon. "My friends, I have a bad feeling we're about to be met by a welcoming party."

Langdon sighed sadly. "I guess Fache still thinks I'm the one he wants."

"Either that," Sophie said, "or he's too far into this to admit he was wrong." Teabing wasn't listening. No matter what Fache thought, they needed to act fast. Don't forget what matters most. The Grail. We're so close. Below them, the landing gear came down with a thud.

"Leigh," Langdon said, sounding very sorry, "I should turn myself in and handle this legally. Leave you all out of it."

"Good heavens, Robert!" Teabing waved it off. "Do you really think they're going to let the rest of us go? I just transported you illegally. Miss Neveu helped you escape from the Louvre, and we have a man tied up in the back of the plane. Come on! We're all in this together."

"Maybe a different airport?" Sophie said.

Teabing shook his head. "If we pull up now, by the time we get clearance anywhere else, our welcoming party will include army tanks."

Sophie slumped.

Teabing sensed that if they were to have any chance of delaying a confrontation with the British authorities long enough to find the Grail, bold action had to be taken. "Give me a minute," he said, hobbling toward the cockpit.

"What are you doing?" Langdon asked.

"Sales meeting," Teabing said, wondering how much it would cost him to persuade his pilot to perform one very unusual move.

CHAPTER 81

The Hawker is on final approach.

Simon Edwards—Executive Services Officer at Biggin Hill Airport—walked back and forth in the control tower, anxiously watching the rain-soaked runway. He never liked being woken up early on a Saturday, but it was especially annoying to be called in to watch the arrest of one of his most valuable clients. Sir Leigh Teabing paid Biggin Hill not only for a private hangar but also a "per landing fee" for his frequent arrivals and departures. Usually, the airfield knew his schedule in advance and followed a strict procedure for his arrival. Teabing liked things done perfectly. The custom-built Jaguar limousine he kept in his hangar had to be fully fueled, polished, and the day's London Times placed on the back seat. A customs official was to be at the hangar waiting for the plane to quickly process the required paperwork and luggage check. Sometimes, customs agents accepted large tips from Teabing in exchange for ignoring the transport of harmless items—mostly luxury foods—French snails, a very ripe Roquefort cheese, certain fruits. Many customs laws were ridiculous, anyway, and if Biggin Hill didn't take care of its clients, other airfields would. Teabing got what he wanted at Biggin Hill, and the employees benefited. Edwards felt nervous as he watched the jet coming in. He wondered if Teabing's habit of sharing his wealth had gotten him into trouble; the French authorities seemed very determined to catch him. Edwards hadn't been told what the charges were yet, but they were clearly serious. At the French authorities' request, Kent police had told the Biggin Hill air traffic controller to radio the Hawker's pilot and order him to go directly to the terminal instead of the client's hangar. The pilot had agreed, apparently believing the unlikely story of a gas leak.

Although the British police didn't usually carry guns, the seriousness of the situation had brought out an armed response team. Now, eight police officers with handguns stood just inside the terminal building, waiting for the moment when the plane's engines shut down. As soon as this happened, a runway worker would place safety blocks under the tires so the plane couldn't move. Then the police would appear and hold the occupants until the French police arrived to handle things.

The Hawker was now low in the sky, passing close to the treetops on their right. Simon Edwards went downstairs to watch the landing from the ground. The Kent police were ready, out of sight, and the maintenance man waited with his blocks. On the runway, the Hawker's nose went up, and the tires touched down in a puff of smoke. The plane slowed down, moving from right to left in front of the terminal, its white body shining in the rain. But instead of braking and turning into the terminal, the jet calmly rolled past the access lane and continued toward Teabing's hangar in the distance.

All the police turned and stared at Edwards. "I thought you said the pilot agreed to come to the terminal!"

Edwards was confused. "He did!"

Moments later, Edwards found himself in a police car racing across the tarmac toward the distant hangar. The line of police cars was still about five hundred yards away as Teabing's Hawker taxied calmly into the private hangar and disappeared. When the cars finally arrived and stopped suddenly outside the open hangar door, the police jumped out, guns drawn. Edwards jumped out too.

The noise was deafening.

The Hawker's engines were still loud as the jet completed its usual turn inside the hangar, positioning itself facing outward for a later departure. As the plane finished its 180-degree turn and moved toward the front of the hangar, Edwards could see the pilot's face, which looked understandably surprised and scared to see the line of police cars. The pilot brought the plane to a final stop and shut off the engines. The police rushed in, taking positions around the jet. Edwards joined the Kent chief inspector, who moved cautiously toward the hatch. After a few seconds, the fuselage door popped open. Leigh Teabing appeared in the doorway as the plane's electronic stairs smoothly lowered. As he looked out at the crowd of weapons pointed at him, he leaned on his crutches and scratched his head. "Simon, did I win some kind of police lottery while I was away?" He sounded more confused than worried.

Simon Edwards stepped forward, clearing his throat. "Good morning, sir. I apologize for the confusion. We've had a gas leak, and your pilot said he was coming to the terminal."

"Yes, yes, well, I told him to come here instead. I'm late for an appointment. I pay for this hangar, and this talk about avoiding a gas leak seemed like overkill."

"I'm afraid your arrival has caught us by surprise, sir."

"I know. I'm behind schedule, I am. Between you and me, the new medication gives me the runs. Thought I'd come over for a checkup."

The policemen all looked at each other. Edwards cringed. "Very good, sir."

"Sir," the Kent chief inspector said, stepping forward. "I need to ask you to stay onboard for another half hour or so."

Teabing looked annoyed as he limped down the stairs. "I'm afraid that is impossible. I have a doctor's appointment." He reached the tarmac. "I cannot afford to miss it." The chief inspector moved to block Teabing from leaving the plane. "I am here on orders from the French Judicial Police. They claim you are transporting wanted criminals on this plane."

Teabing stared at the chief inspector for a moment, and then burst out laughing. "Is this one of those prank shows? Very funny!"

The chief inspector didn't react. "This is serious, sir. The French police claim you may also have a hostage onboard."

Teabing's manservant Rémy appeared in the doorway at the top of the stairs. "I feel like a hostage working for Sir Leigh, but he assures me I am free to leave." Rémy checked his watch. "Master, we really are running late." He nodded toward the Jaguar stretch limousine in the far corner of the hangar. The huge car was black with tinted windows and whitewall tires. "I'll get the car." Rémy started down the stairs.

"I'm afraid we can't let you leave," the chief inspector said. "Please go back to your plane. Both of you. Officers from the French police will be landing soon." Teabing then looked at Simon Edwards. "Simon, this is ridiculous! We don't have anyone else on board. Just the usual—Rémy, our pilot, and me. Maybe you could help? Go look inside and see that the plane is empty." Edwards knew he was stuck. "Yes, sir. I can look."

"The hell you will!" the Kent chief inspector said, knowing enough about private airfields to think Simon Edwards might lie about who was on the plane to keep Teabing's business at Biggin Hill. "I'll look myself." Teabing shook his head. "No, you won't, Inspector. This is private property, and until you have a search warrant, you will stay off my plane. I'm giving you a reasonable option here. Mr. Edwards can do the inspection."

"No deal."

Teabing's face turned cold. "Inspector, I don't have time for your games. I'm late, and I'm leaving. If stopping me is that important to you, you'll have to shoot me." With that, Teabing and Rémy walked around the chief inspector and headed across the hangar toward the parked limousine.

The Kent chief inspector just felt disgusted by Leigh Teabing as the man limped around him in defiance. Rich people always thought they were above the law.

They are not. The chief inspector turned and aimed at Teabing's back. "Stop! I'll shoot!"

"Go ahead," Teabing said without stopping or looking back. "My lawyers will destroy you. And if you dare go on my plane without a warrant, you'll regret it."

The chief inspector was used to power plays and wasn't impressed. Teabing was right that the police needed a warrant to go on his jet. But because the flight started in France, and because the powerful Bezu Fache had given his approval, the Kent chief inspector thought his career would be better if he found out what Teabing was so eager to hide on the plane.

"Stop them," the inspector ordered. "I'm searching the plane." His men ran over, guns raised, and blocked Teabing and his servant from getting to the limousine.

Now Teabing turned. "Inspector, this is your last warning. Don't even think about going on that plane. You'll regret it."

Ignoring the threat, the chief inspector gripped his gun and walked up the plane's steps. At the entrance, he looked inside. After a moment, he stepped into the cabin. What the devil?

Except for the scared pilot in the cockpit, the plane was empty. Completely without anyone. He quickly checked the bathroom, the seats, and the luggage areas, but the inspector found no one hiding... let alone several people. What the hell was Bezu Fache thinking? It seemed Leigh Teabing was telling the truth. The Kent chief inspector stood alone in the empty cabin and swallowed hard. Shit. His face red, he stepped back onto the steps, looking across the hangar at Leigh Teabing and his servant, who were now held at gunpoint near the limousine. "Let them go," the inspector ordered.

"We got a bad tip."

Teabing's eyes were threatening even from across the hangar. "You'll be hearing from my lawyers. And remember, the French police can't be trusted."

With that, Teabing's servant opened the back door of the long limousine and helped his disabled master into the back seat. Then the servant walked to the front of the car, got behind the wheel, and started the engine. Policemen scattered as the Jaguar sped out of the hangar.

"Well done, my friend," Teabing said from the back seat as the limousine sped away from the airport. He looked at the dimly lit front part of the spacious interior.

"Everyone comfortable?"

Langdon nodded weakly. He and Sophie were still crouched on the floor beside the tied and gagged albino.

A few moments earlier, as the Hawker taxied into the empty hangar, Rémy had opened the door as the plane jerked to a stop halfway through its turn. With the police closing in, Langdon and Sophie dragged the monk down the steps to the ground and out of sight behind the limousine. Then the jet engines had roared again, turning the plane around as the police cars skidded into the hangar.

Now, as the limousine raced toward Kent, Langdon and Sophie climbed toward the back of the long limo, leaving the monk tied up on the floor. They sat on the long seat facing Teabing. The Brit gave them both a playful smile and opened the cabinet on the limo's bar. "Can I offer you a drink? Some snacks? Chips? Nuts? Seltzer?"

Sophie and Langdon both shook their heads.

Teabing grinned and closed the bar. "So, about this knight's tomb..."

CHAPTER 82

"Fleet Street?" Langdon asked, looking at Teabing in the back of the limo. A crypt on Fleet Street? Leigh was being deliberately vague about where he thought they would find the "knight's tomb," which, according to the poem, would give them the password to open the smaller cryptex.

Teabing grinned and turned to Sophie. "Miss Neveu, let the Harvard boy try the verse again, will you?"

Sophie reached into her pocket and took out the black cryptex, wrapped in the vellum. They had all decided to leave the rosewood box and larger cryptex in the plane's safe, taking only what they needed: the smaller, easier to carry black cryptex. Sophie unwrapped the vellum and gave the sheet to Langdon.

Langdon had read the poem several times on the jet, but hadn't found any specific location. Now, reading the words again, he studied them slowly, hoping the rhythm would reveal a clearer meaning now that he was on the ground. In London lies a knight a Pope interred.

His labor's fruit a Holy wrath incurred.

You seek the orb that ought be on his tomb.

It speaks of Rosy flesh and seeded womb.

The language seemed straightforward. A knight was buried in London. A knight who worked on something that angered the Church. A knight whose tomb was missing an orb that should be there. The poem's last line— Rosy flesh and seeded womb —clearly referred to Mary Magdalene, the Rose who carried Jesus's seed.

Despite the verse seeming simple, Langdon still didn't know who this knight was or where he was buried. Also, once they found the tomb, it sounded like they would be looking for something missing. The orb that should be on his tomb?

"No ideas?" Teabing clicked his tongue, disappointed, but Langdon felt the Royal Historian liked being ahead. "Miss Neveu?"

She shook her head.

"What would you two do without me?" Teabing said. "Fine, I'll explain it. It's really easy. The first line is key. Please read it." Langdon read, " 'In London lies a knight a Pope interred.' "

"Exactly. A knight a Pope interred." He looked at Langdon. "What does that mean to you?" Langdon shrugged. "A knight buried by a Pope? A knight whose funeral a Pope led?"

Teabing laughed loudly. "Oh, that's funny. Always the optimist, Robert. Look at the second line. This knight clearly did something to anger the Church. Think again. Think about the Church and the Knights Templar. A knight a Pope interred?"

"A knight a Pope killed?" Sophie asked.

Teabing smiled and patted her knee. "Good job, my dear. A knight a Pope buried. Or killed." Langdon thought of the famous Templar round-up in 1307—unlucky Friday the thirteenth—when Pope Clement killed and buried hundreds of Knights Templar. "But there must be many graves of 'knights killed by Popes.' "

"Aha, not so!" Teabing said. "Many were burned and thrown carelessly into the Tiber River. But this poem mentions a tomb. A tomb in London. And few knights are buried in London." He paused, watching Langdon as if waiting for him to understand. Finally, he sighed.

"Robert, come on! The church built in London by the Priory's military group—the Knights Templar themselves!"

"The Temple Church?" Langdon gasped. "It has a crypt?"

"Ten of the scariest tombs you'll ever see."

Langdon had never been to the Temple Church, but he'd read about it a lot in his Priory research. It was once the center of all Templar/Priory action in the UK. The Temple Church was named after Solomon's Temple, which is where the Knights Templar got their name. They also got the Sangreal documents there, which gave them power in Rome. Stories said the knights did weird, secret rituals in the Temple Church's strange sanctuary. "The Temple Church is on Fleet Street?"

"Actually, it's just off Fleet Street on Inner Temple Lane." Teabing grinned. "I wanted to make you worry a bit before I told you."

"Thanks."

"Neither of you has ever been there?"

Sophie and Langdon shook their heads.

"I'm not surprised," Teabing said. "The church is hidden now by bigger buildings. Hardly anyone knows it's there. It's a creepy old place. The architecture is totally pagan." Sophie looked surprised. "Pagan?"

"Completely pagan!" Teabing shouted. "The church is round. The Templars ignored the usual Christian cross shape and built a completely round church to honor the sun." He raised his eyebrows playfully. "A clear message to the guys in Rome. They might as well have rebuilt Stonehenge in London."

Sophie looked at Teabing. "What about the rest of the poem?"

The historian's cheerful look disappeared. "I'm not sure. It's confusing. We need to check each of the ten tombs closely. Hopefully, one of them will clearly be missing an orb." Langdon understood how close they were. If the missing orb showed them the password, they could open the second cryptex. He struggled to picture what they might find inside. Langdon looked at the poem again. It was like an ancient crossword puzzle. A five-letter word that speaks of the Grail? On the plane, they had already tried the obvious passwords—GRAIL, GRAAL, GREAL, VENUS, MARIA, JESUS, SARAH—but the cylinder hadn't moved. Too obvious. It seemed there was another five-letter reference to the Rose's fertile source. The fact that the word was escaping someone like Leigh Teabing made Langdon think it was no ordinary Grail reference.

"Sir Leigh?" Rémy called. He was watching them in the rearview mirror through the open divider. "You said Fleet Street is near Blackfriars Bridge?"

"Yes, take Victoria Embankment."

"I'm sorry. I don't know where that is. We usually only go to the hospital." Teabing rolled his eyes at Langdon and Sophie and muttered, "Honestly, it's like watching a child sometimes. One moment. Please, have a drink and some snacks." He left them, climbing awkwardly toward the open divider to talk to Rémy.

Sophie turned to Langdon, speaking quietly. "Robert, no one knows we're in England." Langdon realized she was right. The Kent police would tell Fache the plane was empty, and Fache would think they were still in France. We are invisible. Leigh's trick had just bought them time.

"Fache won't give up easily," Sophie said. "He has too much to lose if he doesn't arrest us." Langdon had been trying not to think about Fache. Sophie had promised to do everything she could to prove Langdon's innocence once this was over, but Langdon was starting to worry it might not matter. Fache could easily be part of this plan. Although Langdon couldn't imagine the Judicial Police involved in the Holy Grail, he felt too many coincidences tonight to dismiss Fache as an accomplice. Fache is religious, and he is determined to blame me for these murders. Then again, Sophie had said that Fache might just be too eager to make the arrest. After all, the evidence against Langdon was strong. Besides Langdon's name written on the Louvre floor and in Saunière's date book, Langdon now seemed to have lied about his manuscript and then run away. At Sophie's suggestion.

"Robert, I'm sorry you're so involved," Sophie said, putting her hand on his knee. "But I'm glad you're here."

The comment seemed more practical than romantic, but Langdon felt a sudden spark of attraction between them. He gave her a tired smile. "I'm much more fun when I've slept." Sophie was quiet for a few seconds. "My grandfather asked me to trust you. I'm glad I listened to him for once."

"Your grandfather didn't even know me."

"Even so, I think you've done everything he would have wanted. You helped me find the keystone, explained the Sangreal, told me about the ritual in the basement." She stopped.

Somehow I feel closer to my grandfather tonight than I have in years. I know he would be happy about that.

In the distance, now, the London skyline began to appear through the light morning rain. Once known for Big Ben and Tower Bridge, the horizon now featured the Millennium Eye—a huge, modern Ferris wheel that rose five hundred feet and offered amazing views of the city. Langdon had tried to ride it once, but the "viewing capsules" seemed like sealed coffins, and he chose to stay on the ground and enjoy the view from the open banks of the Thames.

Langdon felt a squeeze on his knee, pulling him back, and Sophie's green eyes were on him. He realized she had been talking to him. "What do you think we should do with the Sangreal documents if we ever find them?" she whispered.

"What I think doesn't matter," Langdon said. "Your grandfather gave the cryptex to you, and you should do with it what you feel he would want done."

"I'm asking for your opinion. You obviously wrote something in that manuscript that made my grandfather trust you. He scheduled a private meeting with you. That's unusual."

"Maybe he wanted to tell me I'm completely wrong."

"Why would he tell me to find you unless he liked your ideas? In your manuscript, did you support the idea that the Sangreal documents should be revealed or stay hidden?"

"Neither. I didn't make a decision either way. The manuscript is about the symbols of the sacred feminine—tracing her images throughout history. I certainly didn't pretend to know where the Grail is hidden or whether it should ever be revealed."

"And yet you're writing a book about it, so you obviously feel the information should be shared."

"There's a big difference between talking about a possible different history of Christ, and..." He paused.

"And what?"

"And showing the world thousands of old papers as proof that the New Testament is a lie."

"But you said the New Testament is based on made-up stories." Langdon smiled. "Sophie, every faith in the world is based on made-up stories. That's what faith is—believing in something we think is true, something we can't prove. Every religion uses metaphors, allegories, and exaggerations to describe God, from the ancient Egyptians to Sunday school today. Metaphors help us understand things we can't really understand. The problem is when we start to believe our own metaphors are real."

"So you think the Sangreal documents should stay hidden forever?"

"I'm a historian. I don't want documents destroyed, and I'd like religious scholars to have more information to think about Jesus Christ's special life."

"You're arguing both sides of my question."

"Am I? The Bible is a basic guide for millions of people, just like the Koran, Torah, and Pali Canon guide people of other religions. If we could find documents that went against the holy stories of Islam, Judaism, or Buddhism, should we? Should we announce that we have proof the Buddha wasn't born from a lotus flower? Or that Jesus wasn't really born of a virgin? People who truly understand their faith know the stories are metaphors."

Sophie looked doubtful. "My Christian friends really believe that Christ walked on water, turned water into wine, and was born of a virgin."

"Exactly," Langdon said. "Religious allegory has become part of what people believe is real. And believing in that reality helps millions of people deal with life and be better."

"But it seems their reality is false."

Langdon chuckled. "No more wrong than a math expert believing in the number 'i' because it helps her solve codes."

Sophie frowned. "That's not fair."

A moment passed.

"What did you ask before?" Langdon asked.

"I forgot."

He smiled. "Always works."

CHAPTER 83

Langdon's Mickey Mouse wristwatch showed almost seven-thirty when he stepped out of the Jaguar limousine onto Inner Temple Lane with Sophie and Teabing. The three walked through a maze of buildings to a small courtyard outside the Temple Church. The rough stone gleamed in the rain, and doves cooed in the structures above.

London's ancient Temple Church was built entirely of Caen stone. A striking, round building with an imposing front, a central tower, and a nave sticking out on one side, the church looked more like a military fort than a place of worship. Dedicated on February 10, 1185, by Heraclius, Patriarch of Jerusalem, the Temple Church survived eight centuries of political chaos, the Great Fire of London, and World War I, only to be heavily damaged by German bombs in 1940. After the war, it was restored to its original, stark beauty.

The simplicity of the circle, Langdon thought, admiring the building for the first time. The architecture was rough and basic, more like Rome's sturdy Castel Sant'Angelo than the elegant Pantheon. The boxy addition sticking out to the right was an ugly sight, but it didn't hide the original pagan shape of the main building.

"It's early on a Saturday," Teabing said, hobbling toward the entrance, "so I'm guessing we won't have any services to worry about."

The church's entrance was a recessed stone alcove with a large wooden door. To the left of the door, looking out of place, hung a bulletin board covered with concert schedules and religious service announcements.

Teabing frowned as he read the board. "They don't open to visitors for a couple of hours." He moved to the door and tried it. The door didn't move. Putting his ear to the wood, he listened. After a moment, he pulled back, a sly look on his face as he pointed to the bulletin board.

"Robert, check the service schedule, will you? Who is in charge this week?"

Inside the church, an altar boy was almost done vacuuming the communion kneelers when he heard a knock on the sanctuary door. He ignored it. Father Harvey Knowles had his own keys and wasn't due for a couple of hours. The knocking was probably a curious tourist or a beggar. The altar boy kept vacuuming, but the knocking continued. Can't you read? The sign on the door clearly said that the church didn't open until nine-thirty on Saturday. The altar boy stayed with his chores.

Suddenly, the knocking turned into loud banging, as if someone were hitting the door with a metal rod. The young man turned off his vacuum cleaner and marched angrily toward the door. Unlatching it from the inside, he swung it open. Three people stood in the entryway. Tourists, he grumbled. "We open at nine-thirty."

The large man, clearly the leader, walked forward with metal crutches. "I am Sir Leigh Teabing," he announced, his accent very British. "As you surely know, I am with Mr. and Mrs. Christopher Wren the Fourth." He stepped aside, gesturing to the attractive couple behind him. The woman had gentle features and rich red hair. The man was tall, dark-haired, and looked somewhat familiar.

The altar boy didn't know what to say. Sir Christopher Wren was the Temple Church's most famous supporter. He had made all the repairs possible after the Great Fire. He had also been dead since the early 1700s. "Um... nice to meet you?"

The man on crutches frowned. "Good thing you're not a salesman, you're not very convincing. Where is Father Knowles?"

"It's Saturday. He doesn't come in until later."

The disabled man's frown deepened. "Some gratitude. He promised he would be here, but it seems we'll do it without him. It won't take long."

The altar boy still blocked the doorway. "I'm sorry, what won't take long?" The visitor's eyes narrowed, and he leaned forward, whispering as if to spare everyone embarrassment. "Young man, you must be new here. Every year Sir Christopher Wren's descendants bring a bit of the old man's ashes to scatter in the Temple sanctuary. It's part of his will. No one likes making the trip, but what can we do?" The altar boy had been there for a couple of years but had never heard of this. "It would be better if you waited until nine-thirty. The church isn't open yet, and I haven't finished vacuuming." The man on crutches glared. "Young man, the only reason there's anything left of this building for you to vacuum is because of the gentleman in that woman's pocket."

"I'm sorry?"

"Mrs. Wren," the man on crutches said, "would you please show this rude young man the container of ashes?"

The woman paused, then, as if waking up, reached into her sweater pocket and took out a small cylinder wrapped in cloth.

"There, you see?" the man on crutches snapped. "Now, you can either grant his dying wish and let us sprinkle his ashes in the sanctuary, or I tell Father Knowles how we've been treated." The altar boy hesitated, knowing Father Knowles valued church tradition... and, more importantly, his bad temper when anything made this historic church look bad. Maybe Father Knowles had simply forgotten they were coming. If so, it was riskier to turn them away than to let them in. After all, they said it would only take a minute. What could it hurt?

When the altar boy stepped aside to let the three people pass, he could have sworn Mr. and Mrs. Wren looked just as confused by all of this as he was. Uncertain, the boy returned to his chores, watching them out of the corner of his eye.

Langdon had to smile as the threesome moved deeper into the church.

"Leigh," he whispered, "you lie entirely too well." Teabing's eyes twinkled. "Oxford Theatre Club. They still talk of my Julius Caesar. I'm certain nobody has ever performed the first scene of Act Three with more dedication." Langdon glanced over. "I thought Caesar was dead in that scene." Teabing smirked. "Yes, but my toga tore open when I fell, and I had to lie on stage for half an hour with my 'thing' hanging out. Even so, I never moved a muscle. I was brilliant, I tell you." Langdon cringed. Sorry I missed it.

As the group moved through the rectangular annex toward the archway leading into the main church, Langdon was surprised by the stark simplicity. Although the altar layout resembled that of a standard Christian chapel, the furnishings were bare and cold, lacking any traditional decoration. "Bleak," he whispered.

Teabing chuckled. "Church of England. Anglicans take their religion without any frills. Nothing to distract from their misery."

Sophie motioned through the vast opening that gave way to the circular section of the church. "It looks like a fortress in there," she whispered.

Langdon agreed. Even from here, the walls looked unusually strong.

"The Knights Templar were warriors," Teabing reminded, the sound of his aluminum crutches echoing in this spacious area. "A religious military group. Their churches were their strongholds and their banks."

"Banks?" Sophie asked, glancing at Leigh.

"Heavens, yes. The Templars invented the idea of modern banking. For European nobles, traveling with gold was dangerous, so the Templars allowed nobles to deposit gold in their nearest Temple Church and then withdraw it from any other Temple Church across Europe. All they needed was proper documentation." He winked. "And a small fee. They were the original ATMs." Teabing pointed toward a stained-glass window where the bright sun was shining through a white-clad knight riding a rose-colored horse. "Alanus Marcel," Teabing said, "Master of the Temple in the early twelve hundreds. He and his successors actually held the Parliamentary chair of Primus Baro Angiae."

Langdon was surprised. "First Baron of the Realm?"

Teabing nodded. "The Master of the Temple, some say, had more power than the king." As they got to the circular room, Teabing glanced at the altar boy, who was vacuuming far away. "You know," Teabing whispered to Sophie, "they say the Holy Grail was once kept in this church overnight while the Templars moved it. Can you imagine the four chests of Sangreal papers sitting here with Mary Magdalene's coffin? It gives me chills." Langdon felt chills too as they entered the circular room. He looked around at the room's pale stone wall, seeing carvings of gargoyles, demons, monsters, and suffering faces, all looking in. Under the carvings, a single stone bench went around the whole room.

"Theater in the round," Langdon whispered.

Teabing raised a crutch, pointing to the far left and then to the far right. Langdon had already noticed them.

Ten stone knights.

Five on the left. Five on the right.

Lying flat on the floor, the carved, life-sized figures looked peaceful. The knights wore full armor, shields, and swords, and the tombs made Langdon feel like someone had snuck in and covered the knights in plaster while they were sleeping. All of the figures were worn, but each was clearly different—different armor parts, different leg and arm positions, faces, and markings on their shields. In London lies a knight a Pope interred.

Langdon felt shaky as he moved into the circular room.

This had to be the place.

CHAPTER 84

In an alley full of trash near Temple Church, Rémy Legaludec stopped the Jaguar limousine behind some large trash containers. He turned off the engine and looked around. No one was there. He got out of the car, walked to the back, and climbed into the limousine's main area where the monk was.

Sensing Rémy, the monk in the back came out of a prayer-like state, his red eyes looking more curious than scared. All evening, Rémy had been impressed with how calm this tied-up man could stay. After struggling at first in the Range Rover, the monk seemed to accept what was happening and trust his fate to God.

Rémy loosened his bow tie and unbuttoned his stiff collar, feeling like he could finally breathe. He went to the limousine's bar and poured himself a Smirnoff vodka. He drank it all at once and then poured another. Soon I will be able to relax.

Looking in the bar, Rémy found a regular wine opener and opened the sharp blade. The knife was usually used to cut the foil from wine bottles, but it would be used for something much more dramatic this morning. Rémy turned to face Silas, holding up the shiny blade.

Now fear flashed in those red eyes.

Rémy smiled and moved toward the back of the limousine. The monk pulled away, struggling against his ropes.

"Be still," Rémy whispered, raising the blade.

Silas couldn't believe God had left him. He had even turned the pain of being tied up into a spiritual exercise, asking his aching muscles to remind him of the pain Christ felt. I have been praying all night to be freed. Now, as the knife came down, Silas squeezed his eyes shut.

A sharp pain cut through his shoulder blades. He cried out, unable to believe he was going to die here in the back of this limousine, unable to protect himself. I was doing God's work. The Teacher said he would protect me.

Silas felt the sharp warmth spread across his back and shoulders and could picture his own blood, spilling out over his skin. A piercing pain cut through his thighs now, and he felt the familiar wave of confusion coming—the body's way of dealing with pain. As the sharp heat tore through all his muscles, Silas squeezed his eyes tighter, determined that the last thing he saw would not be his killer. Instead he pictured a younger Bishop Aringarosa, standing before the small church in Spain... the church that he and Silas had built themselves. The beginning of my life.

Silas felt as if his body were burning.

"Have a drink," the man in the tuxedo whispered, his accent French. "It will help your blood flow."

Silas's eyes flew open in surprise. A blurry figure was leaning over him, offering a glass of liquid. A pile of shredded duct tape lay on the floor beside the clean knife.

"Drink this," he repeated. "The pain you feel is the blood going back into your muscles." Silas felt the fiery throbbing turning into a prickling sting. The vodka tasted awful, but he drank it, feeling thankful. Fate had given Silas a lot of bad luck tonight, but God had fixed it all with one amazing turn.

God has not abandoned me.

Silas knew what Bishop Aringarosa would call it.

A miracle.

"I wanted to free you sooner," the servant apologized, "but it wasn't possible. With the police arriving at Château Villette, and then at Biggin Hill airport, this was the first chance I had. You understand, don't you, Silas?"

Silas recoiled, surprised. "You know my name?"

The servant smiled.

Silas sat up, rubbing his stiff muscles, his feelings a mix of disbelief, gratitude, and confusion. "Are you... the Teacher?"

Rémy shook his head, amused by the idea. "I wish I had that kind of power. No, I am not the Teacher. Like you, I serve him. But the Teacher speaks well of you. My name is Rémy." Silas was amazed. "I don't understand. If you work for the Teacher, why did Langdon bring the keystone to your home?"

"Not my home. The home of the world's leading Grail historian, Sir Leigh Teabing."

"But you live there. The chances..."

Rémy smiled, not bothered by the seeming coincidence of Langdon's choice. "It was all very predictable. Robert Langdon had the keystone and needed help. Where better to go than the home of Leigh Teabing? The fact that I live there is why the Teacher contacted me to begin with." He paused. "How do you think the Teacher knows so much about the Grail?"

Now it clicked, and Silas was shocked. The Teacher had recruited a servant who had access to all of Sir Leigh Teabing's research. It was clever.

"I have much to tell you," Rémy said, handing Silas the loaded Heckler Koch pistol. Then he reached through the open divider and took a small revolver from the glove box. "But first, you and I have a job to do."

Captain Fache got off his plane at Biggin Hill and listened in disbelief to the Kent chief inspector's story of what happened in Teabing's hangar.

"I searched the plane myself," the inspector insisted, "and there was no one inside." His voice became arrogant. "And I should add that if Sir Leigh Teabing sues me, I will—"

"Did you question the pilot?"

"Of course not. He's French, and our rules require—"

"Take me to the plane."

At the hangar, Fache quickly spotted a strange blood mark on the ground near where the limousine had been parked. Fache went to the plane and knocked hard on the side.

"This is the captain of the French Judicial Police. Open the door!" The scared pilot opened the hatch and lowered the stairs.

Fache went up. Three minutes later, using his gun, he got a full confession, including a description of the tied-up albino monk. He also learned that the pilot saw Langdon and Sophie leave something in Teabing's safe, a wooden box. The pilot didn't know what was in the box, but said Langdon had been very focused on it during the flight to London.

"Open the safe," Fache demanded.

The pilot looked terrified. "I don't know the code!"

"Too bad. I was going to let you keep your pilot's license." The pilot wrung his hands. "I know some maintenance guys here. Maybe they can drill it open?"

"You have half an hour."

The pilot jumped for his radio.

Fache walked to the back of the plane and poured himself a strong drink. It was early, but he had not slept yet, so this barely counted as drinking before noon. Sitting in a soft bucket seat, he closed his eyes, trying to figure out what was happening. The Kent police's mistake could hurt me badly. Now everyone was looking for a black Jaguar limousine.

Fache's phone rang, and he wished for a moment of quiet. "Hello?"

"I'm on my way to London." It was Bishop Aringarosa. "I'll be there in an hour." Fache sat up. "I thought you were going to Paris."

"I'm very worried. I've changed my plans."

"You shouldn't have."

"Do you have Silas?"

"No. His captors got away from the local police before I landed."

Aringarosa's anger sounded sharp. "You promised you would stop that plane!" Fache lowered his voice. "Bishop, considering your situation, I suggest you don't test my patience today. I will find Silas and the others as soon as I can. Where are you landing?"

"One moment." Aringarosa covered the phone and then came back. "The pilot is trying to get permission to land at Heathrow. I'm his only passenger, but our change of course was unplanned."

"Tell him to come to Biggin Hill Executive Airport in Kent. I'll get him permission to land. If I'm not here when you land, I'll have a car waiting."

"Thank you."

"Like I said when we first talked, Bishop, remember you're not the only one about to lose everything."

CHAPTER 85

Look for the orb that should be on his grave.

Each carved knight in the Temple Church lay on his back, head on a stone block. Sophie felt cold. The poem's "orb" made her think of her grandfather's basement at night.

Hieros Gamos. The orbs.

Sophie wondered if the ritual happened here. The round room seemed perfect for such a pagan act. A stone bench went around a bare floor in the center. A theater in the round, Robert called it. She pictured the room at night, filled with masked people, chanting by torchlight, watching a "sacred communion" in the room's center.

She pushed the image away and went with Langdon and Teabing to the first knights. Teabing wanted a careful search, but Sophie felt eager and walked ahead, quickly looking at the five knights on the left.

Looking closely at these tombs, Sophie saw what was the same and different. Each knight lay on his back, but three had straight legs, while two had crossed legs. This didn't seem to matter for the missing orb. Looking at their clothes, Sophie saw that two knights wore tunics over their armor, while the other three wore long robes. Again, no help. Sophie looked at the only other clear difference—their hand positions. Two knights held swords, two prayed, and one had arms at his side. After looking at the hands for a long time, Sophie shrugged, seeing no sign of a missing orb.

Feeling the cryptex in her pocket, she looked back at Langdon and Teabing. They were moving slowly, still at the third knight, with no luck either. Not wanting to wait, she turned to the second group of knights. As she walked across the open space, she quietly said the poem she now knew by heart.

In London lies a knight a Pope buried.

His work caused a Holy anger.

You seek the orb that should be on his tomb.

It speaks of rosy flesh and a woman's womb.

When Sophie arrived at the second group of knights, she found it was like the first. They all lay in different poses, wearing armor and swords. That is, all except the tenth and last tomb.

Hurrying over to it, she stared down.

No pillow. No armor. No tunic. No sword.

"Robert? Leigh?" she called, her voice echoing in the chamber. "Something's missing over here."

Both men looked up and quickly walked toward her.

"An orb?" Teabing called excitedly. His crutches clicked quickly as he hurried across the room. "Are we missing an orb?"

"Not really," Sophie said, frowning at the tenth tomb. "We seem to be missing a whole knight." Both men arrived beside her and looked down in confusion at the tenth tomb. Instead of a knight lying out in the open, this tomb was a sealed stone casket. The casket was trapezoid-shaped, narrower at the feet, wider at the top, with a pointed lid.

"Why isn't this knight shown?" Langdon asked.

"Interesting," Teabing said, rubbing his chin. "I'd forgotten about this strange thing. It's been years since I was here."

"This coffin," Sophie said, "looks like it was made at the same time and by the same artist as the other nine tombs. So why is this knight in a coffin instead of out in the open?" Teabing shook his head. "It's one of the church's mysteries. As far as I know, no one has ever found an answer for it."

"Hello?" the altar boy said, arriving with a worried look. "Sorry to be rude, but you said you wanted to scatter ashes, and yet you seem to be looking around." Teabing glared at the boy and turned to Langdon. "Mr. Wren, it seems your family's money doesn't buy you as much time as it used to, so maybe we should get the ashes out and get on with it." Teabing turned to Sophie. "Mrs. Wren?"

Sophie played along, pulling the vellum-wrapped cryptex from her pocket.

"Now then," Teabing snapped at the boy, "could you give us some privacy?" The altar boy didn't move. He was watching Langdon closely now. "You look familiar." Teabing sighed. "Maybe because Mr. Wren comes here every year!" Or maybe, Sophie now worried, because he saw Langdon on TV at the Vatican last year.

"I've never met Mr. Wren," the altar boy said.

"You're mistaken," Langdon said politely. "I think we met briefly last year. Father Knowles didn't introduce us properly, but I recognized your face as we came in. I know this is an intrusion, but if you could give me a few more minutes, I've traveled a long way to scatter ashes among these tombs." Langdon spoke his lines with Teabing's convincing tone. The altar boy's face looked even more doubtful. "These aren't tombs."

"I'm sorry?" Langdon said.

"Of course they're tombs," Teabing said. "What are you talking about?" The altar boy shook his head. "Tombs hold bodies. These are statues. Stone tributes to real men. There are no bodies under these figures."

"This is a crypt!" Teabing said.

"Only in old history books. People used to think this was a crypt, but the 1950s renovation proved it wasn't." He turned to Langdon. "And I'm sure Mr. Wren knows that. His family discovered that." An uneasy silence filled the air.

It was broken by a door slamming in the annex.

"That must be Father Knowles," Teabing said. "Maybe you should check?" The altar boy looked unsure but walked back to the annex, leaving Langdon, Sophie, and Teabing looking at each other sadly.

"Leigh," Langdon whispered. "No bodies? What's he talking about?" Teabing looked upset. "I don't know. I always thought... this has to be the place. I don't think he knows what he's talking about. It doesn't make sense!"

"Can I see the poem again?" Langdon asked.

Sophie took the cryptex from her pocket and carefully gave it to him. Langdon unwrapped the vellum, holding the cryptex as he read the poem.

"Yes, the poem definitely talks about a tomb. Not a statue."

"Could the poem be wrong?" Teabing asked. "Could Jacques Saunière have made the same mistake I just did?"

Langdon thought about it and shook his head. "Leigh, you said it yourself. Templars, the Priory's soldiers, built this church. I think the Grand Master of the Priory would know if there were knights buried here."

Teabing looked shocked. "But this place is perfect." He turned back to the knights.

"We must be missing something!"

Walking into the side room, the altar boy was surprised to see no one there. "Father Knowles?" I know I heard the door, he thought, walking until he could see the entrance. A thin man in a tuxedo was standing near the door, scratching his head and looking lost. The altar boy sighed, annoyed that he had forgotten to lock the door after letting the others in. Now some poor guy had wandered in off the street, probably looking for directions to a wedding. "I'm sorry," he called out, walking past a large pillar, "we're closed." A rustle of fabric came from behind him, and before the altar boy could turn, his head jerked back, a strong hand covering his mouth, muffling his scream. The hand over the boy's mouth was snow-white, and he smelled alcohol.

The neat man in the tuxedo calmly pulled out a very small revolver, pointing it right at the boy's forehead.

The altar boy felt his groin get hot and realized he had wet himself.

"Listen carefully," the man in the tuxedo whispered. "You will leave this church quietly, and you will run. You will not stop. Do you understand?"

The boy nodded as much as he could with the hand over his mouth.

"If you call the police..." The man in the tuxedo pressed the gun to his skin. "I will find you." The next thing the boy knew, he was running across the courtyard outside, planning to run until he couldn't anymore.

CHAPTER 86

Like a ghost, Silas moved silently behind his target. Sophie Neveu felt him too late. Before she could turn, Silas pressed the gun against her back and wrapped a strong arm around her chest, pulling her against his large body. She yelled in surprise. Teabing and Langdon both turned, looking shocked and scared.

"What...?" Teabing gasped. "What did you do to Rémy!"

"Your only worry," Silas said calmly, "is that I leave here with the keystone." This recovery mission, as Rémy had called it, was to be clean and simple: Go into the church, take the keystone, and leave; no killing, no fighting.

Holding Sophie tightly, Silas moved his hand from her chest, down to her waist, slipping it inside her sweater pockets, searching. He could smell the soft scent of her hair through his own breath that smelled of alcohol. "Where is it?" he whispered. The keystone was in her sweater pocket before. So where is it now?

"It's over here," Langdon's deep voice echoed from across the room. Silas turned to see Langdon holding the black cryptex in front of him, waving it like a matador teasing a bull.

"Put it down," Silas demanded.

"Let Sophie and Leigh leave the church," Langdon replied. "You and I can deal with this." Silas pushed Sophie away from him and aimed the gun at Langdon, moving toward him.

"Not a step closer," Langdon said. "Not until they leave the building."

"You can't tell me what to do."

"I disagree." Langdon raised the cryptex high over his head. "I will smash this on the floor and break the vial inside if I have to."

Although Silas sneered at the threat, he felt a flash of fear. This was unexpected. He aimed the gun at Langdon's head, his voice as steady as his hand. "You would never break the keystone. You want to find the Grail as much as I do."

"You're wrong. You want it more. You've proven you're willing to kill for it."

Forty feet away, watching from the annex pews near the archway, Rémy Legaludec felt alarm rising. The plan had not gone as expected, and even from there, he could see Silas was unsure what to do. The Teacher had ordered Rémy to forbid Silas from firing his gun.

"Let them go," Langdon demanded again, holding the cryptex high and staring into Silas's gun.

The monk's red eyes filled with anger and frustration, and Rémy feared Silas might shoot Langdon while he was holding the cryptex. The cryptex cannot fall!

The cryptex was Rémy's ticket to freedom and wealth. A little over a year ago, he was just a fifty-five-year-old servant living in Château Villette, catering to the whims of the awful cripple Sir Leigh Teabing. Then he was approached with an amazing offer. Rémy's connection to Sir Leigh Teabing—the world's leading Grail historian—was going to bring Rémy everything he had ever wanted. Since then, every moment in Château Villette had led him to this moment. I am so close, Rémy thought, gazing into the Temple Church and the keystone in Robert Langdon's hand. If Langdon dropped it, all would be lost.

Am I willing to show my face? The Teacher had strictly forbidden it. Rémy was the only one who knew the Teacher's identity.

"Are you sure you want Silas to do this?" Rémy had asked the Teacher less than half an hour ago, when he got orders to steal the keystone. "I am capable myself." The Teacher was firm. "Silas served us well with the four Priory members. He will get the keystone. You must remain hidden. If others see you, they will need to be killed, and there has been enough killing already. Do not show your face."

My face will change, Rémy thought. With what you've promised to pay me, I will become a completely new man. Surgery could even change his fingerprints, the Teacher had said. Soon he would be free—another unrecognizable, beautiful face soaking up the sun on the beach.

"Understood," Rémy said. "I will help Silas from the shadows."

"Just so you know, Rémy," the Teacher had told him, "the tomb isn't in the Temple Church. Don't worry. They're looking in the wrong place." Rémy was shocked. "And you know where the tomb is?"

"Of course. I'll tell you later. For now, you must act fast. If the others figure out where the tomb really is and leave the church before you get the cryptex, we could lose the Grail forever."

Rémy didn't care about the Grail, except that the Teacher wouldn't pay him until it was found. Rémy felt excited every time he thought about the money he'd soon have. One third of twenty million euro. Enough to disappear forever. Rémy had imagined the beach towns on the Côte d'Azur, where he planned to spend his life in the sun and be waited on for a change.

But now, here in the Temple Church, with Langdon threatening to break the keystone, Rémy's future was in danger. He couldn't stand the thought of almost getting there and then losing everything, so Rémy decided to act. The gun in his hand was a small, hidden Jframe Medusa, but it would be deadly enough up close. Stepping out of the shadows, Rémy walked into the circular room and pointed the gun right at Teabing's head. "Old man, I've been waiting a long time for this."

Sir Leigh Teabing's heart almost stopped when he saw Rémy pointing a gun at him. What is he doing!

Teabing recognized the small Medusa revolver as his own, the one he kept locked in the limousine's glove box for safety.

"Rémy?" Teabing stammered in shock. "What's going on?" Langdon and Sophie looked just as stunned.

Rémy moved behind Teabing and pressed the gun into his back, high on the left, right behind his heart.

Teabing felt his muscles tighten with fear. "Rémy, I don't—"

"I'll make it easy," Rémy snapped, watching Langdon over Teabing's shoulder. "Put down the keystone, or I shoot."

Langdon seemed frozen for a moment. "The keystone is useless to you," he stuttered. "You can't open it."

"Arrogant fools," Rémy scoffed. "Haven't you noticed I've been listening tonight as you talked about these poems? Everything I heard, I've told others. Others who know more than you. You're not even looking in the right place. The tomb you're looking for is somewhere else!"

Teabing felt panicked. What is he saying!

"Why do you want the Grail?" Langdon asked. "To destroy it? Before the End of Days?" Rémy called to the monk. "Silas, take the keystone from Mr. Langdon." As the monk moved forward, Langdon stepped back, holding the keystone high, ready to throw it to the floor.

"I'd rather break it," Langdon said, "than see it in the wrong hands." Teabing now felt a wave of horror. He could see his life's work disappearing before his eyes. All his dreams about to be ruined.

"Robert, no!" Teabing cried out. "Don't! That's the Grail you're holding! Rémy would never shoot me. We've known each other for ten—"

Rémy aimed at the ceiling and fired the Medusa. The blast was huge for such a small weapon, the gunshot sounding like thunder inside the stone room.

Everyone froze.

"I am not joking," Rémy said. "The next one is in his back. Give the keystone to Silas." Langdon slowly held out the cryptex. Silas stepped forward and took it, his red eyes shining with the joy of revenge. Putting the keystone in the pocket of his robe, Silas stepped back, still holding Langdon and Sophie at gunpoint.

Teabing felt Rémy's arm tighten around his neck as the servant started to leave, pulling Teabing with him, the gun still in his back.

"Let him go," Langdon demanded.

"We're taking Mr. Teabing for a drive," Rémy said, still moving backward. "If you call the police, he will die. If you try to stop us, he will die. Understand?"

"Take me," Langdon demanded, his voice breaking. "Let Leigh go." Rémy laughed. "I don't think so. He and I have such a good history. Besides, he might still be useful."

Silas was also backing up now, holding Langdon and Sophie at gunpoint while Rémy pulled Leigh toward the exit, his crutches scraping behind him.

Sophie's voice was steady. "Who are you working for?"

The question made Rémy smirk as he left. "You'd be surprised, Mademoiselle Neveu."

CHAPTER 87

The fireplace in Château Villette's drawing room was cold, but Collet still paced in front of it as he read the faxes from Interpol.

Not what he expected at all.

According to official records, André Vernet was a perfect citizen. No police record—not even a parking ticket. He went to prep school and the Sorbonne, earning a degree in international finance with honors. Interpol said Vernet's name sometimes appeared in the newspapers, but always positively. Apparently, he had helped design the security measures that kept the Depository Bank of Zurich a leader in electronic security. Vernet's credit card records showed he liked art books, expensive wine, and classical CDs—mostly Brahms—which he enjoyed on a very high-quality stereo system he had bought years ago.

Nothing, Collet sighed.

The only warning sign from Interpol tonight was a set of fingerprints that belonged to Teabing's servant. The chief PTS examiner was reading the report in a comfortable chair across the room.

Collet looked over. "Anything?"

The examiner shrugged. "Prints belong to Rémy Legaludec. Wanted for minor crimes. Nothing serious. He was kicked out of university for rewiring phone jacks to get free service... later, he did some petty theft, breaking and entering, and skipped out on a hospital bill for an emergency tracheotomy." He looked up, chuckling. "Peanut allergy." Collet nodded, remembering a police investigation into a restaurant that hadn't noted on its menu that its chili contained peanut oil. A customer died of anaphylactic shock at the table after one bite.

"Legaludec probably lives here to avoid getting caught." The examiner looked amused.

"His lucky night."

Collet sighed. "Okay, send this to Captain Fache." The examiner left as another PTS agent rushed in. "Lieutenant! We found something in the barn."

From the agent's worried face, Collet guessed, "A body?"

"No, sir. Something more..." He paused. "Unexpected." Rubbing his eyes, Collet followed the agent to the barn. Inside the musty, large space, the agent pointed to the center, where a wooden ladder reached up to the hayloft, leaning against its edge.

"That ladder wasn't there before," Collet said.

"No, sir. I put it there. We were dusting for prints near the Rolls when I saw the ladder on the floor. I almost ignored it, but the rungs were worn and muddy. This ladder is used often. The hayloft's height matched the ladder, so I put it up and climbed to take a look."

Collet looked up the ladder to the high hayloft. Someone goes up there often? From below, the loft looked like an empty platform, but most of it was hidden from view.

A senior PTS agent appeared at the top of the ladder, looking down. "You need to see this, Lieutenant," he said, waving Collet up with a gloved hand. Nodding, Collet walked to the ladder and grabbed the bottom rungs. The ladder was old and got narrower as he climbed. Near the top, Collet almost slipped on a thin rung. The barn spun below him. Now alert, he kept going, reaching the top. The agent reached out, offering his wrist. Collet grabbed it and stepped onto the platform.

"It's over there," the PTS agent said, pointing into the clean loft. "Only one set of prints up here. We'll know who it is soon."

Collet squinted at the far wall in the dim light. What the hell? Against the wall was a complete computer setup—two CPUs, a flat-screen monitor with speakers, many hard drives, and a sound console with its own power supply.

Why would anyone work up here? Collet went toward the equipment. "Have you checked the system?"

"It's a listening post."

Collet spun around. "Surveillance?"

The agent nodded. "Very advanced surveillance." He pointed to a long table covered with electronic parts, manuals, tools, wires, soldering irons, and other electronic components. "Someone really knows what they're doing. A lot of this equipment is as good as our own. Tiny microphones, photoelectric recharging cells, high-capacity memory chips. He's even got some of those new nano drives."

Collet was impressed.

"Here's a complete system," the agent said, handing Collet a device about the size of a pocket calculator. A foot-long wire hung from it, with a stamp-sized piece of thin foil at the end. "The base is a high-capacity hard disk audio recording system with a rechargeable battery. That foil strip is both a microphone and a photoelectric recharging cell."

Collet knew them well. These foil microphones that used photocells had been a major innovation a few years ago. Now, a hard disk recorder could be placed behind a lamp, for example, with its foil microphone shaped to fit the lamp's base and colored to match. If the microphone got a few hours of sunlight each day, the photocells would keep recharging the system. Bugs like this could listen forever.

"How does it send the information?" Collet asked.

The agent pointed to an insulated wire running from the back of the computer, up the wall, and through a hole in the barn roof. "Simple radio wave. Small antenna on the roof." Collet knew these recording systems were usually placed in offices. They were voice-activated to save disk space, and recorded bits of conversation during the day, sending compressed audio files at night to avoid being detected. After sending the files, the hard drive erased itself and got ready to do it again the next day.

Collet now looked at a shelf holding hundreds of audio cassettes, all labeled with dates and numbers. Someone has been very busy. He turned back to the agent. "Do you know who's being bugged?"

"Well, Lieutenant," the agent said, walking to the computer and starting a program. "It's the strangest thing...."

CHAPTER 88

Langdon felt drained as he and Sophie rushed through a turnstile at the Temple tube station and hurried into the dirty maze of tunnels and platforms. Guilt tore through him. I brought Leigh into this, and now he's in terrible danger.

Rémy's involvement was a surprise, but it also made sense. Whoever was after the Grail had recruited someone on the inside. They went to Teabing's for the same reason I did. Throughout history, those who knew about the Grail had always attracted both thieves and scholars. The fact that Teabing had been a target all along should have made Langdon feel less guilty about involving him. It didn't. We need to find Leigh and help him. Right away. Langdon followed Sophie to the westbound District and Circle Line platform, where she rushed to a pay phone to call the police, despite Rémy's warning against it. Langdon sat on a dirty bench nearby, feeling regretful.

"The best way to help Leigh," Sophie repeated as she dialed, "is to involve the London authorities immediately. Trust me."

Langdon hadn't agreed at first, but as they planned, Sophie's logic started to make sense. Teabing was safe for now. Even if Rémy and the others knew where the knight's tomb was, they might still need Teabing's help to figure out the orb reference. Langdon worried about what would happen after the Grail map was found. Leigh will become a big problem.

If Langdon had any chance of helping Leigh, or of ever seeing the keystone again, he had to find the tomb first. Unfortunately, Rémy has a big head start. Slowing Rémy down was Sophie's job.

Finding the right tomb was Langdon's.

Sophie would make Rémy and Silas wanted by the London police, forcing them to hide or, even better, catching them. Langdon's plan was less certain—to take the tube to nearby King's College, known for its electronic theology database. The ultimate research tool, Langdon had heard. Instant answers to any religious history question. He wondered what the database would say about "a knight a Pope interred."

He stood up and paced, wishing the train would come soon.

At the pay phone, Sophie finally connected to the London police.

"Snow Hill Division," the dispatcher said. "How can I help you?"

"I'm reporting a kidnapping." Sophie knew to be brief.

"Name please?"

Sophie hesitated. "Agent Sophie Neveu, French Judicial Police." The title worked. "Yes, ma'am. Let me get a detective for you." As the call connected, Sophie wondered if the police would believe her description of Teabing's captors. A man in a tux. Could a suspect be easier to spot? Even if Rémy changed, his partner was an albino monk. Hard to miss. Plus, they had a hostage and couldn't use public transport. She wondered how many Jaguar limos were in London.

Sophie waited for the detective. Come on! She heard clicks and buzzes, like she was being moved to another line.

Fifteen seconds passed.

Finally, a man answered. "Agent Neveu?"

Shocked, Sophie recognized the rough voice.

"Agent Neveu," Bezu Fache demanded. "Where are you?" Sophie was silent. Captain Fache had asked the London police to tell him if Sophie called.

"Listen," Fache said, speaking sharply in French. "I made a mistake tonight. Robert Langdon is innocent. All charges are dropped. But you're both in danger. You need to come in."

Sophie's mouth dropped open. She didn't know what to say. Fache wasn't one to apologize.
