"You didn't tell me," Fache went on, "that Jacques Saunière was your grandfather. I'm willing to ignore your disobedience last night because of the emotional stress you're under. But right now, you and Langdon need to get to the closest London police station for safety."

He knows I'm in London? What else does Fache know? Sophie heard what sounded like drilling or machinery in the background. She also heard a strange clicking on the line. "Are you tracking this call, Captain?"

Fache's voice was strong now. "We need to work together, Agent Neveu. We both have a lot at stake. This is about limiting the damage. I made mistakes last night, and if those mistakes lead to the deaths of an American professor and a DCPJ cryptologist, my career will be over. I've been trying to get you to safety for hours."

A warm wind blew through the station as a train came closer with a low rumble. Sophie planned to be on it. Langdon seemed to have the same idea; he was getting ready and walking toward her.

"The man you want is Rémy Legaludec," Sophie said. "He's Teabing's servant. He just kidnapped Teabing inside the Temple Church and—"

"Agent Neveu!" Fache shouted as the train roared into the station. "This isn't something to talk about on a public line. You and Langdon will come in now. For your own safety! That's a direct order!"

Sophie hung up and rushed onto the train with Langdon.

CHAPTER 89

The spotless inside of Teabing's plane was now covered in metal bits and smelled like air and gas. Bezu Fache had sent everyone away and sat alone with his drink and the heavy wooden box found in Teabing's safe.

Running his finger across the rose design, he lifted the fancy lid. Inside, he found a stone tube with dials that had letters on them. The five dials spelled SOFIA. Fache stared at the word for a moment, then lifted the tube and looked at every part of it. Then, pulling on the ends, Fache slid off one of the caps. The tube was empty. Fache put it back in the box and looked out the plane's window at the hangar, thinking about his short talk with Sophie and the information from PTS in Château Villette. The sound of his phone pulled him out of his thoughts.

It was the DCPJ switchboard. The person on the phone apologized. The president of the Zurich bank had called many times, and even though he was told that the captain was in London for work, he kept calling. Reluctantly, Fache told the operator to put the call through.

"Monsieur Vernet," Fache said before the man could speak, "I am sorry I didn't call you sooner. I have been busy. As I promised, your bank's name hasn't been in the news. So, what exactly is your problem?"

Vernet sounded worried as he told Fache how Langdon and Sophie had taken a small wooden box from the bank and then convinced Vernet to help them escape. "Then, when I heard on the radio that they were criminals," Vernet said, "I stopped the truck and demanded the box back, but they attacked me and stole the truck."

"You're worried about a wooden box," Fache said, looking at the rose design on the cover and gently opening the lid to see the white tube. "Can you tell me what was in the box?"

"What's inside doesn't matter," Vernet snapped back. "I am worried about my bank's reputation. We have never had a robbery. Ever. It will ruin us if I can't get this item back for my client."

"You said Agent Neveu and Robert Langdon had a password and a key. Why do you say they stole the box?"

"They murdered people tonight, including Sophie Neveu's grandfather. They obviously got the key and password illegally."

"Mr. Vernet, my men have looked into your background and interests. It's clear you're a man of culture and taste. I imagine you're a man of honor, too. As am I. With that said, I give you my word as head of the Police Judiciaire that your box, and your bank's good name, are completely safe."

CHAPTER 90

High in the hayloft at Château Villette, Collet stared at the computer monitor in amazement. "This system is listening in on all these places?"

"Yes," the agent said. "It seems data has been gathered for over a year now." Collet read the list again, speechless.

COLBERT SOSTAQUE—Chairman of the Conseil Constitutionnel

JEAN CHAFFÉE—Curator, Musée du Jeu de Paume

EDOUARD DESROCHERS—Senior Archivist, Mitterrand Library

JACQUES SAUNIÈRE—Curator, Musée du Louvre

MICHEL BRETON—Head of DAS (French Intelligence)

The agent pointed to the screen. "Number four is clearly a problem." Collet nodded, stunned. He had noticed it right away. Jacques Saunière was being spied on. He looked at the rest of the list again. How could anyone possibly spy on these important people? "Have you listened to any of the audio files?"

"A few. Here's one of the newest." The agent clicked a few computer keys. The speakers crackled to life. "Capitaine, un agent du Département de Cryptographie est arriv é ." Collet could not believe what he was hearing. "That's me! That's my voice!" He remembered sitting at Saunière's desk and radioing Fache in the Grand Gallery to tell him about Sophie Neveu's arrival. The agent nodded. "A lot of our Louvre investigation tonight would have been heard if someone was interested."

"Did you have someone check for the bug?"

"No need. I know exactly where it is." The agent went to a pile of old notes and blueprints on the worktable. He picked out a page and handed it to Collet. "Look familiar?" Collet was amazed. He was holding a copy of an old diagram, showing a simple machine. He couldn't read the handwritten Italian labels, but he knew what it was. A model for a fully working medieval French knight. The knight sitting on Saunière's desk!

Collet's eyes moved to the edges, where someone had written notes on the copy in red marker. The notes were in French and seemed to be ideas about how to put a listening device into the knight.

CHAPTER 91

Silas sat in the passenger seat of the parked Jaguar limousine near the Temple Church. His hands felt a little sweaty on the keystone as he waited for Rémy to finish tying up and gagging Teabing in the back with the rope they had found in the trunk.

Finally, Rémy got out of the back of the limo, walked around, and slid into the driver's seat next to Silas.

"Safe?" Silas asked.

Rémy chuckled, shaking off the rain and looking over his shoulder through the open window at the slumped shape of Leigh Teabing, who was hard to see in the shadows in the back. "He's not going anywhere."

Silas could hear Teabing's quiet cries and realized Rémy had used some of the old duct tape to gag him.

"Shut your mouth!" Rémy shouted over his shoulder at Teabing. Reaching to a control panel on the fancy dashboard, Rémy pressed a button. A dark window rose behind them, closing off the back. Teabing disappeared, and his voice was silenced. Rémy glanced at Silas. "I've been listening to his annoying whining long enough."

Minutes later, as the Jaguar stretch limo drove through the streets, Silas's cell phone rang. The Teacher. He answered eagerly. "Hello?"

"Silas," the Teacher's familiar French accent said, "I'm glad to hear your voice. This means you are safe."

Silas was also comforted to hear the Teacher. It had been hours, and the plan had gone very wrong. Now, at last, it seemed to be going right again. "I have the keystone."

"This is wonderful news," the Teacher told him. "Is Rémy with you?" Silas was surprised to hear the Teacher say Rémy's name. "Yes. Rémy set me free."

"As I told him to do. I'm just sorry you were held captive for so long."

"Physical discomfort doesn't matter. The important thing is that we have the keystone."

"Yes. I need it brought to me right away. Time is crucial." Silas was excited to finally meet the Teacher in person. "Yes, sir, I would be honored."

"Silas, I want Rémy to bring it to me."

Rémy? Silas was crushed. After all he had done for the Teacher, he thought he would be the one to give him the prize. Does the Teacher like Rémy better?

"I can tell you're disappointed," the Teacher said, "which means you don't understand what I'm planning." He lowered his voice. "Believe me, I'd much rather get the keystone from you—a man of God instead of a criminal—but Rémy needs to be taken care of. He didn't follow my orders and made a big mistake that has put our whole mission in danger." Silas felt a chill and looked at Rémy. Kidnapping Teabing wasn't part of the plan, and figuring out what to do with him was a new issue.

"You and I are men of God," the Teacher whispered. "We can't let anything stop us from our goal." He paused ominously. "That's why I'm asking Rémy to bring me the keystone. Do you understand?"

Silas heard anger in the Teacher's voice and was surprised he wasn't more understanding. He had to show his face, Silas thought. Rémy did what he had to do. He saved the keystone. "I understand," Silas said.

"Good. For your own safety, get off the street immediately. The police will be looking for the limousine soon, and I don't want you caught. Opus Dei has a place to stay in London, right?"

"Of course."

"And they want you there?"

"Like a brother."

"Then go there and stay hidden. I will call you as soon as I have the keystone and have taken care of my problem."

"You are in London?"

"Do as I say, and everything will be fine."

"Yes, sir."

The Teacher sighed, as if what he had to do now was deeply sad. "It's time I speak to Rémy."

Silas handed Rémy the phone, sensing it might be the last call Rémy Legaludec ever took.

As Rémy took the phone, he knew this poor, twisted monk had no idea what was going to happen to him now that he had done what he was needed for.

The Teacher used you, Silas.

And your bishop is a pawn.

Rémy still wondered how the Teacher could be so convincing. Bishop Aringarosa had believed everything, blinded by his own need to believe. Aringarosa wanted to believe too much. Rémy didn't really like the Teacher, but he was proud to have earned his trust and helped him so much. I've earned my reward.

"Listen closely," the Teacher said. "Take Silas to the Opus Dei dorm and drop him off a few streets away. Then drive to St. James's Park, next to Parliament and Big Ben. Park the limousine on Horse Guards Parade. We'll talk there."

Then, the line went dead.

CHAPTER 92

King's College, founded by King George IV in 1829, has its Theology and Religious Studies Department next to Parliament on land given by the Crown. The Religion Department at King's College not only has 150 years of teaching and research experience but also established the Research Institute in Systematic Theology in 1982. This institute has one of the most complete and technologically advanced religious research libraries in the world. Langdon still felt unsteady as he and Sophie came in from the rain and entered the library. The main research room was as Teabing had described—a striking octagonal room with a huge round table as its centerpiece. King Arthur and his knights might have felt at home there, except for the twelve flat-screen computer workstations. On the other side of the room, a reference librarian was just pouring tea and getting ready for her day.

"Lovely morning," she said with a cheerful British accent, leaving the tea and walking over. "May I help you?"

"Thank you, yes," Langdon replied. "My name is—"

"Robert Langdon." She smiled kindly. "I know who you are." For a moment, he worried Fache had put him on English television too, but the librarian's smile suggested otherwise. Langdon still wasn't used to these moments of sudden fame. Then again, if anyone would recognize him, it would be a librarian in a Religious Studies library.

"Pamela Gettum," the librarian said, offering her hand. She had a friendly, intelligent face and a smooth voice. The thick glasses hanging around her neck looked scholarly.

"A pleasure," Langdon said. "This is my friend Sophie Neveu." The two women greeted each other, and Gettum turned back to Langdon right away. "I didn't know you were coming."

"Neither did we. If it's not too much trouble, we really need your help finding some information."

Gettum paused, looking unsure. "Normally, we only offer our services by request and appointment, unless you're a guest of someone at the college?"

Langdon shook his head. "I'm sorry, we didn't call ahead. A friend of mine thinks very highly of you. Sir Leigh Teabing?" Saying the name made Langdon feel a little sad. "The British Royal Historian."

Gettum's face lit up, and she laughed. "Oh, yes. What a guy. Obsessed! Every time he comes in, he always searches for the same things. Grail. Grail. Grail. I bet he'll keep looking until his dying day." She winked. "Having time and money lets you do fun things like that, right? He's like Don Quixote, chasing a dream."

"Can you help us?" Sophie asked. "It's really important." Gettum looked around the empty library and then winked at them. "Well, I can't say I'm busy, can I? As long as you sign in, I don't think anyone will mind. What are you looking for?"

"We're trying to find a tomb in London."

Gettum looked unsure. "We have about twenty thousand of them. Can you be more specific?"

"It's the tomb of a knight. We don't know his name."

"A knight. That narrows it down a lot. Much less common."

"We don't have much information about the knight," Sophie said, "but here's what we know." She showed a piece of paper with just the first two lines of the poem on it.

Langdon and Sophie didn't want to show the whole poem to a stranger, so they only shared the first two lines, which named the knight. Sophie called it "divided secrecy." When a spy agency found a code with important information, each code breaker worked on a separate part of it. That way, no one person knew the whole message.

In this case, it was probably too careful. Even if the librarian saw the whole poem, found the knight's tomb, and knew about the missing orb, the information wouldn't help without the cryptex.

Gettum saw a pressing need in the famous American scholar's eyes, as if finding this tomb quickly was vital. The green-eyed woman with him also seemed worried.

Confused, Gettum put on her glasses and looked at the paper they had given her. "In London lies a knight a Pope interred.

His labor's fruit a Holy wrath incurred."

She looked at her guests. "What's this? A Harvard game?" Langdon's laugh seemed fake. "Yeah, something like that."

Gettum paused, feeling she wasn't getting the full story. Still, she was curious and thought about the verse. "This rhyme says a knight angered God, but a Pope buried him in London." Langdon nodded. "Does it sound familiar?"

Gettum walked to a workstation. "Not right away, but let's check the database."

For twenty years, King's College Research Institute in Systematic Theology had used software to scan and translate a huge collection of texts—religious encyclopedias, biographies, scriptures in many languages, histories, Vatican letters, diaries, anything about human spirituality. Now that the collection was digital, the data was much easier to access. Settling at a workstation, Gettum looked at the paper and began typing. "First, we'll search using simple keywords and see what we find."

"Thank you."

Gettum typed:

LONDON, KNIGHT, POPE

As she clicked the SEARCH button, she felt the hum of the big computer downstairs scanning data at 500 MB/sec. "I'm asking the system to find any documents with all three keywords in the text. We'll get more results than we need, but it's a good start."

The screen was already showing the first results.

Painting the Pope. The Collected Portraits of Sir Joshua Reynolds. London University Press.

Gettum shook her head. "Not what you're looking for." She scrolled to the next result. The London Writings of Alexander Pope by G. Wilson Knight.

Again, she shook her head.

As the system worked, the results came up faster than usual. Dozens of texts appeared, many about the eighteenth-century British writer Alexander Pope, whose anti-religious, mock-heroic poetry seemed to have many references to knights and London.

Gettum glanced at the number at the bottom of the screen. The computer guessed how much information would be found by calculating the current number of results and how much of the database was left to search. This search looked like it would return a huge amount of data.

Estimated number of total hits: 2,692

"We need to narrow the search," Gettum said, stopping it. "Is this all the information you have about the tomb? Nothing else to add?" Langdon looked at Sophie Neveu, uncertain.

This is no game, Gettum thought. She had heard about Robert Langdon's experience in Rome last year. This American had been allowed into the most secure library on earth—the Vatican Secret Archives. She wondered what secrets Langdon might have learned there and if his desperate search for a mysterious London tomb was related to that information. Gettum had been a librarian long enough to know why people usually looked for knights in London. The Grail. Gettum smiled and adjusted her glasses. "You're friends with Leigh Teabing, you're in England, and you're looking for a knight." She folded her hands. "I can only guess you're on a Grail quest."

Langdon and Sophie exchanged startled looks.

Gettum laughed. "My friends, this library is a hub for Grail seekers. Leigh Teabing among them. I wish I had a dollar for every time I'd searched for the Rose, Mary Magdalene, Sangreal, Merovingian, Priory of Sion, and so on. Everyone loves a conspiracy." She took off her glasses and looked at them. "I need more information."

In the silence, Gettum sensed her guests' wish for privacy was quickly being overcome by their need for a quick answer.

"Here," Sophie Neveu blurted. "This is everything we know." Taking a pen from Langdon, she wrote two more lines on the slip of paper and handed it to Gettum.

You seek the orb that should be on his tomb.

It speaks of Rosy flesh and seeded womb.

Gettum smiled to herself. The Grail indeed, she thought, noticing the references to the Rose and her seeded womb. "I can help you," she said, looking up from the slip of paper. "May I ask where this verse came from? And why you are seeking an orb?"

"You may ask," Langdon said, with a friendly smile, "but it's a long story and we have very little time."

"Sounds like a polite way of saying 'it's none of your concern.' "

"We would be forever grateful, Pamela," Langdon said, "if you could find out who this knight is and where he is buried."

"Very well," Gettum said, typing again. "I'll play along. If this has to do with the Grail, we should check it against Grail keywords. I'll add a closeness setting and remove the title importance. That will limit our results to only those times the keywords appear near a Grail-related word."

Search for: KNIGHT, LONDON, POPE, TOMB

Within 100 words of: GRAIL, ROSE, SANGREAL, CHALICE

"How long will this take?" Sophie asked.

"A few hundred terabytes with many cross-references?" Gettum's eyes sparkled as she clicked the SEARCH key. "Just fifteen minutes."

Langdon and Sophie said nothing, but Gettum knew this sounded like forever to them.

"Tea?" Gettum asked, standing and walking toward the pot she had made earlier. "Leigh always loves my tea."

CHAPTER 93

London's Opus Dei Centre is a simple brick building at 5 Orme Court, looking out over the North Walk in Kensington Gardens. Silas had never been here, but he felt a growing sense of safety as he walked toward the building. Even though it was raining, Rémy had dropped him off nearby to keep the limousine away from the main roads. Silas didn't mind the walk. The rain felt like it was cleaning him.

As Rémy suggested, Silas had wiped down his gun and thrown it into a sewer. He was happy to be rid of it. He felt better. His legs still hurt from being tied up for so long, but Silas had suffered much worse pain. He wondered, though, about Teabing, whom Rémy had left tied up in the back of the limousine. The Briton must be in pain by now.

"What will you do with him?" Silas had asked Rémy as they drove here. Rémy had shrugged. "That's for the Teacher to decide." His tone seemed very final. Now, as Silas neared the Opus Dei building, the rain started coming down harder, soaking his heavy robe and stinging the wounds from the day before. He was ready to leave behind the wrongdoings of the past day and cleanse his soul. He had finished his task.

Walking across a small courtyard to the front door, Silas wasn't surprised to find it unlocked. He opened it and entered the plain foyer. A soft electronic chime rang upstairs as Silas stepped onto the carpet. The bell was common in these buildings, where the residents spent most of the day praying in their rooms. Silas could hear movement on the creaky wood floors above.

A man in a cloak came downstairs. "May I help you?" His eyes were kind and didn't seem to notice Silas's shocking appearance.

"Thank you. My name is Silas. I am an Opus Dei member."

"American?"

Silas nodded. "I'm only in town for the day. Could I rest here?"

"You don't even need to ask. There are two empty rooms on the third floor. Shall I bring you some tea and bread?"

"Thank you." Silas was very hungry.

Silas went upstairs to a simple room with a window. He took off his wet robe and prayed in his underwear. He heard his host come up and leave a tray outside his door. Silas finished praying, ate his food, and went to sleep.

Three floors below, a phone rang. The Opus Dei member who had welcomed Silas answered it.

"This is the London police," the caller said. "We're looking for an albino monk. We heard he might be there. Have you seen him?" The member was surprised. "Yes, he's here. Is something wrong?"

"Is he there now?"

"Yes, upstairs praying. What's happening?"

"Leave him exactly where he is," the officer ordered. "Don't tell anyone. I'm sending officers over immediately."

CHAPTER 94

St. James's Park is a green space in the middle of London, a public park next to the palaces of Westminster, Buckingham, and St. James's. King Henry VIII once fenced it in and filled it with deer for hunting. Now, St. James's Park is open to everyone. On sunny afternoons, Londoners picnic under the willow trees and feed the pelicans in the pond, whose ancestors were a gift to Charles II from the Russian ambassador.

The Teacher didn't see any pelicans today. The stormy weather had brought seagulls from the ocean instead. The lawns were covered with them—hundreds of white birds all facing the same way, patiently waiting out the damp wind. Despite the morning fog, the park had great views of the Houses of Parliament and Big Ben. Looking across the sloping lawns, past the duck pond and the delicate shapes of the weeping willows, the Teacher could see the spires of the building that held the knight's tomb—the real reason he had told Rémy to come here. As the Teacher walked up to the front passenger door of the parked limousine, Rémy leaned over and opened the door. The Teacher paused outside, taking a drink from the flask of cognac he was carrying. Then, wiping his mouth, he slid in beside Rémy and closed the door. Rémy held up the keystone like a trophy. "It was almost lost."

"You have done well," the Teacher said.

"We have done well," Rémy replied, placing the keystone in the Teacher's eager hands. The Teacher admired it for a moment, smiling. "And the gun? You wiped it down?"

"Back in the glove box where I found it."

"Excellent." The Teacher took another drink of cognac and handed the flask to Rémy. "Let's toast our success. The end is near."

Rémy took the bottle gratefully. The cognac tasted salty, but Rémy didn't care. He and the Teacher were truly partners now. He could feel himself rising to a higher position in life. I will never be a servant again. As Rémy looked down the embankment at the duck pond below, Château Villette seemed miles away.

Taking another drink from the flask, Rémy could feel the cognac warming his blood. The warmth in Rémy's throat, however, quickly changed to an uncomfortable heat. Loosening his bow tie, Rémy tasted an unpleasant grittiness and handed the flask back to the Teacher. "I've probably had enough," he managed, weakly.

Taking the flask, the Teacher said, "Rémy, as you know, you are the only one who knows my face. I placed enormous trust in you."

"Yes," he said, feeling hot as he loosened his tie more. "And your secret dies with me."

The Teacher was quiet for a moment. "I believe you." He put the flask and the keystone in his pocket, then reached for the glove box and took out the small Medusa revolver. Rémy felt a quick flash of fear, but the Teacher just slipped it into his pants pocket. What's he doing? Rémy suddenly felt himself sweating.

"I know I promised you freedom," the Teacher said, his voice sounding sad now. "But given the situation, this is the best I can do."

Rémy's throat tightened suddenly, and he leaned against the steering wheel, grabbing his throat and tasting vomit in his throat. He made a quiet, choked scream, not even loud enough to be heard outside the car. He now tasted the salt in the cognac.

I'm being murdered!

In disbelief, Rémy turned to see the Teacher sitting calmly beside him, staring straight ahead. Rémy's vision blurred, and he gasped for air. I made everything possible for him! How could he do this! Rémy would never know if the Teacher had always planned to kill him, or if it was Rémy's actions in the Temple Church that had made the Teacher change his mind. Terror and anger rushed through him. Rémy tried to lunge at the Teacher, but his body was stiffening and he could barely move. I trusted you with everything!

Rémy tried to lift his clenched fists to honk the horn, but instead he slipped sideways, rolling onto the seat, lying on his side next to the Teacher, clutching at his throat. The rain fell harder now. Rémy could no longer see, but he could feel his brain struggling to hold onto his last clear thoughts as it ran out of oxygen. As his world slowly went dark, Rémy Legaludec thought he heard the soft sounds of the Riviera surf.

The Teacher stepped out of the limousine, relieved that no one was watching him. I had no choice, he told himself, surprised at how little guilt he felt for what he had just done. Rémy brought this on himself. The Teacher had always worried that Rémy might have to be killed when the mission was over, but Rémy's bold appearance in the Temple Church had made it necessary much sooner. Robert Langdon's unexpected visit to Château Villette had given the Teacher both a lucky break and a difficult problem. Langdon had delivered the keystone right to their doorstep, which was a nice surprise, but he had also brought the police with him. Rémy's fingerprints were all over Château Villette, and in the barn's listening post, where Rémy had been doing surveillance. The Teacher was glad he had been careful to hide any connection between Rémy's actions and himself. No one could blame the Teacher unless Rémy talked, and that was no longer a concern. One more loose end to take care of, the Teacher thought, moving toward the back door of the limousine. The police will have no idea what happened... and no one will be left alive to tell them. Looking around to make sure no one was watching, he opened the door and climbed into the spacious back seat.

Minutes later, the Teacher was walking across St. James's Park. Only two people left now. Langdon and Neveu. They were more difficult. But manageable. Right now, though, the Teacher had to focus on the cryptex.

Looking proudly across the park, he could see where he was going. In London lies a knight a Pope interred. As soon as the Teacher had heard the poem, he had known the answer. Still, he wasn't surprised that the others hadn't figured it out. I have an unfair advantage. Having listened to Saunière's conversations for months, the Teacher had heard the Grand Master mention this famous knight sometimes, showing almost as much respect as he had for Da Vinci. The poem's reference to the knight was very simple once you saw it—a sign of Saunière's cleverness—but how this tomb would reveal the final password was still a mystery.

You seek the orb that should be on his tomb.

The Teacher vaguely remembered photos of the famous tomb and, especially, its most noticeable feature. A magnificent orb. The huge sphere on top of the tomb was almost as big as the tomb itself. The orb's presence felt both hopeful and worrying to the Teacher. On one hand, it seemed like a sign, yet, according to the poem, the missing piece was an orb that should be on his tomb... not one that was already there. He was counting on examining the tomb closely to reveal the answer.

The rain was getting heavier now, and he tucked the cryptex deep in his right pocket to protect it from the dampness. He kept the tiny Medusa revolver in his left, hidden from view. Within minutes, he was stepping into the quiet peace of London's grandest nine-hundred-year-old building.

Just as the Teacher was stepping out of the rain, Bishop Aringarosa was stepping into it. On the rainy tarmac at Biggin Hill Executive Airport, Aringarosa emerged from his cramped plane, pulling his cassock tight against the cold damp. He had hoped to be greeted by Captain Fache. Instead, a young British police officer approached with an umbrella.

"Bishop Aringarosa? Captain Fache had to leave. He asked me to look after you. He suggested I take you to Scotland Yard. He thought it would be safest."

Safest? Aringarosa looked down at the heavy briefcase of Vatican bonds clutched in his hand. He had almost forgotten. "Yes, thank you."

Aringarosa climbed into the police car, wondering where Silas could be. Minutes later, the police scanner crackled with the answer.

5 Orme Court.

Aringarosa recognized the address instantly.

The Opus Dei Centre in London.

He turned quickly to the driver. "Take me there now!"

CHAPTER 95

Langdon hadn't looked away from the computer since the search started. Five minutes. Only two hits. Both wrong.

He was starting to worry.

Pamela Gettum was in the next room, making hot drinks. Langdon and Sophie had asked if there might be some coffee along with the tea Gettum offered. Judging by the microwave beeps, Langdon thought they were about to get instant Nescafe.

Finally, the computer pinged.

"Sounds like you got another," Gettum called. "What's the title?" Langdon looked at the screen.

Grail Allegory in Medieval Literature: A Study of Sir Gawain and the Green Knight.

"Allegory of the Green Knight," he called back.

"No good," Gettum said. "Not many mythological green giants buried in London." Langdon and Sophie waited patiently for two more unlikely results. When the computer pinged again, the offering was unexpected.

DIE OPERN VON RICHARD WAGNER

"The operas of Wagner?" Sophie asked.

Gettum peeked back in the doorway, holding a packet of instant coffee. "That seems like an odd pairing. Was Wagner a knight?"

"No," Langdon said, feeling suddenly interested. "But he was a well-known Freemason. Like Mozart, Beethoven, Shakespeare, Gershwin, Houdini, and Disney." Many books had been written about the links between the Masons and the Knights Templar, the Priory of Sion, and the Holy Grail. "I want to look at this one. How do I see the whole thing?"

"You don't want the whole thing," Gettum called. "Click on the title. The computer will show where your keywords appear, along with some text before and after for context." Langdon didn't understand what she meant, but he clicked anyway.

A new window popped up.

...mythological knight named Parsifal who...

...symbolic quest for the Grail that could be seen as...

...the London Philharmonic in 1855...

Rebecca Pope's opera book "Diva's...

...Wagner's tomb in Bayreuth, Germany...

"Wrong Pope," Langdon said, disappointed. Still, he was amazed how easy the system was to use. The keywords with context were enough to remind him that Wagner's opera Parsifal was a tribute to Mary Magdalene and the bloodline of Jesus Christ, told through the story of a young knight searching for truth.

"Just be patient," Gettum urged. "It's about numbers. Let the machine run." Over the next few minutes, the computer found more Grail references, including a text about troubadours —France's famous wandering singers. Langdon knew it was no accident that the words minstrel and minister had the same origin. The troubadours were the traveling servants or "ministers" of the Church of Mary Magdalene, using music to spread the story of the sacred feminine among ordinary people. Even now, the troubadours sang songs praising "our Lady"—a mysterious and beautiful woman to whom they promised themselves forever.

Eagerly, he checked the hypertext but found nothing.

The computer pinged again.

KNIGHTS, KNAVES, POPES, AND PENTACLES: THE HISTORY OF THE HOLY GRAIL

THROUGH TAROT

"Not surprising," Langdon said to Sophie. "Some of our keywords have the same names as cards." He reached for the mouse to click on a link. "I'm not sure if your grandfather ever mentioned it when you played Tarot with him, Sophie, but this game is a 'simple lesson' in the story of the Lost Bride and how she was controlled by the evil Church." Sophie looked at him, not believing it. "I had no idea."

"That's the point. By teaching through a game with hidden meanings, the followers of the Grail hid their message from the Church." Langdon often wondered how many card players knew that their four suits—spades, hearts, clubs, diamonds—were Grail symbols from Tarot's four suits of swords, cups, scepters, and pentacles. Spades were Swords — The blade. Male.

Hearts were Cups — The chalice. Feminine.

Clubs were Scepters — The Royal Line. The flowering staff.

Diamonds were Pentacles — The goddess. The sacred feminine.

Four minutes later, as Langdon started to worry they wouldn't find what they were looking for, the computer found something else.

The Gravity of Genius: Biography of a Modern Knight.

"Gravity of Genius?" Langdon asked Gettum. "Bio of a modern knight?" Gettum poked her head around the corner. "How modern? Please don't say it's about Sir Rudy Giuliani. I thought that one missed the point."

Langdon wasn't sure about the newly knighted Sir Mick Jagger himself, but this wasn't the time to discuss British knighthood politics. "Let's take a look." Langdon pulled up the hypertext keywords.

... honorable knight, Sir Isaac Newton...

... in London in 1727 and...

... his tomb in Westminster Abbey...

... Alexander Pope, friend and colleague...

"I guess 'modern' means different things," Sophie said to Gettum. "It's an old book. About Sir Isaac Newton."

Gettum shook her head in the doorway. "No good. Newton was buried in Westminster Abbey, the heart of English Protestantism. There's no way a Catholic Pope was there. Cream and sugar?" Sophie nodded.

Gettum waited. "Robert?"

Langdon's heart was pounding. He looked away from the screen and stood up. "Sir Isaac Newton is our knight."

Sophie remained seated. "What are you talking about?"

"Newton is buried in London," Langdon said. "His work created new sciences that angered the Church. And he was a Grand Master of the Priory of Sion. What more could we want?"

"What more?" Sophie pointed to the poem. "How about a knight a Pope buried? You heard Ms. Gettum. Newton was not buried by a Catholic Pope."

Langdon reached for the mouse. "Who said anything about a Catholic Pope?" He clicked on the "Pope" link, and the complete sentence appeared.

Sir Isaac Newton's burial, attended by kings and nobles, was led by Alexander Pope, friend and colleague, who gave a moving

eulogy before sprinkling dirt on the tomb.

Langdon looked at Sophie. "We got the right Pope on our second try. Alexander." He paused.

"A. Pope."

In London lies a knight A. Pope buried.

Sophie stood up, looking shocked.

Jacques Saunière, the master of hidden meanings, had shown once again that he was a truly clever man.

CHAPTER 96

Silas woke up suddenly.

He didn't know what had woken him or how long he had slept. Was I dreaming? Sitting up on his straw mat, he listened to the quiet breathing of the Opus Dei residence, the stillness broken only by the soft murmurs of someone praying in a room below. These were familiar sounds and should have calmed him.

But he felt a sudden wariness.

Standing in his underwear, Silas walked to the window. Was I followed? The courtyard below was empty, just as he had seen it when he came in. He listened. Silence. So why am I uneasy? Silas had learned long ago to trust his gut. It had kept him alive as a child on the streets of Marseilles, before prison, before Bishop Aringarosa gave him a new life. Looking out the window, he saw the faint outline of a car through the hedge. On the car's roof was a police siren. A floorboard creaked in the hallway. A door latch moved.

Silas reacted without thinking, rushing across the room and stopping just behind the door as it burst open. The first police officer stormed in, swinging his gun at the empty room. Before he knew where Silas was, Silas slammed the door into him, crushing a second officer coming through. As the first officer turned to shoot, Silas dove for his legs. The gun fired, the bullet going over Silas's head, as he hit the officer's shins, knocking his legs out from under him, and sending the man down, his head hitting the floor. The second officer struggled to his feet in the doorway, and Silas kneed him in the groin, then climbed over the body into the hall.

Almost naked, Silas ran down the staircase. He knew he had been betrayed, but by whom? When he reached the foyer, more officers were rushing through the front door. Silas turned and ran deeper into the residence. The women's entrance. Every Opus Dei building has one. Running down narrow hallways, Silas went through a kitchen, past terrified workers, who scattered to avoid the naked albino as he knocked over bowls and silverware, and burst into a dark hallway near the boiler room. He saw the door he was looking for, an exit light shining at the end.

Running full speed through the door into the rain, Silas jumped off the low landing, not seeing the officer coming until it was too late. The two men collided, Silas's broad, naked shoulder hitting the man's chest with crushing force. He drove the officer backward onto the pavement, landing hard on top of him. The officer's gun fell away. Silas could hear men running down the hall shouting. Rolling, he grabbed the gun as the officers appeared. A shot rang out on the stairs, and Silas felt a burning pain below his ribs. Filled with rage, he opened fire at all three officers, their blood spraying.

A dark shadow loomed behind, coming out of nowhere. The angry hands that grabbed his bare shoulders felt like they had the power of the devil himself. The man roared in his ear. SILAS, NO!

Silas spun and fired. Their eyes met. Silas was already screaming in horror as Bishop Aringarosa fell.

CHAPTER 97

Over three thousand people are buried or honored inside Westminster Abbey. The huge stone building is filled with the remains of kings, leaders, scientists, poets, and musicians. Their tombs, squeezed into every space, range from grand mausoleums—like that of Queen Elizabeth I, whose covered coffin sits in its own private chapel—to simple floor tiles with worn inscriptions. These worn inscriptions leave you wondering whose remains might lie below in the undercroft.

Westminster Abbey, designed like the great cathedrals of Amiens, Chartres, and Canterbury, is neither a cathedral nor a regular church. It's a royal peculiar, meaning it answers only to the King or Queen. Since William the Conqueror's coronation on Christmas Day in 1066, this stunning place has hosted countless royal events and important ceremonies—from making Edward the Confessor a saint, to the wedding of Prince Andrew and Sarah Ferguson, to the funerals of Henry V, Queen Elizabeth I, and Lady Diana.

However, Robert Langdon wasn't interested in the abbey's history, except for one event—the funeral of the British knight Sir Isaac Newton.

In London lies a knight a Pope interred.

Rushing through the large entrance on the north side, Langdon and Sophie were greeted by guards who politely led them through a metal detector—now common in London's historic buildings. They passed through without setting off the alarm and went to the abbey entrance.

As Langdon stepped into Westminster Abbey, the outside world vanished. No traffic noise. No rain. Just a deep silence that seemed to echo back and forth, as if the building were whispering to itself. Langdon and Sophie, like most visitors, immediately looked up at the abbey's towering height. Gray stone columns rose like tall trees into the shadows, curving gracefully over vast spaces before reaching back down to the stone floor. The wide area of the north side stretched out like a canyon, with high walls of stained glass on either side. On sunny days, the abbey floor was a colorful display of light. Today, the rain and darkness gave this large space a ghostly feel, more like the crypt it truly was.

"It's almost empty," Sophie whispered.

Langdon was disappointed. He had hoped for more people, a more public place. He didn't want to repeat their experience in the deserted Temple Church. He had expected to feel safe in the popular tourist spot, but Langdon remembered the crowds and bright abbey from summer visits. Today was a rainy April morning. Instead of crowds and colorful glass, Langdon saw only empty floor space and shadowy areas.

"We went through metal detectors," Sophie reminded, sensing Langdon's worry. "If anyone's here, they can't have weapons."

Langdon nodded but still felt uneasy. He had wanted to bring the London police, but Sophie's fear of who might be involved stopped him from contacting them. We need to get the cryptex back, Sophie had said. It's the key to everything. She was right, of course.

The key to getting Leigh back alive.

The key to finding the Holy Grail.

The key to learning who's behind this.

Unfortunately, their only chance to get the keystone back seemed to be now... at Isaac Newton's tomb. Whoever had the cryptex would have to visit the tomb to figure out the final clue, and if they hadn't already come and gone, Sophie and Langdon planned to stop them.

Walking toward the left wall to get out of sight, they went into a hidden side aisle behind some pillars. Langdon couldn't forget the image of Leigh Teabing being held prisoner, probably tied up in his own limousine. Whoever had ordered the top Priory members killed wouldn't hesitate to get rid of anyone who got in their way. It seemed unfair that Teabing—a modern British knight—was a hostage in the search for his own countryman, Sir Isaac Newton.

"Which way is it?" Sophie asked, looking around.

The tomb. Langdon had no idea. "We should find a guide and ask." Langdon knew better than to wander around aimlessly. Westminster Abbey was a confusing maze of tombs, side rooms, and burial spots. Like the Louvre's Grand Gallery, it had only one entrance—the door they had just come through—easy to get in, but impossible to find your way out. A real tourist trap, one of Langdon's confused colleagues had called it. Following architectural tradition, the abbey was shaped like a giant cross. But unlike most churches, its entrance was on the side, not at the back of the church through the usual entrance. Also, the abbey had a series of large cloisters attached. One wrong turn through the wrong archway, and a visitor would get lost in a maze of outdoor passages surrounded by high walls.

"Guides wear red robes," Langdon said, walking toward the center of the church. Looking sideways across the tall, gold altar to the far end of the south transept, Langdon saw several people crawling on their hands and knees. This bowing pilgrimage was common in Poets' Corner, though it was less holy than it seemed. Tourists making copies of the grave markings.

"I don't see any guides," Sophie said. "Maybe we can find the tomb ourselves?" Without a word, Langdon led her a few more steps to the center of the abbey and pointed to the right.

Sophie gasped, looking down the long center aisle of the abbey, now able to see the whole building. "Aah," she said. "Let's find someone who knows about this place."

Just then, a hundred yards away, hidden behind the choir screen, the grand tomb of Sir Isaac Newton had a single visitor. The Teacher had been studying the monument for ten minutes.

Newton's tomb was a huge black-marble coffin with a sculpture of Sir Isaac Newton on top. He was dressed in old-style clothes and leaning proudly against a stack of his own books: Divinity, Chronology, Opticks, and Philosophiae Naturalis Principia Mathematica. At Newton's feet were two winged boys holding a scroll. Behind Newton was a plain pyramid. The pyramid seemed strange, but the thing that really caught the Teacher's eye was the giant shape halfway up the pyramid.

A sphere.

The Teacher thought about Saunière's puzzling riddle. Find the sphere that should be on his tomb. The large sphere sticking out from the pyramid showed many heavenly bodies—constellations, zodiac signs, comets, stars, and planets. Above it was a picture of the Goddess of Astronomy under a sky full of stars.

So many spheres.

The Teacher had thought that finding the missing sphere would be easy once he found the tomb. Now he wasn't so sure. He was looking at a complicated map of the sky. Was there a missing planet? Had a star been left out of a constellation? He had no idea. Still, the Teacher suspected the answer would be surprisingly simple—"a knight a pope interred." What sphere am I looking for? Surely, you didn't need to be an expert in space science to find the Holy Grail, did you?

It speaks of Rosy flesh and seeded womb.

The Teacher was interrupted by some tourists coming closer. He slipped the cryptex back into his pocket and watched carefully as the visitors went to a nearby table, dropped some money in the cup, and got more of the free supplies the abbey provided for rubbing designs from the graves. With fresh charcoal pencils and large sheets of paper, they headed toward the front of the abbey, probably to the popular Poets' Corner to rub the graves of Chaucer, Tennyson, and Dickens.

Alone again, he moved closer to the tomb, looking at it from top to bottom. He started with the clawed feet under the coffin, moved up past Newton, past his science books, past the two boys with their mathematical scroll, up the pyramid to the giant sphere with its constellations, and finally up to the star-filled ceiling of the alcove.

What object should be here... but is not? He touched the cryptex in his pocket, as if he could somehow guess the answer from Saunière's marble puzzle. Only five letters stand between me and the Grail.

He began to walk near the choir screen, took a deep breath, and looked up the long nave toward the main altar far away. His eyes moved from the gold altar down to the bright red robe of a church guide being called over by two very familiar people. Langdon and Neveu.

Calmly, the Teacher took two steps back behind the choir screen. That was fast. He knew Langdon and Sophie would eventually figure out the poem and come to Newton's tomb, but not this quickly. Taking a deep breath, the Teacher thought about what to do. He was used to dealing with surprises. I have the cryptex.

Reaching into his pocket, he touched the second thing that made him feel confident: the Medusa revolver. As expected, the abbey's metal detectors had gone off when the Teacher walked through with the hidden gun. Also as expected, the guards had immediately stepped back when the Teacher glared angrily and showed his ID card. Official status always got respect.

The Teacher had originally hoped to solve the cryptex alone and avoid problems, but now he felt that Langdon and Neveu's arrival was actually a good thing. Since he wasn't getting anywhere with the "orb" clue, he might be able to use their knowledge. After all, if Langdon had figured out the poem to find the tomb, he probably knew something about the orb. And if Langdon knew the password, then it would just be a matter of using the right force.

Not here, of course.

Somewhere private.

The Teacher remembered a small sign he had seen on his way into the abbey. He immediately knew the perfect place to trick them into going.

The only question now... what to use to lure them.

CHAPTER 98

Langdon and Sophie walked slowly down the north side, staying in the shadows behind the large pillars that separated it from the main area. Although they were more than halfway down, they still couldn't see Newton's tomb clearly. The coffin was set back in a space, hidden from this side view.

"At least there's no one over there," Sophie whispered.

Langdon nodded, relieved. The whole area of the main hall near Newton's tomb was empty. "I'll go over," he whispered. "You should stay hidden in case someone—" Sophie had already left the shadows and was walking across the open floor.

"—is watching," Langdon sighed, hurrying to catch up.

Walking across the large main hall at an angle, Langdon and Sophie were silent as the detailed tomb slowly came into view... a black marble coffin... a statue of Newton lying down... two winged boys... a huge pyramid... and... a very large orb.

"Did you know about that?" Sophie asked, sounding surprised.

Langdon shook his head, also surprised.

"Those look like constellations carved on it," Sophie said.

As they got closer to the space, Langdon felt uneasy. Newton's tomb was covered with orbs—stars, comets, planets. You seek the orb that ought be on his tomb? It might be like trying to find a single blade of grass on a golf course.

"Stars and planets," Sophie said, looking concerned. "And a lot of them." Langdon frowned. The only connection he could think of between planets and the Grail was the pentacle of Venus, and he had already tried the password "Venus" on the way to the Temple Church.

Sophie went straight to the sarcophagus, but Langdon stayed back a few feet, watching the abbey around them.

"Divinity," Sophie said, tilting her head and reading the titles of the books Newton was leaning on. "Chronology. Opticks. Philosophiae Naturalis Principia Mathematica?" She turned to him. "Does any of this help?"

Langdon stepped closer, thinking about it. "Principia Mathematica, if I remember correctly, is about the gravity of planets... which are round, but it seems like a stretch."

"What about the zodiac signs?" Sophie asked, pointing to the constellations on the orb. "You mentioned Pisces and Aquarius earlier, right?"

The End of Days, Langdon thought. "The end of Pisces and the start of Aquarius was supposedly when the Priory planned to release the Sangreal documents to the world." But the year 2000 came and went with nothing happening, leaving historians unsure when the truth would come out.

"It's possible," Sophie said, "that the Priory's plans to reveal the truth are related to the last line of the poem."

It speaks of Rosy flesh and seeded womb. Langdon felt a shiver of possibility. He hadn't thought of the line that way before.

"You told me earlier," she said, "that when the Priory planned to reveal the truth about 'the Rose' and her fertile womb was directly linked to the position of planets—round objects." Langdon nodded, feeling the first hints of a solution appearing. Even so, he felt that astronomy wasn't the answer. The Grand Master's previous solutions had all had a clear, symbolic meaning—the Mona Lisa, Madonna of the Rocks, SOFIA. This clear meaning was missing in the idea of planets and the zodiac. So far, Jacques Saunière had been a careful code writer, and Langdon believed that his final password—those five letters that unlocked the Priory's biggest secret—would be both symbolically right and very clear. If this solution was like the others, it would be obvious once he saw it.

"Look!" Sophie gasped, startling him as she grabbed his arm. From the fear in her touch Langdon thought someone was coming, but when he turned to her, she was staring in shock at the top of the black marble sarcophagus. "Someone was here," she whispered, pointing to a spot on the sarcophagus near Newton's outstretched right foot.

Langdon didn't get why she was worried. A thoughtless tourist had left a charcoal rubbing pencil on the sarcophagus lid, near Newton's foot. It's nothing. Langdon reached to grab it, but as he leaned toward the sarcophagus, the light moved on the shiny black marble, and Langdon stopped. Now he saw why Sophie was scared.

Written on the sarcophagus lid, at Newton's feet, was a faint message in charcoal:

I have Teabing.

Go through Chapter House,

out south exit, to public garden.

Langdon read the words again, his heart racing.

Sophie turned and looked around the nave.

Even though he felt a sense of dread seeing the words, Langdon told himself this was good. Leigh is still alive. There was something else here too. "They don't know the password either," he whispered.

Sophie nodded. Why else would they let them know they were here?

"They might want to trade Leigh for the password."

"Or it's a trap."

Langdon shook his head. "I don't think so. The garden is outside the abbey walls. It's open to everyone." Langdon had once visited the abbey's famous College Garden—a small fruit orchard and herb garden—from when monks grew natural medicines. With the oldest fruit trees in Great Britain, College Garden was a popular place for tourists who didn't need to enter the abbey. "I think sending us outside shows they trust us. So we feel safe."

Sophie looked unsure. "You mean outside, where there are no metal detectors?" Langdon frowned. She had a point.

Looking back at the tomb with the orbs, Langdon wished he knew the cryptex password... something to bargain with. I got Leigh into this, and I'll do anything I can to help him.

"The note says to go through the Chapter House to the south exit," Sophie said. "Maybe from the exit we can see the garden? That way we can check things out before we go out there and risk getting hurt?"

That was a good idea. Langdon remembered the Chapter House as a huge eight-sided hall where the first British Parliament met before the modern Parliament building existed. It had been years since he'd been there, but he remembered it being through the cloister. Stepping back from the tomb, Langdon looked around the choir screen to his right, across the nave to the side opposite where they had come down. A wide arched passageway was nearby, with a large sign.

THIS WAY TO:

CLOISTERS

DEANERY

COLLEGE HALL

PYX CHAMBER

ST. FAITH'S CHAPEL

CHAPTER HOUSE

Langdon and Sophie jogged under the sign, moving too fast to see the small notice saying some areas were closed for repairs. They came out into a high-walled courtyard with no roof, where morning rain was falling. Above, the wind howled across the opening with a low drone, like someone blowing over a bottle. As they entered the narrow, low walkways around the courtyard, Langdon felt his usual discomfort in tight spaces. These walkways were called cloisters, and Langdon felt uneasy noting that these cloisters lived up to their Latin link to the word claustrophobic.

Focusing on the end of the tunnel, Langdon followed the signs for the Chapter House. The rain was spitting now, and the walkway was cold and damp, with gusts of rain blowing through the single row of pillars that let light into the cloister. Another couple hurried past, trying to escape the worsening weather. The cloisters looked empty now, likely the abbey's least appealing spot in the wind and rain. Forty yards down the east cloister, an archway appeared on their left, leading to another hallway. Although this was the entrance they sought, it was blocked off by a drape and an official-looking sign.

CLOSED FOR RENOVATION

PYX CHAMBER

ST. FAITH'S CHAPEL

CHAPTER 99

Sir Leigh Teabing looked regretful as he stared at Robert Langdon and Sophie Neveu over the barrel of his Medusa revolver. "My friends," he said, "since you entered my home last night, I have tried my best to keep you safe. But your stubbornness has now put me in a tough spot."

He saw the shock and betrayal on Sophie's and Langdon's faces, but he was sure they would soon understand how they had all ended up at this strange point.

There is so much I need to tell you both... so much you don't know yet.

"Please believe me," Teabing said, "I never wanted you involved. You came to my home. You came looking for me."

"Leigh?" Langdon finally said. "What are you doing? We thought you were in danger. We came to help you!"

"As I knew you would," he said. "We have a lot to talk about." Langdon and Sophie couldn't seem to look away from the revolver pointed at them.

"It's just to make sure I have your full attention," Teabing said. "If I wanted to hurt you, you would be dead already. When you came into my home last night, I risked everything to save you. I am an honorable man, and I promised myself only to hurt those who had betrayed the Sangreal."

"What are you talking about?" Langdon said. "Betrayed the Sangreal?"

"I found out something terrible," Teabing said, sighing. "I learned why the Sangreal documents were never shared with the world. I learned that the Priory had decided not to reveal the truth after all. That's why the millennium passed without any reveal, why nothing happened as we entered the End of Days."

Langdon drew a breath, about to protest.

"The Priory," Teabing continued, "was trusted with a sacred duty: to share the truth. To reveal the Sangreal documents when the End of Days arrived. For centuries, men like Da Vinci, Botticelli, and Newton risked everything to protect these documents and do their duty. And now, at the most important moment, Jacques Saunière changed his mind. The man with the greatest responsibility in Christian history refused to do what he was supposed to do. He decided it wasn't the right time." Teabing turned to Sophie. "He failed the Grail. He failed the Priory. And he failed the memory of all the generations who worked to make that moment possible."

"You?" Sophie declared, glancing up, her green eyes staring at him with anger and understanding. "You are the one who killed my grandfather?" Teabing scoffed. "Your grandfather and his helpers betrayed the Grail." Sophie felt a fury rising from deep within. He's lying!

Teabing's voice was steady. "Your grandfather sold out to the Church. They obviously pressured him to keep the truth hidden."

Sophie shook her head. "The Church had no influence on my grandfather!" Teabing laughed coldly. "My dear, the Church has two thousand years of experience pressuring those who threaten to reveal its lies. Since the time of Constantine, the Church has hidden the truth about Mary Magdalene and Jesus. It's not surprising that now, they've found a way to keep the world in the dark. The Church may not use crusaders to kill non-believers anymore, but their influence is still strong and sneaky." He paused, as if to emphasize his next point. "Miss Neveu, your grandfather has wanted to tell you the truth about your family for some time now."

Sophie was stunned. "How could you know that?"

"How I know is not important. The important thing is for you to understand this." He took a deep breath. "The deaths of your mother, father, grandmother, and brother were not accidents." The words overwhelmed Sophie. She opened her mouth to speak but couldn't. Langdon shook his head. "What are you saying?"

"Robert, this explains everything. It all makes sense. History repeats itself. The Church has a history of murder when it comes to silencing the Sangreal. With the End of Days coming soon, killing the Grand Master's loved ones sent a clear message. Be quiet, or you and Sophie are next."

"It was a car accident," Sophie stammered, feeling the childhood pain rising within her. "An accident!"

"Stories to protect you," Teabing said. "Consider that only two family members were untouched—the Priory's Grand Master and his only granddaughter—the perfect pair for the Church to control the brotherhood. I can only imagine how the Church threatened your grandfather these past years, threatening to kill you if he revealed the Sangreal secret, threatening to finish what they started unless Saunière convinced the Priory to break its ancient vow."

"Leigh," Langdon argued, now clearly annoyed, "you don't have any proof that the Church had anything to do with those deaths, or that it made the Priory stay quiet."

"Proof?" Teabing snapped back. "You want proof the Priory was influenced? It's the new millennium, and the world still doesn't know! Isn't that proof enough?" In the echoes of Teabing's words, Sophie heard another voice speaking. Sophie, I must tell you the truth about your family. She realized she was trembling. Could this be the truth her grandfather had wanted to tell her? That her family had been killed? What did she really know about the crash that killed her family? Just a few details. Even the newspaper stories were unclear. An accident? Stories to tell children? Sophie suddenly remembered how protective her grandfather was, how he never liked to leave her alone when she was young. Even when Sophie was grown up and away at university, she felt like her grandfather was watching over her. She wondered if there had been Priory members watching her from a distance her whole life, taking care of her.

"You thought he was being controlled," Langdon said, glaring at Teabing in disbelief. "So you killed him?"

"I didn't pull the trigger," Teabing said. "Saunière died years ago, when the Church took his family from him. He was ruined. Now he is free of that pain, released from the shame of not being able to do his sacred duty. Think about it. Something had to be done. Should the world stay ignorant forever? Should the Church be allowed to put its lies into our history books forever? Should the Church be allowed to keep influencing people with murder and threats? No, something had to be done! And now we are ready to carry out Saunière's legacy and fix a terrible wrong." He paused. "The three of us. Together." Sophie felt only disbelief. "How could you possibly think that we would help you?"

"Because, my dear, you are why the Priory didn't release the documents. Your grandfather's love for you stopped him from challenging the Church. He was afraid of what they would do to his only remaining family, so he couldn't act. He never had a chance to explain the truth because you rejected him, tying his hands, making him wait. Now you owe the world the truth. You owe it to your grandfather's memory."

Robert Langdon had stopped trying to understand. Even though he had so many questions, he knew only one thing mattered now—getting Sophie out of here alive. All the guilt Langdon had mistakenly felt earlier for involving Teabing had now been transferred to Sophie.

I took her to Château Villette. I am responsible.

Langdon couldn't believe that Leigh Teabing would be able to kill them in cold blood here in the Chapter House, but Teabing had certainly been involved in killing others during his misguided quest. Langdon had the uneasy feeling that gunshots in this secluded, thick-walled room would go unheard, especially in this rain. And Leigh just admitted he was guilty to us. Langdon glanced at Sophie, who looked shaken. The Church killed Sophie's family to silence the Priory? Langdon felt sure the modern Church didn't kill people. There had to be some other explanation.

"Let Sophie leave," Langdon said, staring at Leigh. "You and I should talk about this alone." Teabing gave a strange laugh. "I can't trust you that much, I'm afraid. But I can offer you this." He stood up straighter on his crutches, awkwardly keeping the gun aimed at Sophie, and took the keystone from his pocket. He swayed a little as he held it out for Langdon. "A sign of trust, Robert."

Robert felt cautious and didn't move. Leigh is giving the keystone back to us?

"Take it," Teabing said, pushing it awkwardly toward Langdon. Langdon could think of only one reason Teabing would give it back. "You already opened it. You took out the map."

Teabing shook his head. "Robert, if I had solved the keystone, I would have disappeared to find the Grail myself and left you out of it. No, I don't know the answer. And I can say that easily. A true knight learns to be humble when facing the Grail. He learns to follow the signs in front of him. When I saw you enter the abbey, I understood. You were here for a reason. To help. I'm not looking for personal glory here. I serve something much greater than my own pride. The Truth. People deserve to know that truth. The Grail found us all, and now it wants to be revealed. We must work together."

Despite Teabing's requests for cooperation and trust, his gun stayed pointed at Sophie as Langdon stepped forward and took the cold marble cylinder. The vinegar inside made a bubbling sound as Langdon held it and stepped backward. The dials were still in random order, and the cryptex remained locked.

Langdon looked at Teabing. "How do you know I won't smash it right now?" Teabing's laugh was a strange chuckle. "I should have known your threat to break it in the Temple Church was just talk. Robert Langdon would never break the keystone. You're a historian, Robert. You're holding the key to two thousand years of history—the lost key to the Sangreal. You can feel the spirits of all the knights who were burned at the stake to protect her secret. Would you let them die for nothing? No, you will prove they were right. You'll join the ranks of the great men you admire—Da Vinci, Botticelli, Newton—each of whom would have been honored to be in your place right now. The contents of the keystone are calling out to us. Wanting to be set free. The time has come. Destiny has brought us to this moment."

"I can't help you, Leigh. I have no idea how to open this. I only saw Newton's tomb for a moment. And even if I knew the password..." Langdon stopped, realizing he had said too much.

"You wouldn't tell me?" Teabing sighed. "I'm disappointed and surprised, Robert, that you don't understand how much you owe me. My job would have been much easier if Rémy and I had gotten rid of you both when you walked into Château Villette. Instead, I risked everything to take the more honorable path."

"This is honorable?" Langdon asked, looking at the gun.

"Saunière's fault," Teabing said. "He and his sénéchaux lied to Silas. Otherwise, I would have gotten the keystone without any problems. How could I have guessed the Grand Master would go to such lengths to trick me and leave the keystone to a granddaughter he didn't even know?" Teabing looked at Sophie with disgust. "Someone so unqualified to hold this knowledge that she needed a symbologist babysitter." Teabing glanced back at Langdon. "Fortunately, Robert, your involvement turned out to be my saving grace. Instead of the keystone staying locked in the depository bank forever, you took it out and walked into my home." Where else would I go? Langdon thought. The community of Grail historians is small, and Teabing and I have a history together.

Teabing now looked pleased with himself. "When I learned Saunière left you a dying message, I had a pretty good idea you were holding valuable Priory information. Whether it was the keystone itself, or information on where to find it, I wasn't sure. But with the police chasing you, I had a feeling you might end up on my doorstep."

Langdon glared. "And if we hadn't?"

"I was planning to help you. One way or another, the keystone was coming to Château Villette. The fact that you brought it to me just proves that I'm right."

"What!" Langdon was shocked.

"Silas was supposed to break in and steal the keystone from you at Château Villette—taking you out of the picture without hurting you, and clearing me of any suspicion. But when I saw how complex Saunière's codes were, I decided to keep you both involved a little longer. I could have Silas steal the keystone later, once I knew enough to continue on my own."

"The Temple Church," Sophie said, her voice full of betrayal.

Light begins to dawn, Teabing thought. The Temple Church was the perfect place to steal the keystone from Robert and Sophie, and its connection to the poem made it a believable distraction. Rémy's orders were clear—stay hidden while Silas gets the keystone. Unfortunately, Langdon's threat to smash the keystone on the chapel floor made Rémy panic. If only Rémy hadn't shown himself, Teabing thought sadly, remembering his own fake kidnapping. Rémy was the only link to me, and he revealed his face!

Luckily, Silas didn't know Teabing's real identity and was easily tricked into taking him from the church and then watching as Rémy pretended to tie up their hostage in the back of the limousine. With the soundproof divider up, Teabing was able to call Silas in the front seat, use the fake French accent of the Teacher, and tell Silas to go straight to Opus Dei. A simple anonymous tip to the police would be enough to get rid of Silas. One loose end tied up.

The other loose end was harder. Rémy.

Teabing struggled with the decision, but in the end, Rémy had proven to be a liability. Every Grail quest requires sacrifice. The easiest solution had been right in front of Teabing in the limousine's bar—a flask, some cognac, and a can of peanuts. The powder at the bottom of the can would be more than enough to trigger Rémy's deadly allergy. When Rémy parked the limo on Horse Guards Parade, Teabing climbed out of the back, walked to the side passenger door, and sat in the front next to Rémy. Minutes later, Teabing got out of the car, climbed into the back again, cleaned up the evidence, and finally got out to finish his mission.

Westminster Abbey was a short walk, and although Teabing's leg braces, crutches, and gun had set off the metal detector, the security guards didn't know what to do. Do we ask him to remove his braces and crawl through? Do we pat down his deformed body? Teabing gave the confused guards an easier solution—a card showing he was a Knight of the Realm. The poor guys practically tripped over each other to let him in.

Now, looking at the confused Langdon and Neveu, Teabing resisted the urge to reveal how he had cleverly made Opus Dei look like they were behind the plot that would soon destroy the entire Church. That would have to wait. Right now, there was work to do.

"My friends," Teabing said in perfect French, "you don't find the Holy Grail, the Holy Grail finds you." He smiled. "Our path together is very clear. The Grail has found us."

Silence.

He spoke to them in a whisper now. "Listen. Can you hear it? The Grail is speaking to us across time. It's begging to be saved from the Priory's mistake. I urge you both to see this chance. There couldn't be three better people here right now to break the last code and open the cryptex." Teabing paused, his eyes bright. "We need to promise each other something. A pledge of trust. A knight's promise to find the truth and share it."

Sophie looked into Teabing's eyes and said firmly, "I will never make a promise with my grandfather's killer. Except a promise that I will see you in prison." Teabing's heart sank, then became firm. "I'm sorry you feel that way, mademoiselle." He turned and pointed the gun at Langdon. "And you, Robert? Are you with me, or against me?"

CHAPTER 100

Bishop Manuel Aringarosa's body had felt many kinds of pain, but the burning heat of the bullet in his chest felt very strange. Deep and serious. Not a wound of the body, but something closer to his soul.

He opened his eyes, trying to see, but the rain blurred his vision. Where am I? He felt strong arms holding him, carrying his limp body like a doll, his black robe flapping. Lifting a tired arm, he wiped his eyes and saw Silas holding him. The large albino was struggling down a wet sidewalk, yelling for a hospital, his voice a sad cry of pain. His red eyes stared straight ahead, tears running down his pale, bloody face.

"My son," Aringarosa whispered, "you're hurt."

Silas looked down, his face twisted in pain. "I am so sorry, Father." He seemed barely able to speak.

"No, Silas," Aringarosa replied. "I am the one who is sorry. This is my fault." The Teacher promised me there would be no killing, and I told you to obey him completely. "I was too eager. Too afraid. We were both tricked." The Teacher was never going to give us the Holy Grail. Held in the arms of the man he had taken in years ago, Bishop Aringarosa felt himself go back in time. To Spain. To his simple beginnings, building a small Catholic church in Oviedo with Silas. And later, to New York City, where he had praised God with the tall Opus Dei Center on Lexington Avenue.

Five months ago, Aringarosa had received terrible news. His life's work was in danger. He remembered clearly the meeting inside Castel Gandolfo that had changed his life, the news that had started all this trouble.

Aringarosa had entered Gandolfo's Astronomy Library proudly, expecting to be praised by many welcoming people, all wanting to congratulate him for his great work representing Catholicism in America.

But only three people were there.

The Vatican secretary. Overweight. Gloomy.

Two high-ranking Italian cardinals. Self-righteous. Smug.

"Secretariat?" Aringarosa said, puzzled.

The round overseer of legal affairs shook Aringarosa's hand and pointed to the chair opposite him. "Please, sit down."

Aringarosa sat, feeling something was wrong.

"I'm not one for small talk, Bishop," the secretariat said, "so let me get straight to the point of your visit."

"Please. Speak freely." Aringarosa glanced at the two cardinals, who seemed to be sizing him up with self-righteous anticipation.

"As you know," the secretariat said, "His Holiness and others in Rome have been worried recently about the political problems caused by Opus Dei's more questionable actions." Aringarosa felt himself tense up immediately. He had already dealt with this many times with the new pope, who, to Aringarosa's disappointment, had become a surprisingly strong voice for liberal change in the Church.

"I want to assure you," the secretariat added quickly, "that His Holiness doesn't want to change how you run your ministry."

I certainly hope not! "Then why am I here?"

The huge man sighed. "Bishop, I'm not sure how to say this gently, so I'll be blunt. Two days ago, the Secretariat Council voted unanimously to end the Vatican's approval of Opus Dei."

Aringarosa was sure he hadn't heard right. "I'm sorry, what?"

"Simply put, in six months, Opus Dei will no longer be part of the Vatican. You'll be your own church. The Vatican will cut ties with you. His Holiness agrees, and we're already preparing the legal documents."

"But... that can't be!"

"Actually, it can. And it must. His Holiness is worried about your forceful recruiting and your self-punishment practices." He paused. "Also, your policies on women. Frankly, Opus Dei has become a problem and a source of shame." Bishop Aringarosa was stunned. "A shame?"

"Surely you're not surprised it's come to this."

"Opus Dei is the only Catholic group that's growing! We have over eleven hundred priests now!"

"True. That worries us."

Aringarosa jumped up. "Ask His Holiness if Opus Dei was a shame in 1982 when we helped the Vatican Bank!"

"The Vatican will always be thankful for that," the secretary said, trying to soothe him, "but some still think your money in 1982 is the only reason you became a prelature."

"That's not true!" The suggestion deeply offended Aringarosa.

"Whatever happens, we plan to be fair. We are preparing severance terms that will include a return of that money. It will be paid in five parts."

"Are you bribing me?" Aringarosa asked. "Paying me to leave quietly? When Opus Dei is the only one making sense!"

One of the cardinals looked up. "I'm sorry, did you say sense?" Aringarosa leaned across the table, making his voice sharp. "Do you wonder why Catholics are leaving the Church? Look around you, Cardinal. People no longer respect it. The strict parts of faith are gone. The doctrine has become a buffet. Giving things up, confession, communion, baptism, mass—pick what you like—choose what pleases you and ignore the rest. What kind of spiritual help is the Church giving?"

"Laws from the third century," the second cardinal said, "cannot be used for today's followers of Christ. The rules don't work in today's world."

"Well, they seem to be working for Opus Dei!"

"Bishop Aringarosa," the secretary said, his voice final. "Because of your organization's connection with the last Pope, His Holiness will give Opus Dei six months to willingly separate from the Vatican. I suggest you mention your disagreements with the Holy See and become your own Christian organization."

"I refuse!" Aringarosa declared. "And I'll tell him that myself!"

"I'm afraid His Holiness doesn't want to meet with you anymore."

Aringarosa stood up. "He wouldn't dare get rid of a personal group created by a past Pope!"

"I'm sorry." The secretary's eyes didn't move. "The Lord gives and the Lord takes away." Aringarosa left that meeting confused and scared. Back in New York, he stared at the skyline, sad and worried about the future of Christianity.

Weeks later, he got the call that changed everything. The caller had a French accent and called himself the Teacher —a common title in the group. He said he knew the Vatican planned to stop supporting Opus Dei.

How could he know that? Aringarosa wondered. He'd hoped only a few powerful people in the Vatican knew Opus Dei was about to be shut down. Apparently, the news had spread. When it came to secrets, the Vatican was like a sieve.

"I hear everything, Bishop," the Teacher whispered, "and I've learned something. If you help me, I can find a sacred object that will give you great power... enough to make the Vatican obey you. Enough to save our Faith." He paused. "Not just for Opus Dei, but for everyone." The Lord takes away... and the Lord gives. Aringarosa felt a surge of hope. "Tell me your plan."

Bishop Aringarosa was unconscious when the doors of St. Mary's Hospital opened. Silas stumbled into the entrance, exhausted and confused. He fell to his knees on the floor and cried for help. Everyone in the waiting area stared at the half-naked albino holding a bleeding priest.

The doctor who helped Silas lift the bishop onto a bed looked worried as he checked Aringarosa's pulse. "He's lost a lot of blood. I don't think he'll make it." Aringarosa's eyes fluttered open, and he saw Silas. "My child..." Silas felt overwhelming guilt and anger. "Father, even if it takes my whole life, I will find the one who tricked us, and I will kill him."

Aringarosa shook his head sadly as they wheeled him away. "Silas... if you've learned nothing from me, please... learn this." He squeezed Silas's hand.

"Forgiveness is God's greatest gift."

"But Father..."

Aringarosa closed his eyes. "Silas, you must pray."

CHAPTER 101

Robert Langdon stood under the high dome of the empty Chapter House and looked into the barrel of Leigh Teabing's gun.

Robert, are you with me, or against me? The Royal Historian's words echoed in Langdon's head.

Langdon knew there was no good answer. If he said yes, he would be betraying Sophie. If he said no, Teabing would have to kill them both. Langdon's years of teaching hadn't given him any skills for dealing with guns, but they had taught him something about answering impossible questions. When a question has no right answer, there's only one honest response. The gray area between yes and no.

Silence.
