Looking at the cryptex in his hands, Langdon simply chose to walk away. Without looking up, he stepped backward into the room's wide open space. Neutral ground. He hoped his focus on the cryptex showed Teabing that working together was possible, and that his silence showed Sophie he hadn't left her.

All the while buying time to think.

Langdon suspected that thinking was exactly what Teabing wanted him to do. That's why he gave me the cryptex. So I could feel the weight of my decision. The British historian hoped that holding the Grand Master's cryptex would make Langdon fully understand how important its contents were, letting his academic curiosity take over, forcing him to realize that not unlocking the keystone would mean losing history itself.

With Sophie at gunpoint across the room, Langdon feared that finding the cryptex's hidden password was his only hope of trading for her release. If I can free the map, Teabing will negotiate. Forcing himself to focus on this important task, Langdon moved slowly toward the far windows... letting his mind fill with the many astronomical images on Newton's tomb. You seek the orb that ought be on his tomb.

It speaks of Rosy flesh and seeded womb.

Turning his back to the others, he walked toward the towering windows, searching for any inspiration in their stained-glass mosaics. There was none.

Imagine you are Saunière, he urged, gazing out into College Garden. What would he think is the orb that should be on Newton's tomb? Images of stars, comets, and planets sparkled in the falling rain, but Langdon ignored them. Saunière was not a scientist. He was a man interested in people, art, and history. The sacred feminine... the chalice... the Rose... the exiled Mary Magdalene... the decline of the goddess... the Holy Grail.

Legend had always shown the Grail as something hard to get, always just out of reach, whispering in your ear, tempting you closer and then vanishing. Looking out at the rustling trees of College Garden, Langdon felt her playful presence. The signs were everywhere. Like a teasing shape in the fog, the branches of Britain's oldest apple tree were full of five-petaled blossoms, all shining like Venus. The goddess was in the garden now. She was dancing in the rain, singing old songs, peeking out from behind the bud-filled branches as if to remind Langdon that the fruit of knowledge was growing just beyond his reach.

Across the room, Sir Leigh Teabing watched confidently as Langdon gazed out the window as if hypnotized.

Exactly as I hoped, Teabing thought. He will agree eventually.

For some time, Teabing had thought Langdon might hold the key to the Grail. It was no accident that Teabing started his plan on the same night Langdon was supposed to meet Jacques Saunière. Listening to the curator, Teabing was sure the man's eagerness to meet privately with Langdon could mean only one thing. Langdon's mysterious manuscript has bothered the Priory.

Langdon has found a truth, and Saunière is afraid it will be revealed. Teabing was sure the Grand Master was calling Langdon to silence him.

The Truth has been quiet for too long!

Teabing knew he had to act fast. Silas's attack would do two things. It would stop Saunière from convincing Langdon to keep quiet, and it would make sure that once Teabing had the keystone, Langdon would be in Paris to help if Teabing needed him. Arranging the fatal meeting between Saunière and Silas had been almost too easy. I knew Saunière's biggest fears. Yesterday afternoon, Silas had called the curator, pretending to be a distressed priest. "Monsieur Saunière, please forgive me, I must speak to you right away. I should never break the rules of confession, but I feel I must in this case. I just heard a confession from a man who said he murdered members of your family."

Saunière's response was surprised but cautious. "My family died in an accident. The police report proved it."

"Yes, a car accident," Silas said, setting the trap. "The man I spoke to said he forced their car off the road into a river."

Saunière fell silent.

"Monsieur Saunière, I would never have phoned you directly, but this man made a comment which makes me now fear for your safety." He paused. "The man also mentioned your granddaughter, Sophie."

The mention of Sophie's name was the trigger. The curator reacted instantly. He ordered Silas to come see him immediately in the safest place Saunière knew—his Louvre office. Then he phoned Sophie to warn her she might be in danger. Drinks with Robert Langdon were immediately canceled.

Now, with Langdon separated from Sophie on the other side of the room, Teabing felt he had successfully divided the two companions. Sophie Neveu remained stubborn, but Langdon clearly understood the situation. He was trying to figure out the password. He understands how important it is to find the Grail and free her.

"He won't open it for you," Sophie said coldly. "Even if he can." Teabing glanced at Langdon as he held the gun on Sophie. He was almost sure now he would have to use the weapon. Although the idea bothered him, he knew he would not hesitate if necessary. I have given her every chance to do the right thing. The Grail is more important than any of us.

At that moment, Langdon turned from the window. "The tomb..." he said suddenly, facing them with a small glimmer of hope in his eyes. "I know where to look on Newton's tomb. Yes, I think I can find the password!"

Teabing's heart leaped. "Where, Robert? Tell me!"

Sophie sounded horrified. "Robert, no! You're not going to help him, are you?" Langdon approached confidently, holding the cryptex before him. "No," he said, his eyes hardening as he turned to Leigh. "Not until he lets you go."

Teabing's optimism faded. "We are so close, Robert. Don't you dare start playing games with me!"

"No games," Langdon said. "Let her go. Then I'll take you to Newton's tomb. We'll open the cryptex together."

"I'm not going anywhere," Sophie declared, her eyes narrowing with rage. "That cryptex was given to me by my grandfather. It is not yours to open."

Langdon wheeled, looking fearful. "Sophie, please! You're in danger. I'm trying to help you!"

"How? By revealing the secret my grandfather died trying to protect? He trusted you, Robert. I trusted you!"

Langdon's blue eyes showed panic now, and Teabing could not help but smile to see the two of them working against one another. Langdon's attempts to be gallant were more sad than anything. He's about to reveal one of history's greatest secrets, and he's worrying about a woman who has shown she doesn't deserve to know it.

"Sophie," Langdon pleaded. "Please... you must leave." She shook her head. "Not unless you either hand me the cryptex or smash it on the floor."

"What?" Langdon gasped.

"Robert, my grandfather would rather his secret be lost forever than see it in the hands of his murderer." Sophie's eyes looked as if they would fill with tears, but they didn't. She stared directly back at Teabing. "Shoot me if you have to. I am not leaving my grandfather's legacy in your hands."

Very well. Teabing aimed the weapon.

"No!" Langdon shouted, raising his arm and suspending the cryptex precariously over the hard stone floor. "Leigh, if you even think about it, I will drop this." Teabing laughed. "That bluff worked on Rémy. Not on me. I know you better than that."

"Do you, Leigh?"

Yes, I do. You need to work on your poker face, my friend. It took me a few seconds, but I see now that you are lying. You have no idea where on Newton's tomb the answer is. "Really, Robert? You know where to look on the tomb?"

"I do."

The hesitation in Langdon's eyes was quick, but Leigh noticed it. It was a lie. A desperate, sad trick to save Sophie. Teabing felt deeply disappointed in Robert Langdon. I am a lone knight, surrounded by unworthy people. And I will have to figure out the keystone myself.

Langdon and Neveu were now just a threat to Teabing... and to the Grail. As hard as it would be, he knew he could do what he had to do without guilt. The only problem would be getting Langdon to put down the keystone so Teabing could safely end this game.

"A show of faith," Teabing said, lowering the gun from Sophie. "Put down the keystone, and we'll talk."

Langdon knew his lie hadn't worked.

He could see the dark determination in Teabing's face and knew the moment was here. When I put this down, he will kill us both. Even without looking at Sophie, he could hear her heart begging him in silent desperation. Robert, this man is not worthy of the Grail. Please don't let him have it. No matter what it costs.

Langdon had already made his decision a few minutes ago, while standing alone at the window looking out over College Garden.

Protect Sophie.

Protect the Grail.

Langdon almost yelled in despair. But I don't know how!

The harsh moments of realization brought a clarity he'd never known. The Truth is right in front of you, Robert. He didn't know where the insight came from. The Grail isn't teasing you, it's calling to someone worthy.

Now, bowing like a servant a few feet from Leigh Teabing, Langdon held the cryptex inches above the stone floor.

"Yes, Robert," Teabing whispered, pointing the gun at him. "Put it down." Langdon looked up at the open space of the Chapter House dome. Crouching lower, Langdon looked at Teabing's gun, aimed right at him.

"I'm sorry, Leigh."

In one quick move, Langdon jumped up, swinging his arm up, and throwing the cryptex straight up toward the dome.

Leigh Teabing didn't feel his finger squeeze the trigger, but the gun fired with a loud bang. Langdon was now standing, almost jumping, and the bullet hit the floor near his feet. Part of Teabing wanted to aim and fire again in anger, but the stronger part made him look up at the dome.

The keystone!

Time seemed to stop, turning into a slow dream as Teabing's whole world became the keystone in the air. He watched it rise to the highest point... hanging for a moment... and then falling down, spinning, back toward the stone floor.

All of Teabing's hopes and dreams were crashing down. It can't hit the floor! I can reach it! Teabing's body reacted without thinking. He let go of the gun and threw himself forward, dropping his crutches as he reached out with his soft, manicured hands. Stretching his arms and fingers, he grabbed the keystone in midair.

Falling forward with the keystone held high, Teabing knew he was falling too fast. With nothing to break his fall, his outstretched arms hit first, and the cryptex smashed hard against the floor.

There was a sickening crunch of glass inside.

For a full second, Teabing didn't breathe. Lying there stretched out on the cold floor, staring down the length of his arms at the marble cylinder in his bare hands, he begged the glass vial inside to hold. Then the sharp smell of vinegar filled the air, and Teabing felt the cool liquid flowing out through the dials onto his palm.

Wild panic took hold of him. NO! The vinegar was pouring out, and Teabing imagined the papyrus dissolving inside. Robert, you fool! The secret is lost!

Teabing felt himself sobbing uncontrollably. The Grail is gone. Everything destroyed. Shaking with disbelief at Langdon's actions, Teabing tried to force the cylinder apart, wanting to catch a last glimpse of history before it disappeared forever. To his surprise, as he pulled the ends of the keystone, the cylinder came apart.

He gasped and looked inside. It was empty except for pieces of wet glass. No dissolving papyrus. Teabing rolled over and looked up at Langdon. Sophie stood beside him, pointing the gun down at Teabing.

Confused, Teabing looked back at the keystone and saw it. The dials were no longer random. They spelled a five-letter word: APPLE.

"The orb from which Eve ate," Langdon said calmly, "bringing on God's holy anger. Original sin. The symbol of the fall of the sacred feminine."

Teabing felt the truth hit him with painful clarity. The orb that should be on Newton's tomb could only be the rosy apple that fell from heaven, hit Newton on the head, and inspired his life's work. His labor's fruit! The rosy flesh with a seeded womb!

"Robert," Teabing stuttered, stunned. "You opened it. Where... is the map?" Without a blink, Langdon reached into his tweed coat pocket and carefully pulled out a thin rolled papyrus. Just a few feet from Teabing, Langdon unrolled the scroll and looked at it. After a long pause, a knowing smile appeared on Langdon's face. He knows! Teabing's heart yearned for that knowledge. His life's dream was right there. "Tell me!" Teabing demanded. "Please! Oh God, please! It's not too late!" As heavy footsteps echoed down the hall toward the Chapter House, Langdon quietly rolled the papyrus and put it back in his pocket.

"No!" Teabing shouted, trying to stand but failing.

The doors crashed open, and Bezu Fache stormed in like a bull, his wild eyes searching and finding his target—Leigh Teabing—helpless on the floor. Relieved, Fache put away his gun and turned to Sophie. "Agent Neveu, I'm glad you and Mr. Langdon are safe. You should have come in when I asked."

The British police followed Fache, grabbing the suffering prisoner and handcuffing him.

Sophie seemed shocked to see Fache. "How did you find us?"

Fache pointed to Teabing. "He made the mistake of showing his ID when he entered the abbey. The guards heard a police report about our search for him."

"It's in Langdon's pocket!" Teabing screamed like a crazy person. "The map to the Holy Grail!" As they lifted Teabing and carried him away, he threw his head back and howled. "Robert! Tell me where it's hidden!"

As Teabing passed, Langdon looked him in the eye. "Only those who deserve it find the Grail, Leigh. You taught me that."

CHAPTER 102

The mist hung low in Kensington Gardens as Silas limped to a hidden spot. Kneeling on the damp grass, he felt warm blood flow from the bullet wound under his ribs. Yet, he stared straight ahead.

The fog made the place look like heaven.

Raising his bloody hands to pray, he watched raindrops touch his fingers, turning them white. As the drops fell harder on his back and shoulders, he felt his body slowly vanish into the mist.

I am a ghost.

A breeze blew past, carrying the wet, earthy smell of new life. With every bit of his broken body, Silas prayed. He prayed to be forgiven. He prayed for mercy. And, most of all, he prayed for his teacher... Bishop Aringarosa... that God would not take him too soon. He has so much work left to do.

The fog swirled around him, and Silas felt so light he thought the mist would carry him away. Closing his eyes, he said a last prayer.

From somewhere in the mist, Manuel Aringarosa's voice whispered to him. Our Lord is a good and merciful God.

Silas's pain finally started to disappear, and he knew the bishop was right.

CHAPTER 103

It was late afternoon when the London sun appeared and the city began to dry. Bezu Fache felt tired as he left the interrogation room and called for a taxi. Sir Leigh Teabing had loudly declared his innocence, but from his confused ramblings about the Holy Grail, secret documents, and mysterious groups, Fache thought the clever historian was preparing his lawyers to claim he was insane.

Sure, Fache thought. Insane. Teabing had shown great skill in creating a plan that protected his innocence at every step. He had used both the Vatican and Opus Dei, two groups that turned out to be completely innocent. His dirty work had been done without their knowledge by a fanatical monk and a desperate bishop. Even smarter, Teabing had placed his electronic listening device in the one place a man with polio could not possibly reach. The actual spying had been done by his servant, Rémy—the only person who knew Teabing's true identity—now conveniently dead from an allergic reaction.

Not the work of someone mentally impaired, Fache thought. The information Collet was sending from Château Villette suggested that Teabing's craftiness was so advanced that Fache himself might even learn from it. To successfully hide bugs in some of Paris's most important offices, the British historian had used the Greeks. Trojan horses. Some of Teabing's targets received expensive gifts of art, others unknowingly bid at auctions where Teabing had placed specific items. In Saunière's case, the curator had been invited to dinner at Château Villette to discuss Teabing's possibly funding a new Da Vinci Wing at the Louvre. Saunière's invitation had a casual note expressing interest in a robotic knight that Saunière was rumored to have built. Bring it to dinner, Teabing had suggested. Saunière apparently had done so and left the knight unattended long enough for Rémy Legaludec to make a small addition.

Now, sitting in the back of the taxi, Fache closed his eyes. One more thing to do before I return to Paris.

The St. Mary's Hospital recovery room was sunny.

"You've impressed us all," the nurse said, smiling at him. "It's quite a miracle." Bishop Aringarosa gave a weak smile. "I have always been fortunate." The nurse finished her work, leaving the bishop alone. The sunlight felt nice and warm on his face. Last night had been the worst night of his life.

Sadly, he thought of Silas, whose body had been found in the park. Please forgive me, my son.

Aringarosa had wanted Silas to be part of his grand plan. But last night, Aringarosa had received a call from Bezu Fache, asking the bishop about his connection to a nun who had been murdered in Saint-Sulpice. Aringarosa realized the evening had taken a terrible turn. News of the four additional murders changed his horror to pain. Silas, what have you done! Unable to reach the Teacher, the bishop knew he had been abandoned. Used. The only way to stop the terrible series of events he had helped start was to confess everything to Fache, and from that moment on, Aringarosa and Fache had been racing to catch up with Silas before the Teacher persuaded him to kill again.

Feeling exhausted, Aringarosa closed his eyes and listened to the television coverage of the arrest of a well-known British knight, Sir Leigh Teabing. The Teacher exposed for everyone to see. Teabing had learned of the Vatican's plans to distance itself from Opus Dei. He had chosen Aringarosa as the perfect tool in his plan. After all, who would be more likely to blindly chase after the Holy Grail than a man like myself with everything to lose? The Grail would have given great power to anyone who possessed it.

Leigh Teabing had hidden his real self well—faking a French accent and a religious heart, and asking for the one thing he didn't need—money. Aringarosa had been too quick to suspect nothing. The twenty million euro price was small compared to getting the Grail, and with the Vatican's payment to Opus Dei, the money worked out fine. People see what they want to see. Teabing's biggest insult was asking for payment in Vatican bonds, so if anything went wrong, the investigation would point to Rome.

"I am glad you're well, My Lord."

Aringarosa knew the rough voice in the doorway, but the face was a surprise—serious, strong features, slicked-back hair, and a thick neck pushing against his dark suit. "Captain Fache?" Aringarosa asked. The kindness and care the captain had shown for Aringarosa's trouble last night had made him think of a much gentler man.

The captain came to the bed and put a familiar, heavy black briefcase on a chair. "I believe this is yours."

Aringarosa looked at the briefcase full of bonds and quickly looked away, feeling only shame. "Yes... thank you." He stopped, rubbing his fingers on the bedsheet, then went on. "Captain, I have been thinking hard about this, and I need to ask you a favor."

"Of course."

"The families of those in Paris who Silas..." He paused, fighting back his emotions. "I know no amount of money can truly make up for it, but if you would be kind enough to divide what's in this briefcase among them... the families of those who died." Fache's dark eyes studied him for a moment. "A good thing to do, My Lord. I will make sure it is done."

A heavy silence filled the room.

On the television, a thin French police officer was giving a press conference in front of a large house. Fache saw who it was and watched the screen.

"Lieutenant Collet," a BBC reporter said, her voice sounding accusing. "Last night, your captain publicly accused two innocent people of murder. Will Robert Langdon and Sophie Neveu demand justice from your department? Will this cost Captain Fache his job?" Lieutenant Collet's smile was tired but calm. "I know that Captain Bezu Fache rarely makes mistakes. I haven't spoken to him about this yet, but knowing how he works, I think his public search for Agent Neveu and Mr. Langdon was a trick to draw out the real killer."

The reporters looked at each other, surprised.

Collet went on. "I don't know if Mr. Langdon and Agent Neveu willingly took part in the trap. Captain Fache usually keeps his inventive methods secret. All I can say now is that the captain has caught the person responsible, and that Mr. Langdon and Agent Neveu are both innocent and safe."

Fache smiled slightly as he turned back to Aringarosa. "Collet is a good man." He paused for a moment. Then, Fache ran his hand over his forehead, smoothing back his hair as he looked down at Aringarosa. "My Lord, before I go back to Paris, there's one more thing I want to talk about—your sudden flight to London. You paid a pilot to change the flight path. By doing that, you broke several international laws."

Aringarosa sagged. "I was desperate."

"Yes. So was the pilot when my men questioned him." Fache reached into his pocket and took out a purple amethyst ring with a familiar handmade design of a mitre and crozier. Aringarosa felt tears filling his eyes as he took the ring and put it back on his finger. "You've been so kind." He reached out and shook Fache's hand. "Thank you." Fache dismissed the thanks, walking to the window and looking out at the city, clearly lost in thought. When he turned back, he seemed unsure. "My Lord, what will you do now?"

Aringarosa had been asked the same question as he left Castel Gandolfo the night before. "I think my future is as unclear as yours."

"Yes." Fache paused. "I think I'll be retiring soon." Aringarosa smiled. "A little faith can work wonders, Captain. A little faith."

CHAPTER 104

Rosslyn Chapel—often called the Cathedral of Codes—is located seven miles south of Edinburgh, Scotland, where an ancient temple once stood. Built in 1446 by the Knights Templar, the chapel is covered in a bewildering number of symbols from Jewish, Christian, Egyptian, Masonic, and pagan traditions.

The chapel's location lines up exactly with the north-south line that goes through Glastonbury. This Rose Line marks the traditional location of King Arthur's Isle of Avalon and is considered the main point of Britain's sacred layout. Rosslyn—originally spelled Roslin—gets its name from this important Rose Line.

The chapel's rough towers were casting long shadows as Robert Langdon and Sophie Neveu drove their rental car into the grassy parking area at the bottom of the hill where the chapel stood. Their short flight from London to Edinburgh had been relaxing, but they were both too excited about what was ahead to sleep. Looking up at the bare building against the cloudy sky, Langdon felt like Alice falling into the rabbit hole. This must be a dream. But he knew that Saunière's final message couldn't have been clearer. The Holy Grail 'neath ancient Roslin waits.

Langdon had imagined that Saunière's "Grail map" would be a simple drawing with an X marking the spot. But the Priory's final secret was revealed in the same way Saunière had spoken to them from the start. Simple verse. Four clear lines that pointed directly to this place. Besides naming Rosslyn, the verse also mentioned some of the chapel's famous architectural features.

Even though Saunière's final message was clear, Langdon felt more confused than enlightened. To him, Rosslyn Chapel seemed like too obvious a place. For centuries, people had whispered about the Holy Grail being hidden in this stone chapel. The whispers had become shouts in recent years when radar showed a surprising structure underneath the chapel—a huge underground chamber. This vault was not only bigger than the chapel above it, but it also seemed to have no way in or out. Archaeologists wanted to start digging through the rock to reach the mysterious chamber, but the Rosslyn Trust strictly forbade any digging at the sacred site. Of course, this only made people wonder more. What was the Rosslyn Trust trying to hide?

Rosslyn had now become a popular place for people seeking mysteries. Some said they were drawn there by the strong magnetic field that seemed to come from the area, others said they came to search the hillside for a hidden entrance to the vault, but most admitted they had simply come to walk around and learn about the Holy Grail. Although Langdon had never been to Rosslyn before, he always chuckled when he heard the chapel called the current home of the Holy Grail. He thought Rosslyn might have been home to the Grail once, long ago... but certainly not anymore. Too much attention had been paid to Rosslyn in recent years, and someone would eventually find a way to break into the vault. True Grail experts agreed that Rosslyn was a fake—one of the clever false leads that the Priory created so well. But tonight, with the Priory's key message offering a verse that pointed straight to this place, Langdon no longer felt so sure. A puzzling question had been on his mind all day:

Why would Saunière go to so much trouble to lead us to such an obvious place?

There seemed to be only one logical answer.

There is something about Rosslyn we have yet to understand.

"Robert?" Sophie was outside the car, looking back. "Coming?" She held the rosewood box Captain Fache had given back. Inside, both cryptexes were put back together as they were found. The papyrus verse was locked safely inside—without the broken vinegar vial.

Walking up the long gravel path, Langdon and Sophie passed the chapel's famous west wall. Visitors thought this strange wall was part of the chapel that wasn't finished. Langdon knew the truth was more interesting.

The west wall of Solomon's Temple.

The Knights Templar designed Rosslyn Chapel to be like Solomon's Temple in Jerusalem—with a west wall, a narrow sanctuary, and an underground vault like the Holy of Holies. Here, the first nine knights found their treasure. Langdon thought it was interesting that the Templars built a modern place for the Grail that was like the Grail's first hiding place. Rosslyn Chapel's entrance was smaller than Langdon expected. The small wooden door had

two iron hinges and a simple, oak sign.

ROSLIN

Langdon told Sophie this old spelling came from the Rose Line where the chapel was. Or, as Grail experts liked to think, from the "Line of Rose"—Mary Magdalene's family line.

The chapel would close soon. Langdon opened the door, and warm air came out, like the old building was sighing after a long day. The arches at the entrance were covered in carved roses.

Roses. The goddess's womb.

Entering with Sophie, Langdon felt his eyes scan the famous chapel, taking it all in. He had read about Rosslyn's strikingly complex stonework, but seeing it in person was overwhelming.

A symbol lover's dream, one of Langdon's colleagues had called it.

Every surface in the chapel was carved with symbols—Christian crosses, Jewish stars, Masonic symbols, Templar crosses, horns of plenty, pyramids, astrological signs, plants, vegetables, pentacles, and roses. The Knights Templar were master stonemasons who built Templar churches all over Europe, but Rosslyn was considered their greatest work of love and respect. The master masons had left no stone uncarved. Rosslyn Chapel was a tribute to all faiths... to all traditions... and, most of all, to nature and the goddess. The chapel was empty except for a few visitors listening to a young man giving the last tour of the day. He was leading them in a line along a well-known path on the floor—an invisible route connecting six key architectural points inside. Generations of visitors had walked these straight lines, connecting the points, and their many footsteps had etched a huge symbol on the floor.

The Star of David, Langdon thought. That's no accident. Also known as Solomon's Seal, this hexagram was once the secret symbol of priests who studied the stars and was later adopted by the Israelite kings—David and Solomon.

The guide had seen Langdon and Sophie enter, and even though it was closing time, he smiled kindly and told them to look around.

Langdon nodded his thanks and started to walk further into the chapel. Sophie, however, stood still in the doorway, looking puzzled.

"What is it?" Langdon asked.

Sophie stared at the chapel. "I think... I've been here." Langdon was surprised. "But you said you hadn't even heard of Rosslyn."

"I hadn't..." She looked around the chapel, seeming unsure. "My grandfather must have brought me here when I was very young. I don't know. It feels familiar." As she looked around the room, she started nodding more confidently. "Yes." She pointed to the front of the chapel. "Those two pillars... I've seen them."

Langdon looked at the two intricately carved columns at the far end of the chapel. Their white, lacy carvings seemed to glow with a reddish light as the last of the day's sunlight streamed in through the west window. The pillars—placed where the altar would normally be—were an oddly mismatched pair. The pillar on the left was carved with simple, vertical lines, while the pillar on the right was decorated with a fancy, flowery spiral. Sophie was already walking toward them. Langdon hurried after her, and as they reached the pillars, Sophie was nodding in disbelief. "Yes, I'm sure I've seen these!"

"I'm sure you've seen them," Langdon said, "but maybe not here." She turned. "What do you mean?"

"These two pillars are the most copied architectural structures ever. There are copies all over the world."

"Copies of Rosslyn?" She seemed doubtful.

"No. Of the pillars. Remember I said Rosslyn itself is a copy of Solomon's Temple? Those two pillars are exact copies of the two pillars that stood at the entrance of Solomon's Temple." Langdon pointed to the pillar on the left. "That's called Boaz —or the Mason's Pillar. The other is called Jachin —or the Apprentice Pillar." He paused. "Almost every Masonic temple in the world has two pillars like these."

Langdon had already told her about the Templars' strong historical links to the modern Masonic secret societies. Their main levels—Apprentice Freemason, Fellowcraft Freemason, and Master Mason—went back to early Templar times. Sophie's grandfather's last verse mentioned the Master Masons who decorated Rosslyn with their carvings. It also mentioned Rosslyn's central ceiling, which was covered with carvings of stars and planets.

"I've never been in a Masonic temple," Sophie said, still looking at the pillars. "But I'm almost sure I saw these here." She turned back into the chapel, as if looking for something to remind her.

The other visitors were now leaving, and the young guide walked over to them with a friendly smile. He was a good-looking young man in his late twenties, with a Scottish accent and strawberry blond hair. "I'm about to close for the day. Can I help you find anything?"

How about the Holy Grail? Langdon thought.

"The code," Sophie blurted out, suddenly realizing. "There's a code here!" The guide looked happy she was so interested. "Yes, there is, ma'am."

"It's on the ceiling," she said, turning to the right-hand wall. "Somewhere over... there." He smiled. "I see this isn't your first time at Rosslyn."

The code, Langdon thought. He had forgotten that little bit of knowledge. Among Rosslyn's many mysteries was an arched doorway from which hundreds of stone blocks stuck out, forming a strange, many-sided surface. Each block had a symbol carved on it, seemingly at random, creating a code of immense size. Some people thought the code revealed the entrance to the vault under the chapel.

Others believed it told the real story of the Grail. But it didn't matter—code breakers had been trying for centuries to figure it out. Even now, the Rosslyn Trust offered a large reward to anyone who could reveal the secret meaning, but the code remained a mystery. "I'd be happy to show..."

The guide's voice faded.

My first code, Sophie thought, walking alone, as if in a dream, toward the coded archway. After giving the rosewood box to Langdon, she felt herself briefly forgetting about the Holy Grail, the Priory of Sion, and all the mysteries of the past day. When she reached the coded ceiling and saw the symbols above her, the memories rushed back. She was remembering her first visit here, and strangely, the memories brought an unexpected sadness. She was a little girl... about a year after her family's death. Her grandfather had brought her to Scotland for a short trip. They had come to see Rosslyn Chapel before going back to Paris. It was late evening, and the chapel was closed. But they were still inside.

"Can we go home, Grand-père?" Sophie asked, feeling tired.

"Soon, dear, very soon." His voice was sad. "I have one last thing I need to do here. How about you wait in the car?"

"Are you doing another grown-up thing?"

He nodded. "I'll be quick. I promise."

"Can I do the archway code again? That was fun."

"I don't know. I have to go outside. You won't be scared in here alone?"

"Of course not!" she said, annoyed. "It's not even dark yet!" He smiled. "Very well then." He led her to the fancy archway he'd shown her before. Sophie immediately sat down on the stone floor, lying on her back and looking up at the puzzle pieces above. "I'm going to solve this before you get back!"

"It's a race, then." He bent down, kissed her forehead, and walked to the side door. "I'll be right outside. I'll leave the door open. If you need me, just call." He went out into the soft evening light.

Sophie lay there, looking up at the code. Her eyes felt heavy. After a few minutes, the symbols blurred. And then they vanished.

When Sophie woke up, the floor felt cold.

"Grand-père?"

There was no answer. She stood up and brushed herself off. The side door was still open. The evening was getting darker. She walked outside and saw her grandfather standing on the porch of a stone house behind the church. He was talking quietly to someone barely visible inside the screen door.

"Grand-père?" she called.

Her grandfather turned and waved, telling her to wait. Then, slowly, he said some final words to the person inside and blew a kiss toward the screen door. He came to her with tearful eyes.

"Why are you crying, Grand-père?"

He picked her up and held her close. "Oh, Sophie, we've said good-bye to a lot of people this year. It's hard."

Sophie thought of the accident, of saying good-bye to her mother and father, her grandmother and baby brother. "Were you saying goodbye to another person?"

"To a dear friend whom I love very much," he replied, his voice full of emotion. "And I fear I will not see her again for a very long time."

Standing with the docent, Langdon had been looking at the chapel walls and feeling more and more worried that they were at a dead end. Sophie had gone off to look at the code and left Langdon holding the rosewood box, which contained a Grail map that now seemed useless. Although Saunière's poem clearly pointed to Rosslyn, Langdon wasn't sure what to do now that they were there. The poem mentioned a "blade and chalice," which Langdon didn't see anywhere.

The Holy Grail 'neath ancient Roslin waits.

The blade and chalice guarding o'er Her gates.

Again Langdon felt that there was still some part of the mystery he hadn't figured out.

"I hate to pry," the docent said, looking at the rosewood box in Langdon's hands. "But this box... may I ask where you got it?"

Langdon gave a tired laugh. "That's a very long story." The young man hesitated, his eyes on the box again. "It's strange—my grandmother has a box exactly like that—a jewelry box. Same polished rosewood, same inlaid rose, even the hinges look the same."

Langdon knew the young man had to be wrong. If any box was unique, it was this one—the box custom-made for the Priory keystone. "The two boxes might be similar but—" The side door closed loudly, making them both look over. Sophie had left without a word and was now walking down the hill toward a fieldstone house nearby. Langdon stared after her. Where is she going? She had been acting strangely ever since they came inside. He turned to the docent. "Do you know what that house is?"

He nodded, also looking confused about why Sophie was going down there. "That's the chapel rectory. The chapel curator lives there. She's also the head of the Rosslyn Trust." He paused. "And my grandmother."

"Your grandmother runs the Rosslyn Trust?"

The young man nodded. "I live with her in the rectory and help take care of the chapel and give tours." He shrugged. "I've lived here my whole life. My grandmother raised me in that house." Worried about Sophie, Langdon moved across the chapel toward the door to call out to her. He was only halfway there when he stopped suddenly. Something the young man said just clicked. My grandmother raised me.

Langdon looked out at Sophie on the bluff, then down at the rosewood box in his hand. Impossible. Slowly, Langdon turned back to the young man. "You said your grandmother has a box like this one?"

"Almost exactly the same."

"Where did she get it?"

"My grandfather made it for her. He died when I was a baby, but my grandmother still talks about him. She says he was incredibly talented. He made all sorts of things." Langdon saw a huge network of connections forming. "You said your grandmother raised you. Do you mind me asking what happened to your parents?"

The young man looked surprised. "They died when I was young." He paused. "The same day as my grandfather."

Langdon's heart beat fast. "In a car accident?"

The docent flinched, looking confused. "Yes. In a car accident. My entire family died that day. I lost my grandfather, my parents, and..." He hesitated, looking down at the floor. "And your sister," Langdon said.

Out on the bluff, the fieldstone house was just as Sophie remembered it. Night was coming, and the house seemed warm and welcoming. The smell of bread floated through the open screen door, and a golden light shone in the windows. As Sophie got closer, she could hear the soft sounds of crying from inside.

Through the screened door, Sophie saw an elderly woman in the hallway. Her back was to the door, but Sophie could see she was crying. The woman had long, beautiful, silver hair that brought back a sudden memory. Feeling drawn closer, Sophie stepped onto the porch stairs. The woman was holding a framed photograph of a man and touching his face with loving sadness.

It was a face Sophie knew well.

Grand-père.

The woman had clearly heard the sad news of his death last night.

A board squeaked beneath Sophie's feet, and the woman turned slowly, her sad eyes finding Sophie's. Sophie wanted to run, but she stood still. The woman's intense gaze never wavered as she set down the photo and approached the screened door. It felt like forever as the two women stared at each other through the thin mesh. Then, like a slowly growing ocean wave, the woman's face changed from uncertainty... to disbelief... to hope... and finally, to overwhelming joy.

Throwing open the door, she came out, reaching with soft hands, cradling Sophie's stunned face. "Oh, dear child... look at you!"

Although Sophie did not recognize her, she knew who this woman was. She tried to speak but could not even breathe.

"Sophie," the woman sobbed, kissing her forehead.

Sophie's words were a choked whisper. "But... Grand-père said you were..."

"I know." The woman placed her gentle hands on Sophie's shoulders and looked at her with familiar eyes. "Your grandfather and I had to say so many things. We did what we thought was right. I'm so sorry. It was for your own safety, princess."

Sophie heard her last name and immediately thought of her grandfather, who had called her princess for so long. The sound of his voice seemed to echo in the old stones of Rosslyn, settling through the ground and bouncing in the hidden spaces below. The woman hugged Sophie tightly, crying harder. "Your grandfather really wanted to tell you everything. But things were hard between you two. He tried so much. There's so much to explain. So very much to explain." She kissed Sophie's forehead again, then whispered in her ear. "No more secrets, princess. It's time you learned the truth about our family."

Sophie and her grandmother were sitting on the porch steps, hugging and crying, when the young guide ran across the lawn, his eyes bright with hope and shock.

"Sophie?"

Through her tears, Sophie nodded, standing up. She didn't know the young man, but as they hugged, she could feel the strength of their shared blood.

When Langdon walked over to them, Sophie couldn't believe that just yesterday she had felt so alone. And now, somehow, in this strange place, with three people she hardly knew, she finally felt at home.

CHAPTER 105

Night had fallen over Rosslyn.

Robert Langdon stood alone on the porch of the fieldstone house, enjoying the sounds of laughter and reunion coming through the screen door behind him. The mug of strong Brazilian coffee in his hand had given him a brief escape from his growing exhaustion, but he knew it wouldn't last long. The tiredness in his body ran deep.

"You slipped out quietly," a voice behind him said.

He turned. Sophie's grandmother appeared, her silver hair shining in the night. Her name, for at least the last twenty-eight years, was Marie Chauvel.

Langdon gave a tired smile. "I thought I'd give your family some time together." Through the window, he could see Sophie talking with her brother.

Marie came over and stood beside him. "Mr. Langdon, when I first heard of Jacques's murder, I was terrified for Sophie's safety. Seeing her standing in my doorway tonight was the greatest relief of my life. I can't thank you enough."

Langdon didn't know how to respond. Although he had offered to give Sophie and her grandmother time to talk in private, Marie had asked him to stay and listen. My husband clearly trusted you, Mr. Langdon, so I do too.

So Langdon had stayed, standing beside Sophie and listening in silent shock as Marie told the story of Sophie's late parents. Amazingly, both had been from Merovingian families—direct descendants of Mary Magdalene and Jesus Christ. Sophie's parents and ancestors had changed their family names of Plantard and Saint-Clair to protect themselves. Their children were the most direct surviving royal bloodline and were therefore carefully guarded by the Priory. When Sophie's parents were killed in a car accident with an unknown cause, the Priory feared the royal line's identity had been discovered.

"Your grandfather and I," Marie had explained, her voice filled with pain, "had to make a difficult decision the moment we got the phone call. Your parents' car had just been found in the river." She dabbed at the tears in her eyes. "All six of us—including you two grandchildren—were supposed to be traveling together in that car that night. Luckily, we changed our plans at the last minute, and your parents were alone. Hearing of the accident, Jacques and I couldn't know what had really happened... or if it truly was an accident." Marie looked at Sophie. "We knew we had to protect our grandchildren, and we did what we thought was best. Jacques told the police that your brother and I had been in the car... our two bodies supposedly washed away in the current. Then your brother and I went into hiding with the Priory. Jacques, being a well-known man, couldn't disappear. It made sense that Sophie, being the oldest, would stay in Paris to be taught and raised by Jacques, close to the Priory's heart and protection." Her voice dropped to a whisper. "Separating the family was the hardest thing we ever had to do. Jacques and I saw each other only rarely, and always in the most secret places... under the Priory's protection. There are certain ceremonies that the brotherhood always follows."

Langdon felt the story was much bigger, but he also knew it wasn't his place to hear it. So he had gone outside. Now, looking up at the spires of Rosslyn, Langdon couldn't shake the uneasy feeling of Rosslyn's unsolved mystery. Is the Grail really here at Rosslyn? And if so, where are the blade and cup that Saunière mentioned in his poem?

"I'll take that," Marie said, pointing to Langdon's hand.

"Oh, thank you." Langdon held out his empty coffee cup.

She stared at him. "I meant your other hand, Mr. Langdon." Langdon looked down and realized he was holding Saunière's papyrus. He had taken it from the cryptex again, hoping to see something he had missed before. "Of course, I'm sorry." Marie looked amused as she took the paper. "I know a man at a bank in Paris who is very eager to get this rosewood box back. André Vernet was a close friend of Jacques, and Jacques trusted him completely. André would have done anything to honor Jacques's wishes for the care of this box."

Including shooting me, Langdon thought, deciding not to mention that he had probably broken the poor man's nose. Thinking of Paris, Langdon remembered the three sénéchaux who had been killed the night before. "And the Priory? What happens now?"

"Things are already happening, Mr. Langdon. The brotherhood has lasted for centuries, and it will continue. There are always those ready to step up and rebuild." All evening Langdon had suspected that Sophie's grandmother was closely involved in the Priory's operations. After all, the Priory had always had women members. Four Grand Masters had been women. The sénéchaux were traditionally men—the guardians—and yet women held a more honored position within the Priory and could reach the highest position from almost any rank. Langdon thought of Leigh Teabing and Westminster Abbey. It seemed like a lifetime ago. "Was the Church pressuring your husband not to release the Sangreal documents at the End of Days?"

"Goodness no. The End of Days is a legend for those who are paranoid. There's nothing in the Priory's teachings that says when the Grail should be revealed. In fact, the Priory has always believed that the Grail should never be revealed."

"Never?" Langdon was shocked.

"It's the mystery and wonder that feeds our souls, not the Grail itself. The beauty of the Grail is in its spiritual nature." Marie Chauvel looked up at Rosslyn now. "For some, the Grail is a cup that will bring them eternal life. For others, it's the search for lost documents and secret history. And for most, I think the Holy Grail is simply a grand idea... a glorious, impossible treasure that somehow, even in today's chaotic world, inspires us."

"But if the Sangreal documents stay hidden, the story of Mary Magdalene will be lost forever," Langdon said.

"Will it? Look around. Her story is being told in art, music, and books. More and more each day. Things are changing. We are starting to understand the dangers of our past... and our harmful ways. We are beginning to feel the need to bring back the sacred feminine." She paused. "You said you're writing a book about the symbols of the sacred feminine, right?"

"I am."

She smiled. "Finish it, Mr. Langdon. Share her story. The world needs storytellers today." Langdon was quiet, feeling the importance of her words. Across the open space, a new moon was rising above the trees.

Turning to Rosslyn, Langdon felt like a child wanting to know its secrets. Don't ask, he told himself. Now is not the time. He looked at the papyrus in Marie's hand, then back at Rosslyn.

"Ask the question, Mr. Langdon," Marie said, smiling. "You have earned it." Langdon felt his face get warm.

"You want to know if the Grail is here at Rosslyn."

"Can you tell me?"

She sighed, pretending to be annoyed. "Why can't men just leave the Grail alone?" She laughed, clearly enjoying herself. "Why do you think it's here?" Langdon pointed to the papyrus in her hand. "Your husband's poem mentions Rosslyn, but it also talks about a blade and chalice guarding the Grail. I didn't see any blade and chalice symbols up there."

"The blade and chalice?" Marie asked. "What do they look like?" Langdon felt she was teasing him, but he joined in, quickly describing the symbols. She looked like she vaguely remembered something. "Ah, yes, of course. The blade stands for everything masculine. I think it's drawn like this, right?" She used her finger to draw a shape on her palm.

"Yes," Langdon said. Marie had drawn the less common "closed" blade, even though Langdon had seen the symbol drawn both ways.

"And the opposite," she said, drawing on her palm again, "is the chalice, which stands for the feminine."

"Correct," Langdon said.

"And you're saying that in all the hundreds of symbols we have here in Rosslyn Chapel, these two

shapes don't appear anywhere?"

"I didn't see them."

"And if I show them to you, will you get some sleep?"

Before Langdon could answer, Marie Chauvel had left the porch and was walking toward the chapel. Langdon hurried after her. Inside the old building, Marie turned on the lights and pointed to the middle of the sanctuary floor. "There you are, Mr. Langdon. The blade and chalice." Langdon looked at the worn stone floor. It was empty. "There's nothing here...." Marie sighed and started walking along the well-known path worn into the chapel floor, the same path Langdon had seen the visitors walking earlier. As his eyes got used to seeing the large symbol, he still felt confused. "But that's the Star of Dav—"

Langdon stopped, speechless with surprise as he understood.

The blade and chalice.

Joined as one.

The Star of David... the perfect joining of man and woman... Solomon's Seal... marking the most sacred place, where the male and female gods — Yahweh and Shekinah — were believed to live. Langdon paused to find his words. "The verse does lead here to Rosslyn. Exactly. Perfectly."

Marie smiled. "So it seems."

The idea made him uneasy. "So the Holy Grail is in the vault below us?" She laughed. "Only in spirit. One of the Priory's oldest duties was to one day bring the Grail back to her homeland of France, where she could rest forever. For centuries, she was moved around the country for safety. Very undignified. Jacques's job when he became Grand Master was to restore her honor by returning her to France and building her a resting place worthy of a queen."

"And he did it?"

Now her face turned serious. "Mr. Langdon, after all you've done for me tonight, and as curator of the Rosslyn Trust, I can tell you for sure that the Grail is no longer here." Langdon decided to push further. "But the keystone is supposed to point to where the Holy Grail is hidden now. Why does it point to Rosslyn?"

"Maybe you're misunderstanding its meaning. Remember, the Grail can be misleading. As could my late husband."

"But how much clearer could he be?" he asked. "We're standing over an underground vault marked by the blade and chalice, under a ceiling of stars, surrounded by the art of Master Masons. Everything points to Rosslyn."

"Alright, let me see this strange poem." She unrolled the papyrus and read the poem aloud slowly and carefully.

The Holy Grail 'neath ancient Roslin waits.

The blade and chalice guarding o'er Her gates.

Adorned in masters' loving art, She lies.

She rests at last beneath the starry skies.

When she finished, she paused for a few seconds, then smiled knowingly. "Aah, Jacques."

Langdon watched her, waiting. "You understand this?"

"As you saw on the chapel floor, Mr. Langdon, there are many ways to look at simple things."

Langdon struggled to understand. Everything about Jacques Saunière seemed to have hidden meanings, but Langdon couldn't see them.

Marie yawned, sounding tired. "Mr. Langdon, I'll admit something to you. I've never officially known where the Grail is right now. But, of course, I was married to someone very important... and I have a strong intuition." Langdon started to speak, but Marie kept talking. "I'm sorry that after all your effort, you'll be leaving Rosslyn without any real answers. But, something tells me you will eventually find what you're looking for. One day you'll understand." She smiled. "And when you do, I trust that you, of all people, can keep a secret." There was a sound of someone coming to the doorway. "You both vanished," Sophie said, walking in.

"I was just leaving," her grandmother said, walking to Sophie at the door. "Good night, princess." She kissed Sophie's forehead. "Don't keep Mr. Langdon out too late." Langdon and Sophie watched her grandmother walk back toward the stone house. When Sophie turned to him, her eyes showed deep emotion. "Not the ending I expected." Me neither, he thought. Langdon could see she was overwhelmed. The news tonight had changed her life. "Are you okay? It's a lot to take in." She smiled quietly. "I have a family. That's where I'll start. Learning who we are and where we came from will take time."

Langdon said nothing.

"Will you stay with us longer?" Sophie asked. "At least for a few days?" Langdon sighed, wanting it very much. "You need time with your family, Sophie. I'm going back to Paris in the morning."

She looked sad but seemed to agree it was best. They were silent for a long time. Then Sophie took his hand and led him out of the chapel. They walked to a small hill. From there, the Scottish countryside spread out before them, lit by pale moonlight through the thinning clouds. They stood in silence, holding hands, both fighting their tiredness. The stars were starting to appear, but to the east, one light shone brighter than the others. Langdon smiled when he saw it. It was Venus, the ancient Goddess, shining her steady light.

The night grew cooler, with a breeze from the lowlands. After a while, Langdon looked at Sophie. Her eyes were closed, her lips relaxed in a happy smile. Langdon felt his own eyes getting heavy. Reluctantly, he squeezed her hand. "Sophie?" Slowly, she opened her eyes and turned to him. Her face was beautiful in the moonlight. She gave him a sleepy smile. "Hi."

Langdon felt sad to be returning to Paris without her. "I might be gone before you wake up." He paused, feeling a lump in his throat. "I'm sorry, I'm not good at—"

Sophie reached out and touched his face gently. Then, she leaned forward and kissed his cheek tenderly. "When can I see you again?" Langdon was taken by surprise, lost in her eyes. "When?" He paused, wondering if she knew how much he wanted to know the same thing. "Well, I'm giving a lecture at a conference in Florence next month. I'll be there for a week with not much to do."

"Is that an invitation?"

"We'd be living well. They're giving me a room at the Brunelleschi." Sophie smiled playfully. "You assume a lot, Mr. Langdon."

He felt bad about how it sounded. "What I meant—"

I would love nothing more than to meet you in Florence, Robert. But on one condition." Her tone turned serious. "No museums, no churches, no tombs, no art, no relics."

"In Florence? For a week? There's nothing else to do."

Sophie leaned forward and kissed him again, now on the lips. Their bodies came together, softly at first, and then completely. When she pulled away, her eyes were full of promise.

"Right," Langdon managed. "It's a date."

Epilogue

Robert Langdon awoke with a start. He had been dreaming. The bathrobe beside his bed bore the monogram HOTEL RITZ PARIS. He saw a dim light filtering through the blinds. Is it dusk or dawn? he wondered.

Langdon's body felt warm and deeply content. He had slept most of the last two days. Sitting up slowly in bed, he now realized what had awoken him... the strangest thought. For days he had been trying to make sense of so much information, but now Langdon found himself focused on something he'd not thought of before.

Could it be?

He remained still for a long moment.

Getting out of bed, he walked to the marble shower. Stepping inside, he let the powerful jets massage his shoulders. Still, the thought fascinated him.

Impossible.

Twenty minutes later, Langdon left the Hotel Ritz and entered Place Vendôme. Night was coming. The lack of sleep had left him confused... but his mind felt strangely clear. He had planned to stop in the hotel lobby for a coffee to clear his head, but instead, his legs took him straight out the front door and into the growing darkness of Paris. Walking east on Rue des Petits Champs, Langdon felt a rising excitement. He turned south onto Rue Richelieu, where the air smelled sweet with jasmine from the elegant gardens of the Palais Royal.

He kept going south until he saw what he wanted—the well-known royal arcade—a shiny stretch of polished black marble. Stepping onto it, Langdon looked at the ground. Quickly, he found what he knew was there—several bronze medallions set into the ground in a perfectly straight line. Each disk was five inches across and marked with the letters N and S.

North. South.

He turned directly south, letting his eyes follow the long line made by the medallions. He started walking again, following the path, watching the ground as he went. As he crossed the corner of the Comédie-Française, another bronze medallion appeared beneath his feet. Yes!

Langdon had learned years ago that the streets of Paris had 135 of these bronze markers, set into sidewalks, courtyards, and streets, running north-south across the city. He had once followed the line from Sacré-Coeur, north across the Seine, and finally to the old Paris Observatory. There he learned the importance of the sacred path it marked. The earth's first prime meridian.

The world's original zero longitude.

Paris's ancient Rose Line.

Now, as Langdon rushed across Rue de Rivoli, he could feel his destination getting closer. Less than a block away.

The Holy Grail 'neath ancient Roslin waits.

The revelations were coming now, one after another. Saunière's old-fashioned spelling of Roslin... the blade and chalice... the tomb filled with art by masters.

Is that why Saunière needed to talk to me? Had I accidentally figured it out?

He started to jog, feeling the Rose Line under his feet, guiding him, pulling him where he needed to go. As he walked into the long Passage Richelieu tunnel, the hairs on his neck stood up in anticipation. He knew that at the end of this tunnel was the most mysterious monument in Paris—planned and ordered in the 1980s by François Mitterrand, a man people said moved in secret groups, a man whose final gift to Paris was a place Langdon had visited just days before.

A different life.

With a final burst of energy, Langdon ran out of the passageway into the familiar courtyard and stopped. Out of breath, he looked up, slowly, in disbelief, at the shining structure in front of him.

The Louvre Pyramid.

Shining in the dark.

He admired it for only a moment. He was more interested in what was to his right. Turning, he felt his feet again following the invisible path of the ancient Rose Line, taking him across the courtyard to the Carrousel du Louvre—the huge circle of grass surrounded by trimmed bushes—once the place for Paris's ancient nature festivals... happy celebrations of fertility and the Goddess.

Langdon felt like he was stepping into another world as he went over the bushes to the grassy area inside. This sacred place was now marked by one of the city's strangest monuments. There in the center, going into the earth like a crystal hole, was the giant upside-down pyramid of glass that he had seen a few nights ago when he entered the Louvre's underground entrance hall. La Pyramide Inversée.

Shaking, Langdon walked to the edge and looked down into the Louvre's large underground area, lit with yellow light. He was looking not just at the massive upside-down pyramid, but at what was directly under it. There, on the floor of the room below, was the smallest of structures... a structure Langdon had mentioned in his book.

Langdon now felt the exciting rush of an incredible possibility. Looking up at the Louvre again, he felt the museum's massive presence surround him... hallways filled with the world's greatest art.

Da Vinci... Botticelli...

Covered in the beautiful art of masters, She lies.

Filled with wonder, he looked down again through the glass at the small structure below. I must go down there!

Leaving the circle, he quickly walked across the courtyard back to the Louvre's tall pyramid entrance. The last visitors of the day were leaving the museum. Going through the revolving door, Langdon walked down the curved staircase into the pyramid. He felt the air getting cooler. When he reached the bottom, he entered the long tunnel that went under the Louvre's courtyard, back toward La Pyramide Inversée. At the end of the tunnel, he entered a large room. Right in front of him, hanging down from above, shone the inverted pyramid—a stunning V-shaped form of glass. The Chalice.

Langdon's eyes followed its narrowing shape down to its tip, hanging only six feet above the floor. There, right below it, was the small structure.

A tiny pyramid. Only three feet tall. The only structure in this huge complex that was built so small.

Langdon's paper, while talking about the Louvre's large collection of goddess art, had briefly mentioned this small pyramid. "The miniature structure itself sticks up through the floor as if it were the tip of an iceberg — the top of a huge, pyramid-shaped vault, hidden below like a secret chamber."

Lit by the soft lights of the empty mezzanine, the two pyramids pointed at each other, their bodies perfectly lined up, their tips almost touching.

The Chalice above. The Blade below.

The blade and chalice guarding Her gates.

Langdon heard Marie Chauvel's words. One day you will understand.

He was standing beneath the ancient Rose Line, surrounded by the work of masters. What better place for Saunière to watch? Now at last, he felt he understood the true meaning of the Grand Master's verse. Raising his eyes to heaven, he gazed upward through the glass to a glorious, star-filled night.

She rests at last beneath the starry skies.

Like the murmurs of spirits in the darkness, forgotten words echoed. The quest for the Holy Grail is the quest to kneel before the bones of Mary Magdalene. A journey to pray at the feet of the outcast one.

With a sudden feeling of reverence, Robert Langdon fell to his knees. For a moment, he thought he heard a woman's voice... the wisdom of the ages... whispering up from the depths of the earth.
